# 道德真经疏义（宋江澂）

**Commentary on the *Dao De Jing* (Moral Teachings of the *Dao De Jing*)**
*Title:* **Commentary on the *Dao De Jing* (Moral Teachings of the *Dao De Jing*)**
*By Jiang Cheng (宋江澄)*
*Fourteen Scrolls*
*Source Text:* *Zhengtong Daozang* (正统道藏), *Dongshen* (洞神) Section, *Yujue* (玉诀) Category

---

**Preface to the Commentary on the *Dao De Jing***

With reverence, I acknowledge the Sacred Sovereign’s enlightenment, whose teachings illuminate the Dao and the True. In moments of leisure and clarity, he selected *Laozi’s* *Dao De Jing*, elucidating each sentence to bless the world.

As a subject among the assembled scholars, a student of the Imperial Academy, I purified my mind and, with humility, read the Sovereign’s work. I reflect: no text is more useful than the *Dao De Jing*, yet *Laozi’s* Five Thousand Words are the very essence of Dao and virtue. Though concise, they encompass the vast; though varied, they harmonize. They restore the natural order and govern the present age. Their words are simple, yet their meaning profound—only a Sage could articulate them thus.

Since the Zhou Dynasty, countless scholars have expounded upon it, numbering over a hundred schools, yet most are clouded by their own biases, failing to grasp the True Dao. Their interpretations, though well-intentioned to honor the Sage’s teachings, often contradict them. Only those who deeply enter the Dao and attain divine insight can truly comprehend it.

I reverently observe that the Emperor, taking One as the standard for the world, embracing Unity as the model for all, embodies simplicity and sincerity, extending governance to both humanity and Heaven. The subtleties of the Dao reside inherently in virtue, yet must be spoken and believed, guiding scholars to their rightful path.

The Sovereign’s learning is profound and complete, like the *Yi Jing* with its *Xici* (系辞), truly the words of a Sage. Yet the Dao, once spoken, cannot be fully perceived by sight or hearing—its application is boundless. How then can I, with limited senses, describe its ineffable depths with finite words? I dare not presume, knowing my understanding falls short.

I have observed how the Emperor’s predecessors, such as the Tang Emperor, composed refined commentaries, and Du Guangting (杜光庭) further expounded with the *Guangsheng Yi* (广圣义). How much less am I, though long instructed, to dare follow in their footsteps? Thus, I humbly offer this commentary, though I know my words are inadequate.

**Jiang Cheng’s Preface**

---

**Commentary on the *Dao De Jing* – Scroll One**
*By Jiang Cheng, Student of the Imperial Academy*

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
*The Dao* is the shared path of all people.
*Virtue* is what the heart naturally attains.
*The Dao* endures through all ages without decay,
while *virtue* fulfills one’s nature and remains constant.

When *Laozi* lived in the late Zhou, the Dao declined and virtue waned. Thus, he wrote ninety-nine chapters to illuminate the eternal principles of Dao and virtue, calling it the *Jing* (经). His words are concise, his meaning profound—scholars must meditate deeply to grasp them.

**Jiang Cheng’s Commentary:**

That all things follow it is called the *Dao*.
The *Dao* pervades all existence, yet is not confined—neither the vastness of Heaven and Earth nor the minutiae of an autumn hair escape its embrace. Even within boundaries, nothing exists without it. How much more so for humanity?

Mencius said: *"The Dao is like a great road."* Yet the character for *Dao* (道) shares its form with *"road"* (道路), for humanity cannot traverse beyond it. Thus, it is clear that the *Dao* is the shared path of all.

That which the *Dao* embodies within us is called *virtue*.
*Virtue* in humanity is complete in life and sufficient in allotment. Before form takes shape, it enables existence; it requires no external cultivation nor reliance on others—it is self-derived. What else could it be?

Yang Zhu said: *"Virtue is what one attains."* The character for *virtue* (德) derives from *"straight heart"* (直心), meaning it is not acquired from others but is self-attained. Thus, *virtue* is what the heart naturally possesses.

Moreover, the *Dao* is boundless while *virtue* is cultivated. Only because the *Dao* is boundless does it remain unchanged through ages, unconstrained by time—ever renewing, never ending. The *Jing* states: *"The Dao is enduring."* This is why it persists through all ages without decay.

Only because *virtue* is cultivated does it expand the four cardinal virtues, transforming myriad forms without alteration, nurturing and fulfilling them without separation. The *Classics* say: *"Virtue is the beginning of nature."* This is why it perpetually resides within one’s nature.

The *Dao* has ascents and descents; *virtue* has flourishing and decline. In eras of order, the *Dao* is exalted and *virtue* thrives, while in times of decay, the *Dao* wanes and *virtue* diminishes. By the late Zhou, the Great Dao had faded, and *virtue* further declined, fragmenting into the myriad doctrines of the Hundred Schools, obscured by conflicting theories—neither comprehensive nor pure. Thus, the arts of the *Dao* became divided across the land.

*Laozi*, pitying that the wondrous *Dao* had no successor and that the people remained unaware, took *emptiness* as his principle and *non-action* as his foundation, aligning with the ultimate patterns of nature. He wrote ninety-nine chapters to illuminate the eternal principles of Dao and virtue, so that those who read his words might perceive the completeness of Heaven and Earth and the grand essence of antiquity. This is why his work differs from the Hundred Schools and is called the *Jing*.

For the *Jing* has a fixed structure, hence it is called *"constant"*—like the *Jing* (经) of stars, which adorn the heavens unmoving, or the *Jing* (经) of weaving, which guides the loom without alteration. Thus, it is called the *Jing*.

Observe his work: it encompasses all subtleties, vast and complete, yet contains no more than five thousand words. Its language is concise. One year cannot exhaust its transformations; a lifetime cannot fathom its depths. Its meaning is profound.

The scholar who merely knows the words yet fails to grasp their meaning, or who attains the meaning yet clings to the words, has not truly understood. The *Yǔ* (语, "Sayings") states: *"Silent reflection reveals its essence."* Mencius says: *"The gentleman cultivates deeply through the Dao, seeking to attain understanding on his own."* The eternal principles of Dao and virtue cannot be fully captured by mere speech or shallow perception; only through the heart’s intuition and divine comprehension can one transcend the words and their intent, recognizing the unrecognizable and thus drawing from their profound depths. This is the true essence of this book.

Thus, the Sage’s composition begins with the first chapter, expounding the marvels of the Dao to awaken the scholar’s mind, offering the instruction to reflect in silence and cultivate deeply.

**Chapter One: The Dao That Can Be Spoken**

The Dao that can be spoken of is not the eternal Dao;
The name that can be named is not the eternal Name.

Huizong’s annotation says: *"The Dao is without beginning; it cannot be spoken of. When spoken of, it is not the true Dao."*

Again, it says: *"The Dao cannot be named."* Yet the Dao that can be spoken of and named—like the unfolding of seasons or the rise and fall of affairs—responds to what is appropriate, waning and waxing in turn, not an unchanging truth. The eternal Dao and eternal Name are self-originating, self-rooted: before Heaven and Earth existed, they were already fixed and enduring from ancient times. The Lord Fu Xi attained this Dao and sat in the *Shǎoguǎng* (少广, "Minor Expansion"). No one knows its beginning, no one knows its end.

The *Shūyì* (疏义, "Explanatory Notes") say: *"What can be spoken of are the coarse aspects of things."* The utmost subtlety of the Dao is far removed from such things, hence it cannot be spoken of.

When Confucius saw Wen Bo Xue (温伯雪), his gaze struck and the Dao was preserved—this is why. Zhuangzi recorded the words of the Dao’s beginning: *"The Dao cannot be spoken of; when spoken of, it is not the true Dao."* Is this what is meant?

At the dawn of creation, there was *wu* (无, "non-being") and *you* (有, "being"). Non-being had no name; when things formed and their numbers were fixed, then names and perceptions arose. The Dao is not confined by things nor trapped by numbers—when viewed, it cannot be seen. Who could name it? This is what Zhuangzi called *"the great Dao does not affirm itself."*

Again, it says: *"The Dao cannot be named."* Is this what is meant?

Indeed, the principles of the world have what is *ke* (可, "affirmable") yet also what is *buke* (不可, "unaffirmable"). When form takes shape, there is what cannot yet be affirmed, and when the pattern emerges, it is both affirmable and unaffirmable, affirmable yet unaffirmable. Things have no fixed appropriateness, affairs no fixed wrongness; the waxing and waning of all things, the flourishing and decline of seasons—spring and summer first, autumn and winter last—each season’s rise and fall, hidden yet manifest, manifest yet hidden, formed then destroyed, destroyed then formed, what was once true now false, what was once discordant now harmonious—all follow the fittingness of the time. When the Dao that can be spoken of and named responds to what is appropriate, waning and waxing in turn, it is not the true eternal Dao.

Although names and realities exist, they belong to things; what can be spoken of and perceived grows ever more distant when spoken of. Though the Dao cannot be spoken of, there remains a Dao that is not spoken of; though the Name cannot be named, there remains a Name that is not named. The Dao that is not spoken of is called the *constant Dao*; the Name that is not named is called the *constant Name*.

The meaning of "constant" lies in its enduring completion without change, its permanence through time. The Dao as constant is spoken of in the *Jing*: *"It stands alone, unaltered."* The Name as constant is spoken of in the *Jing*: *"From ancient times to the present, its name does not fade."* Yet things that arise must have their origins and roots, whereas the constant Dao and constant Name have no origins or roots. Things arise from Heaven and Earth, but the constant Dao and constant Name precede Heaven and Earth in their birth.

Though *qi* (气) gathers and scatters, this Dao has no departure or arrival; though forms emerge and perish, this Dao has no existence or annihilation. *"Self-originating, self-rooted: before Heaven and Earth existed, it was already fixed and enduring from ancient times."* Those who attain this Dao ascend to supreme sovereignty, which is why Fu Xi attained it and inherited the *Qi Mu* (气母, "Mother of Qi").

The *Qi Mu* is the origin of *qi*, the primordial source from which all *qi* arises. Attaining it allows one to wander within the *Tai Chu* (太初, "Supreme Beginning"), as the *Jing* says: *"Having known its children, one must then guard its mother."* This is the way to eternal life, which is why the Western Sovereign Mother sits in the *Shǎoguǎng*, her beginning unknown, her end unknown.

For the Dao has no head at its start, no tail at its end. Those who embody the Dao—who could trace its origin? Who could limit its conclusion? The *Jing* says: *"Having obtained its mother, one knows its children."* The Dao is nameless at its beginning, yet it is the mother of Heaven and Earth; the named is the mother of all things.

Huizong’s annotation says: *"The Dao is ever nameless, yet Heaven and Earth depend upon it to arise—this is what Zhuangzi called 'generating Heaven and generating Earth.'"*

Before Heaven and Earth existed, who could name it? Thus, the nameless is the beginning of Heaven and Earth. With Heaven and Earth, all things then arise, so the named is the mother of all things.

The *Shūyì* say: *"The Dao’s utmost subtlety cannot be sought through wisdom nor found through form—though one desires to name it, it cannot be attained."* The Dao’s name is a borrowed designation; calling it the Dao is merely a forced naming. This is the Dao hidden within the nameless.

*"The Dao is ever nameless"*—this is what is meant. The *Yijing* (易) states: *"The Great Polarity (太极) gives rise to the Two Principles (两仪)."* Heaven and Earth are the greatest of formed things, yet formed things arise from the formless. How then do Heaven and Earth arise? It is from the Dao alone, *"the mother of all beneath the heavens."* This is what Zhuangzi called *"generating Heaven and generating Earth."*

The nameless has no reality—it exists in the void of things. When things coalesce in indistinct unity, before Heaven and Earth arise, it is still void. Thus, before Heaven and Earth existed, who could name it? Though Heaven and Earth are the parents of all things, all things depend upon them to arise. The world has a beginning; calling it the mother of the world means Heaven and Earth depend upon the Dao to manifest.

All things draw their origin from the Great Primordium (*Buqian* 乾元), yet though their source is vast, they still rely upon it to flourish. All things draw their life from the Supreme Receptivity (*Kunyuan* 坤元), yet though their foundation is profound, they still depend upon it to manifest. When we trace what sustains the singular thread of the One, what substance is it? It is but the Dao alone. This is why it is said, *"Only after Heaven and Earth arise do all things come into being."* This is the Dao made manifest in named existence.

The Dao is singular in essence. When gathered, it forms substance; when dispersed, it marks the beginning. From its original source, it remains hidden in the Nameless—this is called the Beginning. From its manifestation, it appears in the Named—this is called the Mother. Though the terms differ, their root is one.

Though Heaven and Earth mark the Beginning, today’s scholars recognize this. If one truly perceives the Dao, then even before Heaven and Earth existed, their origin could be known. This is how the Sage knows Heaven and Earth’s Beginning *after* their existence.

To observe its subtlety, one must remain in the Constant Void;
To grasp its breadth, one must embrace the Constant Manifest.

The *Huizong* commentary states: Zhuangzi said, *"Establish it in the Constant Void."* To erect nothing is the Constant Void. To discard nothing is the Constant Manifest. The Constant Void lies in principle—its upper reaches are unfathomable, the essence of all beneath the heavens, hence its subtlety is observed. The Constant Manifest lies in affairs—its lower reaches are boundless, the transformation of all beneath the heavens, hence its breadth is grasped.

The realms of Having and Not-Having intertwine, where subtlety and breadth reside. Great wisdom unites both perspectives, leaving nothing unattainable. In the hazy and indistinct, there are forms and things. Small wisdom clings to one-sidedness, blinded by partiality, abandoning the Manifest for the Void, or the Void for the Manifest, neglecting the root for the branches—thus the Dao is fractured for all beneath the heavens.

The *Explanatory Notes* say: Without motion, there is generation of the Manifest—this is its utility. When the Manifest reaches its limit, it returns to the Void—this is its function. The Manifest does not depart from the Void, so the Void is not truly Void. The Void does not abandon the Manifest, so the Manifest is not truly Manifest. To establish it in the Constant Void is to affirm the Void that is not Void. To establish it in the Constant Manifest is to affirm the Manifest that is not Manifest. Though transformations are boundless, their foundation remains unchanging—this is why it is called Constant.

Zhuangzi expounds Laozi’s teachings: *"Establish it in the Constant Void."* Yet his text begins with this very principle, deeply grasping the logic of Having and Not-Having. Thus, when purifying away all things, even sage wisdom, righteousness, and propriety would be discarded—this is the Constant Void. When establishing all laws, even affairs, forms, and names would be preserved—this is the Constant Manifest. The Constant Void in principle has never given rise to things; hidden and difficult to know, even divine vision cannot perceive its form. Hence, its upper reaches are unfathomable, revealing the small yet subtle—entering the divine through its minuteness, thus becoming the essence of all beneath the heavens. The Constant Manifest in affairs encompasses all virtues; evident and easily seen, it is manifest yet concealed. Hence, its lower reaches are not obscured, revealing the great yet broad—emerging from clarity and vastness, thus becoming the transformation of all beneath the heavens.

Confucius composed the *Yijing*. When about to act or move, he spoke of nothing finer than the essence of all beneath the heavens—this is because from the Void to the Manifest, principle dictates so. In the arrangement of trigrams and their transformations, he spoke of nothing broader than the transformation of all beneath the heavens—this is because gathering the Manifest into the Void, affairs dictate so.

The Constant Void and Constant Manifest arise from the same source; the utmost subtlety and transformation stem from the same divine origin. Though their names differ, their principle is one.

Though Having and Not-Having are unified, their utility and application shift—
In the Manifest, they are concealed; in the Void, they are revealed.
Called subtle, called wondrous—merely their dwelling place.

Only great wisdom, viewing near and far, knows that the root of the Manifest is not truly Manifest—
Those who cling to it as Manifest still harbor the Void within.
Knows that the Void is not truly Void—
Those who reject it as Void still contain the Manifest.

In the hazy and indistinct, forms are visible;
In the indistinct and hazy, things coalesce indistinctly.

The obscure interplay of Having and Not-Having—
When observed through the Dao, all is exhausted,
And the whole becomes one, leaving nothing unattainable.

Common folk, bound by small wisdom, are blind to the great principle.
Some, obscured by the Dao’s stillness, discard the Manifest for the Void,
Drowning in seclusion, taking the non-Void as Void;
Some, lost in the Dao’s motion, cling to superficiality,
Neglecting the root for the branches, entangled in forms,
Taking the non-Manifest as Manifest;
Or making nothingness the way, fixated on one aspect—
A hundred schools, each boasting their strengths.
Thus the Dao is fractured for all beneath the heavens.

These two—though differing in name, they share the same origin—
Both are called the Mysterious.

The *Huizong* commentary states: The Dao is formless—
Who can grasp its breadth and subtlety?
Things and self share the same root;
Right and wrong arise from the same *qi*—
Thus they are both called the Mysterious.

The deluded of the world seek falsehood for truth,
Moving farther from truth, unaware that
Having and Not-Having are but differing names.

The *Explanatory Notes* say: The true origin is singular and complete,
Without form yet encompassing all—
The form of the formless, exhausting the boundless.
Only breadth and subtlety are indistinct, without division.
Thus the Dao is formless—
Who can grasp its breadth and subtlety?

From the Dao descending, distinctions arise.
The ignorant, clinging to self and objects, give rise to the illusion of self and other.
This is that, and that is this—
Yet from the indistinct emergence of *qi*, *qi* is singular.
Thus, things and self share the same root;
Right and wrong arise from the same *qi*—
Converging all differences into one origin—
Who is truly Having, who truly Not-Having?

Thus, the same is called *Xuan* [玄].
The confused of the world take the *wu* [無] for true and the *you* [有] for delusion,
Gathering delusion to seek truth, moving farther from truth—
Yet they fail to know that *wu* is the subtle *you*, and *you* is the true *wu*.
Names are contrary, yet their essence harmonizes.

*Xuan* again *xuan*—the gateway to all subtleties.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation says: The *Suwen* [素问] states: *Xuan* gives birth to the numinous.
The *Yijing* [易] says: The numinous—
This is the subtle speech that encompasses all things.

To refine subtlety to its utmost is *xuan*.
*Xuan* is the color of Heaven.
What is colored by color is manifest,
Yet the coloring itself is never fully revealed.
*Xuan* again *xuan*—this is what is meant by "coloring."

The subtle principles of *xuan* pervade all things:
Heaven’s movement, Earth’s repose, humanity’s vitality,
The flourishing of all creatures—
All are subtle, all emerging from *xuan*.
Thus it is called the gateway to all subtleties.

Confucius composed the *Yijing*, only reaching the *Shuogua* [说卦] to speak of subtlety,
While Laozi placed this at the beginning of his work.
The sages’ words complement each other as end and beginning.

The *Cushui* [疏义] says:
Wind generates wood, wood generates the liver,
In Heaven it is *xuan*, in humanity it is the Dao.
The Dao generates *yi* [一], thus the Dao descends and emerges,
Emerging to generate wisdom.
*Yi* generates *er* [二], thus *xuan* ascends and enters,
Entering to generate the numinous.

The numinous in its function—
Boundless, formless, pervasive without end,
Light and clear as Heaven, relying on it to move,
Heavy and turbid as Earth, relying on it to rest,
The harmonious *qi* of *Kun* [坤], relying on it to be vital—
Down to all creatures thriving in their roles,
All rely on this to flourish.
Thus, what subtly encompasses all things is none other than the numinous,
And what generates the numinous originates from *xuan*—
Hence it is the gateway to all subtleties.

Truly, when it emerges, it is vast and red;
When it enters, it is small and *xuan*.
Small and subtle—this is *xuan*,
Because it enters and becomes small.

If we speak of *xuan* virtue—
It is virtue entering and becoming small.
If we speak of *xuan* darkness—
It is entering into darkness and becoming small.
Though *xuan* is small, it remains subtle,
Yet it has not yet departed from color—
As the *Yijing* says: "Heaven is *xuan*, Earth is yellow,"
And Zhuangzi says: "The *xuan* sky."
Thus, *xuan* as color has red and black—
Red for *yang*, black for *yin*.
All things bear *yin* and embrace *yang*,
Yet *xuan* can be *yin* and can be *yang*—
Then what is confined to *yin* and *yang*?
Can it truly transcend this?
Much less *xuan* itself!

What constitutes *xuan* is *xuan* again *xuan*—
As the *Liezi* [列子] says: "Coloring."

All things emerge yet their gateway is unseen—
It lies here.

Although Confucius composed the *Yijing*, only reaching the *Shuogua* to speak of subtlety,
While Laozi placed this at the beginning of his work—
The *Yijing*, from exhausting principles to fulfilling nature and destiny,
Is a sequence of entering the Dao through speech,
And returning function to essence.
Laozi’s work, with returning to the root and restoring destiny as its priority,
Is a sudden manifestation of the Dao in action,
Showing that the numinous arises from essence in function.
The *Yijing* uses symbols and numbers, Laozi uses *you* and *wu* to demonstrate *xuan*—
Though their words differ, they complement each other as beginning and end.
Though their teachings seem disjointed, their essence is one.

**Chapter Two: All Under Heaven Know**

All under Heaven know beauty when it is beautiful—then ugliness arises;
All know goodness when it is good—then not-goodness arises.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
The Dao has no differing forms—who is beautiful or ugly?
Nature is inherently unified—who is good or bad?
There is beauty, and ugliness is its opposite,
Thus it is said: All under Heaven know beauty when it is beautiful—then ugliness arises.
There is goodness, and not-goodness is its opposite,
Thus it is said: All know goodness when it is good—then not-goodness arises.

What the world deems beautiful is the numinous and strange,
What it deems ugly is the stinking and rotten.
The numinous and strange transform into the stinking and rotten,
The stinking and rotten transform into the numinous and strange—
Then how can beauty and ugliness be distinguished?

What was once right may later be wrong,
What is now discarded may later be used—
Then how can good and not-good be chosen?

The sage embodies the true *wu* while constantly *you*,
Manifests subtle function while constantly *wu*—
Beauty and ugliness, good and not-good,
Are to be simplified yet cannot be grasped—
How then can ugliness and not-goodness burden?

The *Cushui* says:
Under Heaven, there is no second Dao.
From their unity, the names of beauty and ugliness vanish,
One nature without nature—
From their origin, the distinctions of good and evil do not stand.
Yet there is high and low, with the latter as its subordinate,
Thus there is beauty with ugliness as its opposite,
There is left and right, with the latter as its subordinate,
Thus there is good with not-good as its opposite.

To have one without the other is to desire *yin* without *yang*—
How can this be achieved?

The people of the world know one aspect yet not the other,
Taking the numinous and strange as beautiful,
The stinking and rotten as ugly,
Yet beauty and ugliness fill the heart—
This is deemed right, that is deemed wrong,
And good and not-good oppose each other.

Yet they fail to know that Great Transformation is unceasing,
Instantly shifting—
The straight becomes strange, the good becomes ominous,
The stinking and rotten, the numinous and strange—
Alternating, transforming.
Initially, there is no beauty or ugliness,
Principle has no constant right,
Affairs have no constant wrong—
Sometimes right, sometimes wrong,
Gained then lost.
Initially, there is no good or not-good,
Life and death, giving and taking—
Also divided, also successive—
How can one judge them as different?

Only the sage awakens to this and merges into the mysterious,
Exhausts the truth of things while guarding their root,
Commands the transformation of things while guarding their origin—
Though *wuwei*, yet not neglectful of action,
Embodying true *wu* while constantly *you*,
Though engaged in affairs, yet emerging from non-action,
Manifesting subtle function while constantly *wu*.
Thus, extinguishing preferences in unity,
And entrusting right and wrong to the two processes.

The beautiful is beautiful of itself—I do not know it as beautiful;
The ugly is ugly of itself—I do not know it as ugly.
The good, I affirm as good;
The not-good, I also affirm as good.
Strange and wondrous, the Dao flows as one—
Though one might seek to simplify it, it cannot be done.
Then how can evil and not-good weigh me down?

Thus, the arising of having from non-being,
The completion of easy from difficult,
The contrast of long and short,
The tilt of high and low,
The harmony of sound and response,
The succession of before and after.

**Commentary by Emperor Huizong:**
Before the *Taiyi* was differentiated, all phenomena were of one body.
Once the Two Principles emerged, all things became paired opposites.
These six pairs—
Where myriad transformations intersect,
Where myriad thoughts arise,
Where divergent paths begin—
Are the snares by which the world is ensnared and cannot escape.

Non-being generates being, being returns to non-being—
Thus it is said: *having and non-being generate each other.*
There is difficulty in crossing perilous terrain,
Yet one knows the ease of traversing level ground—
Thus it is said: *difficult and easy complete each other.*
Long and short contrast, as with inches and feet.
High and low tilt against each other, as with mountains and marshes.
Sound strikes and echo responds—
Thus it is said: *sound and response harmonize.*
Form moves and shadow follows—
Thus it is said: *before and after succeed each other.*

The movement of yin and yang,
The progression of the four seasons,
The principles of all things—
Instantly formed, then instantly dissolved.
Its difficulty is like *wuwei* governing the world;
Its ease is like *wuwei* aligning with nature.

That which is long by nature cannot be severed,
That which is short by nature cannot be extended.
The sky’s height is not contrived—*the Dao’s own elevation;*
The earth’s depth is not imposed—*the Dao’s own descent.*

**Explanatory Notes:**
Before the *Taiyi*, the One Qi was undifferentiated,
A complete yet formless whole, undivided and indistinct—
Thus all phenomena were of one body.
The *Yijing* speaks of the *Taiji*, from which the Two Principles arise.
The Dao stands between opposites, giving rise to paired dependencies—
Thus all things become paired opposites.

The True Constant disperses into myriad transformations,
Unity divides into myriad thoughts,
Same origins diverge into varied paths—
None escape these six pairs.

Thus, when extended to the southeast, there is non-being generating being;
When contracted to the northwest, being reaches its limit and returns to non-being.
One and non-one cycle like this—*having and non-being generate each other.*

What is first difficult must later become easy;
What is first easy must later become difficult.
To encounter perilous difficulty,
One knows the ease of traversing level ground—
Thus difficulty and ease contrast like this.

Stretch out a finger to measure an inch,
Stretch out a hand to measure a foot—
Without the length of the foot,
One could not perceive the shortness of the inch—
This is *long and short contrasting each other.*
Mountains are steep and lean, marshes are fat and low—
Without the height of the mountains,
One could not perceive the depth of the marshes—
This is *high and low tilting each other.*

Sound strikes without generating sound, yet generates echo—
Thus sound strikes and echo responds—
This is *sound and response harmonizing.*
Form moves without generating form, yet generates shadow—
Thus form moves and shadow follows—
This is *before and after succeeding each other.*

In their principles and their affairs,
Their forms and their patterns,
Their sounds and their numbers—
Divided into yin and yang,
Arranged into the four seasons,
Dispersed into myriad things—
None escape these six pairs.

Instantly formed, they arise from non-being;
Instantly dissolved, they do not endure.
Applied to human affairs, one knows *wuwei* is not easy;
Retreating into obscurity, one knows *wuwei* is not difficult.

Ascending heights does not make one long—
Long is not excess, and innate long cannot be severed;
Descending lows does not make one short—
Short is not deficiency, and innate short cannot be extended.
The sky’s elevation is not contrived—*the Dao’s own nature;*
The earth’s depth is not imposed—*the Dao’s own nature.*

The **Dao** manifests through resonance and rhythm—
Then some are **official pitches**, some are **angle pitches**, following the drum’s movement.

In their ordered sequence and inherent logic,
The four seasons, long and short, each maintain their proper ranks.

All that enters the realm of **opposition and correspondence**—
Though all are the **Dao’s** abode,
They stray far from the **Dao** itself.

Thus the **sage** dwells in **non-action’s** affairs,
Practices **non-speech’s** teaching.

*Emperor Huizong’s annotation*: Dwelling in **non-action’s** affairs is what *Zhuangzi* calls "employing the world without contriving." Practicing **non-speech’s** teaching is what the *I Ching* calls "teaching through the **Dao’s** divine course, so the world submits."

To act brings **completion and deficiency**;
To speak brings **rightness and error**.
Yet never free from entanglement—
How could the **sage** stand alone above all things,
Transforming all things while none could burden them?

*Explanatory commentary*: What must be done yet concealed is **affairs**.
**Non-action’s** affairs mean action arises from **non-action**—
This is the so-called "employing the world without contriving."

Indeed, to employ the world is already to engage in **affairs**.
Only rooted in **non-action** does one act without touching **contrivance’s** edge.
Thus **Shun’s** inaction—
His decrees issued, yet all things flourished, attaining this.

**Cultivating the Dao** is what **teaching** means.
**Non-speech’s** teaching is through **personal example**, so people follow—
This is the so-called "teaching through the **Dao’s** divine course, so the world submits."

Indeed, to establish **teaching** is already to engage with things,
But when it springs from **divine Dao**, though **teaching** is given, the intent of speech is never uttered.
Thus **Wang Xi’s** upright stillness, never teaching, never deliberating—
He attained this.

Moreover, **non-action** is genuine,
**Action** is artifice.
If one labors in **affairs** and entangles in **human contrivance**,
How could there be **completion** without **deficiency**?

The wise do not speak,
Those who speak lack wisdom.
The **superior** speaks to the **inferior**, the **inferior** is taught—
Forcing instruction without pause,
One cannot avoid **rightness and error**.

The **sage** dawns and perceives **singularity**,
Embodies the **Dao’s** non-existence,
Responds to things while remaining in **non-being**.
In **affairs**, **non-action** yet **completion**;
In **teaching**, **non-speech** yet hearts delight.
Though myriad things fill the world,
They cannot prick his mind—
How could the **six pairs** burden him?

All things arise yet do not cling,
Are born yet do not possess,
Act yet do not rely,
**Completion** achieved yet not claimed.
Only because he does not claim,
Thus he does not depart.

*Emperor Huizong’s annotation*: All things arise together,
Responding to stimuli like a mirror reflecting forms—
Beauty and ugliness fully revealed.
Like valleys echoing sounds,
Good and bad all respond—
No refusal exists.
Hence it is said: "Arise yet do not cling."

They form and transform themselves,
Are born and colored by their own nature—
Each reaching their utmost height and grandeur,
Fulfilling their **innate essence**—
Who could possess them?
Hence it is said: "Are born yet do not possess."

Ordering all things yet not **perverse**,
Nurturing across ages yet not **benevolent**,
Covering heaven and earth,
Carving myriad forms—
Yet not **crafty**,
Hence it is said: "Act yet do not rely."

The **Dao’s** course in the four seasons—
When one **completes**, the other **retires**,
Heaven’s **Dao** indeed.
The **sage** embodies it,
Thus his **power** surpasses the world yet seems not of himself—
If he claims it, then he is already bewildered.
Hence it is said: "**Completion** achieved yet not claimed."

To claim brings **departure**—
Ancient and modern alike.
No claiming within himself,
Things cannot alter him.
Fitting to the **coming** is **timely**,
Fitting to the **going** is **orderly**—
What harm or gain could there be?
Hence it is said: "Only because he does not claim,
Thus he does not depart."

*Explanatory commentary*: With a single body facing the **myriad movements**,
With a single mind bearing the **world’s** demands—
Things may take from him,
Yet he remains **unperturbed**;
**Affairs** may press upon him,
Yet he remains **unmoved**.
Like a mirror responding yet not storing,
Like a valley empty yet able to receive—
Beauty and ugliness, good and bad—
No refusal exists.
If so, then his **universal response** and **harmonious fulfillment**
Are indeed abundant.

If he were to **flee** the **myriad** and **pursue** stillness,
Fearful of **excess** and **deficiency**,
Then how would things **open**?
How would **affairs** be **completed**?
How would the world **depend** on him?
This is called: "All things arise yet do not cling."

Self-formed, self-transformed,
Yet **endless generation and transformation**—
They do not **hoard** their **power**.
Self-shaped, self-colored,
Yet **myriad forms and hues**—
They do not **monopolize** their **completion**.
Those with **form** exert themselves through **form**,
Those with **essence** fulfill themselves through **essence**—
If he claims them, then he is already bewildered.
This is called: "Are born yet do not possess."

The **sage** responds to the world with **non-being** alone—
His ordering of all things is not from a **mind** of ordering.
Like **autumn’s** killing yet gentle in its severity,
Thus he kills yet none resent him.
His nurturing across ages is not from a **mind** of nurturing—
Like **spring’s** warmth yet granting **enlightenment** to all,
Thus his **virtue** extends to ages yet he does not **love** humanity.

Heaven covers without exclusion,
I have the **Dao** to cover what it covers;
Earth bears without rejection,
I have the **Dao** to bear what it bears.
Though a **yellow reed’s** stalk is slender,
If given **support and branches**, it thrives;
Though a **caterpillar’s** body is minute,
If given **uplift and wings**, it ascends.
Though not **carved** or **sculpted** by hand,
They rely on **Dao’s** transformation,
Not on **crafty cleverness**—
If **carving** and **sculpting** myriad forms,
Could this be the **crafty cleverness’** domain?

Ordering all things,
Nurturing across ages,
Covering heaven and earth,
Carving myriad forms—
Yet not **perverse**,
Not **benevolent**,
Not **crafty**,
Then it is **non-action** yet **completion**,
Not relying on its **achievement**.
This is called: "Act yet do not rely."

The four seasons differ in **qi**,
Yet their **Dao’s** course knows no halt—
**Steel** emerges, **Fire** withdraws;
**Wood** flourishes, **Water** decays.
They **consume** and **supplement** each other,
**Fill** and **empty** in turn—
Passing yet leaving no trace,
Heaven’s **Dao** has already **completed** its course.

The **sage** follows Heaven as his **tutor**,
So vast that none can name him,
Yet so towering that his **power** is **achieved**.
Though he has no **intent** to establish **power**,
The world **attributes** it to him.

「When he departs from **achievement** and **renown**,
Returns them to the people,
And does not consider them as his own private share—
How could he take them as real and fixed possessions?
This is called: **"Achievement without clinging."**

The **lamp’s** light is set in its **stand**,
Yet the flame before is not the flame behind;
Its brilliance is borrowed from the **mirror**,
Yet the form now is not the form of old.
What is past becomes **ancient**,
What is present becomes **present**—
Before the thought has fully formed,
The so-called "present" has already shifted to become the **ancient**.
To cling to the **having** is to invite the **losing**;
For the **spirit** is bodiless and does not cling—
Only when it takes **form** does it cling to the people.
Thus, the **sage** holds fast to the **spirit**,
Unshaken by **things**,
So within himself there is no clinging,
And **things** cannot alter him.
He does not bind himself to the **future**,
Yet knows when the **future** arrives;
He does not linger on the **past**,
Yet knows when the **past** departs in order.
What need is there for gain or loss?

Hence it is said:
*"Only by not clinging does one not lose."*

**Chapter Three: Not Exalting Virtue**

Not exalting the **virtuous**,
So the people do not contend;
Not treasuring **hard-to-obtain goods**,
So the people do not become thieves.

The **Emperor Xuan** annotated:
*"To exalt the virtuous leads to excessive knowledge,
To the point where the world is thrown into chaos,
And Confucians and Mohists all rise up in contention."*

*"To treasure goods leads to excessive desire,
To the point where thieves operate even at midday,
And people dig holes in broad daylight to steal."*

Not exalting the **virtuous**,
Then the people each settle into their **fate**,
And have nothing to **boast** or **strive** for,
Hence they do not contend.

Not treasuring **goods**,
Then the people each content themselves with their **fate’s** provisions,
And have no **covetous** schemes,
Hence they do not become thieves.

**Zhuangzi** said:
*"To curtail the deeds of the **Record-Keepers** (曾史),
And to gag the mouths of **Yang** and **Mo**,
Then the world’s **virtue** begins to return to **harmony**."*

The *Traveling to the South* chapter states:
*"Do not treasure rare **things**,
Use common **things** with humility,
Then the people will be content."*

The **Explanatory Notes** say:
*"Exalting means distinguishing and favoring.
If the virtuous are exalted, then the foolish and the wise will deceive one another,
The good and the bad will contradict each other—
The people will first be confused and chaotic,
To the point where the world is thrown into chaos,
And Confucians and Mohists all rise up in contention—
This is what is meant by: 'To promote the virtuous leads to the people trampling one another.'"*

*"Treasure means to value and seek.
If goods are treasured, then the hearts of envy and longing arise,
And the yearning for what is lacking spreads—
Seeing **gain**, they forget **form**;
Seeing **profit**, they forget **truth**—
To the point where thieves operate even at midday,
And people dig holes in broad daylight to steal—
This is what is meant by: 'Hard-to-obtain goods cause people to act recklessly.'"*

Only by not exalting the **virtuous**,
Then the **loyal** is truly **loyal** without knowing it,
The **faithful** is truly **faithful** without knowing it—
The people each settle into their **fate**,
Who would dare to **suppress** their own nature for the sake of **boasting**,
Or to **act** improperly to **strut**?
Each halts at their own station and does not contend—
This is enough.

This is what **Zhuangzi** meant when he said:
*"To curtail the deeds of the **Record-Keepers**,
Then the world’s **virtue** begins to return to **harmony**."*

Only by not treasuring **goods**,
Then the people **dig wells** to drink,
**Cultivate fields** to eat—
Each content with their **fate’s** provisions,
Who would dare to **covet** what they do not have,
Or to **scheme** after what they do not possess?
To **extinguish** their **thieving hearts** and not become thieves—
This is enough.

This is what **Zhuangzi** meant when he said:
*"To strike the **jade** and shatter the **pearls**,
Then petty thieves will not arise."*

**Not seeing what is desirable,**
So the heart does not **disturb**.

The **Emperor Xuan** annotated:
*"When people have **desires**,
They follow after **nature’s** provisions to **contend**,
And **seize**, **plunder**, **flatter**, and **deceive**—
There is nothing they will not do."*

**Bo Yi** saw the **desirability** of **right**,
And starved beneath **Chu Yang’s** slopes.
**Dao Hui** saw the **desirability** of **profit**,
And was slain upon **Dong Ling’s** heights.
Their **heat** scorches like **fire**,
Their **cold** chills like **ice**—
Thus their hearts are **agitated** and **proud**,
And cannot be **attuned** to the **Dao**.
To the point where the **sage**,
Does not **pursue** **profit**,
Does not **defy** **harm**,
Knows that **profit** and **harm** share the same **source**;
Does not **glory** in **prominence**,
Does not **shame** **obscurity**,
Sees **obscurity** and **prominence** as one **consistency**.
**Desires** do not **arise**,
Not a **thread** is **perturbed**—
My heart is **serene**,
No **thing** can **shake** it.
**Responding**, it **communicates**;
**Steady**, it **adapts** and **responds**.
**Halted**, there is no **obstruction**,
Not **stuck** to **stillness**;
**Moving**, there is no **chasing**,
Not **drawn** into **action**—
**Overturning** all **directions**,
**Displaying** them before me,
Yet none can **enter** my **dwelling**.
Who could **disturb** me?

**Confucius’s** "not **disturbed** at forty,"
**Mencius’s** "my heart does not **move** at forty,"
They attained this.

The **Explanatory Notes** say:
*"The common people see **things** but do not see the **Dao**,
So all they see is **desire**.
The **sage** sees the **Dao** but does not see **things**,
So all they see is **no desire**.
To see **no desire** means
To **master** what is seen over what is **glimpsed**,
Thus the **spiritual platform** is **held firm**,
And external **disturbances** are **dispelled** and **consumed**.
To see **only desire** means
To **be swayed** by what is **glimpsed** over what is seen,
Thus they **pursue** **things** and **lose their way**,
**Forget** **truth** and **drown** in **falsehood**.
The eyes **feast** on **colors**,
The ears **feast** on **sounds**,
The mouth **feasts** on **flavors**,
The nose **feasts** on **fragrances**—
These are what people **desire**.
The **foolish** and **ordinary** do not know
That **five colors** **disturb** the eyes,
That **five sounds** **disturb** the ears,
That **five flavors** **muddy** the mouth,
That **five scents** **perfume** the nose—
They follow after **nature’s** provisions to **contend**,
And **seize**, **plunder**, **flatter**, and **deceive**—
There is nothing they will not do.
Those **burdened** by **great profit** sacrifice their **bodies** for profit,
Those **burdened** by **high renown** sacrifice their **bodies** for fame.
Like **Bo Yi** and **Dao Hui**,
One dies for **renown**, one dies for **profit**—
Nearly **revealing** the **desirability** of **profit**!

Their **heat** scorches like **fire**,
Their **cold** chills like **ice**—
Unable to **control** it and preserve their **hearts**,
To the point where they are **agitated** and **proud**,
And cannot be **attuned**—
This is like **using** the **Husband of Sui’s** pearl to **lure** a thousand **ren’s** sparrow—
How could it not **disturb**?

The **sage** differs from this:
Does not **pursue** **profit**,
Does not **defy** **harm**,
Knows that **profit** and **harm** share the same **source**;
Does not **glory** in **prominence**,
Does not **shame** **obscurity**,
Sees **obscurity** and **prominence** as one **consistency**.
**Desires** do not **arise**,
Not a **thread** is **perturbed**—
My heart is **serene**,
No **thing** can **shake** it.
**Responding**, it **communicates**;
**Steady**, it **adapts** and **responds**.
**Halted**, there is no **obstruction**,
Not **stuck** to **stillness**;
**Moving**, there is no **chasing**,
Not **drawn** into **action**—
**Overturning** all **directions**,
**Displaying** them before me,
Yet none can **enter** my **dwelling**.
Who could **disturb** me?

**Confucius’s** "not **disturbed**,"
**Mencius’s** "heart does not **move**"—
They attained this.»

Thus the **Daoist Sage’s** governance:
**Empty** the heart, **fill** the belly,
**Weaken** the will, **strengthen** the bones,
Ever keep the people **ignorant**, **desireless**.

Huizong’s annotation:
*"Grain responds because it is empty,
A mirror reflects because it is empty,
A tube receives because it is empty,
Ears hear because they are empty,
Eyes see because they are empty,
The nose smells because it is empty.
If filled within, there is obstruction here.
The Sage, compelled to govern the world,
Views all equally as one,
Nears the close and lifts the distant,
Follows what is natural, lets it unfold—
What need for deliberation?
When **Emperor Yao** raised **Shun** and used **Gun**,
He nearly achieved this.*

An **empty** heart hears all impartially, without favor or aversion;
A **filled** belly suffices in contentment, without grasping or demand—
Why praise the **virtuous**? Why value wealth?

The Sage acts for the belly, not the eyes;
The belly, unselective, contains all.

The will follows where the heart directs;
The bones are the body’s foundation.
A **strong** will may sacrifice itself for fame without rest,
Or chase wealth without satisfaction,
Or boast of deeds, or pride in skill—
The farther from the **Way**, the worse.
**Weak** bones scatter the steps, clash with things,
Are worn down, sink without return.
The Sage’s will is ever **low**,
Yet people exalt it;
Ever **last**,
Yet people honor it first.
Knowing **masculine**, he guards **feminine**;
Knowing **glory**, he embraces **humiliation**—
This is **weakening** the will.
**Uprightness** halts it—
No thing can displace it.
**Firmness** grasps it—
No change can topple it.
Unbroken, like **Master Guangcheng**,
Living twelve hundred years without decay—
This is **strengthening** the bones.

**Zhuangzi** said:
*"In harmony with **non-knowing**, virtue does not depart;
In harmony with **non-desire**, this is called **primordial simplicity**.
**Primordial simplicity**—then the people’s nature is preserved.
The Sage’s governance seeks only that the people retain their nature.
Excessive knowledge harms the **allocation of life’s essence**,
Excessive desire drowns the **flow of life’s vitality**—
Called ‘governance,’ yet what chaos exceeds this?
Thus, ever keep the people **ignorant**, **desireless**."*

**Cushioning the Interpretation** says:
*"A house with no vacancy invites strife between mother-in-law and daughter-in-law;
A heart without **heavenly wandering** invites the six desires to contend—
Thus the ancients valued **emptiness** so greatly.
From the external world, like grain’s response,
A mirror’s reflection, a tube’s reception;
From within, like ears hearing, eyes seeing, noses smelling—
All because of **emptiness**.
**Emptiness** is the opposite of **fullness**;
**Fullness** brings obstruction,
**Emptiness** brings no gap.
Within the myriad things, within the self—
If **fullness** fills the core,
Obstruction arises here,
Because it cannot be without gaps.
The Sage, compelled to govern the world,
With boundless, impartial love,
Views all as one—
Extends this to that,
Nears the close, lifts the distant,
Follows what is natural,
Makes no distinctions,
Lets it unfold naturally—
No taking, no discarding,
Abandons all burdens, merges with **non-being**—
What need for deliberation?
Though **Shun** was wise and cultured,
**Yao** was not unaware—
He waited for **Shun’s** virtue to be recognized before raising him.
Though **Gun** was doomed to exile,
**Yao** was not ignorant—
He also used him provisionally, as the crowd demanded.
The Sage has no fixed will;
He takes the **Way** as his will—
Thus **Shun** could not but be raised,
**Gun** could not but be used.
Why?
**Emptiness** is not **nothingness**,
But the absence of **fullness**.
The heart contains all things,
Yet what it intends is not;
**Fullness** contains nothing,
Yet what it intends is not—
Thus the heart is fundamentally **empty**.
Only when the heart’s essence is exhausted,
And governance reaches its **emptiness**,
Does the Sage say:
‘The good, I approve;
The unvirtuous, I also approve,’
Listening impartially, observing without bias—
Why praise the **virtuous**?
Those who focus inward find sufficiency in the self;
Those who roam outward seek completeness in things.
Seeking completeness in things brings constant fear of lack;
Finding sufficiency in the self raises no envy.
The Sage does not covet wealth or rare goods,
For he finds sufficiency in the self and fills the belly.
When the belly is filled,
He withdraws vision, reverses hearing,
Guards the spirit within—
Thus contentment is stable, without grasping—
Why value wealth?
The will resides in the heart—
Thus the will is where the heart directs.
The form stands on the bones—
Thus the bones are the body’s foundation.
If the will is **strong** and not **weakened**,
It takes **display** as right.
Some sacrifice for fame without rest,
Some pursue wealth without satisfaction,
Some boast of deeds yet achieve nothing,
Some pride themselves yet grow no wiser—
Their distance from the **Way** is great—
Thus the will must be **weakened**.
If the bones are **weak** and not **strengthened**,
Steps scatter, the center has no anchor,
Clashing with things, worn down, sinking without return—
Drifting, lost, knowing not where to turn—
How could they stand firm?
Thus the bones must be **strengthened**.
The Sage’s use of will:
Low to tend himself,
Ever **last**,
Yet people honor him first.
Knowing **masculine**, he guards **feminine**—
No thing can overcome him;
Knowing **glory**, he embraces **humiliation**—
No thing can soil him.
**Weakening** the will:
**Uprightness** halts it—
No thing can displace it;
**Firmness** grasps it—
No change can topple it.
Deep-rooted, unbroken—
Long life, enduring vision—
Unchanging with **Heaven and Earth**,
Like **Master Guangcheng** nurturing his **form**,
Living twelve hundred years without decay—
This is **strengthening** the bones.
Excessive knowledge brings ruin—
Thus the people must be kept **ignorant**."*

「Nurturing the heart finds no better method than **cultivating few desires**—
Thus the Sage makes the people desireless.

In harmony with **ignorance**,
Their **virtue** becomes as thick as that of an infant,
And such **virtue** will not depart.

In harmony with few desires,
They reveal their **innate simplicity** and **embrace natural purity**—
Little private interest, few desires,
This is called **innate simplicity** and **natural purity**.

**Simplicity** means no admixture,
**Purity** means no dissipation—
**Simplicity** and **Purity** restore the people’s true nature.

The Sage’s presence in the world:
When the people recover their true nature, His work is done.

Who would dare to **excessively know** and thus **erode** the portion of life’s **essence**,
Or **multiply desires** and thus **drain** the emotions of life’s **essence**?

Thus the Sage **represses** the knowing ones,
So they dare not act.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation:
The **eloquent** dare not **flaunt** their words,
The **bold** dare not **exert** their strength,
The **skilled** dare not **boast** of their abilities,
The **wise** dare not **apply** their discernment.

To **fabricate cleverness**,
To **pursue ingenuity**,
To **proliferate laws and decrees**,
And thus **overwhelm** the people—
The Sage **prohibits** all such actions.

When the **Nine Ministers** accomplish their duties,
And the **talented officials** serve in their proper ranks,
Is this not to **utilize knowledge** for **crafty purposes**?
They merely **execute** the ruler’s commands,
And **transmit** them to the people.

Explanatory commentary:
The **eloquent** dare not **flaunt** their words—
They have no occasion to speak.
The **bold** dare not **exert** their strength—
They have no occasion to act.
The **skilled** dare not **boast** of their abilities—
So as not to **exploit** their talents.
The **wise** dare not **apply** their discernment—
So as not to **utilize** their wisdom.

**Discard cleverness**,
**Remove cunning**,
And **reduce punishments**.

All who **fabricate cleverness**,
Who **pursue ingenuity**,
Who **proliferate laws and decrees**,
And thus **overwhelm** the people—
The Sage **prohibits** and **stops** them.
Even if there are those who know,
Who would dare to act?

As in the time of **Shun**,
Where **Gao Yao, Kui, and Ji Ke** and their like
Had the **Nine Ministers** all fulfilling their duties,
And the **talented officials** serving in their proper ranks,
Yet they did not **pursue petty distinctions**,
Nor **exercise sharp discernment**,
Nor **wield wisdom** as a **crafty tool**.

They **heard the music** and **responded**,
They **observed the rituals** and **moved**—
They merely **executed** the ruler’s commands,
And **transmitted** them to the people.

**Acting without action**,
Then there is nothing that cannot be **regulated**.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation:
The Sage’s governance—
Does He **abandon** the people and **sever** all things,
And suddenly **establish** Himself in a realm of **no affairs**?
No—
**Action** arises from **non-action**.

The **variations** of all things lie in the realm of **form and below**.
The Sage **embodies the Dao**,
And **stands above** all things,
**Unifying** their **completion**,
**Regulating** and thus **governing** them.

Things have their **origins**—
**Follow** them to **observe** their **return**.
Things have their **birth**—
**Assist** them to **fulfill** their **growth**—
How could there be anything **unregulated**?

Thus, **governing above**,
The sun, moon, stars, and constellations **obtain** their **order**;
**Governing below**,
Birds, beasts, grasses, and trees **follow** their **nature**.

Explanatory commentary:
Those who **govern** the world,
If they **unify** in **non-action** and **ignore action**,
Then it is like **gathering clods** and **accumulating dust**—
**Non-action** yet **not in accordance with principle**.

If they **unify** in **action** and **ignore non-action**,
Then it is like **waves surging** and **fire racing**—
**Action** yet **not true**.

Only when **action** does not **depart** from **non-action**,
And **non-action** does not **neglect** action,
With **action arising** from **non-action**,
Then there is **ample room** for governing the world.
How could it be that He **abandons** the people and **severes** all things,
And suddenly **establishes** Himself in a realm of **no affairs**?

Now, the realm **above form** is called **Dao**,
From **Dao** descending,
Nothing **escapes** the realm of **things**—
Thus the **variations** of all things lie in the realm of **form and below**.

Only when one **discerns** that the **nature** of all things is **not** things,
Then there is **no gap** between them and **Dao**.

**Encompassing** all the **multitudes**,
**Unifying** them in **one principle**,
**Standing above** all things,
**Unifying** their **completion**,
**Regulating** and thus **governing** them.

Hence, as the Classic says:
“All things **rise together**—
I **observe** their **return**.”

And as **Yang Zhu** said:
“The ruler **completes** the **transformations** of **Heaven and Earth**.”

Thus, **looking upward** to **Heaven**,
One sees **swelling and contracting** with **constant measure**,
**Hidden** yet **manifest** with **constant degree**—
**Above**, the sun, moon, stars, and constellations **obtain** their **order**.

**Looking downward** to the **Earth**,
One sees the **flying, creeping, and growing**,
Each **obtaining** its **proper place**—
**Below**, birds, beasts, grasses, and trees **follow** their **nature**.

If so,
Then the world is **regulated** without **regulation**.

*The Explanatory Commentary on the True Classic of Dao and Virtue, Volume One concludes.
The Explanatory Commentary on the True Classic of Dao and Virtue, Volume Two begins.*

「The **Daoist scholar** Jiang Cheng’s **Explanatory Commentary**
**Chapter Four: The Supreme Purity of the Dao**

When the Dao is **supreme in purity** yet applied,
It is **never overfull**—
**Emperor Huizong’s annotation** says:
“The Dao has **sentience** and **trustworthiness**,
Thus it has **application**.
Without **action** and without **form**,
It is **never overfull**.”

The *Classic* states:
“All things bear **yin** and embrace **yang**,
**Supreme qi** brings them into **harmony**.”

The **principles** of all things:
If they **lean toward yang**, they become **excessively strong**,
Or **err through excess**.
If they **lean toward yin**, they become **weak**,
Or **err through deficiency**.

Neither **excess** nor **deficiency**—
This is called **supreme qi**.
**Supreme qi** is **the middle**,
Thus it is called **great harmony**.

The **high** are **checked**,
The **low** are **raised**,
Those with **excess** are **taken from**,
Those with **deficiency** are **given to**—
The Dao’s **application**,
**Nothing** escapes without attaining **its middle**.

**Pouring** from it, **never full**,
**Measuring** from it, **never exhausted**—
The more one **takes** for others, the more one **owns**,
The more one **gives** to others, the more one **possesses**,
The Dao’s **essence**,
Like the **Great Void**, envelops the **six extremes**,
How could it **be overfull**?

The **Explanatory Commentary** states:
“The Dao can **be transmitted** yet cannot **be received**—
Because it can **be transmitted**, there is **sentience** and **trustworthiness**,
Thus there is **application**.
A **single thread’s** subtlety,
If **nurtured** to **flourish**—
Is this not **sentience**?
A **dragonfly’s** minuteness,
If **supported** to **take wing**—
Is this not **sentience**?
**Cold and heat** **await** it to **come and go**,
Yet it **does not force** the **seasons**—
Is this not **trustworthiness**?
All things **await** it to **generate and grow**,
Yet each **follows** its **kind**—
Is this not **trustworthiness**?

Because it cannot **be received**,
It **acts without action** and **forms without form**,
Thus it is **never overfull**.

**Deep** in its **dwelling** yet **serene** and **unshaken**,
**Floating** in its **clarity** yet **tranquil** and **unstirred**—
Is this not **acting without action**?

**Dim** and **silent**, **hidden** and **mysterious**,
**Seized** yet **not obtained**—
Is this not **forming without form**?

The *Classic* states:
“All things bear **yin** and embrace **yang**,
**Supreme qi** brings them into **harmony**.”

**Yin** **halts** and **becomes still**,
All things **bear** it.
**Yang** **melts** and **becomes smooth**,
All things **embrace** it.

If one **follows Heaven** yet lacks **Earth**,
It **leans toward yang** and **errs through excess**.
If one **follows Earth** yet lacks **Heaven**,
It **leans toward yin** and **errs through deficiency**.

There must be **the middle of yin and yang**,
**Excessively strong** yet **not erring through excess**,
**Weak** yet **not erring through deficiency**—
This is **supreme qi**.

The **nature** of **supreme qi**,
**Heaven One** is its **root**,
**Heaven Five** is its **middle**—
Thus **Kun** is **the middle**,
**The middle** **connects above and below**,
This is called **great harmony**,
The Dao’s **ultimate application** lies here.

Thus:
The **high** are **checked** to **reduce their excess**,
The **low** are **raised** to **supplement their deficiency**,
Those with **excess** are **taken from**,
So **great** yet **not excessive**,
Those with **deficiency** are **given to**,
So **small** yet **not meager**.

The Dao’s **application**,
**Nothing** escapes without attaining **its middle**—
Thus it is said: *“When the Dao is supreme in purity yet applied.”*

**Supreme qi** is **not overfull**,
**Application** is **not empty**—
Not **overfull**,
Thus **pouring** from it **never full**;
Not **empty**,
Thus **measuring** from it **never exhausted**.

Only the Dao **cannot be encompassed** by **fullness** or **emptiness**,
Thus it **lends and fulfills**,
Yet its **application** is **unending**—
The more one **takes** for others, the more one **owns**,
The more one **gives** to others, the more one **possesses**.

Like the **Great Void** enveloping the **six extremes**,
With **no boundaries**,
**Immeasurable**,
Could it **be confined** by **vessels**?
How could it **be overfull**?

**Deep**—
It **seems like** the **source of all things**.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation** says:
“Zhuangzi said:
*The **turtle’s** stillness is **depth**,
**Still water’s** stillness is **depth**,
**Flowing water’s** stillness is **depth**.
**Depth** is **empty** and **still**,
**Not mingling** with things—
This is the Dao’s **essence**.
Only because it is **empty**,
Does it **gather** all **substantial** things.
Only because it is **still**,
Does it **embrace** all **movements**—
This is the **root** of all things,
The **foundation** of **transformation**.
Thus it is said: *‘It seems like the source of all things.’*
Yet the Dao **originally has no roots**,
Things **naturally follow** the Dao—
Thus it is only **seeming** like this.”

The **Explanatory Commentary** states:
“Zhuangzi said:
*The Dao is deep in its dwelling.*
**Depth** reflects **flowing water’s** **returning** **wholeness**,
**Stillness** is **profound** and **level**,
**Inner clarity** yet **outer obscurity**,
Though **turtle’s** **stillness** and **flowing water’s** **stillness** differ,
It remains **deep** as itself—
**Probing** it reveals **greater depth**,
**Exhausting** it reveals **greater distance**,
This can be called **emptiness**.
**Waves** are **not evil**,
**Stillness** is **not beautiful**—
This can be called **stillness**.
The Dao’s **essence** resembles this—
Only **emptiness**,
Thus it **suffices** to **encompass** the **world’s** **substantial** things;
Only **stillness**,
Thus it **suffices** to **embrace** the **world’s** **movements**.
**Not becoming** things yet **able to make** things **become**,
The **root** of all things;
**Not transforming** yet **able to make** transformations **fulfill**,
The **foundation** of **transformation**—
Thus it **seems like** the **source of all things**.”

*The Wenzzi* states:
*“The **Empty and Unrestrained**,
The **source of all things**.”*
Its **meaning aligns** with this.

Though the Dao **responds** to things **occasionally**,
It **originally has no roots**—
**Moving** through all things,
Things **naturally follow** it—
Saying *“the source of all things”*
Is only **seeming** like this, yet **not truly**.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation** says:
*“Sharpness** **harms**,
**Agitation** **mixes**—
**Blunting** its **sharpness**, it **does not contend**,
**Relieving** its **agitation**, it **does not disorder**.
**Harmonizing** its **light**—
Zhuangzi’s *“bright yet not dazzling”*—
**Aligning** with its **dust**—
Zhuangzi’s *“committing with things and sharing their waves”*.
**Inner sincerity** **unbroken**,
**Forming schemes** to **achieve brightness**,
Yet those who **abandon** it **avoid** it—
Is this not *“harmonizing its light”*?
**Adorning intelligence** to **startle the foolish**,
**Refining the self** to **illuminate filth**,
**Shining** as if **displayed**,
**Moving** like **sun and moon**—
Is this not *“aligning with its dust”*?

The **Sage** **blunts** its **sharpness**,
Thus **dwelling** among things **does not harm** things—
Things **cannot harm** it.

**Relieving** its **agitation**,
Then **what need** for **scheming**?
**What need** for **intelligence**?
**What need** for **adhesion**?
All things **lack** what could **disturb** its **heart**—
If so, then **no arrogant** **appearance**,
**No haughty** **qi**,
**Harmonized** yet **not yielding**,
**Greatly unified** with things—
To **communicate** the **world’s** **intentions**,
**Nothing** enters without **finding its own fulfillment**.”

The **Explanatory Commentary** states:
“**Sharpness** like **fire’s** **sharpness**,
Cannot **long endure**—
Thus it **harms**.”

*«Agitation* like the **flux of silk**—
A multitude of threads, **not unified**—
Thus it **entangles**.

**Blunting** its **sharpness** means no **distinct angles** stand erect,
**Whole** and **undifferentiated** in its **essence**—
Who could **compete** with it in **movement**?

This is what **Zhuangzi** spoke of as *“rooting in depth”*.

**Relieving** its **agitation** means the **Spiritual Pivot** (*lingtai* 靈台) **remains steadfast**,
**External obstructions** vanish, **perturbations** dissolve—
Who could **disorder** it?

This is what **Zhuangzi** spoke of as *“binding with restraint”*.

**Fire** **unifies** to form **light**,
Yet **harmony** alone **distinguishes** it—
Then it **preserves** itself without **display**,
**Conceals** itself without **action**,
Never **flaunting** its **course** to **please the multitude**.

In **Zhuangzi’s** *“Cutting Adornments”* chapter, it is said:
**“Luminous yet not dazzling.”**
To **speak** of **employing** its **light** without **boasting** of **illumination**,
Only then does it **align with Heaven’s virtue**.

This is called *“harmonizing its light”*.

**Coarse** yet **not wondrous**,
The **source of dust**—
If **uniform** yet **not divergent**,
Then it **shifts** with the **times**,
**Adapts** with the **world**,
Never **clinging to extremes** to **separate itself**.

In **Zhuangzi’s** *“Geng Sang Pu”* chapter, it is said:
**“With things it drifts and turns, yet follows their currents.”**
To **speak** of the **Classics of Vitality** (*Weisheng Jing* 衛生經),
It lies in **yielding** to things in **flexible harmony**,
Not in **rejecting** the world and **defying customs**.

This is called *“aligning with its dust”*.

But if **inner sincerity** is **not dissolved**,
Unable to **forget the heart**,
**Form** and **scheming** coalesce into **light**,
Yet **obscurity** is **not employed**,
The **abandoner** **avoids** it—
Yet it **cannot** **bury itself** among the people—
Is this not *“harmonizing its light”*?

**Adorning intelligence** to **startle the ignorant**,
Yet **not relinquishing** **intelligence**—
**Refining the self** to **reveal filth**,
Yet **not** like the **ignorant**—
**Luminous** as if **displayed**,
**Moving** like **sun and moon**—
Yet it **cannot** **be** like the **obscured**—
Is this not *“aligning with its dust”*?

Now, **the metal** (*fui* 兌) is **sharp**,
Its **utility** lies in **timely application**—
If **transferred** with **sharpness**,
Then there will be **times** of **ruin** and **breakage**—
How could it **avoid injury**?

Only by **blunting** it—
Then **sharpness** becomes **dullness**—
Who could **injure** it?
Thus, **blunting its sharpness**,
The **Sage** **dwells** among things **without harming** things,
Things **cannot harm** it.

**Agitation** is **woven** from **silk**—
Thus it **separates** **filth** and **impurity**,
**Unification** forms **substance**,
**Division** multiplies **threads**—
How could one **not be disturbed**?

Only by **relieving** it—
Then though **agitated**, it is **sealed**,
No **inner or outer** **thousandfold**—
**Thoughts** do not **sprout**,
**Machinations** do not **rise**,
**Covering** the **ten thousand directions**,
**Laying** them out before it—
Yet they **cannot** enter its **unification**.

Thus, **relieving** its **agitation**,
Then **what need** for **scheming**?
**What need** for **intelligence**?
**What need** for **adhesion**?
All things **lack** what could **disturb** its **heart**—
If so, then **no arrogant** **appearance**,
**No haughty** **qi**,
**Harmonized** yet **not yielding**,
**Greatly unified** with things—
To **communicate** the **world’s** **intentions**,
**Nothing** enters without **finding its own fulfillment**.»

**“Deep and still, yet as if existing.”**
**Xizong’s** (*徽宗*) **annotation** says:
**“The heart** like **dead ashes**,
**The body** like **dry wood’s branches**—
In **tranquil composure** (*taiding* 泰定),
**Heaven’s light** shines **self-illuminating**,
**Dull** yet **as if vanished yet existing**,
**Unformed** yet **spiritual**—
Is this not its **Dao/Way**?

The **Explanatory Commentary** states:
**“The heart** is the **confluence** of **spirit** (*shen* 神),
In the **Five Movements** (*wuxing* 五行) it is **fire**.
When it **has heart**,
It is like **fire** **touching** things and **revealing** its **radiance**—
When it **lacks heart**,
It is like **fire** **extinguished**, leaving only **ashes**.

If the **heart** is like **dead ashes**,
Then **no heart** **stirs** within it.

**Grasses and trees** **have life** yet **no awareness**,
Wood **until it decays** (*gao* 槁)
Not only **lacks awareness** but also **lacks life**.
If the **body** is like **dry wood’s branches**,
Then **life is abandoned**, **self is forgotten**—
Thus it is.

In **tranquil composure**,
**Heaven’s light** shines **self-illuminating**—
This is called *“great tranquility holds it, yet follows with radiance”*.
If so, then **neither acting nor ceasing**,
**Awakened** yet **as if vanished yet existing**;
**Neither presence nor absence**,
**Unformed** yet **spiritual**—
**Deep and still**, always **serene** in its **existence**,
**Resembling** yet **not**—
Thus, *“as if existing”*.

*“As if existing”* is **doubt**.
Is it **stuck** in one **bend** of **speech**?
**Deep and still, yet as if existing**—
This is its **Dao/Way**!

**“I do not know whose child it is,”**
**Xizong’s** (*徽宗*) **annotation** says:
**“Symbol** (*xiang* 象) is the **first manifestation** of **things**.
**Heavenly Sovereign** (*di* 帝) is the **spirit’s** **response** to **things**.
**Things** **emerge**, then **symbol** appears—
**Heavenly Sovereign** **manifests**, then **wondrous things** arise.

**Symbol of Heavenly Sovereign** is the **origin** of all **things**,
Yet **Dao/Way** **truly precedes** it.
This is what **Zhuangzi** said:
**“Spirits and Heavenly Sovereigns are spirits of Heavenly Sovereigns,”**
**“Generating Heaven and Earth.”**

**Visible** yet **not seen**,
**Audible** yet **unheard**,
**Seized** yet **not obtained**—
**Existing** yet **emerging** without **revealing** its **gate**,
Who could **know** it?
Thus it is said:
**“I do not know whose child it is.”*

The **Explanatory Commentary** states:
**“Manifestation** is called **symbol**,
Thus **symbol** is the **first manifestation** of **things**.
**Heavenly Sovereign** **manifests** from **Thunder** (*zhen* 震),
Thus **Heavenly Sovereign** is the **spirit’s** **response** to **things**.
**Entering** into **non-generation**,
Thus **no form** can be **sought**;
**Falling** into **generation**,
Thus **symbols** are **visible**.

**Things** **take form**, then **symbol** appears.

All **things** **emerge** from **Heavenly Sovereign**,
Then they **emerge** without **refusal**—
All **things** **enter** into **Heavenly Sovereign**,
Then they **enter** without **obstruction**—
**Heavenly Sovereign** **manifests**, then **wondrous things** arise.

**Symbol** is the **portent** of **form**,
**Heavenly Sovereign** is the **exalted** of **things**—
Thus it is the **origin** of all **things**.
Yet **symbol** and **Heavenly Sovereign**,
Once **emerging** from **Dao/Way**,
**Dao/Way** **truly precedes** them.

This is what **Zhuangzi** said:
**“Spirits and Heavenly Sovereigns are spirits of Heavenly Sovereigns,”**
**“Generating Heaven and Earth.”**

**Heaven** **exists** in **Heaven’s** **manifestation** (*tianchengxiang* 天成象),
**Dao/Way** **generates** it—
**Heavenly Sovereign** **manifests** **wondrous things**,
**Dao/Way** **spiritually animates** them—
Then **all** the **ten thousand things** under **Heaven**,
Who **precedes** **Dao/Way**?

Although **Dao/Way** **has no color**,
Thus **visible** yet **not seen**;
**No sound**,
Thus **audible** yet **unheard**;
**No form**,
Thus **seized** yet **not obtained**.

Though it **exists** yet **emerges**,
Its **emergence** **has no source**, **no periphery**—
**No gate** is **visible**,
Who could **know** it?
Thus it is said:
**“I do not know whose child it is.”**

**Chapter Five: Heaven and Earth**

Heaven and Earth are not virtuous—
they treat the ten thousand things as sacrificial dogs.
The sage is not virtuous—
he treats the people as sacrificial dogs.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:**
Virtue arises from harm, harm arises from virtue.
If virtue is taken as benevolence, harm follows inevitably.

Heaven and Earth toward the ten thousand things,
the sage toward the people—
they assist their natural unfolding,
with no heart of love or profit.
Virtue cannot be named thus.

Straw bundled as a dog,
used in sacrifices—
when appropriate, it is employed;
when spent, it is discarded.
How could there be room for the heart
in the self-generation of the ten thousand things,
or in the self-governance of the people?

**Explanatory Commentary:**
To love people and benefit things is called *bian* (perverse).
If the heart is set on loving people,
its love is not comprehensive;
if the heart is set on benefiting things,
its benefit is not thorough.
For even when virtue is taken as benevolence,
the heart cannot be entirely absent.

Moreover, the origin of benevolence
always stems from harm,
and the origin of harm
always arises from benevolence.
Where there is harm, benevolence follows;
where there is benevolence, harm ensues—
this is the natural order.

Heaven and Earth have no heart toward the ten thousand things,
the sage has no heart toward the people.
They follow nature without artificial augmentation,
thus loving all without partiality,
benefiting all without exclusion.
Those who receive the gift do not repay,
those who are nourished do not thank—
benefits extend to all ages.
If not for loving people,
how could virtue be named?

Zhuangzi’s *great virtue* is *not virtue*—
just like the sacrificial dog:
Before its use, it is adorned in a wicker basket,
wrapped in embroidered cloth,
and the ritual officiant, in solemn purity, presents it.
Once used, travelers strike its head and spine,
and the poor take it to cook—
and that is all.

How could there be room for the heart
in the self-generation of the ten thousand things,
or in the self-governance of the people?

Between Heaven and Earth—
is it not like the *tuqiao*?

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:**
The *tuqiao* is empty yet receptive,
receptive yet responsive—
thus its response is boundless.
If filled with substance,
it becomes obstructed at every touch.
In the Dao, this is one-sidedness;
in things, this is distortion.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
The *tu* can contain,
the *qiao* can resonate—
both are empty yet receptive,
receptive yet responsive.
How could there be exhaustion in responding to sensation?

Between Heaven and Earth,
one *qi* subtly circulates,
pervading without exclusion,
until the limit of demand is reached—
just like the *tuqiao*.
If one thing fills it,
there is no place without obstruction.
How could Heaven and Earth,
vast as they are,
be confined to one-sidedness,
or stagnant in distortion?

Empty yet unyielding,
moving yet ever emerging.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:**
Empty oneself to roam the world,
and one responds broadly yet precisely—
thus, *empty yet unyielding*.
Pressured into action,
one’s capacity is boundless—
thus, *moving yet ever emerging*.
The sage responds to the Sovereign without the burden of words—
this is it.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
The vastness of Heaven and Earth—
the sage models himself upon it,
emptying his essence,
yet fully manifesting in his actions.
Returning action to essence,
he remains motionless yet encompasses all things,
tilting none to his advantage,
thus responding universally—
what could be more appropriate?

Arising from essence, his actions are boundless—
having already given all to others,
how could there be exhaustion?
Emptying himself to roam the world,
he acts only when pressed—
thus, his entire life speaks without error,
and his deeds are without artifice.

In responding to the Sovereign,
there is ample reserve.
This is what Zhuangzi called
the Sovereign’s function,
the sage’s remaining task.

Much speech leads to exhaustion—
better to hold the center.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:**
The *qiao* is empty, awaiting *qi*—
when *qi* arrives, it sounds;
when *qi* does not come, it is silent.
The sage’s speech is like this.
The sophists’ constraints—
though their speech is much,
they cannot escape entanglement—
better to hold the center than this.

Cultivate your interior,
close your exterior,
retract your gaze, reverse your hearing—
thus returning to see the heart of Heaven and Earth.
This is called *holding the center*.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
All music arises from emptiness.
The *qiao*, as an instrument,
is the origin of pitch, measure, and weight.
Music’s foundation lies here.
Thus the *Odes* speak of “not overstepping the *qiao*,”
meaning *empty to await qi*—
when *qi* arrives, it sounds;
when *qi* does not come, it is silent.

The sage’s speech, when obtained,
resolves resonance to accord with pitch—
responding only when questioned,
never deviating from this.
Thus Confucius desired silence,
and Mencius considered eloquence a reluctant necessity—
this is why.

Those who cannot forget speech,
rambling on to tens of thousands of words,
filling libraries with five chariots of texts—
these are the sophists’ constraints.
Though much, what use is it?

The holder of the center is otherwise:
reaching the truth of things while guarding their root,
commanding their transformation while guarding their origin,
cautious of the exterior without its manifold disputes,
closed to the interior without its thousandfold errors.

The eyes see nothing—
retracting vision,
vision turns to the obscure;
the ears hear nothing—
reversing hearing,
hearing turns to the soundless.
