Returning to self-awareness,
the heart of Heaven and Earth becomes plainly visible.
Guard this without loss—
though one speaks not a word all life,
one has never failed to speak.
Why clamor then?

For the center unites above and below—
this is called the *great root of the world*.
Speech cannot exhaust this—
even if one speaks much to exchange it,
better to hold the center.

**Chapter Six: The Valley Spirit** (*Gǔshén Zhāng*)
The Valley Spirit does not die,

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
What has form must have waxing and waning,
what has measure must have completion and decay—
when form and measure are both complete,
life and death are distinguished,
this is the nature of things.

The Valley Spirit receives all movement yet remains ever empty,
the Mysterious Dao manifests all things yet remains ever still.
Within the eternal truth,
it is one with the Way,
not clinging to form,
not entrapped by measure.
Endlessly generating life,
like the sun and moon,
it ceases not through all ages,
like the Southern Dipper,
it never errs through all ages—
thus it is said, *"The Valley Spirit does not die."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains:
When waxing reaches its peak, it turns to waning;
when waning reaches its extreme, it turns to waxing—
this is true of all that has form.
When completion comes, decay follows swiftly;
when decay comes, completion follows swiftly—
this is true of all that has measure.
Once it has become a thing,
no form or measure can escape it—
this is why all that is born must also perish.
When one yin descends below,
one yang ascends above—
none who remain within yin and yang
can escape this fate.

Yet the Valley Spirit is different.
It receives without accumulating,
responds to all movement yet remains ever empty—
its yin and yang are unfathomable.
It manifests all things yet remains ever still—
within the eternal truth,
it is one with the Way.
It cannot be sought through form,
nor traced through measure—
for form is finite yet this is boundless,
measure is ending yet this is endless.
It transforms beyond all existence,
endlessly generating life—
this is what is meant by *"never having died."*

Like the sun and moon,
it moves onward and returns without ceasing;
like the Southern Dipper,
it revolves southward and northward without error—
how then could one know its end?
This is called the *Dark Mother*.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
All things receive their mandate from the *Non-Existent*,
yet take form in the *Existent*—
the Valley’s function is without likeness,
the Spirit’s essence is without boundary—
this is the mandate from which all things arise.
The *Dark* is the color of Heaven,
the *Mother* is the type of Earth—
this is the form in which all things are completed.

The Valley Spirit and the Dark Mother
illustrate the eternal Way’s constancy,
and the marvelous workings of creation.

The *Cushui* commentary explains:
Continuous without interruption is called *mandate*—
from what does this mandate arise?
It is the Valley Spirit.
When things are born and their principles are established, this is called *mandate*—
what bestows this form?
It is the Dark Mother.
Empty yet skillful in response,
when viewed, no form is seen—
this is the Valley’s function without likeness.
Round yet ever in motion,
when sought, no trace of its origin is known—
this is the Spirit’s essence without boundary.
Here lies the source of all things’ mandate.

Red and black are the *Dark*—
thus the *Yijing* speaks of "Heaven’s Dark and Earth’s Yellow,"
and the *Dark* is the color of Heaven.
Yang is the *Male*, yin is the *Female*—
thus the *Yijing* speaks of "Kūn’s advantage in the *Female* horse’s correctness,"
and the *Female* is the type of Earth.
Here lies the basis of all things’ form.

The Valley Spirit and the Dark Mother—
different names, yet the same reality.
Speaking of its eternal existence,
it is called the Valley Spirit,
to illustrate the eternal Way’s constancy.
Speaking of its generating life,
it is called the Dark Mother,
to reveal creation’s marvelous workings.
Names are contrary,
yet their essence flows in harmony.

The gate of the Dark Mother—
this is called the *Root of Heaven and Earth*.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
Zhuangzi said:
All things have a source of life yet no one sees its root,
they have a place of emergence yet no one sees its gate—
only the Sage can see it.
Thus here it is made clear:
The gate of the Dark Mother
is called the *Root of Heaven and Earth*.
Heaven and Earth are the above and below of all things.
Things and Heaven and Earth—
in origin, there is no before or after.
Yet when the great Way’s order is made clear,
there is Heaven and Earth,
and then there are all things.
But the source from which Heaven and Earth emerge—
it is the Dark Mother.
Who could see the One that gave birth before Heaven and Earth?

The *Cushui* commentary explains:
The birth of things—
its emergence has no origin,
thus no one sees its root.
Its coming has no progression,
thus no one sees its gate.
Only the Sage sees what others do not see,
and perceives the unseen form—
the self-originating, self-rooted,
yet the gate through which all marvels pass.
Knowing it as one sees,
it is directly pointed to its fundamental essence,
and declared: *"The gate of the Dark Mother—
this is called the Root of Heaven and Earth."*
This is what Zhuangzi meant when he said:
All things have a source of life yet no one sees its root,
they have a place of emergence yet no one sees its gate.

Moreover, Heaven opens above,
Earth opens below,
and humans dwell within—
thus Heaven and Earth are the above and below of all things.
Heaven and Earth are born with me,
all things are one with me—
how then could one observe things and Heaven and Earth as prior or subsequent?
There is Heaven and Earth,
and then there are all things—
this is merely the sequence of the Way.

The source from which Heaven and Earth emerge—
it is called the Dark Mother—
then who could see the One that gave birth before Heaven and Earth?

Endless, as if eternally present,
its use requires no effort.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
Self-originating, self-rooted,
it has existed since ancient times—
the transmission of fire knows no end.
This is what is meant by *"endless, as if eternally present."*

When things are born, it is Heaven’s work,
leaving the single *qi* to move on its own—
when Earth transforms, it is swift,
entrusting all forms to their own proliferation—
the *Yijing*’s *Qian* is easily understood,
the *Yijing*’s *Kūn* is simple in ability—
not by force, but how could there be effort?

The *Cushui* commentary explains:
*Endless*—this means unceasing.
*As if eternally present*—this means not perishing.
The Classic speaks of *"deep and vast, as if there might be a few traces remaining"*—
this is it.
The utmost of the Way,
before Heaven and Earth yet without beginning,
after Heaven and Earth yet without end—
it seems to perish yet remains,
and can be said to have existed since ancient times.
Like the transmission of fire,
it relies on fuel to illuminate,
and when the green is consumed, it returns to the fuel—
though it ends, it never truly ends.
This is what is meant by *"endless, as if eternally present."*

Yet Heaven is merely the accumulation of *qi*—
the beginning from which all things draw their resources.
But Heaven’s creation of things
leaves the single *qi* to move on its own—
it does not exert force to create them.
Earth is merely the accumulation of form—
the basis from which all things draw their growth.
Yet Earth’s transformation of things
entrusts all forms to their own proliferation—
it does not use power to change them.

**Great is the Primordial of Heaven (乾元),**
by its ease we come to know it;
**Profound is the Primordial of Earth (坤元),**
by its simplicity it achieves all.
Its wondrous functions are complete—
no contrivance is needed,
naturally so—
what labor is there?

**Chapter Seven: Heaven’s Longevity and Earth’s Endurance (天长地久)**
Heaven and Earth endure long because
they do not generate themselves—
thus they endure forever.

**Commentary by Emperor Huizong (徽宗注):**
The vault of Heaven arches above,
with the Sun and Moon as its longitudinal threads,
and stars as its latitudinal weaves—
and all things are sheltered beneath it.
The Earth is solid and square below,
forming mountains and valleys,
merging into rivers and lakes—
and all things rest upon it.

All things are sheltered and borne by Heaven and Earth,
yet Heaven and Earth hold no intention toward them—
thus Heaven moves with steadfast certainty,
and Earth remains with unyielding stability—
this is how they endure long.

If Heaven and Earth were to harbor intent toward life,
then Heaven would soon be measured,
its sheltering shallow,
and Earth would soon be fathomed,
its bearing thin.
Thus would they depend on others—
how could they then endure?

**Explanatory Notes (疏义):**
Light and pure is Heaven, round and in motion,
so its form arches above.
Heavy and turbid is Earth, square and at rest,
so its form is solid below.
Heaven’s *qi* ascends—
the Sun and Moon, the stars—
all are accumulations of *qi* in Heaven,
and all beneath the sky are what it shelters.
Earth’s forms settle—
mountains and rivers—
all are accumulations of form in Earth,
and all upon the land are what it bears.

Heaven shelters without partiality,
Earth bears without favor—
Heaven and Earth hold no intent toward all things.
Thus, with effortless ease, their motion never ceases,
and with simple steadfastness, their stance never falters—
this is how Heaven and Earth endure long.

If one were to rely on contrivance,
depend on cleverness,
and carve or chisel mere grains of matter—
this would be to harbor intent toward life.
How could such labor compare to the divine efficacy of Heaven and Earth?
Surely it would be shallow and insufficient in its sheltering and bearing.
Thus would it depend on others—
what choice would it have with things?

Only by not generating itself,
and abiding in the realm of non-generation,
can it endure forever.

Therefore, the Sage places his own self last,
yet his self comes first;
sets his self aside,
yet his self is preserved.

**Commentary by Emperor Huizong (徽宗注):**
Heaven moves above, Earth rests below,
and the Sage dwells between them.
As *San Cai* (三才)—Heaven, Earth, and Sage—
their functions are mutually resonant.
As *San Ji* (三极)—three poles—
their forms stand distinct.
As *San Ling* (三灵)—three spirits—
their divine mystery knows no separation.

Thus, Heaven, Earth, and the Sage all attain the Dao,
and the Sage’s cultivation of his self
is already that of Heaven and Earth.
Hence, this chapter first speaks of Heaven and Earth’s non-generation,
then follows with the Sage’s non-possession of his self.

All take precedence—
only the Sage takes last,
saying, *"I receive the filth of the world"*—
this is called placing his self last.
By placing his self last,
he does not contend with things,
and thus none can contend with him—
hence, *"placing his self last, his self comes first."*

On the path, he does not vie for advantage in peril or ease;
in winter and summer, he does not cling to the harmony of yin and yang;
he transcends life and death,
sets aside fortune and calamity—
yet his spirit is never constrained—
this is called setting his self aside, yet his self is preserved.

Thus is the Sage’s cultivation of his self—
how much more so for matters beyond the self?
He encounters without resisting,
passes without clinging,
embracing nature’s essence and nurturing the spirit,
to wander between the world’s affairs.
His form will naturally align,
self and things become one—
born before Heaven and Earth yet not enduring,
longer than ancient times yet not aging—
this is its effect.

**Explanatory Notes (疏义):**
Heaven’s duty is to generate and shelter—
thus it moves above.
Earth’s duty is to form and bear—
thus it rests below.
The Sage’s duty is to teach and transform—
thus he dwells between Heaven and Earth.

Their functions are mutually resonant—
hence called *San Cai*.
Their forms stand distinct—
hence called *San Ji*.
Their spirits are inseparable—
hence called *San Ling*.
Thus, observing Heaven, Earth, and the Sage,
though their paths differ, their Dao is one.

The Sage’s cultivation of his self
need not be sought elsewhere—
it is evident in Heaven and Earth.
Hence, this chapter first speaks of Heaven and Earth,
then follows with the Sage.

For not generating itself is to forget generation,
not possessing the self is to forget the self—
forgetting generation, yet enduring;
forgetting the self, yet the self is preserved—
this is how Heaven and Earth are Heaven and Earth,
how the Sage is the Sage.

Since others take precedence,
the Sage alone takes last—
as the Classic says, *"To be first among men,
one must place his self last."*
Rivers and lakes accept filth,
rulers bear the nation’s shame—
thus, *"receiving the world’s filth"*—
the Classic says, *"receiving the nation’s filth,
this is the duty of the sovereign."*
All these are acts of placing his self last—
only by placing his self last
does he embody yielding, weakness, and humility,
and not contend with things.
Only by not contending
does he empty himself to wander in the world—
who could harm him?
Thus, none can contend with him—
this is the way of *"placing his self last, yet his self comes first."*

To walk with haste—
not avoiding peril or ease in rivers and valleys—
this is what others contend for,
yet he does not vie for advantage in peril or ease on the path.
To seek warmth and shun cold—
this is natural harmony of yin and yang,
yet he does not cling to it in winter and summer.
To delight in life and fear death,
to seek fortune and avoid calamity—
this is human nature,
yet he transcends life and death,
sets aside fortune and calamity.

This has no other secret—
knowing that the body is not mine to possess,
while the four limbs and myriad bones will soon become dust and filth,
thus he can be in perfect harmony with all things,
and not cling to his own body.

If so, then he houses the myriad bones as mere forms of ears and eyes,
while his spirit is never burdened or constrained—
what thing could harm him?
This is what is meant by *"transcending his body yet his body remains."*

No one values his own body more than its own worth—
the Sage governs it thus,
how much more so for external matters?
When they come, he does not resist them,
knowing that their arrival cannot be prevented;
when they pass, he does not cling to them,
knowing that their departure cannot be stopped.
He does not burden himself with form,
while his true nature embraces the spirit,
and he wanders among people as their equal,
through the world of the mundane.

Form will naturally align itself,
and things and self become one—
to live long and see far,
and to endure alongside Heaven and Earth,
then to be born before Heaven and Earth,
and not to be aged by longevity,
what could be more difficult?
Is it not because he has no selfishness?
Thus he can fulfill his own true nature.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"The greatest virtue of Heaven and Earth is called ‘life’ (生),
and the greatest treasure of the Sage is called ‘position’ (位).
The Dao/Way makes them public—
Heaven and Earth embody the Dao/Way,
thus they have no selfishness,
and no selfishness brings enduring longevity.
The Sage embodies the Dao/Way,
thus he has no selfishness,
and no selfishness ensures his eternal existence.
To cultivate oneself for selfish gain—
none have succeeded in fulfilling their selfishness.
The *Commentary* states:
Heaven and Earth are the greatest of all forms,
their difference from myriad things lies in their enduring longevity.
The Sage’s distinction from the common folk lies in his eternal existence.
The *Book of Changes* (《易》) says:
‘The greatest virtue of Heaven and Earth is called life,
and the greatest treasure of the Sage is called position.
Heaven and Earth thus give birth and endure,
the Sage thus preserves his position and remains eternal,
all because of their lack of selfishness.’

To obstruct the Dao/Way with selfishness—
one cannot attain the Dao/Way.
The Dao/Way makes all things public,
and one can only be without selfishness
by embodying the Dao/Way.
Han Feizi says:
‘To cultivate oneself for selfish gain is selfishness,
to abandon selfishness for the public is virtue.
Since the body does not generate itself,
and one transcends his own body,
how could he cultivate for selfishness?
The means by which he attains enduring longevity and bodily preservation—
this is what is meant by *fulfilling his own true nature*.

Chapter Eight: The Supreme Good is Like Water
From the section *"The Supreme Good is Like Water"* (上善若水)

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*The Classic of Changes* says: “The interplay of one yin and one yang is called the Dao/Way; what follows it is virtue.”
Zhuangzi says: “To depart from the Dao/Way for the sake of virtue—once the name of virtue is established, the very body of the Dao/Way is impaired.”

Yet, Heaven’s One generates Water, and though it departs slightly from the Dao/Way, its depths are vast and empty, its stillness clear—this is called the utmost refinement under Heaven. Hence, *the Supreme Good is like Water*.

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
Yin and yang are the great movements of *qi*.
From the perspective of *qi* before it manifests, it is neither yin nor yang—this is the Dao/Way.
All things bear yin within and embrace yang; from their operation in the myriad things, it is said that they do not depart from yin and yang—this too is the Dao/Way.
*The Classic of Changes* says: “The interplay of one yin and one yang is called the Dao/Way”—this is merely speaking of things.
Descending from the Dao/Way, what humans desire most is virtue, and so it is said that what follows the Dao/Way is virtue.
Since the Dao/Way is singular, once it is called virtue, it is divided from the Dao/Way.
Their division is their succession, hence Zhuangzi says: “To depart from the Dao/Way for the sake of virtue.”
For once the name of virtue is established, the undivided whole is split, and the body of the Dao/Way is impaired.

Yet, the Nine Categories begin with the Five Phases, and the Five Phases prioritize Water—thus, Heaven’s One generates Water, though it departs slightly from the Dao/Way.
Though it overflows, it does not lose its measure—it is deep and empty.
When clarified, it reveals even the finest hairs; when still, it is luminous.
What under Heaven is more refined than this? And so, *the Supreme Good is like Water*—unlike those who merely know virtue as virtue.

Water is good at benefiting all things without contention,
dwelling where all detest it—
thus it approaches the Dao/Way.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
When it melts, it becomes rain and dew, nourishing all;
when it congeals, it becomes frost and snow, shaping all.
When dispersed, it becomes rivers and streams; when gathered, it forms lakes and marshes.
Deep as springs, vast as seas—it is drawn for use, stored for reserve, and abundant.
It generates and nourishes all things, yet never exhausts them.
Who benefits all things better than this?

Its beneficence to all things—
in Heaven, as rain and dew, all things receive its blessings;
on Earth, as springs and depths, all things accept its gifts.
Yet its nature is yielding and weak,
always dwelling in non-contention, receiving filth and defilement,
not seeking its own good, unburdened by self-regard—
thus it approaches the Dao/Way.
*Ji* means “near.”

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
Heaven’s One generates Water, flowing without end.
In Heaven, when yang-*qi* prevails, it melts into rain and dew,
spreading to nourish all things, thus all are nourished.
When yin-*qi* prevails, it congeals into frost and snow,
engraving to complete all things, thus all treasures are formed.
On Earth, it disperses as rivers and streams,
gathers as lakes and marshes;
its encompassing return to wholeness is the depth of springs—
when measured, its end is unseen—
the vastness of seas—
drawn for use, stored for reserve, and abundant.
Its generation and nourishment are inexhaustible;
all things rely on it without depletion,
taking as they need, given as they require—
who benefits all things better than this?

Its beneficence to all things—
in Heaven, as rain and dew, all things receive its blessings;
on Earth, as springs and depths, all things accept its gifts.
Yet its nature is yielding and weak,
always dwelling in non-contention, receiving filth and defilement,
not seeking its own good, unburdened by self-regard—
thus it approaches the Dao/Way.
*Ji* is the subtle beginning of movement—
*ji* means “near,” for it is the movement before reaching its destination.
To approach the Dao/Way is to be near the Dao/Way.

**Residing in the good of place,**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
It flows through the earth, yet never overflows.

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
Water is called “moistening the low”—
thus it moves through the earth.
It fills the depressions before advancing—
thus it flows without overflowing.

**The mind is good at depth,**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
The deeper it is measured, the deeper it becomes;
the farther it is explored, the vaster it grows.

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
A thousand *ren* of height is insufficient to fathom its depth—
the deeper it is measured, the deeper it becomes;
a thousand *li* of breadth is insufficient to contain its vastness—
the farther it is explored, the vaster it grows.

**To act with goodness is to be benevolent,**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
It loves all without selfishness, giving without discrimination.

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
Wherever frost and dew fall, there is no far or near—
all under Heaven is encompassed by its blessings,
truly loving all without selfishness, giving without discrimination.

**To speak with goodness is to be trustworthy,**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
It avoids obstacles to reach the seas,
traverses dangers without losing its course.

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
It studies the sea and arrives at the sea—
this is to avoid obstacles to reach the seas.
Though it twists a myriad times, it must return to the east—
this is to traverse dangers without losing its course.

**To govern with goodness is to bring order,**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
It cleanses the impure, smooths the perilous,
following the natural course of things, without contrivance—
thus nothing remains ungoverned.

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
By its ebb and flow, it attains purity—
thus it cleanses the impure.
Its measure is even and balanced—
thus it smooths the perilous.
If it follows its natural course,
there is no contrivance of the heart.
Thus, though it appears undirected,
nothing remains ungoverned.

**To act with goodness is to be capable,**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
It adapts to the terrain to form curves and straight paths,
conforms to vessels to become round and square,
its changes are ever-varying yet ever-constant—
who is as capable as this?

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
Its flow follows natural paths—
thus it adapts to the terrain to form curves and straight paths.
It responds as taken—
thus it conforms to vessels to become round and square.
It can be struck to rise above the forehead,
or stirred to fill the mountains—
yet its balance is ever-true,
and the master craftsman takes it as his model.
Its changes are ever-varying,
yet ever-constant in balance—
who is as capable as this?

**To move with goodness is to act in season.***
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
When yang releases it, it becomes mist;
when yin congeals it, it becomes ice.
In the east, it gathers as streams;
in the west, it flows westward—
its movement is unrestrained,
proper only to the seasons.

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
When the season is spring, yang-qi rises from below,
and ice melts into water—
yang releases it, making it flow.
When the season is winter, yin-qi reaches its peak,
and water congeals into ice—
yin congeals it, making it solid.
It does not stagnate in one corner,
nor cling to one curve.
In the east, it flows eastward;
in the west, it flows westward—
moving with things, unrestrained,
proper only to the seasons.

Only the non-contending thus has no blame.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
The Sage embodies the Dao to govern himself,
and thus endures eternally.
He embraces all goodness to benefit beings,
dwelling in the non-contending.

Zhuangzi says:
*"Is it easy to have and yet do nothing with it?"*
To act rashly is contrary to Heaven’s will.
To act without effort and yet commit few faults—this is easy;
to act with effort and yet never rest—this is difficult.
If one seeks to benefit beings through action,
then avoiding blame becomes difficult.

The highest goodness benefits beings,
like water’s nature—
though it benefits all without discrimination,
it does not contend with things,
and thus nothing can contend with it—
hence, no blame.

Thus it is said:
*"Under Heaven, none are as soft and weak as water,
yet none can surpass it in overcoming the firm and strong."*

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
The Sage responds to the world—
in his self-cultivation, he embodies the Dao within,
thus governing himself with enduring permanence,
equal to Heaven and Earth.
In his actions toward others, he embraces all goodness,
thus benefiting beings.
Dwelling in the non-contending,
his movement is like water’s.

Zhuangzi says:
*"Is it easy to have and yet do nothing with it?"*
To act rashly is contrary to Heaven’s will.
The gentleman, before taking action,
first purifies himself to refine his virtue—
thus, action cannot be rash.
To act without effort and yet commit few faults—this is easy;
to act with effort and yet never rest—this is difficult.
If one makes benefiting beings his purpose,
then he cannot avoid action,
and thus avoiding blame becomes difficult.

Moreover, it is improper to favor the upper over the lower,
or the left over the right—
all these lead to blame.
Only the highest goodness benefits beings,
like water’s nature—
extending and contracting without left or right,
benefiting all without discrimination,
not contending while nothing can contend with it—
in all transformations, ever one,
nothing can overcome it—
hence, no blame.

This aligns with the *Classic*’s saying:
*"Under Heaven, none are as soft and weak as water,
yet none can surpass it in overcoming the firm and strong."*

**Chapter Nine: "Holding Fullness"**
To hold fullness to the brim
is not as good as stopping in time.
To sharpen to a point
cannot be long preserved.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
Fullness overflows;
sharpness dulls—
this is the nature of all things.
Fullness must have its loss;
to not know how to hold back in advance,
to grasp emptiness while governing fullness,
and to indulge in the desire for fullness—
this only increases the risk of ruin.
Thus, it is better to stop in time.

Things’ transformations are boundless,
yet human wisdom is limited.
Preemptive foresight is the flower of the Dao,
the beginning of folly.

To probe things’ nature and yet rush forward—
wisdom will falter in time.
How can it be long preserved?

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
Things have fixed measures—
thus, fullness overflows,
and sharpness dulls—
fullness must have its loss;
this is the nature of all things.
If one does not wield the adaptable power of compliance,
hold back in advance,
or grasp the handle of humility to govern fullness—
if one indulges in the transformations of growth and decay,
changing with the times,
and yet drowns in the desire for fullness—
this only increases the risk of ruin.
Thus, to hold fullness to the brim
is not as good as stopping in time.

The *Transmission* says:
*"Those who embody the Dao can hold fullness without tipping."*

Indeed, this is different.
To face myriad movements with one body,
to shoulder ten thousand affairs with one mind—
to exhaust one’s clarity and wisdom in response—
this will inevitably lead to exhaustion.
Though one’s foresight may be keen,
it may still fail to address all circumstances,
for things’ transformations are boundless while human wisdom is limited.

To wear out one’s spirit in reckless speculation—
this is called preemptive foresight.
Preemptive foresight leads to clinging to trifles while forgetting the root,
laboring the mind until it grows dull—
this is called the flower of the Dao,
the beginning of folly.

To use wisdom as a chisel,
probing things’ nature and yet rushing forward—
wisdom will falter in time.
Even if gained, it will be lost—
how can it be long preserved?

The *Transmission* says:
*"Those who embody the Dao rest without exhaustion,
while those who rely on schemes labor without success."*
Is this not so?

Gold and jade fill the hall,
yet none can guard them.
Riches and honor breed arrogance,
and thus invite their own ruin.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
Gold, jade, riches, and honor
are not the essence of life and death—
they are external things one cannot rely upon.
To treasure gold and jade is to be burdened by things.
If burdened by things, how can one avoid loss?
Thus, none can guard them.
Riches and honor breed arrogance,
and thus harm one’s virtue.
If virtue is harmed, how can one avoid ruin?
Thus, one invites blame upon oneself.

The Sage does not cling to the world’s transient gains as his own,
nor does he seek dominion over all as his own display.
How could gold and jade be treasured,
or riches and honor be burdensome?

Thus, in the most prosperous of states,
wealth and honors converge—
its nobility is unmatched,
its riches unparalleled—
yet the Dao remains unchanged.

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
Within the realm of self-cultivation, nothing is untrue;
outside it, nothing is unfalse.
Gold, jade, riches, and honor are external things,
not the essence of life and death.
External things cannot be relied upon—
gain and loss follow in turn.
Thus, they are not things one can possess.

What is treasured within oneself
exists everywhere;
what is treasured in things
is lost as soon as gained.
To treasure gold and jade is to be burdened by things—
when things depart, how can one avoid loss?

Thus, none can truly hold onto it.

Wealth and honor with humility—this is the virtue of the noble.
Arrogance, licentiousness, and boastfulness—these lead to ruin.
Wealth and honor with arrogance harm one’s virtue;
harming virtue is what people reject—how could one avoid misfortune?
Thus, one invites one’s own blame.

The Sage does not cling to the benefits of a single age as his private gain,
but rejoices in shared prosperity;
nor does he seek dominion over all as his own display,
but finds joy in the Way and forgets worldly power.

Is it not the case that precious goods like gold and jade are not true treasures,
that fame and profit are not the heart’s desire,
and that wealth and honor are burdensome?

Thus, all ten thousand things are complete within oneself—
in the most prosperous of states, wealth and resources converge—
yet the riches of Jin and Chu are not what is meant by true wealth.
To govern all things, surpassing all others—
in the most noble of states, rank and honor converge—
yet the nobility of Zhao and Meng is not what is meant by true nobility.
Its nobility is unmatched, its riches unparalleled,
eternal through ages past and present—
thus, the Dao remains unchanged.

When achievement is complete, reputation fulfilled,
one withdraws—this is the Way of Heaven.

The *Commentary of Emperor Huizong* says:
When achievement is complete, it decays;
when reputation is fulfilled, it falters.
As the sun reaches its zenith, it declines;
as the moon is full, it wanes—
this is the nature of things.
The Sage perceives how completion and decay follow one another,
recognizes the cycles of fullness and emptiness,
rises above all things with ease,
unburdened by external things,
free from the perils of arrogance and excess—
this is true wisdom.
Who else can match this understanding?
Thus it is said: *When achievement is complete, reputation fulfilled, one withdraws—this is the Way of Heaven.*

The cycles of the four seasons—
when achievement is complete, it departs—
this is the Way of Heaven.
If one knows how to advance but not retreat,
if one knows how to preserve but not perish,
if one knows how to gain but not lose—
how could one avoid regret?

Yi Yin said:
*"I do not dwell on favor and profit at the height of success."*

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
When achievement reaches its peak, it is destined to decline;
when reputation is perfected, it is destined to falter.
Gazing upward at the heavens,
the sun governs the yang, the moon the yin—
yet even the sun and moon have their eclipses.
How much more so the fleeting fortunes of worldly success and fame?

The Sage comprehends the principles of all things,
knows that completion is swiftly followed by decay,
sees how completion and decay are interdependent,
understands the cycles of fullness and emptiness,
and their inevitable order.
Not clinging to what others possess,
he finds true fulfillment within himself,
unburdened by external things,
unconstrained by them,
modest yet receptive, balanced yet complete,
free from the breath of arrogance and excess,
harmonizing with the ebb and flow of fullness and emptiness,
moving with the times.
Viewing wealth and honor as fleeting shadows passing by,
or as gnats before one’s face—
how could they be burdensome?

Who else, if not the knower of Heaven, could attain this?
Thus it is said: *When achievement is complete, reputation fulfilled, one withdraws—this is the Way of Heaven.*

Now, when *Wu* emerges, *Jing* retreats;
when *Jia* flourishes, *Gui* declines—
the cycles of the four seasons—
when achievement is complete, it departs—
this is the Way of Heaven.

If one knows how to advance but not retreat,
endlessly pursuing progress;
if one knows how to preserve but not perish,
clinging to existence without adaptation;
if one knows how to gain but not lose,
fearing loss after acquisition—
how could one avoid regret?

Yi Yin said:
*"I do not dwell on favor and profit at the height of success."*

Indeed, to dwell is to invite departure—
when achievement is complete, reputation fulfilled, one withdraws—
thus, by not dwelling, one does not depart.
Those who uphold this principle should reflect deeply upon it.

**Chapter Ten: On the Nourishment of the *Pò* (魄)**

Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*Pò* is the Yin aspect—
it adheres to the body and finds its resting place,
hence the Master Lao spoke of the *Pò* as **nourishment** (營).
*The Hun* (魂) is the Yang aspect—
it rides upon the *qi* and knows no obstruction,
hence the *Yì* (易) spoke of the *Hun* as **wandering** (游).
The Sage governs form with the *Shén* (神),
and controls *Pò* with the *Hun* (魂);
thus, the *Shén* always **nourishes** (載) *Pò* but is never **nourished** by it—
like a chariot’s movement, carrying all things within it,
like the passage of time, where cold and heat succeed one another.
With **heavenly wandering** (天游) in the heart,
the six *Chè* (彻) mutually interpenetrate—
transcending Heaven and Earth,
leaving all things behind,
yet the *Shén* is never constrained—
how much less could it be **obstructed** by *Pò*?

The *Cūnyì* (疏義) states:
*Pò* is born from **color** (色)—
its character is composed of *bái* (白).
*Bái* is the Yin hue.
If it enters without transformation,
knowing no obstruction,
then it adheres to the body and finds its resting place—
hence the Master Lao spoke of the *Pò* as **nourishment**.
**Nourishment** implies a resting place.
*The Hun* (魂) is the domain of *qi*—
its character is composed of *yún* (云).
*Yún* is the Yang *qi*.
If it moves in and out, dispersing and gathering,
while the *Shén* responds to it,
then it rides upon the *qi* and knows no obstruction—
hence the *Yì* spoke of the *Hun* as **wandering**.
**Wandering** implies no obstruction.

The Sage governs form with the *Shén*,
not allowing the *Shén* to be burdened by the form;
and controls *Pò* with the *Hun*,
not allowing the *Hun* to be confined by *Pò*.
Thus, the *Shén* always **nourishes** *Pò* but is never **nourished** by it—
like a chariot’s movement, carrying all things within it,
unspoiled in its core,
like the passage of time, where cold and heat succeed one another,
and all beings ride upon it.

With **heavenly wandering** in the heart,
one rises above the mundane and transcends the dust of the world,
yet fully encompasses the body—
this differs from those who restrict themselves to a narrow dwelling.
The six *Chè* (彻) mutually interpenetrate—
from the *Chè* of the eyes to the *Chè* of wisdom,
filling all without boundary—
this differs from the six *Záo* (凿) that repel one another.
Knowing that Heaven and Earth were born with me yet able to transcend them,
knowing that all things share existence with me yet able to leave them behind,
entering and exiting the six *Hé* (合),
spanning and weaving all directions,
yet the *Shén* is never constrained—
how much less could it be confined by form and **obstructed** by *Pò*?

Can one who **embraces Unity** (抱一) avoid separation?

Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
**Heaven’s Unity** (天一) generates water,
which in things is **Essence** (精).
**Earth’s Two** (地二) generates fire,
which in things is **Spirit** (神).
**Spirit** and **Essence** arise from the Dao,
the form is born from **Essence**,
and if one guards them without loss,
they align with **Spirit**—
then **Essence** and **Spirit** merge without separation.
With **Essence** gathering **Spirit**,
with **Spirit** animating the form,
and with the form preserving **Spirit**,
**Essence** is complete without depletion,
**Spirit** functions without exhaustion,
and the form lives without decay—
like the sun and moon adorning the sky,
like grasses and trees adorning the earth,
they never part.

I have often expounded this:
When a person is born,
**Essence** gathers **Spirit**,
and the bodily form is thus complete.
The fourfold flow spreads and converges,
reaching all extremes—
above it meets Heaven,
below it coils with Earth,
transforming and nurturing all things,
beyond any likeness—
this is called **Emperor-like** (同帝).
Yet the foolish of the world
enslave themselves to things,
lose their nature in worldly affairs,
and in no moment of rest
preserve **Spirit** within.
They chase endless desires,
abandon **Essence** externally,
and the *Hun* follows *Pò*,
while the form burdens **Spirit**—
thus they descend with all things into transformation,
is this not bewilderment?

The Sage is not so.
**Nourishing** *Pò* to harmonize,
**embracing Unity** to guard,
**preserving** **Spirit** to stillness—
then the form will naturally rectify itself.
Its **Spirit** rests in the **Great Mountain** (太山) yet does not change,
dwells in the **Deep Springs** (渊泉) yet is not dampened—
who knows its origin?
Who knows its end?
Hence it is said: *The Sage values **Essence**.*

The *Cūnyì* states:
The Dao generates **Unity** (一),
and **Unity** is water—
hence **Heaven’s Unity** generates water,
which in things is **Essence**.
**Unity** generates **Two** (二),
and **Two** is fire—
hence **Earth’s Two** generates fire,
which in things is **Spirit**.
All things possess *qi*,
and **Spirit** and **Essence** are the primal source of *qi*;
all things possess **numbers** (數),
and **Spirit** and **Essence** precede the manifestation of **numbers**.
Thus, **Spirit** and **Essence** arise from the Dao—
because the **illuminating** (昭昭) is generated from the **obscure** (冥冥).

Heaven and Earth contain **Essence**,
and all things are transformed and born—
the four seasons disperse **Essence** to become all things,
and thus the form is born from **Essence**—
because the **ordered** (有倫) is generated from the **formless** (無形).

If one **embraces Unity** (抱一) without detachment,
guards them without loss,
then **Spirit** is grasped and steadfast,
aligning with **Spirit**—
hence **Essence** and **Spirit** merge without separation,
because the **ultimate Essence** of the world can merge with the **ultimate Spirit** of the world.

With **Essence** gathering **Spirit**,
and **Spirit** not shaken by **Essence**,
then **Essence** is complete without depletion.
With **Spirit** animating the form,
and **Spirit** embraced in stillness,
then **Spirit** functions without exhaustion.
With the form preserving **Spirit**,
and **Spirit** guarding the form,
then the form lives without decay.
**Essence** complete and **Spirit** complete,
**Spirit** complete and the form complete—
like the sun and moon adorning the sky yet enduringly shining,
like grasses and trees adorning the earth yet enduringly standing,
they never part.

Moreover, when a person is born,
**Essence** is the root of the body—
when **Essence** is complete, **Spirit** follows,
thus **Essence** gathers **Spirit** and the bodily form is complete.
**Essence** emerges from the **ultimate Yin** (至阴),
**Spirit** from the **ultimate Yang** (至阳).
The Yin **Essence** is water,
the Yang **Spirit** is fire.
Water and fire originally have no form—
when sought with the **balance scale** (镒) and **fire drill** (燧),
they naturally arise,
and thus it is known that **Spirit** and **Essence** flow fourfold and converge—
reaching all extremes.

The **ultimate Yin** is solemn and still,
the **ultimate Yang** is radiant and vast—
when the two intermingle in harmony,
things are born,
and thus my body and Heaven and Earth share the same root.
Therefore, it is fitting that above it meets Heaven,
below it coils with Earth,
transforming and nurturing all things—
this is called **Emperor-like**.
Yet the great fools of the world,
throughout their lives, remain unenlightened—
heavy on externals, light on internals,
their wills chasing ends, abandoning roots,
enslaving themselves to things,
losing their nature in worldly affairs,
their hearts restless and never still,
unable to even pause for a moment—
no moment of rest to preserve **Spirit** within,
exhausted by endless desires,
they do not know where their **Spirit** returns,
abandon **Essence** externally,
and cannot gather **Essence** to contain the *Hun*—
the *Hun* follows *Pò*,
they cannot govern the form with **Spirit**,
the form burdens **Spirit**—
unable to distinguish themselves from things,
they descend with all things into transformation—
is this not bewilderment?

The Sage does not act thus.
He aligns the *Pò* with **Spirit** to manifest **Way** and resonate with **Virtue**,
embraces the **One** to preserve **Stillness** and thus attune with the **Dao**.

With neither sight nor hearing,
he alone guards **Spirit**—
and by embracing **Spirit** in **Stillness**,
he does not diminish its essence.
Cautiously guarding his form,
his body naturally rectifies itself.

His **Spirit** flows through **Great Void** without alteration,
its vastness unbounded;
dwells in **Deep Source** without dampness,
its subtlety unconfined.

Approach it, and you see no beginning—
who can trace its origin?
Follow it, and you find no end—
who can grasp its conclusion?
All stems from the **One’s** pure penetration.

Thus **Zhuangzi** said:
*"The Sage focuses on **Essence**."*
**Shun’s** admonition to **Yu** declared:
*"Focus only on **Essence**, focus only on the **One**."*
Did he know this truth?

Can you concentrate **Qi** to achieve **Softness**,
like the innocence of an infant?

**Emperor Hui’s** annotation states:
*"The **Yijing** says: **Qian** [乾], in its **Stillness**, is absolute in focus."*

**Yang Xiong** declared:
*"Harmonious **Softness** suffices to settle all things."*

**Stillness** without distraction is called **focused**,
**Harmony** without excess is called **soft**.
An infant, in repose, knows not what to do,
in motion, knows not where to go,
harbors neither judgment of right and wrong
nor taste for good and evil—
thus his **Qi** is concentrated,
achieving **Softness**.

**Mencius** said:
*"The faltering and rushing—
this is **Qi**,
yet it disrupts the heart."*

If the heart cannot focus **Qi**,
then **Qi** falters and rushes unnaturally,
and the heart grows **arrogant and unchecked**—
unable to be contained.

The Sage empties himself to engage the world,
his heart free from forcing **Qi**—
thus his **Stillness** remains undistracted,
his **Harmony** unexcessive—
what difference lies between him and an infant?
Hence it is said: *"Can you be like an infant?"*

**Mencius** also said:
*"When **Qi** is at its utmost vast and firm,
nourished upright without harm,
it fills the space between Heaven and Earth."*

Yet **Laozi** spoke of concentrating **Qi** to achieve **Softness**—
why so?

Utmost **Firmness** aligns with **Righteousness**,
**Softness** returns to **Nature**—
ancient **Dao/Way** and its techniques
are nowhere absent.

The **Annotations** state:
*"The **Yijing**, in the **Xìcí** [系辞], says:
**Qian** [乾], in its **Stillness**, is absolute in focus.*
*Its focus comes from **Stillness**—
thus **focused** means **Stillness** without clinging.*

*Yang Xiong, in the **Tàixuān** [太玄], says:
**Harmonious Softness** suffices to settle all things.*
*Its **Softness** settles all things through **Harmony**—
thus **soft** means **Harmony** without excess.*

Moreover, in human life within the **Great Transformation** [大化], there are four stages:
In infancy, before thoughts take form,
his repose is not from intent to still—
thus he rests without knowing what to do.
His motion is not from intent to act—
thus he moves without knowing where to go.
Following **Nature** and yielding to **Principle**,
free from contrivance,
he harbors neither judgment of right and wrong
nor taste for good and evil—
thus his **Qi** is **Stillness** without distraction,
capable of **Softness** without excess.

Indeed, **Will** commands **Qi** like a general,
a unified **Will** suffices to move **Qi**.
**Mencius** said:
*"The faltering and rushing—
this is **Qi**,
yet it disrupts the heart."*

Thus, concentrating **Qi** originates from the heart’s **One** and unchanging nature.
If **One** and unchanging,
then all things cannot sway the heart,
and **Qi** naturally concentrates.

If the heart fails to focus **Qi**,
then **Qi** falters and rushes unnaturally,
and the heart grows **arrogant and unchecked**—
unable to be contained.
This is like a strong beam
not bending to avoid death—
how can it remain like an infant?

The Sage empties himself to wander through the world,
and when adversity comes,
he regards it as a **hollow boat** drifting on **floating tiles**,
thus able to preserve **Softness**.
His heart, free from forcing **Qi**,
what difference lies between him and an infant?

**Mencius** said:
*"When **Qi** is at its utmost vast and firm,
nourished upright without harm,
it fills the space between Heaven and Earth."*

Yet **Laozi** spoke of concentrating **Qi** to achieve **Softness**—
why so?

Utmost **Vastness** aligns with the **Dao/Way**,
utmost **Firmness** aligns with **Righteousness**.
**Mencius** spoke of utmost **Firmness**,
emphasizing **action in Righteousness**,
discussing **Qi’s** application.
**Laozi** spoke of **Softness**,
emphasizing **return to Nature**,
discussing **Qi’s** root.

If **Guan Yin** [关尹] takes its **pure Qi** [纯气],
and **Huzi** [壶子] takes its **balanced Qi** [衡气],
their words each hold truth—
thus it is.

The ancient spread of the **Dao/Way**,
nowhere absent—
this is why.

**Purification** and **profound insight**,
can they be without flaw?

**Emperor Hui’s** annotation states:
*"The Sage uses this to cleanse the heart,
thus purifying all actions without attachment.
He uses this to retreat and hide in **secrecy** [密],
thus gazing into **mysteries** and recognizing **principles** in silence."*

If such is the case,
his **body** remains **pure and simple** without burden,
his errors pass without regret,
his actions align without self-congratulation—
what flaw could there be?

The **Annotations** state:
*"The ancients held that before **things** began to exist was **unattained** [未至],
and only when reaching **before things began to exist** [未始有物]
was it **attained**—
thus **purification** of all actions without attachment.
Using this to cleanse the heart,
who could have even a hair’s breadth of disturbance?
That which has **words** and **nearness** is not **true** [真],
and only when reaching **forgetting words and ceasing contrivance** [忘言息迩]
is it **true**—
thus **profound insight** into **mysteries** and recognizing **principles** in silence.
Using this to retreat and hide in **secrecy**,
who could have **words** to burden him?
If such is the case,
his **body** remains **pure and simple** without burden,
unmixed with anything, undiminished.
His **Spirit** rides the **times** [时] to act,
though errors occur,
in **times** he cannot avoid them—
thus errors pass without regret.
Following **principles** to move,
his alignment is inevitable—
in **principles** he cannot avoid it—
thus actions align without self-congratulation.
What flaw could there be?"

To love the people and govern the realm,
can one be without **action** [为]?

**Emperor Hui’s** annotation states:
*"With **benevolence** [仁] to love the people,
with **wisdom** [智] to govern the realm,
spreading **teaching** and **refining laws**,
to unify the world with **goodness**—
how difficult is this in terms of **non-action** [无为]!

The Sage’s benefit and nourishment extend to all ages,
not from loving the people.
His **achievements** overshadow the world,
yet he does not claim them—
thus **non-action**,
using the world while having abundance."

The **Annotations** state:
*"Loving people and benefiting things is called **benevolence**,
using **benevolence** to love the people
brings forth a heart of love and benefit.
Right and wrong, near and far is called **wisdom**,
using **wisdom** to govern the realm
brings forth a heart of right and wrong."*

Though teaching and cultivation cannot be abandoned,
and refining laws cannot be halted,
to unify the world through goodness—
all still fall short of **non-action**,
for how difficult it is!

The Sage does not act thus.
Though he acts, he never departs from **non-action**,
and his actions arise from **non-action**—
thus his benefit and nourishment extend to all ages,
yet he does not love the people.
He never intended to love the people,
yet his achievements overshadow the world,
as if they were not his own.
He never intended to govern the realm—
thus **non-action**,
using the world while having abundance.

When the **Heavenly Gate** opens and shuts,
can it be **feminine** [雌]?

**Emperor Hui’s** annotation states:
*"The Classic says: Know the **masculine** [雄], hold to the **feminine** [雌], and become the **valley** [溪] of the world.
The Sage embodies the transformations of the **Dao**,
expanding and contracting, opening and closing without defying the seasons,
manifesting **softness** and **strength** as needed.
Yet he never leads and always follows,
never overcomes things and always yields to them—
thus the world delights in pushing him forward without tiring,
and he is truly **feminine**."*

The **Annotations** state:
*"The Classic says: Know the **masculine**, hold to the **feminine**, and become the **valley** of the world.
For the **masculine** conquers things with **strength**,
while the **feminine** abides in **softness**.
To know the **masculine** while holding to the **feminine**—
this is to not conquer with **strength** yet still abide in **softness**,
thus becoming the **valley** of the world.
For the **valley** is where water gathers after flowing downward.

The Sage responds to transformations,
moves with **Heaven’s** course,
thus embodying the **Dao’s** transformations.
Rolling and unrolling, opening and closing,
shifting with the times—
thus his rolling and unrolling, opening and closing,
never defies its season.
Knowing **softness**, knowing **strength**,
knowing subtlety, knowing clarity—
all are employed as needed.
Knowing to hold back while appearing first,
never leading yet always following;
knowing to guard **softness** while being strong,
never overcoming yet always yielding—
thus his goodness is never exhausted,
the world delights in pushing him forward without tiring,
and he is truly **feminine**."*

**Illuminated and all-pervading, can one truly be without knowing?**

**Emperor Hui’s** annotation states:
*"With **cleverness** and **wisdom**, one guards with **stupidity**—
this carries the same meaning."*

The **Annotations** state:
*"True knowing is **non-knowing**,
and **non-knowing** is knowing—
thus one can be without knowing,
yet without **non-knowing**.
Gongzi said: With **cleverness** and **wisdom**, one guards with **stupidity**.
**Cleverness** is for plotting,
**wisdom** is for discernment,
**sageliness** means nothing is beyond reach,
**wisdom** means nothing is unknown—
thus **cleverness** and **wisdom** may be called **illuminated and all-pervading**.
Yet the **Perfect One** penetrates the boundless yet seems dim,
is vast yet seems obscured—
this is guarding with **stupidity**.
What else could it be but **non-knowing**?

In ancient times, **Zhi Yong** observed thieves,
**Wenzi** knew his death was unjust,
**Yanzi** appeared foolish,
and **Confucius** praised him—
thus, **illuminated and all-pervading** yet able to **non-knowing**,
he attained the **Dao** of **holding fullness**."

To **nourish and accumulate** [生之畜之],
to **nourish without possessing**,
to **act without relying**,
to **lengthen without controlling**—
this is called **Mysterious Virtue** [玄德].

**Emperor Hui’s** annotation states:
*"The Sage preserves **spirit** and knows transformation,
uniting with the **Dao**—
thus he aligns with the **Divine Bright**,
nurtures all things without limit.
He **nourishes** to fulfill their nature,
**accumulates** to perfect their sustenance.
Without a heart of love and benefit,
thus he **nourishes without possessing**.
Without boastful conduct,
thus he **acts without relying**.
Without artificial craft,
thus he **lengthens without controlling**.
Such is his virtue—deep, distant, and contrary to things—
thus it is called **Mysterious Virtue**.
**Heaven’s** **Dao** ascends to the north,
then intertwines with things.
**Mysterious** is **Heaven’s** hue—
when the Sage embodies **Heaven’s** **Dao** as **virtue**,
nothing short of **Mysterious** suffices to name it."*

The **Annotations** state:
*"The Sage’s **spirit** is wondrous yet unfathomable,
only he preserves it, uniting with the **Divine**.
Transformation follows form and shifts—
only he knows it, uniting with **change**.
The Sage preserves **spirit** and knows transformation,
thus uniting with the **Dao**.

Only by uniting with the **Dao** does he align with **Heaven** and **Earth’s** virtue,
becoming the foundation for all things—
even **Heaven** and **Earth’s** **Divine Bright** suffice to match him,
and all things, though many, suffice to be nurtured by him,
with nothing beyond his capacity.
Thus his **nourishing** fulfills their nature—
this is the **Dao** of all things finding their proper place.
His **accumulating** perfects their sustenance—
this is the **Dao** of all things thriving when properly nourished.
Though he initiates movement,
yet all things self-generate and self-transform,
treating all things as **straw dogs**—
he never harbors a heart of love and benefit—
who could possess this?
Thus he **nourishes without possessing**.

Though he manifests in transformations,
yet the people daily improve without knowing him—
he never engages in boastful conduct—
who could rely on this?
Thus he **acts without relying**.

Though he appears in unification,
yet his governance is self-originating while great measures are not imposed—
he never employs artificial craft—
who could control this?
Thus he **lengthens without controlling**.

The **nourisher** often errs by possessing while able to **not possess**,
the **actor** often errs by relying on completion while able to **not rely**,
the **controller** often errs by carving and refining while able to **not control**—
yet his subtlety and obscurity are beyond measure,
transcending all things—
thus his virtue is profound, distant, and contrary to things.
No virtue is more subtle and profound—
thus it is called **Mysterious Virtue**.

The *Classic of Changes* says: **Heaven is Mysterious, Earth is Yellow.**
Thus **Mysterious** is **Heaven’s** hue.
Spring is the **Azure Heaven**—
**Mysterious** is the **Dao’s** return to winter,
the time of the **Northern Gate’s** distinction.
When the Sage embodies **Heaven’s** **Dao** as **virtue**,
refined and subtle—
**Heaven’s** **Dao** has already unfolded—
nothing short of **Mysterious** suffices to name it."*

**Chapter Eleven: The Thirty Spokes**

Thirty spokes converge at a single hub—
when the hub is empty, the carriage’s use is complete.
Mold clay into a vessel—
when the vessel is empty, its use is complete.
Carve doors and windows for a dwelling—
when the dwelling is empty, its use is complete.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation:
The interplay of presence and absence is the ultimate principle—
utility emerges from their interplay.
Presence and absence differ in form:
presence as substance, absence as function—
the operation of yin and yang, the logic of all things.

The carriage’s use lies in motion,
the vessel’s in containment,
the dwelling’s in emptiness.
Mysterious utility arises from the utmost absence,
transformation is concealed in non-attachment—
like a balance without form,
manifesting through things yet unseen.
The Perfect Person’s mind ever resolves this truth.

Explanatory commentary:
Observing the ultimate principle,
one perceives presence yet it is not truly present,
absence yet it is not truly absent.
In the interplay of presence and absence,
all utility arises—
thus presence and absence align,
utility flows—
this is the ultimate principle.

Observing yin and yang, all things:
what is manifest and visible is called presence,
what is obscure and unfathomable is called absence.
Distinguishing presence and absence in their forms—
presence as substance, absence as function—
the operation of yin and yang, the logic of all things.

Thus thirty spokes converge at a hub,
clay is shaped into vessels,
doors and windows are carved for dwellings—
all are manifest in presence,
and utility lies therein.
Yet what enables the carriage to move,
the vessel to contain,
the dwelling to be empty?
Mysterious utility arises from the utmost absence,
yet no trace is seen;
transformation is concealed in non-attachment,
yet no boundary is glimpsed.
Like a balance without form,
manifesting through things yet unseen—
neither forcing nor resisting,
responding yet not hoarding—
neither presence nor absence,
yet adapting to things without harm.
The Perfect Person’s mind ever resolves this truth.

Thus presence is for utility,
absence is for function.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation:
Presence is solid, absence is void—
solidity possesses form, likeness, sound, color, and substance,
voidness enables motion, measure, response, and fulfillment without end.
Between Heaven and Earth,
the Dao manifests through vessels,
thus absence never abandons presence;
vessels embody the Dao’s mystery,
thus presence inevitably returns to absence.
Wood bends, water moistens,
fire radiates, metal hardens,
earth balances, and grains emerge—
this is presence, yet humanity relies on it for utility.
Heaven’s motion, Earth’s stability,
the seasons’ progression, all things flourishing—
who governs this?
This is absence.
Yet the world fails to perceive its operation,
so its utility is inexhaustible.

Presence and absence generate each other—
Laozi elucidates these three principles.
Explanatory commentary:
Presence entails obstruction, absence is not mere emptiness;
absence entails continuity, yet is not mere void.
Solidity possesses form, likeness, sound, color, and substance—
thus presence is for utility.
Voidness enables motion, measure, response, and fulfillment without end—
thus absence is for function.
The Dao descends into the dual realm:
above form, it does not depart from presence,
yet presence also reveals it—
thus the Dao manifests through vessels,
absence never abandons presence.
Below form, it does not extend beyond absence,
yet presence also conceals it—
thus vessels embody the Dao’s mystery,
presence inevitably returns to absence.

The Five Phases disperse:
water and fire for function,
metal and wood for vessels,
earth and grain for utility.
Wood bends, water moistens,
fire radiates, metal hardens,
earth balances, and grains emerge—
all within Heaven and Earth,
what does not depend on this?
Thus humanity relies on it for utility.

Descending into the realm,
Heaven moves, Earth rests,
the seasons progress, all things thrive—
as if governed by unseen mechanisms,
as if turning yet unable to halt—
who directs this?
It is all Heaven.
Thus the world fails to perceive its operation,
yet absence is true presence,
presence is genuine absence—
presence and absence generate each other,
like a cycle without end.
From the vastness of Heaven and Earth
to the minutiae of autumnal hairs—
nothing escapes this.

Through the carriage’s motion,
the vessel’s containment,
the dwelling’s capacity,
one understands all things under Heaven—
their motion, their containment, their capacity—
by analogy.
Laozi expounds these three principles,
thus one knows ten thousand things from a single example.

**Chapter Twelve: The Five Colors**

The five colors blind the eyes,
the five tones deafen the ears.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation:
The eyes encompass two [yin lines],
their seeing is innate—
penetrating to clarity, wisdom arises,
sufficient to resolve all doubts.
The ears contain one [yang line],
their hearing is innate—
penetrating to acuity, strategy arises,
sufficient to communicate all intentions.
Those who perceive the Dao,
above form, gaze with spirit alone,
beyond sound, listen with qi alone—
what need for ears and eyes?
How could colors and sounds obstruct them?

Explanatory commentary:
Two broken lines yield a female,
yin within, yang without—
this is the symbol of the eyes,
thus the eyes encompass two.
Derived from the eight trigrams as fire,
fire’s light projects outward—
thus innate seeing is vision.
Penetrating vision becomes wisdom,
wisdom resolves all doubts—
what five colors could disrupt this clarity?

A whole line yields a male,
yin without, yang within—
this is the symbol of the ears,
thus the ears contain one.
Derived from the eight trigrams as water,
water’s clarity illuminates within—
thus innate hearing is perception.
Penetrating hearing becomes acuity,
acuity devises strategy,
sufficient to communicate all intentions—
what five tones could drown this clarity?

Those who perceive the Dao comprehend this,
and grasp the subtleties of seeing and hearing.
Above form, they gaze with spirit alone—
thus they see what is unseen.
They grasp the subtleties of hearing beyond sound,
beyond voice, they listen with qi alone—
thus they hear what is unsounded.
Seeing nothing unseen, hearing nothing unheard,
their vision and hearing transcend ears and eyes,
yet all they perceive is true—
what sounds or colors could ever obscure them?

Common folk cannot bear what they see,
nor comprehend what they hear.
They harmonize with the unsounded,
enslaved by ears and eyes to external burdens,
delight in brightness yet indulge in color—
who knows the Dao’s true hue?
They delight in acuity yet indulge in sound—
who knows the Dao’s great resonance?

When cleverness fades and wisdom dims,
the deaf are not truly without ears,
the blind are not truly without eyes—
what choice between them?
Yet people blind and deaf themselves—
could sounds and colors make them so?

The five flavors leave the mouth parched,
hunting and chasing drive the heart to madness.
*(Note: The Dao’s utterance is subtle, without taste.)*
The five flavors all share a common craving,
yet rich flavors are truly poisonous—
thus they leave the mouth parched.

Human life is brief, the body small,
yet the heart aligns with the transformations of the cosmos.
The sage’s heart moves and weaves the myriad realms,
stills and balances heaven and earth.

Common folk pursue hunting and chasing,
losing their true nature in the process,
unable to master themselves—
is this not the greatest sorrow?

*(Explanatory commentary:)*
People eat grains and livestock,
deer and elk eat grasses,
minnows and maggots eat snakes,
owls and crows feast on mice—
which knows the Dao’s true flavor?
The true flavor is the Dao’s utterance:
subtle, without taste.

All know the flavors they crave are shared,
yet they do not know the flavor of the Dao exists—
thus they take grains, livestock, and rice as sufficient.
Yet rich flavors are truly poisonous,
though they may nourish the body,
they lose the greater essence.
This is why the five flavors leave the mouth parched.

*(Note: Parched—meaning errant.)*
The mouth parched loses its true flavor.
Moreover, human life is brief and small—
what belongs to humanity is a body not exceeding a few feet,
yet vast is the heart that aligns with the cosmos,
companion to the transformations of nature.

The Sage exhausts the truth of the heart,
moves and weaves the myriad directions—
then flows unrestricted, without question.
In stillness, he weighs heaven and earth—
then illuminates boundlessly, without limit.
Now he governs the six *qi*’s distinctions,
to wander the boundless—
why then engage in hunting and trapping?

The ignorant do not know:
to conquer oneself is strength.
They labor only in chasing and pursuing,
sometimes stumbling in haste to agitate the heart,
yet cannot find themselves—
and thus lose their true heart.
Is this not called great sorrow?

Rare treasures obstruct one’s conduct.
**Huizong’s Annotation:** *Li* nourishes people, but *huò* transforms them—
thus, when *li* and *huò* interact, both are sustained.

The Sage does not prize rare treasures,
does not prize curiosities,
holds common goods in low regard—
wishing people to rest content in their allotted portion,
without coveting.

To prize rare treasures
leads to neglecting the essence of life and death,
yet gluttonously seeking wealth and rank—
what conduct can endure this?
Thus, rare treasures obstruct one’s path.

**Zhonghu’s praise of Tang** says:
*"Do not cultivate wealth and profit."*
**Confucius told Zigong:**
*"You, Shì, did not receive your mandate yet engage in commerce—"*
So great is the obstruction of wealth to conduct.

**Explanatory Notes** say:
The ancient kings encouraged migration,
harmonizing abundance and scarcity,
to enrich and circulate wealth,
so that *li* and *huò* interact and both are sustained.
Indeed, *li* nourishes people,
but when transformed, it becomes *huò*—
thus, when *li* and *huò* interact, both are sustained.

Rare treasures—
clothing that cannot be worn,
food that cannot be eaten—
if these are prized,
then one neglects the essence of life and death,
yet gluttonously seeks wealth and rank,
plundering and deceiving without restraint.
To make people rest in the essence of life and death,
only conduct can guard this—
how difficult!

Thus, the Sage does not prize curiosities,
holds common goods in low regard—
for no other reason
than to make people content in their portion,
without coveting.

Therefore, he discards gold on the rugged peaks,
casts pearls into the Five Lakes’ depths—
not prizing rare treasures,
so that nourishment for self and others is complete,
and none are obstructed in their path.

**Zhonghu’s praise of Tang** says:
*"Do not cultivate wealth and profit."*
—meaning that *li* should not govern the conduct of the noble.

**Confucius told Zigong:**
*"You, Shì, did not receive your mandate yet engage in commerce—"*
—to admonish that *li* is not the conduct of the gentleman.

**Zhuangzi** desired to shatter jade and destroy pearls,
valuing instead those who do not covet wealth—
for this reason.

Thus, the Sage nourishes the belly, not the eyes—
hence, he discards the latter to take the former.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
The *guà* of *Kūn* is the belly—
thick and bearing, containing all.
The *guà* of *Lí* are the eyes—
outerly perceiving, brightly discerning.
Thick and bearing, it receives all without discrimination,
collecting and storing—
nothing is left out, nothing is rejected—
thus, it receives without limit.
Outerly perceiving, it cannot help but distinguish—
it sees a hundred paces away,
yet cannot see its own eyelashes—
thus, it has its limits.

The Sage effaces distinctions,
unifies good and evil,
taking the world as measure—
thus, he takes this containing belly,
without partiality or selection,
without taking or discarding,
not governing each matter or scrutinizing each thing.
Hence, he discards those outer-seeing eyes.

**Zhuangzi** said:
*"No thief is greater than virtue with a heart,
and a heart with eyes."*
Thus, the Sage rejects them.

**Explanatory Notes** say:
*Kūn* is thick and bearing, containing all things,
nurturing and transforming with radiance—
taken near to the body, it is the belly,
thick and bearing, containing all.
Outer *yáng*, inner *yīn*—
the two *yáng* make *Lí*, taken near to the body, are the eyes,
outerly perceiving, brightly discerning.

Thick and bearing, it never discriminates—
collecting and storing, nothing is left out, nothing is rejected—
thus, it receives without limit.
Outerly perceiving, it cannot help but distinguish—
it sees a hundred paces away,
yet cannot see its own eyelashes—
thus, it has its limits.

The Sage effaces distinctions,
unifies good and evil,
taking the world as measure—
thus, he takes this containing belly,
without partiality or selection,
without taking or discarding,
not governing each matter or scrutinizing each thing.
Hence, he discards those outer-seeing eyes.

**Zhuangzi** said:
*"No thief is greater than virtue with a heart,
and a heart with eyes."*
Indeed, *dé* opens heaven,
but *zéi* (harm) opens humanity—
a heart with eyes opens humanity and brings harm—
thus, the Sage does not make them eyes.

**Chapter Thirteen: On Honor and Humiliation**
Honor and humiliation are like a startling fright,
great calamities are like a threat to one’s life.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
Honor is below, rank is above—
to dwell in honor and take it as pride is humiliation.
To dwell in rank and take it as a burden is no greater calamity.
Those who regard wealth as right cannot refuse office,
those who regard eminence as right cannot refuse fame.
Those who cling to power cannot entrust it to others—
to hold it makes one tremble,
to relinquish it brings sorrow.
This is why honor and humiliation are like a startling fright?

Pain and anguish, ease and contentment—
these gather in the body,
urgent dread and joyful delight—
these drown the heart.
This is why great calamities are like a threat to one’s life?

**Explanatory Notes** say:
Honor is what people prize—
the way of those below.
Rank is what people burden themselves with—
the way of those above.
To dwell in honor and take it as pride is to exhaust its fullness—
exhaust its fullness, and it becomes humiliation.
Or, to dwell in rank and take it as a burden is to be proud of one’s rank—
to be proud of one’s rank is no greater calamity.

Those who regard wealth as right cannot refuse office—
burdened by abundant gain.
Those who regard eminence as right cannot refuse fame—
burdened by high renown.
Those who cling to power cannot entrust it to others—
because power and authority are not uniquely theirs,
the proud then grow sorrowful.
Thus, these three:
to hold them brings trembling—
this is *"having gained it, fearing to lose it"*—
to relinquish them brings sorrow—
this is *"parting with it, finding no joy"*—
thus, honor and humiliation are like a startling fright?

What gathers in the body—
if not pain and anguish from the assault of *yīn* and *yáng*,
then ease and contentment.
What drowns the heart in human affairs—
if not urgent dread from proximity to *yīn*,
then joyful delight from leaning toward *yáng*.
The myriad breaths of one’s life rise and clash—
to dwell in rank and take it as a burden,
its calamities are no different.

「Why does this great *qi* weigh upon one like the body?
What is meant by *"honor and humiliation"*?
Honor is low—
*Huizong*’s annotation says: The dragon, as a creature,
shifts and transforms at will,
unrestrained, impossible to tame.
If it can be covered by the ancient [ritual],
then its spirit is set on domestication—
this is the path of humiliation.

The ancients who excelled in *shi*’s way:
The rank of three *jīng* flags was insufficient to shake their resolve,
the wealth of ten thousand *chóng* measures could not shift their steadfastness.
To dwell in favor yet ponder peril,
to hold fortune yet act as though empty—
then what humiliation could there be?

To covet rank and crave wealth,
to know advancement yet not retreat,
to grasp gain yet not foresee loss—
then one is already debased.
Thus, to receive favor from others
is the way of the lowly.

The *Shūyì* elaborates:
The heavens’ matchless transformations are like the dragon—
united, it forms a body;
scattered, it becomes a pattern.
This is shifting and transforming at will,
unrestrained, impossible to tame.

Unable to see yet unable to contain [its nature],
it has not yet escaped the ancient [ritual]’s cover—
if one strives to domesticate it for harness and riding,
then regret is inevitable.
This is why—
if it can be covered by the ancient [ritual],
then its spirit is set on domestication—
this is the path of humiliation.

The reason honor becomes humiliation
is thus.

Therefore, the ancients who excelled in *shi*’s way:
The rank of three *jīng* flags was insufficient to shake their resolve,
for they knew that what lies within me
is nobler than the rank of three *jīng* flags.
The wealth of ten thousand *chóng* measures could not shift their steadfastness,
for they knew that what lies within me
is richer than ten thousand *chóng* measures.

To master the art of retaining favor,
one dwells in favor yet ponders peril;
to grasp the way of preserving fortune,
one holds fortune yet acts as though empty—
thus, they can endure wealth and rank without losing themselves,
and their blessings and fortunes endure long and steady.
What humiliation could there be?

If one covets rank yet falsifies oneself to gain nobility,
or craves wealth yet indulges in corruption to gain riches,
knowing advancement yet not retreat,
obscuring the logic of growth and decline,
grasping gain yet not foreseeing loss,
ignoring the cycles of fullness and emptiness—
then the nobility of *Zhao Meng*
is but a step toward debasement.

Thus, to receive favor from others
is the way of the lowly.

To gain it is like a startling fright,
to lose it is like a startling fright—
this is *"honor and humiliation are like a startling fright"*.

*Huizong*’s annotation says:
The cap and robes upon one’s person
are not the Dao/Way of life and death.
External things arrive like transient guests—
if they come, they cannot be refused;
thus, the sage does not take delight in gaining them.
If they depart, they cannot be detained—
thus, the sage does not sorrow over losing them.
Yet now, if they depart, one finds no joy,
and if received, one takes pleasure—
so gain and loss burden the heart,
how could one not be startled?

*Liu Xià Huì* served as a magistrate,
thrice dismissed yet refusing to leave,
*Zhèng Kǎo Fù* received three commands,
yet fled along the walls—
how different from this!

The *Shūyì* elaborates:
True nobility lies within oneself—
enough to rival the titles of a kingdom.
Yet the cap and robes upon one’s person
are not the Dao/Way of life and death.
External things cannot be clung to,
and seeking them brings no benefit to what is already gained—
for they arrive like transient guests.
Only time is without end,
so their coming cannot be refused,
and their gain—
what does it add to me?
Thus, the sage does not take delight in gaining them.

Only division is without permanence,
so their departure cannot be detained—
and their loss—
what harm does it bring to me?
Thus, the sage does not sorrow over losing them.
The deluded do not understand the extremes of destitution and abundance,
like the sequence of winter and summer—
if they depart, one finds no joy,
or one may fall into poverty and lowliness.
If received, one takes pleasure,
or one may be bent by wealth and rank.
Gain and loss wage war within the chest,
even the spirit platform is obstructed—
how few escape being startled!

*Liu Xià Huì* served as a magistrate,
thrice dismissed yet refusing to leave,
*Zhèng Kǎo Fù* received three commands,
yet fled along the walls—
could it be that gain and loss burdened their hearts?
What is meant by *"the greatest calamity of nobility is like the body"*?

*Huizong*’s annotation says:
To grasp advantage and power,
to monopolize rewards and punishments,
to wield blessings and threats—
the world fears them like gods and reveres them like emperors.
This is nobility indeed.

Yet the sage does not let nobility weigh upon them,
so they can long endure nobility without exhaustion.
Like a person whose limbs and body fall into ruin,
whose ears and eyes lose their acuity,
who abandons form yet is not bound by it,
who discards wisdom yet does not cling to it—
to merge with the Great Unity,
then there is none who does not find themselves at ease.
The world’s people exchange their nature for things,
so they are burdened by things yet cannot forget their power.
They let form weigh upon the heart,
so they lose the heart yet cannot forget form—
their calamity is great.

The *Shūyì* elaborates:
To dwell in nobility as one’s own,
one cannot escape exhaustion—
only if one does not claim nobility as one’s own
can one truly guard it.
Like a person’s body:
to think it is mine brings constant hindrance;
to know the body is not truly mine,
then one can preserve it.

Now, if one grasps the weight of advantage and power,
holds the authority of rewards and punishments,
and monopolizes the tools of blessings and threats—
the world fears them like gods and reveres them like emperors,
their nobility is unmatched.
Yet only the sage, wielding the void to govern the full,
does not dwell in nobility as their own—
so though they are noble, they suffer no exhaustion.

Like a person’s body:
to contain the hundred bones yet let limbs falter,
to embody ears and eyes yet let wisdom dim,
to abandon form yet not be constrained by it,
to discard wisdom yet not cling to it—
then one attains Great Permeation,
constructing the formless,
and there is none who does not find themselves at ease.
Who could take the body as exhaustion?

The vulgar and shallow cannot exhaust their nature while fixating on power,
so they are burdened by things, exchanging their nature for them;
they cannot exhaust their heart while clinging to form,
so they are weighed down by form, losing their heart.
To exchange one’s nature for things,
to let form weigh upon the heart—
their calamity is great.

The reason I have great calamity
is that I have a body.
When I have no body,
what calamity could I have?

*Huizong*’s annotation says:
Human birth—
the hundred bones, nine orifices, six viscera are complete and stored within.
Who could I truly call my own?
To recognize and claim them all
is but delusion.
Those who embody the Dao understand this,
so Confucius said:
*"Morning hearing of the Dao,
evening death would be acceptable."*
Mencius said:
*"Life and death are not two separate things."*
Yan Ziyuan said:
*"Yan [Child] sat and forgot himself."*」

The greatness of life and death is vast indeed,
yet it does not alter one’s self—
how much more so the gains and losses, calamities and blessings, that lie between them?

This has no other cause
than knowing that within one’s form and body dwell the true *Dao/Way* and sovereign.
Thus, the *Zhi Ren* of antiquity
did not let profit weigh upon their form,
