nor did their form weigh upon their heart.
They saw all things as inseparable from themselves,
yet could not choose among them—
then their body was not truly theirs.

Above, they played with the *造物者* [Creator],
below, they befriended the external, endless life and death.
Who could truly weary them?
Favored by the world, yet they took it as dishonor;
held in esteem by all, yet they saw it as no burden—
for they knew the external things could not be relied upon,
and this was the natural order.

If one can truly possess without possessing,
then though favored, they would not be shamed,
though esteemed, they would not be weary.
Yi Yin did not dwell on favor and success,
while Yao did not take his position as pleasure—
this is nearly it.

*Huizong*’s annotation says:
The world is a great vessel,
but only the *Dao/Way* can move it;
the world is a divine vessel,
but only the *Dao/Way* can guard it.
The sage embodies the *Dao/Way*,
so when they govern the world, the world finds joy in their benevolence without tiring.
The lesser ones know that to value their body is not to demean themselves in serving things,
as though they could entrust it—
to love their body is not to harm themselves in being constrained by things,
as though they could rely upon it.

Thus it is said:
The true *Dao/Way* is used to govern oneself,
its remnants to govern the state,
and its dregs to govern the world.
The vulgar *junzi* of the world
endanger their body and abandon their life to serve things—
is this not tragic?

The *Shu Yi* says:
Within the six directions and beyond,
there is no end or limit—
the world is a great vessel.
There must be what transcends the vessel
before it can be moved—
what transcends the vessel is the *Dao/Way*.
Established in the unfathomable,
acting without fixed methods—
the world is a divine vessel.
There must be what aligns with the divine
before it can be guarded—
what aligns with the divine is the *Dao/Way*.
The sage embodies this within themselves,
so they govern the world without corrupting their nature,
bear the world’s burdens without altering their *virtue/power*,
and from west to east, from south to north,
none do not submit—
the world finds joy in their benevolence without tiring.

The lesser ones understand that things are not truly things,
yet they do not know how to govern the world.
They know that one limb is heavier than one kingdom,
two arms are heavier than the world,
and the body is heavier than two arms—
to value their body is not to harm themselves,
to love their body is not to demean themselves—
as though they could entrust and rely upon it.

Is it not that they mutilate life and harm their nature,
sacrificing their body—
restlessly enslaved by things,
wearily burdened by things,
not knowing the way to preserve themselves?

This is exactly what Zhuangzi meant by:
The true *Dao/Way* is used to govern oneself,
its remnants to govern the state,
and its dregs to govern the world.

The vulgar *junzi* of the world do not know that the body is the master of life,
so they kill their body to achieve fame,
use many means to destroy their life,
chase after things without returning—
endangering their body and abandoning their life to follow things,
like using the *Su Hou’s pearl* to shoot a thousand *ren*-high sparrow,
losing far more than they gain—
is this not tragic?

**Chapter 14: The Vision That Does Not See Forms**
The vision that does not see forms is called *yi* [level/plain].

*Huizong*’s annotation says:
The eyes are the master of vision,
vision is used to distinguish things—
*yi* is level and without distinction,
not within the reach of vision,
so it is called *yi*.

The *Tai Yi* [Great Oneness] is unseen,
yet *qi* is already there.

The *Shu Yi* says:
Heaven gives birth to wood in threes,
which in humans becomes the liver—
the liver opens to the eyes,
so in the five *zang* organs, it governs vision.
Thus the eyes are the master of vision.

The *ming liang* [clear vision] is *li* [separation] in two,
symbolized in the eyes,
and the eyes, regarding colors,
unite only after seeing—
thus vision is used to distinguish things.

Yet when vision can see,
it has not yet separated from colors—
whether high or low,
it can be distinguished.
The wondrous *Dao/Way* is not a color—
green and empty, void and empty,
not the slightest trace or sign—
vision is insufficient to see it.
Its level and boundless nature—
who could distinguish high from low?

The *Liezi* states: *The Tai Yi* [Great Oneness] is unseen, yet *qi* is already there.

In the beginning of *hunlun* [chaotic unity], *qi* has not yet manifested, its level and boundless nature is known, hence it is called *yi* [evenness].

When hearing does not hear, it is called *xi* [subtle/rare].

The *Huizong* [Emperor Huizong] annotated: The ears govern hearing, and hearing is used to discern things. *Xi* [subtle/rare] implies a faintness with gaps, beyond what hearing can perceive—hence it is called *xi*.

*The great sound, rare and faint, is already there.*

The *Shu Yi* [loose exposition] says:
Heaven one gives birth to water,
in the *zang* [viscera] it becomes the kidneys—
the kidneys open to the ears,
so in the five *zang* organs, it governs hearing,
and thus the ears are the master of hearing.

Water reaching its extreme becomes *kan* [the abyss],
symbolized in the ears,
and the ears, regarding sounds,
distinguish them only after perception—
thus hearing is used to discern things.

Yet when hearing can perceive,
it has not yet separated from sounds—
whether clear or murky,
none can distinguish them.

The wondrous *Dao/Way* has no sound—
still and vast, *leao* [silent and empty],
never the slightest echo or resonance,
hearing is insufficient to perceive it.

The wind ceases, the rustling subsides,
faint and with gaps—
the *jing* [classic] says: *The great sound, rare and faint, is already there.*

It moves without direction,
dwells in the *yaoming* [profoundly obscure],
its faintness is known—
hence it is called *xi*.

When it cannot be grasped, it is called *wei* [minute].

The *Huizong* annotated: *Minute, how minute!*—
reaching the point of formlessness,
who could grasp it?

*The great form is formless, is already there.*

The *Shu Yi* says:
When *qi* transforms, only then does form arise,
and when form arises, it can be perceived.

The essence of the *Dao/Way* is profoundly obscure—
before anything began,
following it, yet unable to grasp it—
this is called *minute, how minute!*
reaching the point of formlessness.

When form exists, it can be grasped,
but the *Dao/Way*’s formlessness—
who could grasp it?

The *jing* [classic] says: *The great form is formless*—
then what form is greater than the *Dao/Way*?

It is only because it is formless,
that its greatness knows no bounds.

These three cannot be defined with precision—
hence they merge into one.

The *Huizong* annotated:
*The Tai Yi* [Great Oneness] is undifferentiated—
who could distinguish high from low?

*The great sound, rare and faint*—
who could discern clear from murky?

*The great form is formless*—
who could define vast from minute?

The eyes have no use for their brightness,
the ears no application for their sharpness,
the body no exertion for its strength—
here lies the *Dao/Way*'s complete essence.

It cannot be exhausted by inquiry,
nor attained by exploration.

When *qi*, form, and substance are present yet undivided,
thus they merge into one.

Yet once they have become one,
how could it not be called *formless*?

The *Cushui* [commentary] says:
*The Tai Yi* [Great Oneness] is yet unjudged—
the *Liezi*’s "color-color" never reveals this.
"Color-color" is without color,
how could it distinguish high from low?

*The great sound is faint*—
the *Liezi*’s "sound-sound" never emits this.
"Sound-sound" is without sound,
how could it discern clear from murky?

*The great form is formless*—
the *Liezi*’s "shape-shape" never displays this.
"Shape-shape" is without form,
how could it define vast from minute?

Even if *Lizhu* [legendary seer] were to polish his eyes at noon and gaze,
he would still not perceive its form—
thus the eyes have no use for their brightness.

Even if *Shigang* [master of hearing] were to bow his head all night and strain his ears,
he would still not hear its sound—
thus the ears have no application for their sharpness.

Even if the wisest and most voracious scholars were to search endlessly,
they would never attain it—
thus the body has no exertion for its strength.

From its unseen aspect, it is called *formless*;
from its unheard aspect, it is called *faint*;
from its untouchable aspect, it is called *minute*.

These three share the same essence though different names,
pointing to one reality—
here lies the *Dao/Way*'s complete essence.

It cannot be exhausted by inquiry,
nor attained by exploration—
this is the *Liezi*’s "indistinct and chaotic."

Called "indistinct and chaotic,"
it is because *qi*, form, and substance are present yet undivided,
and they have not yet separated.

Once they have become one,
how could it not be called *formless*?

Its uppermost aspect is not *illuminated*,
its lowermost aspect is not *obscured*.

The *Huizong* annotated:
That which is beyond form—
the inscrutable interplay of *yin* and *yang*,
dark yet not easily lost—
this is called *ultimate mystery*, hence not *illuminated*.
"Illuminated" means "bright," and not *illuminated* means profound and difficult to know.

That which is beneath form—
one *yin*, one *yang*,
distinct yet numbered—
this is called *ultimate Dao/Way*, hence not *obscured*.
"Obscured" means "dark," and not *obscured* means clear and discernible.

The *Yijing* [Classic of Changes] says:
*The mysterious and the bright*—
this depends on the person.

The *Cushui* [commentary] says:
That which is beyond form is called *Dao/Way*,
and the mysterious is indeed its marvel.
Thus the mysterious is inherently beyond form.

*Yin* knows the mysterious exists within *yin*,
yet does not know it also exists within *yang*;
*Yang* knows the mysterious exists within *yang*,
yet does not know it also exists within *yin*.
Hence the mysterious is inscrutable in *yin* and *yang*.

Swift yet not hasty, moving yet not arriving,
lost yet truly present—
this is called the uppermost not *illuminated*.

That which is beneath form is called *instrumentality*,
yet the *Dao/Way* truly resides within it—
thus the *Dao/Way* is also beneath form.

It does not lean toward *yang* alone,
nor is it born solely from *yang*;
it does not cling to *yin* alone,
nor is it formed solely from *yin*.
Hence the *Dao/Way* is one *yin* and one *yang*.

It can be confined, it can be dispersed,
in the void yet manifest—
this is called the lowermost not *obscured*.

For "illuminated" and "illuminated" are as the same,
"illuminated" meaning "bright"—
not *illuminated* means profound and difficult to know.

For "obscured" and "obscured" are as the same,
"obscured" meaning "dark"—
not *obscured* means clear and discernible.

Yet if not the person,
the *Dao/Way* does not act in vain—
thus it is said: *The mysterious and the bright*—
this depends on the person.

Boundless yet nameless,
it returns to *non-existence*.

The *Huizong* annotated:
The *Dao/Way*'s essence—
like the regular succession of day and night,
yet its profound and bright nature is unfathomable.
Could it be captured in appearance, sound, or color?
Hence it returns to *non-existence*.

The *Cushui* [commentary] says:
The *Dao/Way*’s *thread*—
like the measure of things,
ensuring nothing strays from its straightness.
"Boundless" means it does not exceed the limits of regulation and scrutiny.

The *Dao/Way*'s *pattern* is orderly yet not disordered,
like the regular succession of day and night—
one dark, one bright, never failing.
Hence it is called "boundless."

Yet profound and marvelously obscure,
it has no name, no nameable origin,
and its profound and bright nature is unfathomable—
thus it cannot be named.

If so, then it returns to its root,
leaving not even a trace,
not only *non-existence* at the beginning,
but also *non-existence* beyond the beginning of *non-existence*—
how could it be captured in appearance, sound, or color?
This is called returning to *non-existence*.

This is called *the state of the stateless*,
the *form of the formless*—
this is called *dimly discernible*.

The *Huizong* annotated:
*The state of the stateless*,
the *form of the formless*—
dimly discernible, yet within it is substance;
obscure, yet within it are forms.

Like the *Great Void* [Tai Xu] containing all forms,
yet not revealing their outlines.

Like one *nature* [xing], one *spiritual intelligence* [lingzhi],
self-sufficient yet unfathomable in its workings.

Called "having yet not having,"
called "not having yet having"—
like the sun and moon, though distant,
when seeking water in their reflection or fire in their rays,
the water and fire arrive without delay.

Where do the water and fire truly reside?
*The state of the stateless*,
the *form of the formless*—
this is the same.

The *Cushui* [commentary] says:
There is a state, hence it can be seen,
yet the *Dao/Way* is unseen—
also not separate from seeing—
thus it is the *state of the stateless*.

There is a form, hence it can be represented,
yet the *Dao/Way* is formless—
also not separate from form—
thus it is the *form of the formless*.

*The state of the stateless*,
the *form of the formless*—
it suggests "having yet not having,"
dimly discernible yet containing substance,
it suggests "not having yet not having,"
obscure yet containing forms—
like the *Great Void* [Tai Xu],
one void giving rise to all things,
yet never confined,
containing all forms without revealing their outlines.

Like one *nature* [xing] without *nature*,
responding to all things without end,
deep yet unfathomable,
spiritual intelligence self-sufficient,
yet its marvelous workings are unfathomable.

It is having yet not having,
having in reality yet not having;
it is not having yet having,
not having in reality yet having.

As when the sun and moon are distant from people,
yet seeking water in the moon’s reflection
or fire in the sun’s rays with a *square mirror* [fangzhu] or *sunburner* [yangsui],
the water and fire arrive in no time.

「Water and fire, as things,
cannot be confined by the limits of having or not having.
Without form yet possessing form,
without substance yet containing essence—
this too is the case,
hence it is called *dimly discernible* [huánghū].

Approach it and you do not see its beginning,
follow it and you do not see its end.
Emperor *Huizong*’s annotation states:
*"Its beginning lacks a head,
its conclusion lacks a tail;
thus, when one approaches or follows it,
there is something one cannot fully perceive."*

The *Explanatory Commentary* says:
*"The complete body of the Dao
is a harmonious unity, whole and perfect.
When it precedes all things,
who can trace its origin?
When it suddenly appears,
who can grasp its conclusion?
This is what Zhuangzi meant by
its beginning lacking a head,
its conclusion lacking a tail.
Approach or follow it—
how could one ever truly see it?"*

To uphold the Dao of antiquity
and govern the present’s existence—
one who knows the ancient origins
is called *Dao’s fundamental principle* [dàojì].

Emperor *Huizong*’s annotation states:
*"One yin and one yang—this is the Dao."*

Those who follow Heaven’s Dao without Earth’s
may be blinded by its dynamism,
leaning upon its overwhelming yang—
those who follow Earth’s Dao without Heaven’s
may be drowned in its stillness,
halting at withered decay.

The *Moists*, who embrace universal love,
prioritize others while abandoning *rightness* [yì],
reaching a point where they see no extremes.
The *Yangists*, who uphold self-interest,
abandon *benevolence* [rén],
swinging wildly without law, without balance, without deviation—
they are confined by one-sidedness.
Is this the Dao of antiquity?

The Dao of antiquity
cannot be exhaustively defined yet is not nonexistent—
it is *dimly discernible* yet not *non-existent*.
Nothing exists outside its embrace,
thus it is called *dimly discernible* yet not *non-existent*.

To wield it for practical effect,
to examine it for validation—
to respond to all transformations,
to meet all that comes—
its adaptability is sufficient to exhaust all benefits,
its vitality is sufficient to exhaust all spiritual potency,
and in governing the world,
there is nothing it cannot address.
This is the Dao’s enduring constancy,
unchanging from ancient times—
the Dao that has always existed since before Heaven and Earth.

It is the *pivotal axis* [gāngzǎi] that governs all.
All who are bound by things,
without transcending this truth,
cannot stand firm.
The Dao’s enduring constancy knows no change.

Only those who can probe
the origins of all things
and know their beginnings
hold within themselves
the * Dao’s fundamental principles* [lúnjīng].
Thus it is said:
*"This is called Dao’s fundamental principle."* [dàojì]

The *Explanatory Commentary* on the *True Classic of Dao and Virtue* concludes:

*"The Dao of Heaven is established as yin and yang,
yet though the Dao is not yin and yang,
it never departs from them.
Those who are ignorant of the Dao
split it into yin and yang alone—
some delight in life and burden the form,
thus blinded by the Dao’s dynamism,
leaning upon its overwhelming yang;
others pursue emptiness and forget the body,
thus drowned in the Dao’s stillness,
halting at withered decay.

The *Moists*, who embrace universal love,
prioritize others while abandoning *rightness* [yì],
reaching a point where they see no extremes—
this is their deviation.

The *Yangists*, who uphold self-interest,
abandon *benevolence* [rén],
swinging wildly without law, without balance, without deviation—
they are confined by one-sidedness.
Is this the Dao of antiquity?

The Dao of antiquity
has neither what is discarded nor what is retained—
it cannot be exhaustively defined yet is not nonexistent,
and nothing exists outside its embrace.
Thus it is called *dimly discernible* yet not *non-existent*.

To wield it for practical effect,
to examine it for validation—
to respond to all transformations,
to meet all that comes—
its adaptability is sufficient to exhaust all benefits,
its vitality is sufficient to exhaust all spiritual potency,
and in governing the world,
there is nothing it cannot address.
This is the Dao of antiquity.

This Dao,
long before the Upper Antiquity,
was not yet old—
before Heaven and Earth existed,
it had always been preserved.
It is the *pivotal axis* [gāngzǎi] that governs all.
All who are bound by things,
without transcending this truth,
cannot stand firm.
The Dao’s enduring constancy knows no change.

Only those who can probe
the origins of all things
and know their beginnings
hold within themselves
the * Dao’s fundamental principles* [lúnjīng].
Thus it is said:
*"This is called Dao’s fundamental principle."* [dàojì]

End of Volume Three of the *Explanatory Commentary* on the *True Classic of Dao and Virtue*.

#1 The text likely omits 『一』 after 『为』 in *"To wield it for practical effect"* (操此为验).
#2 The original likely has 『离』 after 『明两作』, with 『为』 omitted before it (明两为离).」

Chapter Fifteen: The Mastery of the *Dao’s Virtuous Way* [Shànwéi Shì Zhāng] of Antiquity

The ancient masters of the *Dao’s Virtuous Way* [shànwéi shì] were subtle, wondrous, and profound—so deep as to be unrecognizable.
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: The ancient *Dao’s Virtuous Way* [shì] differed from that of today, and the masters who excelled in it differed from those who did not.
Thus, subtlety [wēi] aligns with the Dao, wondrousness [miào] harmonizes with the *Divine* [shén], profundity [yuān] matches the heavens, and penetration [tōng] reveals the *Sage’s* [shèng] essence. Yet, their depths are so concealed that they cannot be measured.

The *Book of Documents* [Shū] says: *"The Dao’s heart is but subtle."* [dàoxīn wéi wēi]
Thus, subtlety [wēi] is the Dao itself.
The *Book of Changes* [Yì] says: *"The Divine, indeed, is wondrous in all things and is named thus."* [shén yě zhě miào wànwù ér wéi yán]
Thus, wondrousness [miào] is the Divine.
The *Book of Changes* says: *"The heavens are profound, and the earth is vast."* [tiān xuán ér dì huáng]
Thus, profundity [yuān] is the color of the heavens.
The *Commentary* [Zhuàn] says: *"There is no obstruction in all things—this is called the Sage."* [shì wú bù tōng zhī wéi shèng]
Thus, penetration [tōng] is the Sage’s work.
The depths of water can be measured, and the depths of pits can be explored.
Yet the ancient masters of the *Dao’s Virtuous Way* [shànwéi shì], their names and deeds unseen, awaken responses from the heels—so profound that they cannot be measured or explored.
Lü Yu was in Zhengpu for forty years, yet no one recognized him.
Laozi spoke to Confucius: *"A fine merchant hides his wares as if they were none; a Sage of virtue, though full of power, appears as if foolish."*
Is this what he meant?

The *Explanatory Commentary* [Shūyì] states: There was the Upper Antiquity, and the Middle Antiquity; the ancient *Dao’s Virtuous Way* [shì] refers to the Upper Antiquity.
There were the Upper Masters [shàngshì] and the Lower Masters [xiàshì]; the masters who excelled in the *Dao’s Virtuous Way* [shànwéi shì] were of the Upper Master class.
Thus, the ancient *Dao’s Virtuous Way* [shì] differed from that of today, and the masters who excelled in it differed from those who did not.
The masters who excelled in the *Dao’s Virtuous Way* [shànwéi shì] possessed talents reaching the heavens, their aspirations aligned with the Dao and the Divine, their wisdom matched the heavens, and their penetration the Sage’s.
So profound as to be unmeasurable, they were subtle, wondrous, profound, and penetrating—deep beyond recognition.
For they were unseen in form, the Dao had no shape, and could not be perceived by the eyes—only known subtly, hence their unity with the Dao.
This is what the *Book of Documents* [Shū] meant by *"The Dao’s heart is but subtle."* [dàoxīn wéi wēi]
The Divine’s wondrousness is without desire—only those who enter wondrousness can know it, for it cannot be sought with fixed expectations.
This is what the *Book of Changes* [Yì] meant by *"The Divine is wondrous in all things."* [shén miào wànwù]
Profoundity [yuān] as the heavens’ hue bears both *qi* [chì] and *essence* [yáng], for it can embrace both yin and yang.
Thus, the heavens’ color is in this, as the *Book of Changes* [Yì] says: *"The heavens are profound, and the earth is vast."* [tiān xuán ér dì huáng]
If one can be profound and vast, then one aligns with the heavens.
Penetration [tōng] means unrestricted passage—nothing can obstruct it.
Thus, the Sage’s work is in this, as the *Commentary* [Zhuàn] says: *"There is no obstruction in all things—this is called the Sage."* [shì wú bù tōng zhī wéi shèng]
If one aligns with the *Great Penetration* [Dàtōng], then one enters the Sage’s domain.
The ancient masters of the *Dao’s Virtuous Way* [shànwéi shì], subtle, wondrous, profound, and penetrating—so deep as to be unrecognizable—are like the heavens and earth.
As the following passage states, they are but named in approximation.
*"Cautious as one crossing a river in winter,
hesitant as if fearing four neighbors."*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"Cautious"* [yù] means to plan rest before action.
*"Hesitant"* [yóu] means to consider doubt after completion.
The ancient ones who embodied the Dao took inner cultivation as their duty, not outer penetration as their delight.
They lived in constant fear and reflection, acting only when compelled—like crossing a river in winter, guarding without loss.
As if fearing four neighbors, this is what the *Book of Changes* [Yì] means by *"With this, one purifies and restrains."* [yǐ cǐ zhī jièjiè]

The *Explanatory Commentary* [Shūyì] states:
To plan before an event is called *cautious* [yù], hence *cautious* means to plan rest before action.
To plan after an event is called *hesitant* [yóu], hence *hesitant* means to consider doubt after completion.
The ancient ones who embodied the Dao took inner cultivation as their duty, not outer penetration as their ultimate goal.
They found forgetfulness of things as good, not delight in mastering things.
In constant fear and reflection, they dared not indulge—
before an event’s arrival, they responded only when moved, acted only when pressed, rising only when compelled.
Thus, they planned before events, like crossing a river in winter.

For matters already accomplished, one stands in solemn warning—
vision becomes still, action becomes slow,
planning comes after the deed,
as though fearing one’s neighbors.

For the river is where danger lies,
and crossing in winter is what people dread—
if one crosses in winter, how could it be otherwise?
Neighbors are those who stand close and care for one another,
ensuring mutual protection—
if one fears one’s neighbors, how could one lose oneself?

Yet then, is there anything that can be easily attained?
The *Book of Changes* says:
*"When the gentleman is about to act or move,
he must first purify and refine his virtue/power to manifest its clarity."*

This is what it means.

**Solemn as in reverence,**
Huizong’s annotation:
*The Analects* says: *"Gazing upon him, he appears solemn."*
*The Records* says: *"Solemn, as though in thought."*
Zhuangzi says: *"Things have no inherent Dao—
rectify one’s bearing to awaken them,
so that people’s intentions dissolve."*

The fully virtuous/powerful one—
from afar, he inspires awe;
up close, he does not weary.
Thus his demeanor is upright yet unassuming.

The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
When entering and distinguishing among things,
one risks entanglement in limbs and bones,
and the body becomes something to be trodden upon.
When exiting and interacting with things,
one manifests in movement and stillness,
and one’s bearing and posture become observable.
**Solemn as in reverence**—
this is the moment of exiting to interact with things,
when bearing and posture are fit to be observed.
*The Analects’* *"Gazing upon him, he appears solemn,"*
and *The Records’* *"Solemn, as though in thought"*
both convey this meaning.

Zhuangzi says: *"Things have no inherent Dao—
rectify one’s bearing to awaken them,
so that people’s intentions dissolve."*
Indeed, the fully virtuous/powerful one’s appearance is utterly simple and empty,
standing beside others and transforming them—
what more, when seen in gestures and expressions?
Adjusting his garments and dignifying his gaze,
from afar, he inspires awe—
for others, this means no ill intent;
up close, he does not weary—
for himself, this means no exhaustion.
Thus his power to transform others is effortless.
The ancient *True Persons* were upright yet unassuming—
how could they ever slouch and fawn to invite humiliation?

**Dissolving like ice about to thaw,**
Huizong’s annotation:
*"Water congeals to form ice, ice melts back to water—
in essence, they are one."*

Those ensnared by one-sidedness
are like water congealing;
those attuned to the Great Unity
are like ice thawing.
The *Book of Changes* says:
*"Dissolving—separation."*
To abandon things and people without attachment—
this is what it means to dissolve.

The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
Water, though clear, freezes into unclean ice;
the spirit, though luminous, solidifies into an opaque form.
Yin congeals to form ice—
just as the spirit manifests as form.
Yang melts to become water—
just as form returns to the spirit.
Water and ice are one in essence.

Those ensnared by one-sidedness
know only to guard the form and not to exhaust the spirit,
thus like water congealing—
born from water yet obstructing its flow.
Those attuned to the Great Unity
know to forget the form and merge with the spirit,
thus like ice thawing—
spreading freely without hindrance.

The *Book of Changes* pairs wind over water as *"Dissolving"* in *Hexagram Variations*,
yet in *Appended Commentary* it says *"Dissolving—separation."*
Thus *"Dissolving"* in meaning
refers to abandoning things and people without attachment.
Only by abandoning things and people
does one dissolve like ice thawing—
who then would have attachments?
Compared to the frozen, rigid ice,
the difference is vast.

*The Great Origin* says: *"Without attachments—this is the Sage."*
**Dissolving like ice about to thaw**—
is this not the way to enter Sagehood?

**Thick as in sincerity, like uncarved wood,**
Huizong’s annotation:
*"Thick" is the utmost of depth.
Nature is originally profound,
like wood before it is shaped into vessels.*

The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
The *Book of Changes* speaks of *"thick virtue"*—
virtue as the utmost depth;
*The Records* speaks of *"thick teaching"*—
teaching as the utmost depth.
Thus *"thick"* is the utmost of depth.

Only when people’s lives are profound
does their nature remain profound.
Yet when they shift with circumstances,
the profound becomes shallow.
Only the virtuous scholar
restores the original nature,
does not shift with things,
and thus is like uncarved wood—
uncarved yet encircled with visible grain,
not yet shaped into a vessel,
its depth truly profound.
Compared to hewing it with an axe,
splitting its wholeness,
carving it into sacrificial vessels,
and painting it with green and yellow—
the difference is vast.

**Vast as in emptiness, like a valley,**
Huizong’s annotation:
*"Vast" is the extreme of breadth.
The heart originally has no bounds,
like a valley’s emptiness—
receiving and responding without limit.*

The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
*The Odes* speaks of *"vast wilderness"*—
referring to land’s utmost breadth;
*The Commentary* speaks of *"vast days"*—
referring to time’s utmost breadth.
Thus *"vast"* is the extreme of breadth.

When the inch of the heart aligns with the boundless void,
the heart originally has no limits.
Without boundaries yet not confined,
its chamber remains ever empty.
Only the virtuous scholar,
at the peak of emptiness,
exhausts the truth of the heart,
like a valley’s ability to receive—
receiving without accumulation,
like a valley’s ability to respond—
responding without attachment.
Its breadth is truly profound.
Compared to the six orifices clashing,
narrowing one’s dwelling,
and obstructing the Dao with solidity—
the difference is vast.

**Undifferentiated as in turbidity.**
Huizong’s annotation:
*"Not carved nor polished to achieve harmony,
not exaggerated to stand out—
undifferentiated, yet complete, like turbidity."*

This differs from those who refine themselves to reveal impurity.

The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
The Perfect One’s way in the world
is to blend light with dust,
to rectify angles and round them,
to be greatly modest without resentment,
to act without rigid rules—
never carving nor polishing to achieve harmony.
Not seeking distinction,
they follow the common—
never exaggerating to stand out.
Moving with danger yet yielding,
adapting to things with flexibility,
not erecting rigid corners,
undifferentiated, yet complete.

While others are bright and clear,
I alone appear muddled—
seeming turbid yet not turbid.
This differs from those who refine themselves to reveal impurity.

Who could make turbidity still to let clarity arise gradually?
Who could make stillness move to let life unfold slowly?

The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
The Daoist adept moves yet remains still,
ranging freely yet returning to their roots,
established yet able to respond,
boundless yet fulfilling all demands.
Thus, when stillness lets clarity arise gradually, nothing can muddy them;
when movement lets life unfold slowly, nothing can unsettle them.

The *Classic of Changes* says:
*"Come slowly, slowly."*
Slowly means to proceed with ease and self-adjustment.

The Perfect One’s use of mind
is not to prize stillness as an end in itself,
nor to labor over life’s emergence as a feat.
Following its natural course,
they entrust it to unfold naturally,
without haste or coercion—
thus clarity arises gradually.
When stillness lets clarity arise gradually,
ten thousand things cannot perturb their mind—
who could muddy them?
When movement lets life unfold slowly,
ten thousand things cannot ensnare their thoughts—
who could unsettle them?

If stillness implies cessation,
being bound by things, then it is cessation—
how could one respond to things without harm?

The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
*"Self-soothing, like winter crossing a river,
utterly undifferentiated, as if turbid—
this is how one embodies the Dao in the world.*
Only by embodying the Dao
does one move and remain still at the right times,
unburdened by things—
thus guarding stillness as a vessel.

Responding to things through movement,
one moves yet remains still,
ranging freely yet returning to their roots—
not lost in movement,
so movement does not scatter them—
who could muddy them?
They are established.
Responding and thus communicating,
established yet able to respond,
boundless yet fulfilling all demands—
not clinging to stillness,
so cessation does not obstruct them—
who could unsettle them?

That things cannot muddy them
is because stillness lets clarity arise gradually.
That things cannot unsettle them
is because movement lets life unfold slowly.

Stillness letting clarity arise gradually
is not to prize stillness as an end in itself,
nor to labor over life’s emergence as a feat.
Though overflowing, they do not lose their overflow,
swept along yet ever self-same—
how could ten thousand things perturb their mind?
Movement letting life unfold slowly
is not to labor over life’s emergence as a feat,
nor to force its emergence.
Not confined to one corner,
they emerge in due time to respond—
how could ten thousand things ensnare their thoughts?

Truly, *slowly* means to proceed with ease and self-adjustment,
in accord with the *Classic of Changes*’ *"Come slowly, slowly."*
Each movement and stillness,
following its natural course,
not contrary to principle,
entrusted to unfold naturally,
not contrary to nature—
all proceed with ease and self-adjustment.
Who then has cause for haste or labor?

If stillness implies cessation,
acting yet not ceasing is extreme,
ceasing yet not acting is also extreme.
Acting and ceasing in due measure,
not bound by things—
who could harm them?
For if bound by things, then it is cessation—
how could one respond to things without harm?

Those who preserve the Dao do not seek fullness.
The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
*"There is accumulation, so there is insufficiency.
There is no hoarding, so there is abundance."*
The Perfect One has no accumulation—
only emptiness.

To preserve the Dao while claiming all the world’s beauty as one’s own
is already small-minded—
thus they do not seek fullness.
The *Classic* says:
*"Great whiteness seems like shame,
vast virtue seems insufficient."*

The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
*"Store grain in a granary—
when the grain is gone, the granary is empty.
Store water in a vessel—
when the water is gone, the vessel is empty.
Grass gathers things—
take from it as you will, yet it remains.
Praises gather things—
yet they sometimes run dry.
Because there is accumulation, there is insufficiency.
The Perfect One, boundless yet fulfilling all demands,
utterly empty yet responding to all reality—
because there is no hoarding, there is abundance.
The Dao flows without accumulation;
its use may not be full—
the Perfect One preserves the Dao without accumulation,
only emptiness.

If one claims all the world’s beauty as one’s own,
it is like monopolizing a ravine to be self-sufficient—
also small-minded.
When autumn waters arrive, the River Spirit rejoices,
yet is laughed at by those of great vision.
The *Classic* says:
*"Great whiteness seems like shame,
vast virtue seems insufficient."*
How could there be the burden of excess?

Only the unfull can renew without ceasing,
can complete without failing.
The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
*"There is accumulation, so there is decay.
There is completion, so there is ruin.
New and decay alternate, like the four seasons;
completion and ruin follow, like all things.
From the Dao descending to forms and numbers,
none escape this.
Only the Dao has no form—
empty yet not full—
thus it can decay and renew,
complete and ruin,
transcending forms and numbers,
yet never decaying, never ruined.
Thus it is said:
Only the unfull can renew without ceasing,
can complete without failing.

Wood begins flourishing yet ends withered,
fire first shines yet finally extinguishes—
because there is renewal, decay follows.
The sun reaches its zenith and declines,
the moon is full and then wanes—
because there is completion, ruin follows.
The Daoist adept differs from this.

The *Explanatory Commentary* states:
*"Form brings renewal and decay,
numbers bring completion and ruin.
Spring precedes summer, waxing then waning—
the cycle of the four seasons follows this.
Speech and action alternate, flourishing then declining—
all affairs follow this.
These all fall within the realm of forms and numbers,
so when new, decay follows;
when complete, ruin comes—
none can escape it.
Only the Dao has no form,
unbound by shape—
thus it can renew and decay yet never decay,
unbound by numbers—
thus it can complete and ruin yet never ruin,
because it is empty yet not full.

Now, wood as a thing
flourishes in spring yet falls in winter—
thus it begins flourishing and ends withered.
Fire as a thing,
the more it is used, the brighter it burns,
yet when extinguished, it is gone—
thus it first shines and finally extinguishes.
Rising at dawn, setting at dusk—
though it is the essence of yang,
it cannot escape midday decline."*

The fullness of three and five wanes,
the emptiness of three and five returns—
though the moon is the essence of yin,
it cannot escape the fate of fullness leading to deficiency.

All that is confined to forms and numbers
thus has newness followed by decay,
completion followed by ruin—
this is why it must be so.
The Daoist attains emptiness yet never overflows,
transcending forms and numbers—
thus they can decay and renew yet never truly decay,
can complete and ruin yet never truly ruin,
because they are empty yet never depleted.

To attain emptiness to its utmost,
and to uphold tranquility with resolve—
*Emperor Huizong’s annotation*: Nothing is more precious than emptiness,
nothing more excellent than tranquility;
emptiness and tranquility are the root of all things.
Empty, they suffice to embrace all substance;
tranquil, they suffice to respond to all movement.
Utmost is where all gather and reach their limit;
resolve is where effort is fully exerted to its end.
To govern emptiness while seeking its limit,
and to uphold tranquility until resolve is achieved—
then though myriad appearances are varied,
my mind remains ever clear,
and though myriad transformations differ,
my mind remains ever still.
This is called *Heaven’s Music*—
only those who embody the Dao can partake in it.

*Explanatory commentary*: When one attains emptiness,
emptiness becomes vast—
vast, they can encompass all without omission,
thus *Liezi* says, "Nothing is more precious than emptiness."
When emptiness is attained,
tranquility follows—
tranquility brings clarity—
clarity allows them to illuminate all without exclusion,
thus *Liezi* says, "Nothing is more excellent than tranquility."
To be without obstruction is emptiness—
and nothing in the world escapes emptiness.
To remain unchanging in singularity is tranquility—
and nothing in the world’s movement departs from tranquility.
This is why *Zhuangzi* speaks of emptiness and tranquility
as the root of all things.
The *Classic* says: "O profound! Like the source of all things"—
it is no different from this.

For all things are hindered by form,
constrained by number—
bound by form, they become a burden of form;
bound by number, they become enslaved by number.
Only those who transcend form and depart from number
can achieve this—
is it not emptiness and tranquility?

Empty, they suffice to embrace all substance;
tranquil, they suffice to respond to all movement—
because they do not hinder form,
nor constrain by number.

Yet to explore the origin of emptiness and tranquility,
though naturally attained,
to achieve their fullness requires diligent governance and steadfastness.
When emptiness is attained to its limit,
and tranquility upheld with resolve—
then stillness and emptiness find their true dwelling.
Limit is like the apex of a house—
where all gather and reach their end.
Resolve is like a horse straining its utmost—
a thousand miles can be traversed,
for it is where effort is fully exerted to its end.

To attain emptiness to its limit
means to purge all external desires,
so not even a flaw is seen—
not only does nothing begin to exist,
but even the *non-beginning* of existence is transcended;
this is attaining emptiness to its limit.

To uphold tranquility with resolve
means to remain ever serenely still,
never disturbed—
not only to depart from movement and attain stillness,
but to be still even amid movement;
this is achieving resolve in tranquility.

When emptiness is attained to its limit without obstruction by substance,
and tranquility upheld with resolve without deviation by movement—
then though myriad appearances are varied,
my mind remains ever clear,
and though myriad transformations differ,
my mind remains ever still.

The myriad things, each with its own nature and artifice,
are called myriad appearances.
The restless threads of change,
unending and irregular,
are called myriad transformations.
Though myriad appearances vary,
the mind remains ever clear—
because emptiness suffices to contain them all.
*Clear* shares the same root as *mind’s clarity*—
the penetration of wisdom.

Though myriad transformations differ,
the mind remains ever still—
because tranquility suffices to respond to them.
*Still* shares the same root as *serene immobility*.

To attain emptiness and uphold tranquility,
fully achieving this,
is the equilibrium of Heaven and Earth,
the perfection of Dao and virtue—
this is the *Great Root, Great Source*,
harmonizing with Heaven.
What greater *Heaven’s Music* is there?

Observing *Zhuang Zhou’s* discourse on emptiness and tranquility,
he says, "A single mind steadfast, and the world is ruled,"
and, "A single mind steadfast, and all things submit."
It is only by steadfastness that one can respond—
this is called *ever-clear, ever-still*,
the state of a single mind steadfast.

Only when the mind is steadfast can emptiness and tranquility
be extended to Heaven and Earth,
pervading all things—
how boundless is the joy?

When all things arise together,
I observe their return.
*Emperor Huizong’s annotation*: The transformations of all things
are at the end of the Dao;
those who embody the Dao dwell above all things.
Their birth springs from a germ—
their end returns to a destination.
When they surge forth along the path of movement,
I observe that the moving must eventually still,
and that the emerging must inevitably return—
thus I perceive the mind of Heaven and Earth,
remaining amidst all things yet not changing with them;
this is called observing their return.

*Explanatory commentary*: When *qi* transforms, form arises;
when form transforms, life emerges—
below the Dao, there is no movement without change.
Thus the transformations of all things
are at the end of the Dao.
The Sage embodies the Dao,
standing above all things,
unifying their principles and governing them—
this is why they dwell above all things.
Moreover, the birth of all things
is constrained by *creation and transformation*,
following sequence and order,
bridging and guiding one another—
emerging from *mechanism* and flowing in form,
then life has its origin in a germ,
entering *mechanism* and returning to essence,
then its end finds its destination.
If one lacks true sovereignty and does not grasp its manifestation,
when all things surge forth together,
they proceed along the path of *Kàn* (restraint),
reaching the vast thoroughfare of *Zhèn* (thunder),
sprouting and emerging,
germinating and becoming clear—
their myriad forms displayed,
rushing toward the path of movement.
The Sage comprehends the principles of all things,
within the midst of emptiness and tranquility,
gradually observing them broadly—
knowing that all things, though active,
originate from *non-action* and grow,
though moving, never depart from stillness,
though emerging, never truly fail to return.
Observing that the moving must eventually still,
and that the emerging must inevitably return—
thus I perceive the mind of Heaven and Earth.
In the *Yì* *Classic’s* *Fù* (Return) hexagram, it says:
"Return—
does it not reveal the mind of Heaven and Earth?"

The *Return* is indeed the moment when the small and subtle distinctions of things first emerge.
When distinctions emerge,
it is the state of utter stillness before any disturbance begins,
and nothing can exceed or evade it.

The Sage has no fixed mind,
but returns to the fundamental Dao of Heaven and Earth.
Though the world seems chaotic, it is sealed within harmony;
though it appears turbulent, it remains at peace.
In interacting with things yet not transforming with them,
it is not that one avoids interaction to attain distinction,
but rather that one distinguishes while interacting.

Thus, when all things arise together,
one observes their *Return*.
Though Laozi speaks of the *Return* as the "arising together,"
it is because there is a cessation of arising.

The principles may differ,
but their path to insightful observation is one and the same.

Things are myriad and varied,
each returning to its root.
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"Myriad and varied"* refers to the forms of movement and emergence.
All things emerge from *Zhèn* (Thunder),
meet in *Lǐ* (Fire),
and thus arise together in myriad forms—
their radiant essence unfurling outward.
They delight in *Duì* (Joy),
toil in *Kǎn* (Abyss),
and then discard their brilliance for substance,
returning to their innate nature and dwelling.

*Life* is the root of *nature*,
and *nature* is the root of *fate*.
*Essence* is the mother of *spirit*,
and *spirit* is its child.
When essence is complete, spirit reigns supreme;
when nature is perfected, one attains fate.

The *Cushioned Explanation* says:
*"Things arise like the myriad grasses,*
slowly moving and emerging—
this is the image of myriad forms.
Yet the movement and emergence of things
each follow their own time.
Observing the cycles of the four seasons,
and the subtle transformations of the eight trigrams,
all things emerge with them yet do not refuse,
and all things return with them yet do not linger.
Thus, from spring to summer,
it is Heaven’s emergence and humanity’s reception;
from autumn to winter,
it is humanity’s return and Heaven’s acceptance.

Speaking of their emergence and humanity’s reception,
they arise from *Zhèn* (Thunder),
which governs the east and corresponds to spring—
when all things germinate and move.
They meet in *Lǐ* (Fire),
which governs the south and corresponds to summer—
when all things flourish and bloom,
thus the myriad forms arise together,
and their radiant essence unfurls outward.

Speaking of their return and Heaven’s acceptance,
they delight in *Duì* (Joy),
which governs the west and corresponds to autumn—
when all things reach their withered and gathered state;
they toil in *Kǎn* (Abyss),
which governs the north and corresponds to winter—
when all things return to their roots,
thus discarding brilliance for substance,
and returning to their innate nature and dwelling.

When myriad forms arise together,
it is the spring qi emerging and all grasses sprouting.
When their radiant essence unfurls outward,
then they grow and flourish.
When they discard brilliance for substance,
then autumn arrives and all treasures mature.
When they return to their innate dwelling,
then they vanish into the void of nothingness.

The *Yì Classic* says:
*"Each attains its own nature and fate,*
preserving the Great Harmony."

Those who cast the Great Pivot and congeal form
have already uniformly received nature and fate.
Zhuangzi says:
*"Spirit arises from the Dao,*
and form originates from essence."
Those who scatter and focus essence,
conceiving the vital qi,
have already harmonized spirit and nature.

Yet Heaven bestows upon me this *fate*,
and this *fate* within me is called *nature*.
To follow one’s fate,
one must first perfect one’s nature—
for *fate* is the root of *nature*,
and *nature* is the root of *fate*.

Humanity’s possession of life
begins with essence and follows with spirit.
To preserve one’s essence,
one must gather one’s spirit—
for *essence* is the mother of *spirit*,
and *spirit* is its child.

Only when one knows that nature attains fate,
can one return to the fundamental root,
preserving life without harming nature;
only when one gathers spirit through essence,
can one grasp the mother and know the child,
guarding it without loss.

When it is said that *essence complete, spirit reigns supreme*,
is it not through gathering spirit through essence?
When it is said that *perfecting nature attains fate*,
is it not through knowing nature attains fate?

Zhuangzi expounds on the Dao of pure simplicity,
beginning with the unity of essence and ending with
*"no depletion of spirit"*—
thus the perfection of essence and the supremacy of spirit are clear.

Mencius discusses the path of *exhausting the heart*,
beginning with *"nurturing nature to serve Heaven"*
and ending with *"cultivating the body to establish fate"*—
thus perfecting nature to attain fate is clear.

To grasp this truth,
one need only observe the *Return* as a secondary matter
in comprehending the principles of all things.

Returning to the root is called *stillness*,
and *stillness* is the *Return* to fate.
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"Retaining movement to generate things,*
things generated manifest their principles—
this is called *form*.
*Form* preserves spirit,
each having its own measure—
this is called *nature*.
Before *form* emerges, there is division,
yet it arises naturally without pause—
this is called *fate*.
*Fate* spans antiquity to the present and remains constant,
*nature* changes through myriad forms yet does not alter,
and preserving the life of form—
the person who abandons knowledge and clings to the natural—
returns to tranquil repose.
Things cannot sway them."

The *Cushioned Explanation* says:
*"The realm retains movement to generate form,*
displaying true images of appearance and sound,
who does not receive the natural principles of things?
Zhuangzi calls this *form*—
the text speaks of the form of things as such.

The realm transforms to preserve spirit,
with flourishing, resounding, and complete vitality—
all enticed upward, who does not possess their inherent measures?
Zhuangzi calls this *nature*—
the *Book of Odes* says, *'Things have form and measure'*—
as such.

Life brings forth nature,
and nature brings forth form.
Before form emerges,
though there is division,
it arises naturally without pause—
this is what is called *fate*.

Life is called *nature*,
*nature* is received from *fate*,
*fate* endures without limit,
not bound by beginning or end,
nor constrained by time or measure—
it spans antiquity to the present and remains constant.

*Nature* accompanies life,
neither elevated nor diminished by noble or low,
neither increased nor lost by life or death—
it changes through myriad forms yet does not alter.

Only those who preserve the life of form
remain whole without decay,
embracing life without harm,
abandoning knowledge and clinging to the natural,
following Heaven’s principles,
returning to tranquil repose—
then things cannot sway them."

Abandon wisdom and old ways—
as when one is said to know nothing, rely on no cleverness.

Return to stillness and repose—
as when one is said to be motionless and tranquil, steadfast in great constancy.

Only thus does one know that *fate* abides within and does not transform with things.
Like spring turning to summer,
then autumn, then winter—
the active becomes still,
the creative rests,
and one knows where to cease.

Spring speaks of Heaven’s creation of things,
autumn of human completion,
summer of human caution,
winter of Heaven’s return.
From spring to summer,
Heaven’s essence descends to humanity;
from autumn to winter,
humanity returns to Heaven’s way.

Spring and summer precede,
autumn and winter follow—
gathering the scattered into unity,
stripping away the superficial to reveal the substance,
so that all flowing forms
return to their roots, unaware.

When *nature* is refined, it returns to *virtue*,
and *virtue* attains harmony with its origin—
thus it is.

If so, then one embodies *nature* and embraces *spirit*,
examining within to restore *fate*’s constancy—
what more could one desire?

Zhuangzi said:
*"Do nothing to return to simplicity,
embody nature and embrace spirit."*

To do nothing and return to simplicity
is to guard purity and naturalness,
thus one can embody *nature* and embrace *spirit*.

The *Classic of Changes* says:
*"Thickly restore without regret,
examine within."*

To follow *nature* and deepen it
is to need nothing from without,
thus one can examine within.

Only thus does one remain serene and do nothing,
act in accordance with Heaven’s movement—
what hindrance is there to restoring *fate*?

The *Scripture of Mixed Creation* states:
*"Silent and vast,
independent yet unchanging.
Ending with ‘passing’ it calls ‘far’,
‘far’ it calls ‘return.’"*

It is to return to stillness and repose
that one may restore *fate*’s constancy.

Thus it is first said: *"Return to the root,"*
then: *"Restore fate."*

*Fate* restored is constancy.
The Huizong annotation says:
*"Constancy is the opposite of change."*

To restore *fate* is to be unmoved by myriad changes,
uninterrupted and ceaseless,
one with the Dao,
grasping Heaven and Earth,
inheriting the *qi*-mother.

The *Explication* says:
*"Observing the warp and weft of constancy and change,
the warp has a fixed form, hence it is constancy;
the weft intertwines and moves, hence it is change.
Constancy and change are like warp and weft—
thus constancy is the opposite of change."*

Yet what is constant in things is exhausted by constancy,
while what is constant in the Dao is sustained by change.
What is constant in things is worn out by constancy,
what is constant in the Dao endures through change.

Only by restoring *fate* does one return to origin and root,
enduring from antiquity—
neither the wave’s turbulence nor the depths’ stillness is inherently good or bad.
Though traversing countless changes beyond measure,
its true constancy remains unshaken—
this is what is meant by *"myriad changes cannot sway it."*

If so, then its *spirit* is unobstructed—
how could things enter it?
This is called *"no interval."*

It does not shift with time’s passage,
yet never ceases—
this is called *"no rest."*

Without interval or rest,
one with the Dao,
it spans antiquity to the present,
independent and unchanging,
as the Xīwéi lineage grasped Heaven and Earth,
enduring eternally,
as Fuxi inherited the *qi*-mother,
boundless in duration.

Its constancy cannot be surpassed.

To know constancy is clarity.
The Huizong annotation says:
*"Knowing the Dao’s constancy,
unmoved by things,
the six orifices act in harmony,
sufficient to mirror Heaven and Earth,
sufficient to illuminate all things."*

The *Explication* says:
*"Unchanging unity is the utmost stillness.
The Dao’s constancy does not shift with things—
this is achieved through stillness alone.
Only stillness prevents it from rushing with movement.
Amidst the flourishing of all things,
one observes their return;
amongst their teeming forms,
one knows their roots.
Observing return and roots,
its stillness cannot be overturned by anything."*

Thus the six orifices act in harmony,
sufficient to mirror Heaven and Earth,
sufficient to illuminate all things.
The *orifices* are unobstructed by things—
the eye, unclouded by color, sees clearly and is bright;
the ear, unobstructed by sound, hears distinctly and is keen;
the nose, unobstructed by scent, perceives fragrance as *tian*;
the mouth, unobstructed by taste, finds sweetness;
the mind, unobstructed by thought, attains knowledge;
knowledge, unobstructed by things’ principles, attains *virtue*—
this is what is called the *"six orifices acting in harmony."*

From eye to knowledge,
the six act in sequence without obstruction,
through emptiness and unity, through stillness.

Emptiness brings stillness,
stillness brings clarity,
clarity lets *spirit* flow freely in all directions,
reaching extremes without limit.
Ascending to Heaven, descending to Earth,
transforming and nurturing all things—
beyond representation.

Though Heaven and Earth are vast,
they can be observed here;
though myriad things abound,
they can be shaped here.

Stillness is the balance of Heaven and Earth,
the illumination of all things—
only those who know constancy can attain this.

To not know constancy is reckless action—
disaster.

The Huizong annotation says:
*"The sage knows the Dao’s constancy,
so actions align with principle,
always meeting with good fortune.
Those who do not know constancy
follow things’ shifts,
entangled in misfortune,
thus they mistakenly judge good and evil,
contradicting the Dao,
mistakenly generate right and wrong,
diverging from the Dao,
and cannot even preserve their own *fate*—
hence disaster."*

The *Classic of Changes* says:
*"To restore is to act without error;
lost, one does not know to restore."*

This is recklessness.

The *Explication* says:
*"When the heart aligns with the Dao,
action is without falsehood;
when the heart departs from the Dao,
movement is without fail.
The sage is the Dao’s fulfillment,
thus knowing its constancy and not shifting with things.
Actions align with principle,
no movement is false,
always meeting with good fortune,
no step is taken without examining omens—
to act with *virtue* and rest the heart is called *rest*,
to do good and receive a hundred blessings."*

「Those who do not know the *constant Dao*,
act without fail—this is recklessness.
The farther one strays from the Dao,
the more one conflicts with its manifestations,
wandering with things and shifting with circumstances,
thus they say, *"to battle with the heart"*—
stumbling into misfortune, hence their reckless judgment of good and evil.

Drawn by personal likes and dislikes,
they fail to recognize that all good and evil arise from a single principle,
thus they contradict the Dao,
mistakenly creating distinctions of right and wrong.
Confused by false praise and blame,
they do not know to transform right and wrong through *twofold forgetting*,
thus they diverge from the Dao.

For the Dao cannot be separated for even a moment.
Once one contradicts it and diverges from it,
they exhaust their form and nature,
ravage *yin* and *yang*,
and cannot even preserve their own *fate*—
no greater calamity exists, hence disaster.

The *Classic of Changes* in its *"Sequence of the Hexagrams"* says:
*"To restore is to act without error."*
This means that when one restores the small and discerns through things,
it is called *without recklessness*.

*Without recklessness* is the mandate of Heaven,
to which all things submit.
Thus its *symbol* states:
*"When thunder rolls beneath the heavens,
things align with *without recklessness*."*
*Without recklessness*, things attain their true nature.

But when one is lost and cannot restore,
they lose their nature and reach its end,
unable to nurture life—
this is recklessness indeed.

**Knowing the constant Dao, one attains *rest*.**
The *Explication* says:
*"He who knows the Dao’s true constancy
sees that all things arise from One.
Thus, when observing the world,
though myriad and conflicting,
all are equally the utmost marvel—
who can distinguish right and wrong, good and evil?

To reject what is wrong and uphold what is right
arises from clinging to fixed views;
to love what is good and hate what is evil
arises from indulging private desires.
The perfected sage abandons the burden of fixed views,
forgets right and wrong,
and transcends private desires,
harmonizing good and evil—
this is no other than knowing the constant Dao.

To know the constant Dao is to empty oneself and wander through the world,
achieving flawlessness.
Right and wrong, good and evil do not dwell in the heart,
thus one can embrace all without exclusion.

The sage’s governance empties the heart—
this is how.

**Embracing all is *public*.**
The *Explication* says:
*"Without a heart of embracing,
one does not solely love one’s own kin,
nor solely cherish one’s own children—
what private desires remain?

To perceive things through their forms
is to indulge private desires;
to merge with the Dao without heart
is to attain universal harmony.
Thus the sage governs the world as a family,
nurtures all people equally,
and without a heart of embracing,
loves without limit,
showers mercy without bounds—
what private desires remain?

Even Heaven and Earth, when viewed,
cover all without partiality,
bear all without discrimination—
all within the realm of the two cannot escape their embrace.
Thus embracing all is *public*—
this is how the sage is seen in Heaven and Earth.

**Being *public* is *royal*.**
The *Explication* says:
*"The Dao’s practice is universal,
what all people share and follow—
this is what the *Records of Rites* cite,
the words of Zhongni,
explaining why the Dao’s practice is universal,
and the world rejoices in its embrace without weariness.

The essence of governance lies in knowing the Dao,
while the sage’s Way serves all hearts.
The sage governs the world through the Dao,
receives the Dao with the heart—
thus people are drawn to emulate and return to them,
their sincerity and service flowing naturally,
enduring forever without ceasing,
as the world willingly embraces it without restraint.

This is what the *Classic of Changes* means by:
*'The people align with what is able.'*

**Royal is Heaven, Heaven is the Dao.**
The *Explication* says:
*"Heaven and Earth establish their positions,
the sage fulfills their abilities—
within the realm, the Four Great Ones,
the king stands at their center.

This is he who unites Heaven, Earth, and people,
standing at the center of all—
the king.

One is not separate from the realm of numbers,
but the origin of all counts;
Great is not separate from form,
but the culmination of all bodies.
Light and clarity reside above,
broadly covering and embracing—
this is One, great above, without exclusion—
Heaven.

Heaven attains it and fulfills its mandate to cover with *qi*,
Earth attains it and fulfills its role to bear with form,
The sage attains it and fulfills teaching and transformation—
this is what all Heaven, Earth, and people follow—
the Dao.

The king is the ultimate of human Dao.
If one exhausts the human Dao,
they align with Heaven—
is this not *exhausting all people aligns with Heaven*?

Zhuangzi says:
*'The king is the Dao of Heaven.'*
Thus, the king is Heaven—
this is clear.

Heaven is the great source of the Dao.
If one takes Heaven as their origin,
they merge with the Dao—
is this not *embodying Heaven aligns with the Dao*?

The *Classic* says:
*'Heaven follows the Dao.'*
Thus, Heaven is the Dao—
this is clear.

The *Book of Poetry*’s *"Great Odes"* speak of
*the silent and odorless* yet call it *"the model and punishment of King Wen"*—
King Wen’s deeds truly aligned with Heaven.
To explore Heaven’s Dao,
one must follow the model and punishment of King Wen alone.

This is *exhausting all people aligns with Heaven*.

Again, it speaks of
*the unknowing and unperceiving* yet calls it *"following the Emperor’s standard"*—
the marvelous Dao’s practice truly aligns with Heaven.
To seek the Dao’s marvel,
one must follow the Emperor’s standard alone.

This is *embodying Heaven aligning with the Dao*.

The Zhou dynasty’s prosperity
came from the sage’s clarity and the people’s reverence,
guarding the same Dao,
sung in the *Book of Songs*—
this is how the Three Dynasties shone,
and kings always attain this.

**The Dao is enduring;**
one who follows it does not perish in life.
The *Explication* says:
*"The Dao endures through ten thousand ages,
all things attaining it flourish—
it encompasses all sages without limit,
spans Heaven and Earth without ceasing—
thus one who follows it does not perish in life.

Perishing is near to disaster,
nearness is close to fortune—
not perishing means no reckless action leads to disaster,
and none but those who know the constant Dao can achieve this."*」

The *Explication* states:
*"Its blessings extend to ten thousand generations,
outlasting the ancient past—
this is the Dao’s enduring nature,
and thus it knows no decay across ages.

What is meant by ‘gathering the past to secure existence’
is that it bends to fulfill without omission,
flows and measures without depletion—
thus all things that attain it flourish.

The Sage embodies the Dao as its ultimate expression,
refining through ten thousand years into perfect unity—
thus it encompasses all sages without end.

Spanning past and future without decay,
it encompasses Heaven and Earth without ceasing.
All things coexist harmoniously without harm—
who could threaten them?
Truly, one who follows it does not perish in life.

Disaster arises from negligence,
and thus is near to calamity—
as the saying goes: *‘Negligence overcomes reverence, and the dike is breached.’*

Fortune comes from vigilance,
and thus is near to blessing—
as the saying goes: *‘Fortune’s warning is its first sign.’*

To avoid disaster means all actions align with principle,
ever meeting with fortune,
and shunning reckless deeds that invite calamity.

None but the keen-witted and sagely wise,
sufficiently attuned to the Dao’s constancy,
can achieve this.

**Chapter Seventeen of the *Taishang* Section**
*The Taishang*—
the lowly know its existence."*

The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:
*"To govern the world with leniency, one attains tranquility with all under Heaven. Without the joy of striving, praise does not reach him; without the toil of exhaustion, fear of regret does not come. Actions occur without effort, yet they are ever natural—thus the lowly know its existence."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains:
*"The world has its inherent nature, and that nature has its inherent virtue. To abide within it is to preserve without perishing—this prevents excess; to yield to it is to release without abandoning—this guards against deviation. If one does not exceed its nature nor alter its virtue, then the world will transform of itself, and one attains serenity with all under Heaven. The Sage governs the world through the Dao, ever ensuring the people have no knowledge, no desire—so that even the wise dare not act. Though people may possess knowledge, there is no use for it; how then could there be the joy of striving or the labor of exhaustion? Without the joy of striving, the heart remains undisturbed, and praise does not reach him; without the labor of exhaustion, the heart remains at ease, and fear of slander does not come. Without desire yet self-sufficient, without action yet naturally settled, effortlessly expansive and harmonious—one attains what one attains without doing, for this is the way of forgetting in the Dao. This is what is meant by ‘the lowly know its existence.’ As Mencius said of the ruler’s people: ‘Their virtue shines like the sun and moon.’ Only at this moment is this true.

*"Next comes the state of being praised and honored. The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:
*"When benevolence extends to the people and law endures for the ages, all under Heaven love the ruler as a parent—thus he is honored. His renown rises like the sun and moon—thus he is praised. This is the governance of the sage-king, where the traces of praise and honor are manifest, yet the Way of Great Unity is lost.

*"Zhuangzi said: ‘Shun’s virtue was pure, and the people rejoiced in him.’ The *Odes* speak of the people of King Wen first gathering at Lingtai. The *Cushui* commentary explains:
*"Nothing benefits the people more than benevolence. Shun’s virtue of cherishing life permeated the hearts of the people; in the Zhou, governance was refined and benevolence was applied—this is the benevolence extending to the people. Nothing establishes order better than law. Shun established laws for all under Heaven, which could be passed down to future generations; in the Zhou, all affairs were fully attended to—this is the law enduring for the ages. When benevolence extends to the people and law endures for the ages, ruling with such care is like nurturing an infant. All under Heaven love the ruler as a parent—who would not cherish and honor him? When such governance is applied, his fame spreads far and wide, his renown rises like the sun and moon—who would not rejoice and praise him? The rise of the sage-emperor and the completion of the sage-king’s work—his virtue and deeds surpass all under Heaven, beyond concealment. None have not rooted themselves in this, which is why the governance of the sage-king bears the clear traces of praise and honor. Yet if this is so, then the Way of Great Unity is diminished, and there is a question between this and ‘the lowly know its existence.’

*"Indeed, in Great Unity, the people have no knowledge, no desire—how then could there be praise and honor? In ancient times, Shun’s virtue was pure, and the people of Dengxu came in tens of thousands; King Wen’s spiritual virtue made the people joyful, and the *Odes* of Lingtai marked the beginning of their allegiance. The means by which the sage-king’s governance brings praise and honor is this. Yet the Sage does not truly intend the people’s praise and honor. When great virtue and mighty deeds are bestowed upon the world, praise and honor naturally follow, without expectation.

*"Next comes the state of being feared and reviled. The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:
*"He governs with laws and unifies with punishments—thus he is feared. If all under Heaven are rewarded, the virtuous are not encouraged; if all under Heaven are punished, the wicked are not deterred. There were lords like the Duke of Chu questioning the weight of the tripod, and ministers like Yang Hu stealing great bows and jade—these are the customs of a declining age, hence he is reviled."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains:
*"The Sage governs the world through the Dao, and laws and punishments, rewards and penalties, serve only to assist the Dao in its practice. Laws and punishments clarify the world’s defenses, so the people have something to uphold; rewards and penalties demonstrate the world’s preferences, so the people know what to avoid—all rooted in the Dao. If one relies solely on laws and punishments without the Dao, it may reform outward appearance but cannot transform the heart—thus he is feared, and even reviled.

*"To govern through virtue is already to govern through laws. To govern through laws is to lack virtue. To unify through ritual is already to unify through punishments. To unify through punishments is to lack ritual. To govern through laws and unify through punishments is to be feared because the people’s hearts are scattered. If all under Heaven are rewarded, the virtuous are not encouraged; if all under Heaven are punished, the wicked are not deterred. There are those like the Duke of Chu and Yang Hu who revile, for they do not know the Dao’s governance of the world. If laws are used to govern and punishments to defend, and if legal measures are clear—so that rewards are given without encouragement and punishments without fear—then encouragement and deterrence are naturally aligned with the Dao. This is not the lesser path of deficiency, but the greater path where the Dao still endures. Thus, trust is insufficient, and distrust arises.

*"The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:
*"What binds and separates is not done with glue and lacquer; what restrains and loosens is not done with ropes and cords—this is the utmost trust. When the Shang made oaths, the people began to doubt; when the Zhou established assemblies, the people began to doubt—this is because trust was insufficient. When the Sage’s governance is such that ‘the lowly know its existence,’ then actions align without the people knowing it as trust. Next comes the state of being feared and reviled, where cunning, deceit, and scheming arise, and distrust follows.

*"The *Classic* says: ‘Within it lies trust.’ Thus, trust comes naturally. As Zhuangzi said: ‘What binds and separates is not done with glue and lacquer; what restrains and loosens is not done with ropes and cords’—this is the utmost trust, obtained naturally."*

*The Classic of Rites* says: **"Great trust requires no binding."**
Thus, trust is naturally sincere in others, needing no coercion or command.

*The Classic of Rites* also states: *"When the Shang made oaths, the people began to rebel; when the Zhou established assemblies, the people began to doubt."* This is because trust was insufficient, relying on coercion and command.

Indeed, **ultimate trust** arises from what is inherently true—never giving rise to doubt. When trust is insufficient, it loses its essential truth, and thus distrust arises.

In the **highest governance**, the people, in their natural simplicity, are unaware of its presence, yet their actions align without knowing it as trust. In the next stage, when they are feared and scorned, their customs decay, the pure and genuine disperse, and the vulgar and artful emerge—thus, deceit and scheming arise, and distrust follows.

*The Book of Changes* says: **"Without words, yet trusted—this lies in virtue and conduct."**
For when the essence is profound and the heart is in accord, this is called **ultimate trust**. But if one resorts to cunning and deceit, confusing the people with clever arguments and shamelessness, what trust remains? It is because trust was insufficient.

**Ah! How precious are words.**
The **Emperor’s commentary** says: *"When viewed through the Dao, the ruler of the world is upright—how then can words be spoken lightly?"* One must be cautious and never reckless. Thus, when words are spoken, they become the Dao for the world; when conduct is carried out, they transform the world.

The **explanatory notes** say: *"Words are words, but their meaning is not yet fixed."* When viewed through the Dao, the speaker has never truly spoken—only the true ruler exists in stillness, thus the world’s ruler is upright. Words are like waves—how then can they be spoken lightly? One must be cautious and never reckless. Words cannot be spoken lightly—that is what ‘Ah! How precious!’ means. Words must not be spoken lightly—that is what ‘precious words’ means.

The ancients inscribed warnings on golden statues and guarded against blemishes on white jade—thus it is clear that words cannot be spoken lightly. Therefore, the Sage speaks, and the people do not doubt; thus, words become the Dao for the world. When one reveals the truth and acts accordingly, conduct becomes the Dao in itself, transforming the world.

Why is this so? Because when spoken, it resonates with the law; when acted upon, it aligns with the Way—thus, none under the heavens fail to be sincere. When deeds are accomplished and affairs fulfilled, the people say, *"This is natural to us."* The **Emperor’s commentary** says: *"The achievements of the Sage are the remnants of the Emperor’s work."* To make the people relish their food, admire their clothing, find comfort in their customs, and take joy in their livelihoods—these are the remnants sufficient to fulfill the Emperor’s achievements.

Yet they say, *"This is natural to us,"* as if the Emperor’s power had no part in it. This is called **the highest governance**.

The **explanatory notes** say: *"The Emperor’s work is accomplished without action, yet the world’s affairs are fulfilled—only the traces remain, as if naturally so. This is called the Emperor’s achievements, the Sage’s remnants."* When deeds are accomplished and affairs fulfilled, the heavens are at ease, and the people dwell in peace and tranquility. They have no need to exert their cleverness—only to relish their food, admire their clothing, find comfort in their customs, and take joy in their livelihoods.

Food is taken in moderation, hence relished;
Clothing follows proper adornment, hence admired;
Customs are undisturbed, hence comfortable;
Livelihoods sustain life, hence joyous.

All these are the remnants of the Sage’s work, sufficient to fulfill the Emperor’s achievements. Thus, the people say, *"We use it without knowing,"* and claim, *"The Emperor’s power has no part in us."*

In ancient times, when **Emperor Yao** governed the world, there was the song of the broad streets: *"None but the extreme!"*—this was the **highest governance** of that time.

**End of Volume Four of the Explanatory Notes on the Dao De Jing**
**Beginning of Volume Five of the Explanatory Notes on the Dao De Jing**

**Explanatory Notes on the Dao De Jing by Jiang Rong, Student of the Imperial Daoist Academy**

**Chapter Eighteen: The Dao and Virtue/Power in Decline**

When the Great Dao declines, there arises **virtue** and **righteousness**;
The **Emperor Hui Zong**’s annotation states: *"When the Dao is lost, then virtue emerges; when virtue is lost, then righteousness arises; when righteousness is lost, then propriety follows. Righteousness establishes others, propriety establishes oneself—yet this is ever farther from the Dao."*

**Han Yu** failed to grasp the Sage’s true intent regarding **Dao** and **virtue/power**, instead claiming that **virtue** and **righteousness** are fixed names, while **Dao** and **virtue/power** are mere empty positions. The **Master Lao**’s minor **virtue** and **righteousness** reflect a limited perspective—what **Zhuang Zi** called the *"veiled and ignorant folk."*

The **Explanatory Notes** clarify: *"The fullness of the Dao lies hidden in the undifferentiated chaos, while virtue/power is a division of the Dao, manifesting as the interplay of firmness and yielding. Since the Dao cannot be fully attained, its loss gives rise to virtue/power. Since virtue/power cannot be fully realized, its decline spawns righteousness. Righteousness can be cultivated, and when cultivated, it approaches propriety—thus, when righteousness is lost, propriety emerges, making one ever more distant from the Dao. In its essence, the Dao is singular—just as **Yang Zi** said: *‘When unified, it is whole; when divided, it scatters.’* Han Yu, failing to grasp the Sage’s intent, resorted to subjective distortion, positing that **virtue** and **righteousness** are fixed names while **Dao** and **virtue/power** are empty terms. He labeled the **Master Lao**’s minor **righteousness** as a narrow view, yet he did not realize that the **Seven Virtues** are all contained within **Dao** and **virtue/power**. If **Dao** and **virtue/power** do not decline, why would one need the **Seven Virtues**? Examining the root, even if one rejects **virtue** and **righteousness**, they already operate within the realm of **Dao** and **virtue/power**. Could his heart truly believe that **virtue** and **righteousness** are insufficient for governing the world? His minor **virtue** and **righteousness** are merely a means to elevate them—precisely what **Zhuang Zi** called the *‘veiled and ignorant folk.’*

Later generations, obscured by worldly learning, lacked higher insight. Upon hearing the **Master Lao**’s teachings, they either dismissed them as madness or defamed them as crude and shallow. In the final age, scholars spend their lives in vain, only to become **petty Confucianists**—Han Yu’s critique is well-founded.

When wisdom and insight emerge, there arises **great deceit**;
