The **Emperor Hui Zong**’s annotation states: *"When the people’s wisdom and strength are exhausted, deceit follows."*

The **Explanatory Notes** clarify: *"In an age of perfect virtue/power, the people act without knowing why, move without knowing where they go. They follow their nature and find contentment; they accept their lot and remain at peace. Ambition and craft are forgotten. What need have the people’s hearts for wisdom and strength? When desire and calculation first arise, external temptations lure them. When wisdom fails, they grow perplexed; when strength fails, they grow sluggish. Losing their natural state, they sink into artificial contrivance—this is when the people’s wisdom and strength are exhausted, and deceit takes over. The Sage, in governing with forbearance, seeks to restore the people to their natural state, yet does not rule through wisdom and strength alone. Thus, **Lie Zi** says: *‘The Sage relies on the Dao’s transformation, not on wisdom and cleverness.’*

When familial bonds are fractured, there arises **filial piety and kindness**;
When the state is in chaos, there arise **loyal ministers**.

The Emperor Huizong annotated: *"Names arise from insufficiency."*

Zhuangzi said: *"A filial son does not flatter his parents; a loyal minister does not fawn upon his lord."* This is the peak of the subject’s virtue.

The *Cushui* commentary states: *"A name is the guest of reality. If reality is present, the name follows naturally. Yet names always arise from insufficiency."*

When a gentleman achieves renown, nothing surpasses loyalty and filial piety. Serving one’s parents, he does not choose a place to settle—this is filial piety. If he merely agrees with his parents’ words and approves of their deeds to flatter them, this is not true filial piety. Serving his lord, he does not choose a task to undertake—this is loyalty. If he merely agrees with his lord’s words and approves of his deeds to fawn upon him, this is not true loyalty. Only when he neither flatters his parents nor fawns upon his lord does the heart of loyalty and filial piety reach its fullness—this is the peak of the subject’s virtue.

**Chapter Nineteen: Discarding the Sage and Abandoning Wisdom**

Discard the Sage and abandon wisdom, and the people’s benefit increases a hundredfold.

The Emperor Huizong annotated: *"The Dao aligns with nature, unified without mixture. Departing from the Dao to establish virtue is what is called sage-wisdom. When sage-wisdom stands, then the world first sees the conduct of arrogance, obscurity, fierceness, and eccentricity. Shocking the ignorant to reveal the filthy, praising Yao while condemning Jie—then the benefit of sage-wisdom to the world is little, while its harm is great. If one discards and abandons it, aligning with the Dao as one body, then each finds peace in their natural state and life’s essence. Its benefit is vast."*

The *Cushui* commentary states: *"At the origin of reception, nature and the Dao are indistinct. After reception, nature and the Dao diverge. Only when indistinct with the Dao is there no distinction—this is the ‘Dao aligning with nature, unified without mixture.’ Only when diverging from the Dao are there boundaries—this is the ‘departing from the Dao to establish virtue, what is called sage-wisdom.’"*

At the origin of nature, the subtle root is complete and whole. Sage and fool alike initially share no distinction. Those who depart from the Dao externally establish their virtue, losing the true and sinking into artifice, lost and never returning. Thus, the ignorant display wisdom, and the name of the Sage arises. When sage-wisdom stands, they cannot follow nature’s spontaneity, and the world first sees the conduct of arrogance, obscurity, fierceness, and eccentricity. Arrogance leads to excess; obscurity plunges into depth; fierceness defies reach; eccentricity stands apart—none are the conduct of simplicity and rectitude. Thus, they adorn wisdom to shock the ignorant, refine themselves to reveal the filthy, praise Yao while condemning Jie, yet they do not know to forget all and transform in the Dao. Then the benefit of sage-wisdom to the world is little, while its harm is great—this aligns with Zhuang Zhou’s words: *"Do you delight in the Sage? Then you are like the man who admires a withered tree. Do you delight in wisdom? Then you are like the man who admires a blemish."*

Only when one knows to discard and abandon it, aligning with the Dao as one body, does one follow nature’s spontaneity and bring the world to governance through non-action. Only then does one find peace in their natural state and life’s essence. Truly, when the people return to simplicity and find peace in their nature and life, they align with the age and attain tranquility—can their benefit be calculated? Indeed, this is what is meant by *"its benefit is vast."*

**Discard Benevolence and Abandon Righteousness, and the People Return to Filial Piety and Kindness.**

The Emperor Huizong annotated: *"Filial piety and kindness are innate nature. To hobble for benevolence, to tiptoe for righteousness—this is to replace nature with benevolence and righteousness. If one discards benevolence and abandons righteousness, the people will return to their nature and restore their origin, not merely loving their own parents, not merely cherishing their own children—what then is filial piety and kindness?"*

The *Cushui* commentary states: *"All conduct stems from filial piety as its root; the three treasures prioritize kindness first. The heart of filial piety and kindness arises from innate nature, not from artificiality or external imposition. As for hobbling for benevolence—conduct is unnatural—and tiptoeing for righteousness—effort is forced—this is to replace nature with benevolence and righteousness. If one discards benevolence and abandons righteousness, the people’s nature will refine and return to virtue, virtue reaching back to its origin—this is returning to nature and restoring the beginning. If so, then the Great Dao is practiced, and the world is held in common: not merely loving one’s own parents, but extending care to all elders; not merely cherishing one’s own children, but nurturing all youth. Loving one another without knowing it as benevolence, uprightness without knowing it as righteousness—then filial piety and kindness belong to all, scarcely knowing why it is so."*

**Discard Artifice and Abandon Gain, and Thieves and Robbers Will Cease.**

The Emperor Huizong annotated: *"Those who practice cunning artifice have no shame. Those with desires for gain disregard righteousness. These are all of the ‘borrowed’ kind."*

The *Cushui* commentary states: *"Without shame for vice, there is no way to know shame. Without knowing shame, there is no way to practice righteousness. For a person to be human, it is noble to act with shame and to prioritize righteousness when seeing gain. If one understands this, then one will not do what should not be done and will not desire what should not be desired, returning to the path of the gentleman. As for those who practice cunning artifice, their purity is incomplete, and the Dao does not record them—this is having no shame. As for those with desires for gain, they rely on benevolence while stepping on righteousness, counterfeit the true with the false—this is disregarding righteousness. Having no shame and disregarding righteousness, they do what should not be done and desire what should not be desired. Truly, few differ from those who take what is not theirs. This is why they are said to be of the ‘borrowed’ kind."*

These three principles—though insufficient as doctrine—are thus assigned their place.

The Emperor Huizong annotated: *"The ancient kings governed the world through humanity. By the Zhou, refinement grew excessive, and by its decline, ornament overran substance, with ornament in excess while substance was insufficient. The world lost its original simplicity and sank into the habits of private desire. Laozi, in the late Zhou, sought to loosen these flaws and restore them to their root. Thus, he discarded benevolence and righteousness, abandoned ritual and learning—even sage-wisdom was to be rejected."*

Was his heart truly convinced that the Seven Virtues of sage-wisdom were insufficient to govern the world? The Way of the ancient kings, like the cycle of seasons, returns again. To rely on refinement (*wén*), one must first establish substance (*zhì*), hence their proper place. This is what is meant by "seeing the unadorned and embracing simplicity, reducing private desires and minimizing wants."

**Explanatory Notes (Cishu):**
Cold accumulates to become heat—not in a single day. Observing the rhythms of heaven to understand human affairs, we see that the ancient kings governed the world through humanity, progressing from simplicity to fullness, until by the Zhou, refinement grew excessive. At that time, institutions were established for every matter, and intricate regulations were set to guard against excess. Never was refinement more elaborate. Yet when refinement reaches its extreme, it leads to decay—substance is lost. There was too much emphasis on ornament while substance was insufficient, and the world lost its original simplicity, withering into falsehood. It sank into private desires and was obscured by petty wisdom—how could one not address this decay?

Laozi, in the late Zhou, sought to ease these flaws and restore them to their root. His intent lay here. Thus, he discarded the Seven Virtues and returned to the natural state, abandoning ritual and learning to merge in Daoist practice—even sage-wisdom was rejected, no longer held in esteem. He wished the world to value the fundamental over the superficial, to loosen rigid adherence to the past. Was his heart truly convinced that benevolence and sage-wisdom were insufficient to govern the world?

The Xia emphasized loyalty, the Shang simplicity, and the Zhou refinement—sometimes adapting, sometimes revolutionizing, sometimes diminishing, sometimes adding. The Way of the ancient kings, like the seasons, returns in cycles. Yet when refinement falters, it must be restored by substance, just as summer returns to autumn. To rely on refinement, one must first establish substance—hence their proper place.

**Seeing the Unadorned (*Jiànsù*):**
Emperor Huizong annotated: *"The Analects say: 'Painting begins with the blank canvas.' Before receiving color, the blank canvas is pure and unadorned."*
**Explanatory Notes (Cishu):**
Painting begins with the blank canvas, hence the Analects say, *"Painting begins with the blank canvas."* Before receiving color, the blank canvas is white. When color is applied, the original purity is obscured—this is the true nature of things. It is called *sù* because it remains unstained by external influences. To see the unadorned is to be clearly enlightened, without blemish, inwardly complete, and free from deceit—this is purity without admixture. This aligns with Zhuangzi’s *"clear and entering the unadorned"* and the idea that *sù* means unmixed.

**Embracing Simplicity (*Bàopǔ*):**
Emperor Huizong annotated: *"The Classic says: 'The undifferentiated (*pǔ*) disperses to become things.' If the undifferentiated is not carved, embracing simplicity means stillness and unity without change."*
Zhuangzi said: *"To align with desirelessness is called simplicity."* Simplicity restores the people’s true nature.

**Explanatory Notes (Cishu):**
Things derive from simplicity as their root, hence the Classic says, *"The undifferentiated disperses to become things."* If the undifferentiated is not carved, its form remains whole, untouched by artificial refinement. Simplicity is the true nature of things. It is called *pǔ* because it is uncarved, relying only on human artifice. To embrace simplicity is to be sincere and whole, returning to natural spontaneity, tranquil and at ease—this is stillness and unity without change. This aligns with Zhuangzi’s *"pure and unbroken simplicity."* Thus, simplicity is the people’s natural state. Restoring this natural state means desirelessness, self-fulfillment, and aligning with Zhuangzi’s *"to align with desirelessness is called simplicity."* Simplicity restores the people’s true nature.

**Reducing Private Desires and Minimizing Wants (*Shǎosī-guǎyù*):**
Emperor Huizong annotated: *"Self-seeking is private; nothing nourishes the heart better than minimizing wants."*
Reducing private desires and minimizing wants clarifies the distinction between inner and outer, discerns true from false, preserves virtue whole, and restores true nature—thus the title of sage-wisdom fades.

**Explanatory Notes (Cishu):**
To be obscured by self-interest is to lose the greater harmony. Self-seeking is private. To be entangled in desires is to drown in agitation. Nothing nourishes the heart better than minimizing wants. Private desires and wants are external distractions, not inner reflections—they cultivate falsehood, not true nature. Only by reducing private desires and minimizing wants can one distinguish inner from outer, know what to value, discern true from false, preserve virtue uncorrupted, and restore true nature. Thus, in non-action and effortless action, the title of sage-wisdom vanishes—who then governs the world?

**Chapter 20: "Discarding Learning, There Is No Worry" (*Juéxué-wúyōu*):**
Emperor Huizong annotated: *"Learning seeks to exhaust principles. While striving to exhaust principles through learning, how can one avoid worry over right and wrong, success and failure in governance?"*
Learning leads to the Dao. When one attains the Dao, learning is discarded—reducing further, reducing until non-action yet nothing remains undone. One follows the natural flow of life and nature, finding joy without end, thus free from worry.

**Explanatory Notes (Cishu):**
*Li* is like a boundary that can be measured; only through learning can one explore its depths. *Dao* is like a path all share; only by discarding learning can one reach its utmost.

Learning seeks to exhaust principles—this is the beginning of learning, hence the Classic says, *"In learning, there is daily increase."* While striving to exhaust principles through learning, one deliberates over right and wrong within the heart, examines governance through past and present—this is not yet free from the burden of thought. How can one avoid worry? Confucius worried about learning not being practiced—this is it.

Learning leads to the Dao—this is the end of learning, hence the Classic says, *"In the Dao, there is daily reduction."* When one attains the Dao and discards learning, limbs are discarded, senses are abandoned, form and knowledge are set aside, and all affairs vanish. Reducing further, reducing until nothing remains—then in non-action, yet nothing is left undone. Thus, motionless yet responsive, one follows the natural flow of life and nature, finding joy in forgetfulness, joy without end—thus free from worry.

Confucius took delight in coarse rice and water, finding joy therein—this is it.

Master Wenzi said: *"The heart has no discontent."*

Without discontent yet acting without end, this may be called attaining the Dao.

Thus, as the Dao is daily reduced, one finds joy in the Dao here—this is clear.

How far apart are *"unique"* and *"affirmative"*?
How different are *"good"* and *"evil"*?

Huizong annotated:
*"Unique" and "affirmative"* share the same sound;
*"Good"* and *"evil"* stem from one nature.
The petty-wise cling to self, separating them into two.
The enlightened embrace the whole—
their essence is not truly distinct.

The Sage governs the world in ancestral temples and courts.
Speaking to the great officers, he does not speak uniformly thus—
suppressing evil and promoting virtue, fearing it may not be thorough.
What people fear, one cannot fail to fear—this is so.

The *Cushui* explains:
If one merges the Dao with things, then distinctions arise from concealment.
If one divides things by the Dao, then distinctions arise from establishment.
Speaking from human inclination,
*"Unique"* is reverence, *"affirmative"* is arrogance.
Good lies in what is cherished, evil in what is despised—
this is inherently different.

Yet observing principle,
*"Unique"* and *"affirmative"* emerge from one sound,
*"Good"* and *"evil"* merge in one nature—
what then distinguishes them?

The petty-wise, clinging to self, follow their emotions.
Following emotion and self, each plants their own perspective,
arbitrarily drawing boundaries—
this is what is meant by *"separating into two."*

The enlightened embrace principle.
Embracing principle to observe,
they encompass all particulars,
unifying them in supreme subtlety—
this is what is meant by *"essence not truly distinct."*

The Sage merges his heart with the Dao,
seeing no singular thing,
yet toward the world’s good and evil,
he does not remain indifferent—
for he does not wish to differ from the world.

Thus, when he emerges to govern,
in ancestral temples and courts,
he readily distinguishes governance as his task;
speaking to lesser officers, he maintains harmonious discourse;
speaking to greater officers, he shows reverent deference.
Thus, he adapts to emotions through ritual,
and through ritual distinguishes differences—
hence his speech is not uniform.

If so, then evil is suppressed, virtue is exalted,
judging the world’s right and wrong with impartiality,
demonstrating the world’s likes and dislikes—
fearing it may not be thorough,
he takes heed of what people fear,
and cannot fail to fear—
bowing to align with them.

What people fear, one cannot fail to fear.
Huizong annotated:
*"The Dao stirs all things yet does not share the Sage’s worry.*
*Calamity and fortune breathe with the people—this is governance.*
*Those who embody the Dao have no worry;*
*those who engage in affairs have reverence."*

What people fear, yet failing to take warning,
few can avoid exhaustion.
Thus, the virtuous cultivate through awe and reflection.

The *Classic of Poetry* says: *"Fear the might of Heaven."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"Responding in accord is the Dao.*
*The Dao requires no thought or deliberation—*
*it stirs all things yet does not share the Sage’s worry,*
*this is what is meant by ‘motionless yet responsive.’*
*Acting in concealment is governance.*
*Governance involves thought and action—*
*calamity and fortune breathe with the people,*
*this is what is meant by ‘responding to the world’s affairs.’*
*Those who embody the Dao retreat to accompany Heaven,*
*thus they have no worry;*
*those who engage in affairs emerge to interact with people,*
*thus they have reverence.*

What people fear, yet failing to take warning,
few can avoid exhaustion.
Only with solemn vigilance does one achieve reverence,
and with diligent caution does one attain prudence—
then action will surely lead to auspiciousness,
without fear of regret or hesitation.

The *Book of Changes* says: *"The virtuous cultivate through awe and reflection."*
The *Classic of Poetry* says: *"Fear the might of Heaven."*
These are the utmost warnings.

Indeed, cultivating through awe and reflection,
thinking to prevent exhaustion—
like Boyi’s vigilance without worry—
fearing Heaven’s might,
to preserve the world—
like Gaozong’s stern reverence—
observing this, one knows
what people fear cannot fail to be feared—
its principle is clear.

Boundless, its end is without measure.

Huizong annotated:
*"The myriad complexities of worldly affairs,*
*entangled and inexhaustible,*
*without beginning or end—*
*this is what is meant by ‘good and evil not yet settled.’*
*Only the enlightened know they merge as one."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"The vastness of the six directions,*
*the multitude of things,*
*endless threads of turmoil,*
*daily pressing forward,*
*entangled and chaotic,*
*too vast to respond to—*
*this is the inexhaustible complexity of worldly affairs.*

*Circulating like a turning wheel,*
*repeating like a saw’s pull,*
*clustered and distant, without trace,*
*this is what is meant by ‘inexhaustible and unfathomable.’*

*‘This is right,’ ‘That is wrong’—*
*each a distinct judgment—*
*this is what is meant by ‘good and evil not yet settled.’*

*Common folk take good as what is approved,*
*evil as what is rejected—*
*thus what they reject is not what they approve,*
*though there are judgments of approval and rejection,*
*all originate from the realm of ‘this’ and ‘that.’*
*How could they know of the vast, mysterious, and unified Dao?*

Only the enlightened release their intellect,
turning their light to illuminate Heaven—
then what things call ‘that,’
does it truly have a fixed nature?
Without fixed nature, then all is ‘that.’

What things call ‘this,’
does it truly have a fixed nature?
Without fixed nature, then all is ‘this.’

If things have no ‘this’ or ‘that,’
then one knows they merge as one—
beauty and ugliness, good and evil,
would be simplified yet cannot be—
why then distinguish them so?

The common crowd rejoices,
as if feasting on the grand sacrifice,
as if ascending the spring platform.

Huizong annotated:
*"All things flourish with yang, congeal with yin.*
*Rejoicing is the outward manifestation of flourishing.*
*The common crowd loses the stillness of nature,*
*pursuing external affairs—*
*like delighting in rich flavors to nourish body and mouth,*
*like gazing upon grandeur to entertain heart and will.*
*Those lost in pleasure drift ever farther from the Dao."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"Yin and yang are the great movements of qi."*

The essence of things lies in their qi—
when yang flourishes, it is vibrant and harmonious,
for yang melts and completes itself;
when yin congeals, it is still and tranquil,
for yin halts and settles.

Sometimes vibrant, sometimes congealed—
it depends on the season and the thing.
Thus, the flourishing is but the outward display of vitality.

The common crowd loses the stillness of nature,
transforming with things,
pursuing external affairs while neglecting inner observation.

They delight in rich flavors to nourish body and mouth,
yet never know the subtlety of simplicity—
not merely feasting on the grand sacrifice.
They gaze upon grandeur to entertain heart and will,
yet never perceive the boundless void—
not merely ascending the spring platform.

Those lost in pleasure are burdened by things,
thus drifting ever farther from the Dao.

If one attains the wondrous observation of singular nature,
then all dharmas are complete—
movement and stillness merge,
true joy is preserved whole,
and what distance remains from the Dao?

I alone fear the unmanifested—
like an infant before its first stirrings.

Huizong annotated:
*"The Classic says: Return to the infant state.*
*Zhuangzi says: If not yet in infancy, then who begins?*
*The infant’s thoughts are yet unformed,
indifference to near and far is equal,
serene and still—
harmony accumulates within,
while brilliance does not yet manifest externally,
thus like an infant before its first stirrings."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"When a person is born, their form and essence are densely transforming.
In infancy, their nature is empty and without knowing—
as the Classic says: *‘Constant virtue does not depart,’*
and then: *‘Return to the infant state.’*
When a child reaches toddlerhood, affection already stirs—
as Zhuangzi says: *‘Born five months, able to speak—
if not yet in infancy, then who begins?’*
Only in infancy, without knowing,
does one reside without purpose,
walk without destination,
thoughts unformed and nothing to ponder,
indifference to near and far, nothing to choose.
Serene and still—
qi is harmonious yet not violent,
nature pure yet undispersed.
Harmony accumulates within while brilliance does not manifest externally—
thus, following nature naturally,
without artificial adornment,
like an infant before its first stirrings.
For *‘fear’* means the heart receives nothing.
When the heart receives nothing,
it is serene and empty of all things,
embracing the One and guarding the True—
what choice between this and the infant before its first stirrings?
The Master Lao established his teaching in the world,
desiring the people to return to the infant state—
thus, in *‘Cultivating qi and attaining softness,’* he says: *‘Can one be like an infant?’*
And in *‘The depth of virtue,’* he says: *‘Compared to the uncarved block.’*
Though his words differ,
his intent is to refine nature and restore virtue—
when virtue reaches its original state, it is One.

*Buying and selling, returning to the origin.*
Huizong annotated:
*"The *Yijing* says: *‘At the proper time, ride the six dragons to govern heaven.’*
*Buying and selling*—
following the season and principle without leaning to one side,
thus appearing to have no destination."

The *Cushui* explains:
*"The transformations of all things are chaotic and uneven.
Only by adapting to change, without attachment,
can one speak of *buying and selling.*
The *Yijing* says: *‘At the proper time, ride the six dragons to govern heaven.’*
The dragon rides the time—
speaking of the *Yi* principle transforming through timely adaptation.
Only by adapting to the time,
following its inherent principle and riding it,
why need one drive?
Thus, *buying and selling* means holding fast without constraint,
following principle without resistance,
naturally aligning with things yet not leaning to one side—
thus appearing to have no destination.
This aligns with *‘All things are threads of the One, none sufficient to return to.’*

The common crowd all have excess.
Huizong annotated:
*"Someone asked: ‘What is the common crowd?’
Answered: ‘Those born to wealth and nobility.’
Those who feast on life and covet gain—
their extravagant hearts prevail,
while frugality fades—
thus, they all have excess.
In the Dao, this is called *‘excess food, superfluous actions.’*"

The *Cushui* explains:
*"The Sage values the Dao above wealth,
while the common crowd values wealth above the Dao.
The Sage asks: *‘Is one practicing the Dao?’*
The common crowd replies: *‘Is one amassing riches?’*
Yang Xiong sought to remedy the times,
thus posing the question: *‘What is the common crowd?’*
and answering: *‘Those born to wealth and nobility.’*
The wealth of Jin and Chu was wealth for gain—
who compares to preserving what is most fitting?
The nobility of Zhao Meng was nobility of rank—
who compares to preserving true nobility?
Only the common crowd sees things yet not the Dao,
demands life to indulge their passions,
covets gain to exhaust their desires—
their extravagant hearts prevail, and the wasteful are many,
while frugality fades, and those who restrain themselves are few—
thus, they all have excess,
yet never know that in the Dao it is called *‘excess food, superfluous actions.’*
For where the Dao resides,
depth is its root,
and restraint its measure.
Extravagant appearance and licentious will—
in eating, it is excess;
in action, it is superfluous—
this is called *‘theft of splendor,’*
not the Dao indeed."

I alone am as if abandoned.
Huizong annotated:
*"Function covers the world,
yet seems not of oneself."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"The Sage, through the Dao, lends to the world,
nurtures all things yet does not claim righteousness,
benefits countless ages yet does not claim benevolence,
covers heaven and earth, carves all forms yet does not claim skill—
never has he acknowledged his deeds as his own.
*Zhuangzi’s Inner Chapters* discuss the governance of the enlightened king:
*‘Function covers the world, yet seems not of oneself,’*
saying he *‘plays in the void.’*
For the *‘void’* is the mysterious operation of the Dao—
the Sage, with ultimate emptiness, responds to all things,
thus accomplishing the emperor’s function—
this is truly incidental.
How could he claim it as his own and possess it?
I, a foolish man, am pure—
*pure and simple.*
Huizong annotated:
*"Confucius said: *‘When I spoke with Ran You, all day he did not contradict me—
as if he were simple.’*
*Pure and simple*—
heavenly mechanism does not unfold,
yet silently harmonizes with the Dao—
this is called great wisdom."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"The virtuous person’s abundant virtue
appears as simplicity—
is this not Ran You?
Observe his explanation of *‘heart’s fasting’* and the marvel of *‘sitting-forgottenness’*—
the Sage, recognizing his profound insight,
said: *‘When I spoke with Ran You, all day he did not contradict me—
as if he were simple,’*
meaning *‘his appearance was simple.’*"*

The true nature of the single essence,
serene and ever-present,
quiet and effortless yet without the stir of heaven’s mechanism,
empty and tranquil, silently harmonizing with the Dao—
then pure whiteness is fully within,
and morning clarity reveals its singularity.
This is truly great wisdom.

The *Zhuzi* says:
*"He who lacks the dim and hidden intent
has no radiant clarity;
he who lacks the sluggish and dull affairs
has no resplendent achievement."*
Is this what is meant?

Yet I, this foolish man’s heart—
*pure and simple*—
am I truly foolish?
No—it is only that lesser wisdom is discarded
while great wisdom shines forth.

The common man is radiant,
while I alone seem dim;
the common man is keen,
while I alone am muddled.

Huizong annotated:
*"If one conforms to the common flow,
then radiance is taken for clarity—
yet its clarity is small.
Keenness is taken for wisdom—
yet its wisdom is hollow.
To abandon things and people,
standing apart from the world’s habits,
then one appears as if lost yet exists,
as if dull yet sharp—
how could the vulgar world perceive this?"*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"To preserve light without revealing it
is the utmost clarity.
To act without contrivance
is the true wisdom.
The emotions of the common masses,
obscured by shallowness,
adorn themselves with vulgarity and align with it—
thus they take radiance for clarity,
yet their clarity is small,
not the ultimate clarity;
they take keenness for wisdom,
yet their wisdom is hollow,
not the true wisdom.

Those who embody the Dao differ thus:
subtle, mysterious, and profound—
unfathomable.
Abandoning things, they bear no burden;
separating from people, they are not enticed.
Loftily detached from the vulgar,
they awaken as if lost yet exist—
as in *‘serene, as if perhaps existing’*—
they appear dull yet are sharp—
as in *‘the more dull, the more sharp.’*
Their emergence and disappearance,
their uses hidden—
deeply subtle and obscure,
impossible to discern.
How could the vulgar world perceive this?

Mencius said:
*The deeds of the virtuous person
the common people cannot recognize.*

*Damp and vast, like the sea.*

Huizong annotated:
*"Deep and still, nature settled—
the complete body of the Dao."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"Deep in dwelling, pure in clarity—
deep and still, nature settled,
inner preservation, outer undisturbed—
*damp and vast, like the sea*—
this is the complete body of the Dao.
Though the Dao’s essence is unseen,
observe the greatness of the sea’s waters—
neither shifting with time nor advancing with measure,
the ancient principle truly resembles this.

*Swift and unrestrained, as if boundless.*

Huizong annotated:
*"Changing yet not fixed—
the Dao’s practical application."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"Moving yet ever emerging,
its momentum inexhaustible—
changing yet not fixed,
not stagnating in things—
*swift and unrestrained, as if boundless*—
this is the Dao’s practical application.
Though the Dao’s use is unseen,
observe the gusting wind—
it stirs all things yet shows no agitation,
howls through all openings yet measures no sweeping—
its wondrous, formless use truly resembles this.

All people have their purposes,
while I alone am stubborn and crude.

Huizong annotated:
*"Osmanthus is edible, so it is cut down.
Lacquer is useful, so it is stripped.
All know the use of utility,
yet none know the use of non-utility.
All people have their purposes—
this is called the use of utility.
I alone am stubborn and crude—
this is called the use of non-utility.
*The Classic* says:
*The heart not conforming to the patterns of virtue and righteousness is crude.*
Anciently, *‘refined’* was called beautiful,
*‘uncultivated’* was called crude.
Crude means not adorned with wisdom,
uncultivated means not seen as beautiful—
the divine person thus is without talent."

The *Cushui* explains:
*"The way of governing the world
considers non-utility as the utmost use.
Osmanthus is edible, so it is cut down.
Lacquer is useful, so it is stripped—
these are uses of utility,
the breath of the vulgar.
All know how to flaunt their wisdom,
boast their abilities—
this is the use of utility,
yet they do not know how to fragment their virtue—
this is the use of non-utility.
All people have their purposes—
this is the use of utility,
because of their vulgarity.
I alone am stubborn and crude—
this is the use of non-utility,
because of my lack of talent.
*Crude* is the same as *‘dim crude’*—
*crude* means without knowledge.
*Uncultivated* is the same as *‘utterly crude’*—
*uncultivated* means without refinement.
The divine person thus is without talent,
yet this lack of talent is what makes him the greatest talent—
thus the use of non-utility is clearly use.

Zhuangzi in *The Worldly Realm*
first spoke of the crooked plowshaft,
then of the great tree of Shangqiu,
and finally of osmanthus cut because it is edible,
lacquer stripped because it is useful—
clearly showing how the use of non-utility differs from the use of utility.

Yet to wander *The Worldly Realm*
and share in the people’s joys and sorrows—
how could one not know this?

I alone differ from others,
yet seek nourishment from my mother.

Huizong annotated:
*"An infant longs for the colt’s following—
only the Dao’s seeking remains.
The Dao generates, nurtures, grows, and rears—
all things draw from it.
It has the intent of a mother.
Only the Dao’s seeking is this,
so as to differ from those who lose their nature in vulgarity."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"The Dao acts through all things,
lending and completing,
covering and nurturing without limit—
it can be the mother of the world.
All that has life and form—
how could any depart from the Dao?
Yang Xiong’s *Asking About the Dao* says:
*‘An infant longs for the colt’s following,’*
to show that all things seek the Dao’s origin in nature,
not by coercion.
The Dao, from emptiness and stillness,
transforms into the myriad things,
generates them to fulfill their nature,
nurtures them to perfect their growth,
lengthens them to completion,
rears them to fullness—
all things go to draw from it without depletion,
how different from the mother’s embrace?"*

The myriad things arise from the Dao, thus the Dao is the mother and the things are her children. The *Classic* states:
*"There is the mother of all named things."*
And again:
*"The world has its beginning, which becomes the mother of the world."*
Thus, the Dao’s maternal intent is clearly seen.

People of the world not only fail to grasp their mother but cannot even preserve her. They abandon the true for the false, and the Dao grows ever more distant. Hence, the ancients spoke of *"seeking the sea with a spear"* as their only recourse.

But those who seek only the Dao—
how different are they from those who lose their nature in vulgarity?

**Chapter Twenty-One: The Virtue-Power of the Sage’s Container**
The virtue-power of the Sage’s container follows only the Dao.

*The Huizong Commentary* says:
*"The interplay of yin and yang is called the Dao. When things arise through it, that is called virtue-power."*
The Dao is ever nameless—how could it be described?
Thus, its virtue-power is made divine.

Virtue-power has a defined form, which all may share,
and through this, the Dao is revealed.
When nature is refined and returns to virtue-power,
virtue-power reaches back to its origin—
thus, it follows only the Dao.

*The Cushui Explanation* says:
*"The Dao of Heaven is established as yin and yang.
Yin and yang together constitute the Dao.
To generate without possession is called the Mysterious Virtue-Power.
When things arise through it, that is called virtue-power.
The Dao is hidden and nameless, without form to be seen—
thus, it cannot be described in words.
Virtue-power has a fixed form, and all that is born is complete within it,
so it is what all may share.

Yet virtue-power encompasses the Dao,
and the Dao is what makes virtue-power manifest.
Thus, the nameless Dao divinizes its virtue-power;
the Dao disperses into virtue-power,
and virtue-power illuminates the Dao’s obscurity.
Thus, when virtue-power is refined and returns to its origin,
it aligns with the initial state—
virtue-power, returning to the nameless origin,
becomes one with the Dao.
This is why it follows only the Dao.

The Dao, as a thing, is but vague and indistinct.

*The Huizong Commentary* says:
*"The Dao’s essence is ultimate emptiness, yet its function is wondrous manifestation—
thus it is a thing, though all things are the Dao.
‘Vague’ refers to the visible forms that may be glimpsed.
‘Indistinct’ refers to the numbers that may be traced.
Yet what is called ‘having’ is but doubt of the ‘none’—
hence, the Dao as a thing."*

*The Cushui Explanation* says:
*"The Dao has essence and function.
Speaking of its essence, it has never been a thing—
this is called ultimate emptiness.
Speaking of its function, it responds without exhaustion—
this is called wondrous manifestation.
Ultimate emptiness reveals itself as wondrous manifestation—
this is why it is a thing.
Wondrous manifestation arises from ultimate emptiness,
so all things are the Dao.
The Dao as a thing is seen in its vagueness and indistinctness.
‘Vague’ has no fixed form—only the mind can conceive it—
thus, it is a visible form that may be glimpsed.
‘Indistinct’ marks the beginning of numbers—
numbers arise from it—
thus, it is a sequence that may be traced.
Vague and indistinct—
sometimes it seems to have form, sometimes not—
this is called ‘having’ while doubting ‘none.’
The Dao as a thing—
is this not its subtle presence?

Vague and indistinct,
yet within them are forms.
Indistinct and vague,
yet within them are things.

*The Huizong Commentary* says:
*"What is seen is called form; what takes shape is called thing.
Within the indistinct and vague, forms and things are complete—
like the Great Void transforming into wind and thunder,
like the Great Block becoming water and fire,
to bring about transformation and move the numinous—
this is the Dao’s marvel."*

*The Cushui Explanation* says:
*"Things arise, and then forms appear—
thus, form is the precursor to auspiciousness, hence ‘seen’ is called form.
The four seasons disperse their essence into things—
thus, things are confined to having shape, hence ‘shape’ is called thing.
Things and forms emerge from the indistinct and vague—
like wind sweeping through the mountains, thunder shaking the heavens,
changing the Great Void;
like water flowing downward, fire blazing upward,
transforming the Great Block.
Thus, they bring about transformation—
insects enter and exit plants, life and death all depend on this to complete.
Thus, they move the numinous—
from shaped beings to the formless, from sentient to the unsentient,
all depend on this to act.
The Dao’s marvel is seen in this.

Mysterious and dark,
yet within them is essence.

*The Huizong Commentary* says:
*"‘Mysterious’ is the utmost depth of seclusion.
‘Dark’ is the hidden source of brightness.
Within the mysterious and dark lies the origin of ultimate yin,
where Heaven’s One manifests, and essence truly arises."*

*The Cushui Explanation* says:
*"Seclusion in a cave is hard to see—thus, ‘mysterious’ is the utmost depth of seclusion.
The sun hides for six days—thus, ‘dark’ is the hidden source of brightness.
Above the Great Brightness lies the origin of ultimate yang,
so within the mysterious and dark lies the origin of ultimate yin.
The origin of ultimate yin, in the five phases, is north.
Heaven’s One generates water in the north, which in humans is essence.
Thus, within the mysterious and dark lies the source of essence.

Its essence is most true,
and within it lies sincerity.
From ancient times to now,
its name does not fade.

*The Huizong Commentary* says:
*"Essence is the utmost of Heaven’s virtue.
It is upright and without deceit—hence, ‘most true.’
It is singular and unchanging—hence, ‘sincerity.’
Thus, it endures without interruption, and its name does not fade."*

*The Cushui Explanation* says:
*"Essence is what the One generates, and Heaven attains the One to become pure—
thus, essence is the utmost of Heaven’s virtue.
The *Book of Changes* speaks of the seven virtues of *Qian*, and ‘pure’ is one—
thus, essence is Heaven’s virtue, is it not clear?
Attaining the One to be born, the unformed has its portion—
thus, it is singular and unchanging—is this not sincerity?
If so, then all things from beginning to end are thus,
and the essence of the One pervades without interruption—
this is why its name does not fade.

To observe the transformations of the myriad beginnings—
how do I know the myriad beginnings are thus?
Because of this.

*The Huizong Commentary* says:
*"The transformations of the myriad beginnings are ever-passing without halt.
The utmost essence is ever-present without fading.
The Sage values essence, thus able to observe the transformations of the myriad beginnings
and know their reason."*

To remain without thought, yet be serene in stillness,
To act without doing, yet move without motion—
Yet when stirred by response, the Way of all things unfolds,
Then thought arises as the beginning of action,
And the traces of deeds are left behind.
Who but the utmost essence of the world
Could partake in this?

*The Commentary on the Meaning* says:
Divine marvels transform into stench and decay,
Stench and decay transform into divine marvels—
The transformations of the myriad beginnings
Are thus ever-passing without halt.
The One’s essence, harmonized with Heaven’s order,
Is why the utmost truth remains ever-present without fading.
The Sage values essence,
And thus attains the Dao from ancient times to today—
Its name never fading.

In the ebb and flow of waxing and waning,
Ending only to begin anew,
He speaks of the grand principle’s method,
Discourses on the nature of all things—
Thus, he observes the transformations of the myriad beginnings,
Knowing their reason without knowing why.

The Sage, through this, purifies the heart,
Ever without thought, serene in stillness,
Retreating into hidden depths,
Ever without doing, unmoved—
Yet when stirred, the Way of all things unfolds.

Once without thought, yet thought must arise;
Once without doing, yet action must follow.
Thought emerges from non-thought,
Action from non-action—
Thus, grand achievements and abundant deeds leave their mark.
Who but the utmost essence of the world
Could accomplish this?

This chapter first speaks of the virtue of the utmost essence,
And finally speaks of knowing the transformations of the myriad beginnings—
For this very reason.

*[Note: "曲则全" (Quzequan) is a chapter title, meaning "Flexibility Brings Wholeness."*]

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Yielding to things like a serpent’s wave, it preserves its form and life without loss."*

Zhuangzi says: *"The outwardly bent conforms to others as a disciple."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"Dulling its radiance yet not obscuring, blending with dust yet not differing—greatly harmonizing with things, it twists with them, fittingly like bending. Then its body leads while the body remains, and it withdraws its body while the body survives. Such bending is how it preserves its form and life without loss."*

Zhuangzi takes *"yielding to things like a serpent’s wave"* as the essential principle of vitality, and says: *"The outwardly bent conforms to others as a disciple."* Only by bending outwardly does one not offend others’ sentiments—is this not *flexibility brings wholeness*?

**Gunzezhi (Crookedness Brings Straightness):**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"When abandoned, one does not grieve; when confined, one does not lament—thus inwardly straight without losing its uprightness."*

The *Yi* says: *"The inchworm’s bending is to seek its truth."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"When abandoned and left to perish, one should grieve yet does not; when confined and made destitute, one should lament yet does not. This seems like crookedness. Yet straightness without rigidity is how it preserves its straightness. Those who take accusation for straightness only harm their integrity. Such crookedness is what is called *straightening its uprightness*."*

The *Yi* says: *"The inchworm’s bending is to seek its truth."* Indeed, bending is how it seeks its truth—is this not *crookedness brings straightness*?

**Woxiying (Hollow Brings Fullness):**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Without hoarding, there is abundance."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"Hide mountains in marshes, boats in ravines, and they vanish without trace. Hide grain in困, water in vessels, and they are easily exhausted. Only by possessing what is within—able to move without accumulation—can one then use it, and it will overflow with abundance. This is what Zhuangzi means by *without hoarding, there is abundance*."*

Illustrated by the nature of myriad things, *hollow brings fullness*—this is clear.

**Biezixin (Exhaustion Brings Renewal):**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"If winter’s enclosure is not firm, spring’s growth will not flourish."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"Only by returning to root can one hope for its teeming. With the season of killing, one can hope for its flourishing. Only by storing in winter—firmly—can spring’s abundance necessarily thrive."* Illustrated by the cycles of the four seasons, *exhaustion brings renewal*—this is clear.

**Shaozeduo (Fewness Brings Gain, Multitude Brings Confusion):**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The Dao’s essence is not in complexity; many words and sights are not as good as simplicity. If one takes branches for the essence, one will never comprehend—this is great confusion."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"The Dao is one and only. Thus its essence is not complex. Many words must be held with simplicity; many sights must be held with clarity. Only by having many words and sights yet returning to simplicity can one gain the Dao. If the learned are confined to branches as their essence, their speech will be exhausted, and they cannot avoid entanglement. Those in great confusion will never comprehend—are these not such people?"*

Zhuangzi says: *"The Dao does not desire mixture."* This is the same meaning.

Thus, the Sage embraces *Oneness* as the model for all under Heaven.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Its manifestation is not dual, so its creation of things is boundless. Only the utmost essence of the world can become the utmost spirit of the world. The Sage embraces Oneness to guard it, unshaken in its essence, so when speaking, it becomes the Dao for all under Heaven; when acting, it becomes the standard for all under Heaven."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"Only the utmost sincerity of the world can exhaust the nature of things. Exhausting the nature of things, one can assist Heaven and Earth in their nurturing transformation, participating with them. Its manifestation is not dual—this is sincerity. Its creation of things is boundless—this is assisting Heaven and Earth in their nurturing transformation. Only Oneness can preserve essence; only essence can gather spirit. What is this Oneness? It is sincerity itself. The penetration of Oneness’s essence ensures spirit’s preservation—essence precedes, and spirit follows. Only the utmost essence of the world can become the utmost spirit of the world. Embracing Oneness alone, the Sage exhausts this."*

If Oneness remains unchanging, guarding it without loss, then the utmost truth’s essence is internally preserved and not scattered. Thus, spirit and essence flow in all directions—upward reaching, downward curling—transforming and nurturing myriad things, beyond representation. When compelled to speak, speech forms patterns and all under Heaven follow. When compelled to act, action forms virtue and all under Heaven emulate it.

As the *Records* say: *"When speaking, it becomes the Dao for all under Heaven; when acting, it becomes the standard for all under Heaven—this is the model for all under Heaven."*

**Not seeing oneself, hence clarity:**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Not being obscured by one’s own views, it illuminates without bounds, hence clarity."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"With four eyes and four ears, broadening vision and hearing without obstruction, it can illuminate the farthest reaches and know people’s true sentiments. Not being obscured by one’s own views is how it can illuminate without bounds and be clear."*

**Not insisting on oneself, hence brilliance:**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Not clinging to one’s own correctness, yet following what is correct, then great deeds and fame shine, and all under Heaven submit—hence brilliance."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"Consulting the many, abandoning self to follow others, not using oneself yet praising what is correct—thus achieving resplendent deeds and universal peace. Not clinging to one’s own correctness, yet following what is correct is how one achieves great deeds and fame, making all under Heaven submit and thus shining."*

**Not praising oneself, hence merit:**
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The *Book of Documents* says: *You alone do not boast; none under Heaven will contend with you for merit.*"*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"The Classics say: *Those who praise themselves have no merit.* The *Book of Documents* says: *Having goodness, yet losing it.* Able not to boast of merit, so others do not contend, and its merit does not vanish."*

Yu’s command to Yu the Great said: *"You alone do not boast, and none under Heaven will contend with you for merit."* Could this be what is meant?

Not self-aggrandizing, hence enduring.
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The Classics say: You alone do not boast, and none under Heaven will contend with you for ability."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"The Classics say: Those who boast do not endure. The Classics say: Boast of one’s abilities, and one loses one’s merit. Only by not boasting of one’s abilities does one ensure others do not contend, and the common people align with one’s merit."* Yu’s command to Yu the Great said: *"You alone do not boast, and none under Heaven will contend with you for ability."* Could this be what is meant?

He who does not contend, hence none can contend with him.
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"All take precedence, yet he alone remains last, saying, ‘I receive the filth of the world.’ If such, he always dwells in the realm of non-contention—who can contend with him?"*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"The multitude values strength over virtue and seeks to surpass others, hence all take precedence. The Sage does not dare to lead the world but follows the Way in humility, thus he alone remains last. Remaining last, he knows white yet embraces black, knows honor yet embraces disgrace—honor and disgrace are seen equally, and he does not burden his heart with self-indulgence. This is called receiving the filth of the world. If so, he presents humility and weakness as his exterior, yet his interior is vast and unspoiled, preserving all things. Dwelling in non-contention, he accumulates countless small victories into great ones. Always in non-contention, who can contend with him? The ancients spoke of ‘yielding to preserve wholeness’—was this not truth? Truly whole, and thus it returns.*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The Sage’s actions flow like water, interacting with all yet never diminishing his wholeness. His responses align like a plumb line, following principle yet never losing their straightness. Knowing that emptiness brings fullness, he bears no burden of excess. Knowing that decay brings renewal, he leaves no trace of arrogance. Like nature acting of itself, unaware of its doing, he merely attains yielding. Thus, whole and returning, he can preserve his life, fulfill his years, yet remain unaware of their end. This is called the person of perfect virtue—was this not truth?"*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"Water conforms to the vessel’s shape, round or square—none can contend with it. The Sage’s actions are thus, enabling him to yield to all while preserving his form and life, as said: ‘His actions flow like water, interacting with all, yet never diminishing his wholeness.’ The plumb line is gathered and tied, its nature ever straight. The Sage’s responses are thus, enabling him to follow things’ crookedness while his straightness remains within, as said: ‘His responses align like a plumb line, following principle, yet never losing their straightness.’ Knowing that emptiness brings fullness, he thus attains great fullness like a void, humbly tending himself—what burden of excess? Knowing that decay brings renewal, he harmonizes his brilliance and waits in obscurity, never boasting—what trace of arrogance? All this is not a forced understanding of yielding and crookedness but a natural alignment, as if by instinct, emptying himself to respond, not clashing with things, merely attaining yielding. Thus, whole and returning, he can preserve his life without fear of peril, fulfill his years without fear of early death, harmonizing with Heaven and Earth, enduring yet unaware of his end. Such is the person of perfect virtue—was this not truth? Truly whole, and thus it returns.*

**Chapter Twenty-Three: *Hishen Ziran* (Rare Speech, Natural)**
Rare Speech, Natural.
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Rare—standing alone above all things, not opposing them, as the *Liezi* speaks of ‘doubtful solitude.’ Discarding knowledge and artifice, following Heaven’s principle, and not laboring externally, hence ‘natural.’"*

The *Cushui* commentary explains: *"The *Dao De Jing* says: *Listening yet not hearing is called rare.* The *De Jing* says: *Great sound is rare in voice.* Rare—broad yet with gaps, not heard by the ear—this is standing alone above all things, not opposing them. The *Liezi* speaks of ‘non-being as doubtful solitude’—non-being can generate life, non-being thus doubts its solitude, none can pair with it—could this be the meaning of ‘rare solitude’? Thus, he discards his sharpness and wisdom, abandons knowledge and artifice, harmonizes with Heaven’s virtue, and follows Heaven’s principle, standing firm in his place, not laboring externally—this is not what others can achieve, hence ‘natural.’ Therefore, gusts of wind do not last a morning, sudden rains do not last a day. Who does this? Heaven and Earth."*

---

Heaven and Earth themselves cannot endure long—how much less can they be matched by humans?

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
When Heaven and Earth create all things, the wind disperses them, allowing all forms to transform naturally, while rain nourishes them, letting all things grow spontaneously. Thus, they neither augment life nor spur completion, yet all things flourish between Heaven and Earth—this is how they endure long.

When gusts become relentless (*piāofēng*) and rains sudden (*zhòuyǔ*), yin and yang suffer discord and conflict. If one forces intervention, harm befalls the things—thus they cannot endure long.

**Exegesis:**
Heaven bestows, Earth generates—all things thrive together. Yet nothing disrupts life more swiftly than wind, nor nourishes it more deeply than water. Thus, the wind stirs and animates all forms, entrusting their transformation so that things may flourish freely; rain moistens and enriches all things, allowing them to grow luxuriant. It acts through natural course, not by augmenting life or aiding growth. Thus, life and transformation unfold in form and color between Heaven and Earth—this is the Way of enduring long.

But when wind grows fierce and swirling (*piāozhuǎn*), and rain gathers violently (*zhòujù*), this is not the harmony of yin and yang but the calamity of discord. To things, it brings augmentation and forced completion, not natural course—thus they suffer harm. How could such endure long?

Therefore, those who follow the Dao:
—Those aligned with the Dao are one with the Dao;
—Those aligned with virtue are one with virtue;
—Those aligned with loss are one with loss.

Those aligned with the Dao gain the Dao;
Those aligned with virtue gain virtue;
Those aligned with loss gain loss.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
Rarity (*xī*) means no external striving—beyond sound’s silence, awakening solely through innate awareness, merging with the Dao yet not entangled with things. How could virtue be named, or loss be burdened?

Only those unaware of the natural transformation of solitude mistake the Dao as difficult to know, difficult to practice—as if climbing to Heaven, beyond reach. Thus they labor mind and body in pursuit of the Dao, clinging to one-sidedness or obscured by narrowness, and the Dao is rent for all under Heaven.

Those aligned with the Dao are one with the Dao,
Those aligned with virtue are one with virtue,
Those aligned with loss are one with loss—
yet if they do not attain their own attainment,
then their attainment is itself a kind of loss.

**Exegesis:**
To stand alone above all things, not laboring externally—this is rarity. Able to discard sharpness and wisdom, silently recognizing harmony in soundlessness, alone in hearing the true resonance—thus merging with the Dao, beyond pairing with things.

When the Dao reaches this, there is neither gain nor loss—what virtue can be named, what loss can be burdened? The ignorant cannot dawn upon solitude, thus they do not know the natural transformation of solitude.

The Dao is inherently easy to know, yet none under Heaven knows it—thus it is deemed difficult to know.
The Dao is inherently easy to practice, yet none under Heaven practices it—thus it is deemed difficult to practice.

Some seek the Dao in the distant heights, as if climbing to Heaven and unreachable, unaware that the lower one descends, the more it is attained—unconstrained by heights. Though their minds may fixate, straining their thoughts, or their skills may weary their bodies in pursuing the Dao—what could they attain?

To pursue it thus is to grasp one-sidedness in the Dao, to cling to narrowness in things—the Dao’s wholeness is thus fractured.

Mankind is born with inherent solitude, unchanging in nature, yet if they pursue the Dao, my so-called "standing alone above all things" is now subservient to the Dao. Though they attain virtue or loss in three degrees, it is not their own attainment—only converging in loss.

Faith is insufficient, thus there is doubt.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
Faith means no deception. Deception arises from true and false—if one takes the Dao as true and things as false, then faith is insufficient, and doubt arises. The disciples of Hui Shi and Han Fei all strayed far from the Dao because of insufficient faith.

**Exegesis:**
The Classic says: *"The trustworthy, I trust; the untrustworthy, I also trust—this is true trust."* Though all things differ, none are without substance. To regard the untrustworthy as trustworthy is true trust.

Only those who know the Dao does not contradict things, yet do not take the Dao as true knowledge, nor things as false—thus there is nowhere without trust.

But those with insufficient faith arise from delusion, taking the Dao as true and things as false, unaware of the whole, and thus generate distinctions. Hence, those with insufficient faith also have doubt.

Like Hui Shi’s love of paradoxes, Han Fei’s rigid legalism—unaware that true and false merge in unity, and those who separate them stray far from the Dao—all because of insufficient faith.

**End of *Dao Xuē Zhēn Jīng* Exegesis, Volume Five** #1: *"Then then"*—likely a scribal error.
**Volume Six of *Moral Virtue True Classic* Exegesis**
By the Imperial Scholar Jiang Cheng’s Commentary.

**Chapter Twenty-Four: "The Unsteady Cannot Stand"**

The unsteady cannot stand; the overreaching cannot move.
*Huizong*’s Annotation: To stand while unsteady, to move while overreaching—these defy nature’s norm, yet hope for bodily ease. How difficult!

If one follows *virtue/power* as their guide, then all who have sufficient grounding will arrive.
The Exegesis says: The unsteady leans but does not stand firm, yet still they cannot doubt that standing is possible. The overreaching strides but does not hasten, yet still they cannot doubt that they can move with ease. To stand while unsteady, to move while overreaching—these are those who cannot rest in tranquility, cannot align with nature, and thus cannot cease. To defy nature’s norm, yet hope for bodily ease, how difficult!

Only those who *follow their nature* and find self-sufficiency, who take *virtue/power* as their guide, and do not force or distort things as false—only these will arrive where they have sufficient grounding. Like the childless *Master Shushan* and *Wang Dai*, who lacked feet yet reached everywhere.

Those who see only themselves are not enlightened; those who insist on their own correctness do not shine; those who boast of their own merits have no true accomplishment; those who pride themselves in their abilities do not endure.
In the Dao, this is called *excessive consumption* and *superfluous action*.

*Huizong*’s Annotation: Self-perception leads to insufficient wisdom to encompass all things, hence lack of enlightenment. Insisting on one’s own correctness means *virtue/power* is insufficient to harmonize with the multitude, hence lack of brilliance. They possess merit, yet lose it; they boast of their abilities, yet lose their efficacy. Where the Dao resides, it takes *depth* as its root and *simplicity* as its measure. Excessive display and lustful ambition—how could these be the Dao?

Thus, in eating, it is called *excess*; in action, it is called *superfluous*. Both are external adornments, not intrinsic.
Some may detest them, hence the Daoist does not dwell there.

*Huizong*’s Annotation: Luxury beyond nature leads to excess, which is what the heavens lack, the earth transforms, and people abhor. Hence, the Daoist does not dwell there.

The Exegesis says: The Dao of heaven lacks excess and instead fosters humility; the Dao of earth transforms excess into humility; the Dao of humanity abhors excess and cherishes humility. If one indulges in excessive display and lustful ambition, they are luxuriating beyond nature and filling themselves with excess. They should be what the heavens lack, the earth transforms, and people abhor. The Sage does not so. They always present *gentleness, weakness, humility, and modesty* as their outward form. Those who do not know that fullness brings burden and excess strains nature, yet indulge in self-swelling, should be what the Daoist does not dwell in.

**Chapter Twenty-Five: "The Formless Mixture"**

There is a formless mixture that arises before heaven and earth.
*Huizong*’s Annotation: *Qi* and form are complete yet undifferentiated—this is *primordial chaos*. To merge with *primordial chaos* means their completion is unmarred. This is what the *Yijing* calls the *Great Polarity*. Heaven and earth also depend on this before they arise, hence it is said to arise before heaven and earth. Yet it is not the ineffable mystery of non-arising, hence it is called *thing*.

The Exegesis says: *Great Beginning* is the origin of *qi*; *Great Purity* is the origin of form. Before these three are differentiated, they are called *primordial chaos*. In the initial state of *primordial chaos*, the *Great Uncarved* has not yet dispersed—this is what the *Yijing* calls the *Great Polarity*. Thus, to merge with *primordial chaos* means their completion is unmarred. The *Yijing* has the *Great Polarity*, which gives rise to the *Two Principles*. The *Great Polarity* is the source from which the *Two Principles* emerge, hence heaven and earth depend on it to arise. To enter *non-arising*, then *qi* is undifferentiated and *primordial chaos* is merged in the ineffable profundity. To descend into *arising*, then the light and clear separate from the heavy and turbid, manifesting in forms. Arising—how could this be the ineffable profundity of non-arising? It is called *thing*, and this is its proper designation.

Silent and boundless,
*Huizong*’s Annotation: Silent and boundless means it does not engage in movement, nor does it interact with things. It is pure and complete in itself.

The Exegesis says: Silent and unmoving means the silent has never engaged in movement. *Great Emptiness* is boundless and vast, meaning the boundless has never interacted with things. Not engaging in movement, not interacting with things—then it has no sound, no scent, pure and complete in itself.

Independent yet unchanging,
*Huizong*’s Annotation: Great constancy sustains it, unyielding to transformation—this speaks to the Dao’s essence.

The Exegesis says: From its own root and foundation, ancient and unshaken, nothing can topple it, no change can displace it. This is called *great constancy sustaining it*. If so, then all transformations are boundless, yet the Dao remains ever the same. Is this not the Dao’s essence?

Circulating endlessly without exhaustion,
*Huizong*’s Annotation: It benefits all things in their arising and passing, moving ceaselessly—this speaks to the Dao’s function.

The Exegesis says: When things arise, it arises with them; when things pass, it passes with them. All people rely on it, and wherever it goes, it endures. This is called *benefiting all things in their arising and passing*. If so, then in every arising and passing, yet it is always inexhaustible. Is this not the Dao’s function?

It can be the mother of all under heaven.
*Huizong*’s Annotation: All things depend on it for their arising.

The Exegesis says: That which is born cannot give birth to itself—only that which does not itself arise can give birth to all things. Therefore, all things must rely upon the Dao to arise. The Classic says: *"All things depend upon it to arise, yet it does not decline."* It also says: *"The world has a beginning, and it is called the Mother of the world."* I do not know its name, so I call it *Dao*, and forcefully name it thus.

*Huizong’s Annotation:* Things arise, and only then do they take form; they take form, and only then do they flourish; they flourish, and only then do they take measure. Names arise from reality, and reality has its measure—thus, naming is but the flourishing. The Dao is ever nameless, so when we name it with the greatest term, it is in contrast to the lesser. Thus, it can be named.

The Dao’s subtlety is small and profound; the Dao’s centrality is great and manifest.
*The Exegesis says:* Left Scholar says: Things arise, and only then do they take form; they take form, and only then do they flourish; they flourish, and only then do they take measure. Things arise with water—water gathers and flourishes, this is flourishing. That which can be gathered into clusters, dispersed into branches, this is measure. Flourishing is the preliminary appearance of measure, before it congeals into form. Measure is the completed flourishing, fixed within a solid body. When things are formed and their measure is set, their abundance or scarcity becomes apparent—this is where names arise. Thus, names arise from reality, and reality has its measure.

Naming and giving it a childlike designation—this is the meaning of naming, so naming is but flourishing. The nameless is the beginning of heaven and earth; the Dao generates heaven and earth, so the Dao is ever nameless. The subtlest of subtleties, the most vast of vastness—thus, the great is the contrast to the small. The nameless is the Dao’s subtlety; the Dao’s subtlety is small and profound, hence this. Therefore, naming it as great is the Dao’s centrality; the Dao’s centrality is great and manifest, hence this. Thus, it can be named.

When manifest in the utmost transformation, it is great and interacts with all things. When returning to the utmost profundity, it is small and distinguishes itself from all things. Only in its interaction with all things can it be named as great—covering and nurturing all things, viewing them equally with benevolence, encompassing all without distinction. Only in its distinction from all things can it be named as small—action through stillness, creation through repose, returning to its Dao without departing from its nature.

When interacting with all things, it is as the *Classic* says: *"In speaking of the far, it is unconstrained."* When distinguishing itself from all things, it is as the *Classic* says: *"In speaking of the near, it is still and correct."* The Dao’s distance from its root is far—its departure runs without pause, responds without exhaustion. Far, it is unconstrained—is this not the time of its interaction with all things? The Dao’s return to its root is near—it returns to its root in stillness, stillness as returning to its command. Near, it is still and correct—is this not the time of its distinction from all things?

Thus, the Dao is great; heaven is great; earth is great; the Sage-King is great.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* The Dao covers and nurtures heaven and earth. Heaven covers all without exception; earth bears all without omission. The Sage-King occupies the central position between heaven and earth, aligning with them—thus, he too is great.

*The Exegesis says:* The Dao precedes the Supreme Ultimate yet is not lofty; it lies beneath the Six Extremes yet is not deep. As Liezi says: *"Beyond heaven and earth, there is no greater heaven and earth."* Thus, the Dao can cover and nurture heaven and earth. The light and pure gather above as heaven—when its expanse is boundless, stars and all things are without exception covered. The heavy and turbid settle below as earth—when its breadth and depth are vast, mountains and seas are without omission borne. The Sage-King embodies this, positioned within, harmonizing the Three Powers with mutual communication, distinguishing the Three Extremes with their own essences. Thus, he can emulate heaven and earth within himself. This is why the four great things within the realm are so.

Yet, though not inherently great, they are ranked as great—still not departing from form. From the Supreme One, divided into four—still not departing from measure. Though having form yet not obstructed by form, though having measure yet not constrained by measure—this is how it is great.

Within the realm, there are four great things, and the Sage-King occupies one of them.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* Descending from the Dao, there are square forms—thus, it is called the realm. Stillness is sagely; action is kingly. One who unifies the Three Powers and harmonizes human conduct—this is the utmost.

---

Thus, he shares the same essence as the Dao, the same function as heaven and earth, yet is still called great. *Cushiyi* says: Before the Dao descends, it is unified in the boundless void with no division, hence the earlier reference to the "mixed and complete Dao." Once dispersed, it is confined within square forms and thus visible, hence the current mention of the "realm."

The square form does not depart from the original essence of the Heavenly Dao—this is why it is said to be still and sagely. When it manifests in the rise of the Sage-King, this is why it is said to be active and kingly. Only when one transitions from stillness to action, from sagely virtue to kingly power, can he harmonize the Three Powers and fulfill the ultimate extent of human conduct.

As for the Sage-King’s undertakings and achievements, one who surpasses others in this is the king. One who surpasses others in this is akin to heaven, for the king’s capacity to fulfill human conduct is the same as heaven’s. The *Sutra* states: "The king is heaven." Is this not the Dao’s utmost perfection?

Within the realm, the four great things are thus, and the Sage-King resides in one of them. Now, the Dao is hidden and nameless—only he who dwells with it can comprehend it. Since he shares the same essence as the Dao, and heaven and earth establish their positions, while the Sage-King perfects his abilities, they all share the same function. Thus, the Dao, heaven and earth, and the Sage-King all originate from the same source, unified and harmonized, fulfilling human conduct to become one with heaven, embodying heaven to become one with the Dao—there is no distinction to be observed.

Humans follow the earth, the earth follows the heavens, the heavens follow the Dao, and the Dao follows *ziran* [自然].
*Huizong’s Annotation:* Humans refer to the Sage-King. The heavens do not act yet all things transform; the earth does not grow yet all things nurture. The Sage-King acts without effort yet achieves the world’s prosperity—what he follows is the Dao’s *ziran* alone.

The Dao follows *ziran* because it responds to things. *Ziran* is not the Dao in its entirety, but when it emerges to respond to things, the Dao descends to follow it as well.
*Cushiyi* says: Earlier, it was said that the Dao is great, the heavens are great, the earth is great, and the Sage-King is also great. Here, it is said that humans follow the earth—thus, it is known that "humans" refers to the Sage-King.

The heavens act without effort, yet remain clear; they allow all things to flourish naturally, thus not "producing" yet all things transform—this is the divine essence of the heavens. The earth acts without effort, yet remains tranquil; it entrusts all forms to their own growth, thus not "lengthening" yet all things are nurtured—this is the richness of the earth. Above, the Sage-King must act without effort to govern the world; below, he must act with purpose for the world’s benefit. To govern without effort yet achieve the world’s prosperity—this is the greatness of the Sage-King. These three principles are all derived from following the Dao’s *ziran*.

Indeed, humans do not fail to follow the heavens, yet it is said they follow the earth; the earth does not fail to follow the Dao, yet it is said to follow the heavens. This is because when speaking of the Dao, there must be an order. Ultimately, all originate from *ziran*—thus, it concludes with "the Dao follows *ziran*."

Even if discussing things, *ziran* is above them; if not discussing things, *ziran* is below them. From *ziran* arises the other, and from *ziran* comes extinction—*ziran* is not the Dao in its entirety. Yet the Dao follows it, emerging to respond to things, and thus its following is a descent to lower realms.
Zhuangzi said: "To respond in accordance with *ziran*—this is the Dao."
Is this not what is meant?

**Chapter Twenty-Six: The Heavy as the Light’s Root, Stillness as the Lord of Agitation**
The heavy serves as the root of the light, stillness as the lord of agitation. Thus, the virtuous one walks all day without straying from his burden.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* The heavy does not sway or be stolen away, yet remains steadfast—thus, it serves as the root of the light. The still does not move recklessly, yet maintains control—thus, it lords over agitation.

With stillness and heaviness to sustain oneself, few will lose their way. The virtuous one walks all day without straying from his burden—thus, though he treads cautiously, he fears no mishap.

*Cushiyi* says: Yang Xiong said: "Take the four heavy things." Heavy means one does not grow light. The *Sutra* states: "Hold steadfast to stillness." Stillness means one does not falter. Only when one does not grow light can things not displace him, and his center remains unshaken—thus, he is not swayed or stolen away, yet remains steadfast. Only when one does not falter can he not rush forward recklessly, yet respond to all movements—thus, he is not moved recklessly, yet maintains control.

Heaviness with steadfastness is why it serves as the root of the light;
Stillness with control is why it lords over agitation.
To be able to guard oneself with stillness means all movements cannot displace;
To be able to sustain oneself with heaviness means external things cannot overwhelm.
Few will lose their way—this is indeed his nature.

All things in motion have burdens; thus, they are heavy and slow. Without burdens, they are light and swift.
Movement is swift when light, yet it must rely on burdens to sustain itself. Though he treads cautiously, he fears no midway困 [困], hence no mishap.

Though there are grand spectacles, he must first attain tranquil repose to rise above them.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* Grand spectacles belong to things; tranquil repose belongs to the self. Only when the self is at ease can one enjoy the things around him.

*Cushiyi* says: Zhuangzi said: "When colors are paired, the five hues are disordered, and intricate patterns scatter the pure and the yellow in dazzling confusion." This is why grand spectacles belong to things. Mencius said: "The four limbs are for ease and repose." This is why tranquil repose belongs to the self. King Wen of Zhou, in his leisure, first ascended to the shore—his body was at ease, and then the pleasures of the terrace and pond appeared in his spirit. Only when the body is at ease can one enjoy things—this is evident here. Thus, though there are grand spectacles, one must first attain tranquil repose to rise above them.

How can a ruler of ten thousand chariots govern the world with a light heart?
*Huizong’s Annotation:* The world is a great thing. To have great things is to be solemn, as if driving six horses with a rotting rein—thus, one cannot govern the world with a light heart.

*Cushiyi* says: To act without effort and commit few faults is easy; to act with purpose yet bear no burdens is difficult. Can one who governs the world easily undertake such deeds? Yu praised Yao, saying, "Only the Emperor finds it difficult," and Tang said of himself, "I tremble with fear and anxiety." All who act with purpose find it difficult because of the burden of not being without effort. Thus, to have great things, one must be solemn as if driving six horses with a rotting rein—thus, one cannot govern the world with a light heart.

Lightness leads to losing one’s ministers, haste leads to losing one’s subjects.

*Huizong’s Annotation:* If one does not weigh actions solemnly, there will be no authority, hence one loses one’s ministers. If one does not lead but merely harmonizes, one transgresses proper distinctions, hence one loses one’s subjects.

*Cushiyi* says: A dignified bearing naturally commands respect—this is the way of things. Without such weight, there is no authority. The ruler holds supreme dignity while ministers are lowly; only the ruler’s dignity matters, so one must not act lightly.

The ruler initiates, the ministers follow—this is the natural order. If the ruler does not lead yet the ministers harmonize, they overstep their roles. The ruler must act first, so one must not be hasty.

**Chapter Twenty-Seven: The Virtue of Benevolent Conduct**
Benevolent conduct leaves no trace.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* What is preserved is the Dao, what is manifested is transformation—thus, no trace remains.
*Cushiyi* says: The Dao is formless and boundless, yet it abides in harmony. Thus, what is preserved is the Dao. Transformation arises through the shifting of forms and is subject to traces—thus, what is manifested is transformation. Those who dwell in the Dao preserve without seeking, while those who dwell in traces pass through without guarding their steps. Such conduct should leave no trace behind.

Benevolent words bear no flaws or reproach.
