*Huizong’s Annotation:* In harmony with the Dao’s natural flow, unfolding without constraint—thus, no flaws or reproach remain.
Words and deeds are grand in scope: they begin with contemplation and end in transformation. Only the Sage can perfect words and deeds, achieving the marvel of transformation—thus, benevolent conduct leaves no trace to follow, and benevolent words bear no reproach to burden.

*Cushiyi* says: *Tianni* refers to the origin of nature and life. *Manyan* refers to boundless change. If one aligns with the origin of nature and life, and follows with boundless change, then words spoken may endure all day, and words spoken may exhaust the Dao. Such words should bear no reproach.

Moreover, considering the *Yijing*:*
The *xiang* are the most refined patterns of the world—gentlefolk emulate them in speech.
The *yao* are the most dynamic forces of the world—gentlefolk act in accordance with them.

Words and deeds rest with humanity, while transformation belongs to Heaven and Earth.
Words and deeds begin with contemplation and end in transformation—thus, words and deeds are the pivot and mechanism of the gentlefolk.

Only the Sage comprehends the essence of words, making them the foundation of conduct. With such words, one can respond to distant affairs as if at hand, and govern the movement of Heaven and Earth.

The marvel of transformation is complete within oneself. If one acts thus, then conduct leaves no trace—coming and going alone, following only the grand Dao. What trace could there be?

If one speaks thus, then words conform to the law, set forth with divine intent, bearing no stain. What reproach could burden?

Benevolent planning does not rely on calculations.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* To grasp unity is to master all things—what need for numbers? Thus, calculations are unnecessary, yet myriad changes unfold as if counted one by one.

*Cushiyi* says: To know unity is to know all things, for all things in the world arise from unity. To grasp unity is to complete all affairs. Once one has achieved unity, one transcends numbers. To transcend numbers yet generate them is even more wondrous—how much more so to not rely on numbers yet bring them forth? It is fitting that no calculations are needed, for myriad changes, though seemingly countless, unfold as if counted one by two.

Benevolent closure requires no bolts or bars yet cannot be opened.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* Sealing the path guards the Dao, retreat hides in profound mystery.

*Cushiyi* says: Closing the mouth and sealing the gate, one holds fast to the Dao’s essence—none can alter its truth. *Tunna* guards the Dao thus. Zhuangzi said: "Without seeing or hearing, embracing the Dao in stillness—this is near the mark." The small mound and deep roots, the hidden position and concealed boundaries, yet the world cannot perceive its traces. Retreat hides in profound mystery—Zhuangzi said: "Hidden in the measureless rhythms of the Dao—this is near the mark." If one abides thus, though no bolts or bars are used, who could open it? This is called benevolent closure.

Benevolent binding requires no cords or fetters yet cannot be undone.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* To depend on cords and fetters for binding is to diminish one’s nature. Some do not speak yet nourish others with harmony; they stand alongside others yet transform them—binding without cords, who could undo it?

*Cushiyi* says: The world has its constants—one’s true nature is the origin, born with life, steadfast and unyielding. Why depend on cords and fetters for binding? To depend on cords and fetters is to harm one’s true nature and scatter one’s purity—thus, it diminishes one’s nature. The benevolent binder does not act thus: they water others’ hearts with perfect harmony, needing no words. They align with the Dao’s essence, causing others’ intentions to dissolve without relying on force. This is Zhuangzi’s meaning: "Some do not speak yet nourish others with harmony; they stand alongside others yet transform them"—precisely this.

If so, then attachment and separation do not require glue or lacquer, and bonds do not require ropes or knots. Who could undo it?

Thus, the Sage always benevolently rescues people, so none are abandoned.
The Sage always benevolently rescues things, so none are abandoned.

*Huizong’s Annotation:* Benevolence is the continuation of the Dao. To abide in the Dao is to transcend named benevolence. Once benevolence is named, the Dao emerges and the world is transformed. The Sage embodies the Dao to benefit the world—thus, these five benevolences extend to people and things without abandoning any.

Yet the Sage’s way of loving people and benefiting things, allowing them to flourish and for humanity to rejoice in their nature, is not a matter of intention. It is to return to their constant state, following the Dao’s natural flow.

The world has lost the Dao. All under Heaven have abandoned their tranquil, solemn, still, and vast nature, and daily sink into the realm of suffering. Who but the Sage can save them?

*Cushiyi* says: The *Yijing* says: "One yin and one yang—the Dao. To continue it is benevolence." Thus, benevolence is the continuation of the Dao, returning to the origin of the Great Dao. Then, benevolence and evil are merely distinctions that cannot be fully separated—thus, there is no named benevolence.

When benevolence is sung of as a virtue to be celebrated, it is rooted in the Dao, and thus its goodness encompasses all under Heaven—this is how the name of benevolence is established.

From virtuous deeds to perfect harmony, the Sage’s five benevolences extend without abandoning any, for they embody the ability to embody the Dao and thereby save all under Heaven.

The Sage loves and rescues people, letting them rejoice in life, and benefits and rescues things, letting them fulfill their nature—what need is there for deliberate intent? Returning to the One without trace, following the Dao without deviation, conforming to their natural state, riding the rites without obstruction, and letting all things fulfill their roles while the people govern themselves—this is all.

Yet if not for such a person, the Dao cannot act in vain, nor can it flourish in the world. Thus, the people lose their tranquil, solemn, still, and vast nature, becoming confined and chaotic, weary and burdened, daily sinking into the realm of suffering. Without one to nurture and guide them, how can they avoid corrupting their nature or straying from their virtue?

It is not the duty of those who teach and transform to bear the weight of the world with the Dao, to bring forth the Dao and benefit the world, so that the people and things may each find peace in their natural essence—who else could achieve the function of perfect benevolence and salvation?

This is called *clothing in brightness* (袭明).
**Huizong’s Annotation:** *Clothing* means to reveal without display.
Clothing in brightness shines yet does not dazzle.

**Explication:** The dragon, as a creature, can be seen and can hide. *Clothing* means to hide while not yet revealed. Because it hides, when its scales are within, *clothing* carries the meaning of revealing without display—akin to the *clothing* in *"inciting and clothing"* (惕袭) that does not follow sequentially.

Clothing in brightness removes the slippery doubt of dazzling and the scheming brilliance of form, for it does not flaunt its own light. The Sage’s way in the world is to be a boundless source of wisdom, yet outwardly appears dim—this is why it is so.

Thus, the virtuous person is the teacher of the unvirtuous; the unvirtuous person is the resource of the virtuous.
**Huizong’s Annotation:** *Resource* speaks to its utility—only when there is unvirtue does one recognize the utility of virtue.

**Explication:** All paths of utility can be transformed into wealth or used as bribes, but *resource* refers to secondary utility. Thus, *resource* speaks to utility, showing that when virtue is evident, one must preserve it, and when unvirtue appears, one must reflect upon it. Only then, with the existence of the unvirtuous, does the function and utility of the virtuous become apparent—this is why the unvirtuous serve as the resource of the virtuous.

Confucius said: *"When three men walk together, there is surely one who can be my teacher. I select his good qualities and follow them; I select his bad qualities and change them."* This is the same meaning.

Yet the Sage does not prize his teacher nor cherish his resource.
**Huizong’s Annotation:** When all under Heaven know virtue as virtue, then unvirtue already exists. Virtue and unvirtue are both forgotten—there is no room for deliberate intent, so what is there to prize or cherish? This is how the Sage achieves great harmony with all things.

**Explication:** In discussing the nature of things, virtue and unvirtue are unified. From the perspective of ordinary people, there is virtue, with unvirtue as its opposite. From the Sage’s perspective, both virtue and unvirtue are embraced—his heart is empty, with no preference or rejection. What is there to prize in the teacher of the unvirtuous? What is there to cherish in the resource of the virtuous? They are indistinct, forgotten, and transformed in the Dao.

Knowing that the distinction between virtue and evil arises from delusion, why would one cling to fine distinctions and generate the heart of prizing or cherishing?

Great harmony with all things is seen in the Sage.
Even the wise are utterly confused—this is called *the essential mystery* (要妙).
**Huizong’s Annotation:** The essential mystery of the Dao lies in not perceiving the multitude of virtues, having no use for wisdom. The Seven Sages were all confused, with no path to inquire about. The meaning aligns here.

**Explication:** Virtue is a deviation from the Dao; wisdom arises from the Dao’s manifestation. The essential mystery of the Dao merges virtue and evil into unity, so one does not perceive the multitude of virtues to seek them with wisdom, nor does one have any use for wisdom. Difficult to exhaust, difficult to fathom, difficult to measure or recognize—this is not the realm of cleverness or daring. This is why they are utterly confused.

In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor was about to meet the Great Spirit (大魄) at Mount Jutzi (具茨之山), arriving at the wilds of Xiangcheng (襄城之野), where the followers of Fangming and Huaji (方明滑稽之徒) led the Seven Sages astray, leaving them utterly lost, with no path to ask. The Great Spirit is a metaphor for the essential mystery of the Dao; the Seven Sages are a metaphor for those not free from using wisdom.

The Seven Sages were all confused, with no path to ask—this demonstrates that the essential mystery of the Dao cannot be known through wisdom. The writings of Zhuang Zhou complement those of Laozi, evident here.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight: Knowing the Heroic**
Know the heroic, yet hold to the yielding—
Thus you become the valley of all under Heaven.
As the valley of all under Heaven,
Your constant virtue never departs,
Returning to the infant.
**Huizong’s Annotation:** The heroic is the strong overcoming things; the yielding is gentle and still. The Sage’s wisdom knows how to overcome things, yet he himself dwells in gentleness and stillness. All things come to him, drawing from him without depletion—thus he is the valley of all under Heaven.

The valley is low, and flowing water rushes toward it—
This is to hold without force, yet sustain with humility,
So that virtue and nature remain forever united—
This is called *constant virtue*, returning to the infant.
Qi is harmonious yet not violent;
Nature is pure yet undispersed—
This is the infant.
Mencius said: *"The noble person does not lose the heart of the infant."*

**Explication:** The heroic is the yang, firm in body and riding upon things, thus able to overcome them with strength. The yielding is the yin, gentle in body and receiving things, thus only gentle and still.

The Sage’s wisdom can awe the world and move the heavens and earth—this is knowing how to overcome things, which is called *knowing the heroic*.

Though firm in strength, he must yet be hidden and abide in gentleness;
Though active in motion, he must yet be profound and abide in stillness—
Thus he is able to preserve the yielding.

Only by abiding in gentleness can he never be severed from things;
Only by abiding in stillness can he suffice to respond to all movement.
When he gives to others as he does to himself, his virtue overflows abundantly,
Never exhausted—
This is how he becomes the valley of all under Heaven.

Moreover, the earth does not fill the east and south,
So all rivers and floods return to it—
Thus the valley, being low, is rightly where flowing water rushes.

The Sage preserves the yielding with gentleness and stillness,
Receiving all things without refusal.

Though the heroic involves firmness and overcoming with strength,
And the yielding involves humility and overcoming with gentleness—
The Sage, when firm, remains submerged and hidden,
Thus he does not employ strength;
When yielding, he establishes his foundation,
Thus he sustains it with humility.

Not employing strength yet sustaining with humility—
This is truly knowing the heroic while preserving the yielding.

Virtue arises from nature, originally never separated—
If one leans solely toward the heroic, harmony is consumed;
If one leans solely toward the yielding, there is nothing to establish.
Only by knowing the heroic while possessing firmness,
And preserving the yielding while completing with gentleness—
Can one achieve the refinement of harmony,
Filling all that is natural,
And thus the Sage’s virtue is complete—
What difference is there between him and the infant?

For the infant’s qi is focused and his will is singular,
Thus he is harmonious yet not violent;
His desires and thoughts are not yet fully formed,
Thus he is pure yet undispersed—
Only when virtue and nature are forever united
Does he return to the infant.

As Mencius said: *"The noble person does not lose the heart of the infant."*

Knowing the bright, guarding the dark—
This is the model for all under Heaven.

To be the model for all under Heaven,
His constant virtue never errs,
Returning to the Boundless.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
Brightness symbolizes virtue manifesting;
Darkness symbolizes the Way returning.
The Sage reveals his luminous virtue,
Yet silently unites with the Way—
Without flaw, he is the standard for all under Heaven.

His joy is extended without exhaustion,
Thus he is the model for all under Heaven.

He is upright yet not arbitrary,
Trustworthy as the four seasons,
Never deviating—
Such as this, difficult to exhaust, without end,
Never reaching an extreme—
Thus it is said: *constant virtue never errs, returning to the Boundless.*

The *Book of Documents*, in *The Grand Pivot*, speaks of the Kingly Way:
*"Return to its having an extreme."*
Laozi speaks of being the model for all under Heaven:
*"Return to the Boundless."*

Extreme means *the central way*.
Having an extreme refers to virtue manifesting in deeds,
Where centrality is the utmost.
The Boundless refers to virtue returning to the Way,
Which cannot be attained.

**Explication:**
Brightness is the center of yin, corresponding to the west,
The place where all treasures are completed—
Thus it symbolizes virtue manifesting.
Darkness explores its origin, corresponding to the north,
The time when all things return to their root—
Thus it symbolizes the Way returning.

The Sage emits the radiance of Heaven,
Illuminating the lower lands—
Thus his revealed virtue is the singular nature—
This is knowing the bright.
Retreating and hiding in seclusion,
Not revealing his essence—
Thus his silent unity is rooted in the Way—
This is guarding the dark.

With virtue and the Way fully complete,
Without flaw,
He is the standard for all under Heaven—
His example is followed, and all under Heaven conforms.
Without thought, all submit;
His joy is extended in his time,
His abundant virtue is unforgettable,
Not exhausted by the ages—
Thus he is the model for all under Heaven.

Only by being the model for all under Heaven
Does his uprightness remain unchanging and unshifting,
His timing follow naturally and inevitably.

Upright yet never erring,
He never acts arbitrarily—
Not expecting as he does,
The four seasons’ virtue reaches this—
What deviation could there be?

Such as this, the origin of true essence aligns with Heaven’s order—
Who can fathom its end?
Who can exhaust its limits?

Truly, all transformations have no end—
This is why it is said: *constant virtue never errs.*

Although the *Book of Documents*, in *The Grand Pivot*, speaks of the Kingly Way:
*"Return to its having an extreme,"*
And Laozi speaks of being the model for all under Heaven:
*"Return to the Boundless,"*
Why is this so?

The *Grand Pivot* was composed by Ji Zi to elucidate the subtleties of the Way,
While the *Dao De Jing* was written by Laozi to reveal the Way’s brilliance.

To elucidate the Way is to take the central harmony as the foundation,
Thus it is said: *having an extreme.*
For virtue manifesting in deeds,
Centrality is the utmost.

To reveal the Way is to take the utmost clarity as the foundation,
Thus it is said: *the Boundless.*
For virtue returning to the Way,
It cannot be attained.

Extreme means *the central way*,
Like the ridgepole of a house, where all beams converge,
Like the celestial pole, where all stars gather.
Whether present or absent,
Each has its proper place.

Knowing the honored, guarding the humble—
This is the valley of all under Heaven.

To be the valley of all under Heaven,
His constant virtue is complete,
Returning to the Uncarved.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
Beyond nature and life, there is nothing but things.
Common people regard gain as honored, loss as humble,
Abundance as honored, restraint as humble.
Only the Sage can view honor and humiliation equally,
Without preference or rejection.

Yet he does not aspire to extravagance,
But takes restraint as his measure—
This is emptiness alone,
Thus he is the valley of all under Heaven.

The valley is empty yet able to receive,
Responding without storing—
When virtue reaches this, it is complete and exhaustive,
Nothing more can be added—
Thus it is said: *constant virtue is complete.*

The Uncarved is the whole of the Way;
Returning to the Uncarved is to fully embody the Way.

The solitary and the orphaned do not fill the valley,
Yet kings and dukes consider themselves worthy—
Thus, embracing the Uncarved, all under Heaven becomes guest.

**Explication:**
One nature, concentrated and still—
Pure emptiness alone.
Beyond the self, nothing is not false—
Thus all beyond nature’s division is things.

Yet common people rejoice as if startled when they gain,
And grieve as if startled when they lose—
This is regarding gain as honored, loss as humble.

Some indulge in slander through wealth and nobility,
Others perish in poverty and lowliness—
This is regarding abundance as honored, restraint as humble.

The Sage knows that when things come, they cannot be rejected,
so he does not regard gain as honored,
and when they depart, they cannot be stopped,
so he does not deem loss as humiliating.

Honor and shame are seen equally, with no inclination to take or discard—
dwelling within the Uncarved, yet not aspiring to the adorned.
Within, he preserves the undisturbed,
and regards restraint as his standard,
attaining the utmost emptiness of the world,
yet without a single particle of perturbation—
thus he becomes the world’s valley.

Indeed, the valley as a thing
is empty yet capable of receiving,
receives yet does not hoard,
is empty yet capable of responding,
responds yet is never exhausted.

In his own virtue/power,
its emptiness is thus—
so he can respond to the world’s myriad fullness.
Nothing more can be added—
thus it is said: *constant virtue is complete.*

Within emptiness and stillness,
there exists a formless unity—
the essence of the Dao/Way.

Wood as the Uncarved,
before it is shaped into vessels,
its substance remains whole.

The Dao’s whole essence lies here—
if one can return to the Uncarved,
then he understands the Great Origin and Great Root,
and thus fully embodies the Dao/Way.

Though the solitary and orphaned lack the world’s nourishment,
which common people detest,
while kings and dukes esteem their own nobility—
all recognize the Dao/Way’s root in what is honored.

Embracing the Uncarved, one attains the Dao/Way;
those who attain the Dao/Way receive abundant assistance—
thus the world becomes their guest.
Embracing the Uncarved, the world becomes their guest—
as the Classic says: *The Uncarved though small, none under Heaven can subdue it.*
If lords and kings can guard it,
all things will naturally become their guests.

The Uncarved, once shaped, becomes vessels.
**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
That which transcends form is called the Dao/Way;
that which is beneath form is called vessels.
There are forms and names to distinguish,
and divisions to guard—
the Dao/Way is whole,
Heaven and man merge as one;
vessels are shaped,
Heaven and man separate as two.

**Explication:**
That which surpasses the Great Ultimate is the Dao/Way,
so it is called that which transcends form.
That which remains within the square and round is vessels,
so it is called that which is beneath form.
The Dao/Way and vessels
have forms that can be named,
and divisions that can be guarded.
Speaking of the Dao/Way itself,
the Great Changes are yet undivided,
and formless unity exists—
thus it is whole,
Heaven and man merge as one.
Speaking of vessels,
the Great Uncarved has already been shaped,
hidden and revealed are divided—
thus they are shaped,
Heaven and man separate as two.

The Sage applies it,
and thus becomes the ruler.
**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
The Dao’s wholeness, the Sage uses to govern himself.
The Dao’s shaping, the Sage uses to govern the world.

There are forms to be named,
and divisions to be guarded—
thus he assigns duties and leads subjects,
and the world is ordered.
This is called the ruler.

*The Classic of Changes* says:
*Know the subtle and the manifest,*
*know the yielding and the firm.*
The expectations of ten thousand men,
share the same meaning.

**Explication:**
The Dao’s truth is used to govern oneself—
as it is said: *The Dao’s wholeness, the Sage uses to govern himself.*
The Dao’s practical applications are used to govern the world—
as it is said: *The Dao’s shaping, the Sage uses to govern the world.*

Only by shaping the Dao to govern the world,
does one who lifts and places have divisions and standards to guard;
its forms are visible,
and its names can be spoken.
With forms and names,
its ability can be employed,
and its materials can be entrusted.
With forms to be named,
and divisions to be guarded—
thus duties are assigned and subjects led,
and the world is ordered.

Zhuangzi’s discourse on the great Dao/Way’s sequence
says that when virtue/power is clear,
then divisions and standards follow,
and when divisions and standards are clear,
then forms and names follow—
continuing to *necessarily divide its names,*
*necessarily follow its names*—
this is called the utmost attainment of *Great Peace and order.*
The meaning aligns with this.

The Sage applies it,
and thus becomes the ruler—
this is evident here.

*The Classic of Changes* speaks of knowing the subtle and the manifest,
knowing the yielding and the firm—
its virtue/power is fully complete,
virtue and righteousness both attained,
sufficient to be the expectations of ten thousand men—
is this not the meaning of the ruler?

Thus, the great measures do not cut.
**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
Transforming and adapting,
lies in change—
carving myriad forms,
yet not for craftiness.

**Explication:**
Following form and altering it is called transformation.
Severing form abruptly is called change.
From transformation to change,
this is the Dao’s sequence.
The Sage knows the Dao of transformation and change,
and presages change—
he shapes things yet is not shaped by them,
as *The Classic of Changes* says: *Transforming and adapting, lies in change.*

Though he shapes things,
he does not distinguish them with rigid boundaries—
carving myriad forms,
yet not for craftiness—
this is called *square yet not cutting.*
It is not that the great measures cannot reach this point.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine: *The Way of the World’s Mastery***
He who would take the world and act upon it,
I see him as one who cannot help but fail.
**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
The world is a great thing.
Having great things,
one cannot be a thing—
being a thing yet not a thing,
thus one can shape things.
Understanding that the shaper of things is not a thing,
and having no desire to take the world,
one can entrust the world to others.

He who would take the world and act upon it,
uses wisdom and relies on strength—
far from attaining it.
Thus the Sage follows Dao/Way’s transformation,
not extolling wisdom and strength—
Qin failed through force,
perhaps this is what is meant.

**Explication:**
All that has appearance, sound, or color,
are things.
What is the distance between things and things?
Though the world is vast,
it can be called a great thing.

Yet without understanding the Dao/Way,
even with great things,
one cannot shape things.
Only when one is unified with the Dao/Way,
and not shaped by things,
can one govern all things,
command the myriad movements,
and shape the world’s things.

Indeed, life is sustained by movement,
none can escape being shaped—
there must be one who is not constrained by things,
yet can shape things.
The shaper of things is the Dao/Way.
If one understands that the Dao/Way is not a thing,
then the universe lies within his grasp,
and he can entrust the world to others—
capable of bearing the world’s responsibility.

「Zhuangzi speaks of the *great thing* (大物), which cannot be a *thing* (物). By *thinging* (物) it and thus concluding it, one may realize that the *thinger* (物物者) is not a *thing* (物). Is this Dao/Way merely for governing the world (治天下)? It is precisely this meaning.

Yet the world’s *great instruments* (大器) are not to be wielded—one cannot govern the world without understanding the Dao/Way. To seize the world and act upon it is to rely on *intelligence* (智) and *strength* (力). To rely on *intelligence* is to invite *constraint* (困), and to rely on *strength* is to invite *exhaustion* (殆). This is far from the Dao/Way.

Thus, the Sage *entrusts* (任) the Dao/Way to transform (化) and does not *exalt* (尚) *intelligence* or *strength*—for the Dao/Way transforms without being constrained by *things*, while *intelligence* and *strength* are themselves constrained by *things*.

Only by not being a *thing* can one *thing* all *things*—this is why the Sage *entrusts* the Dao/Way to transform and does not *exalt* *intelligence* or *strength*.

The *Qin* of *Ying* (嬴秦) merely *exalts* *intelligence* and *strength*—how could this suffice to govern the world? Therefore, the ancients said that *Qin* lost through its *forcefulness* (强). Yang Xiong called *Qin* a *wolf* (狼), and this too is because of this.

The world’s *divine instrument* (神器):
**Huizong’s Annotation (徽宗注):** *Bound by form and number* (制於形数), *constrained by limits* (囿於方体), and *confined within the dual span of heaven and earth* (域於覆载之两问), this is what is called an *instrument* (器).
*Established in the unfathomable* (立乎不测), *acting without fixed boundaries* (行乎无方), those who *wield* it fail, and those who *cling* to it lose. Hence, it is called the *divine instrument*.

**Explication (疏义):** If one calls it the *Six Unities* (六合), then it is *bound by form and number*. If one calls it the *Universe* (宇宙), then it is *constrained by limits*. Though it spans the *dual realms of heaven and earth* (覆载之两间), it is not the *heaven and earth* themselves—thus, though vast, it is still an *instrument*.

Yet to *establish* it in the unfathomable without seeing its *advancement* (进), or to *act* without fixed boundaries yet never *encountering limits* (封), to *wield* it without knowing *non-action* (无为), then one must fail. To *cling* to it without knowing to *follow the times* (趋时), then one must lose. Though it has not departed from being an *instrument*, its *instrumentality* (为器) is indeed divine. Yet it cannot be *wielded*.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** To govern all *things* and command the myriad movements, there must be one who is not an *instrument*—only then can the world be governed. Hence it is said: *"The Sage must practice non-action yet employ the world."*

**Explication:** *Things* have *form*, then they can be named; they have *division*, then they can be guarded—this is what is called an *instrument*. There must be one who is not an *instrument*, only then can they *operate* their *instruments*.

The one who is not an *instrument* is the one who is not constrained by *things*—only by not being constrained by *things* can myriad *things* though numerous, and movements though diverse, all remain within the bounds of *prevention and guidance* (防范). Though they do not *intend* to govern and command, they naturally govern all *things* and command the myriad movements.

All who understand that the *thinger* (物物者) is not a *thing* (物) can thus achieve *non-action* (无为) yet fulfill the world’s tasks (功). Therefore, though they do not *govern* the world, the world is naturally governed.

The *Classic* says: *"The Dao/Way is ever non-acting yet nothing it does not act upon. If rulers could guard this, all things would naturally transform."* Zhuangzi says: *"The Sage must practice non-action yet employ the world."*

Those who face south and rule the world, if they *cling to deeds* (事为) and their *traces* (迹), yet do not know to take *non-action* as constant, to *pursue the bounded* (有涯) while abandoning the *boundless* (元涯), then their *intelligence* will be constrained—how could they employ the world and transform all *things*?

Hence Zhuangzi says: *"Excessive knowledge leads to defeat."* Those who *wield* meet with *defeat*.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** One who can *wield* yet cannot *non-act* will have their *intelligence* constrained.

Zhuangzi says: *"Excessive knowledge leads to defeat."*

**Explication:** To *enter* and *distinguish* with *things* is to differ; to *unify* is to practice *non-action*. To *exit* and *interact* with *things* is to *discriminate*—one cannot avoid *action*.

If one cannot avoid *action*, then they will inevitably encounter *opposition* (戾)—how could they avoid *defeat*?

Only by taking *non-action* as the *root* (本) and *action* as the *branches* (末), by *non-acting* yet *acting* without fail, can one *respond to things* with *abundance* (裕) and not *exhaust* their *intelligence*—who could be constrained?

Guangchengzi says: *"Excessive knowledge leads to defeat,"* meaning that by *having no knowledge* (无知), one can *know nothing* (无不知)—what *defeat* is there?

Those who *cling* will *lose*—
**Huizong’s Annotation:** The Dao/Way’s most precious virtue is *timeliness* (时). To *cling* without transforming is to lose *timeliness*—this is called *contrary to the Dao*.

**Explication:** *Timeliness* cannot be *stopped*, the Dao/Way cannot be *blocked*—*timeliness* shifts without pause, the Dao/Way adapts to change, hence the Dao/Way’s most precious virtue is *timeliness*.

*Things* have no fixed *appropriateness* (宜)—*appropriateness* lies in *following the times*. If one *clings* without *losing*, if they are *stuck in one narrow view* (泥於一曲), then to *lose* is to *exceed*, and to *follow* is to *fall short*—how could they avoid *loss*?

Only by *transforming with the times* (与时俱化) and *having no fixed action* (无有专为) can one neither *lose timeliness* nor *contrary to the Dao*.

The *Yijing* says: *"The meaning of following the times is vast indeed."*

Hence, *things* either *move* (行) or *follow* (随), *inhale* (嘘) or *exhale* (吹), *strengthen* (强) or *weaken* (赢), *bear fruit* (载) or *wither* (隳).

**Huizong’s Annotation:** The *principles* (理) of myriad *things*: some *move*, some *follow*—like the *eclipse* (往来) of sun and moon. Some *inhale*, some *exhale*—like the *cycles* (相代) of the four seasons. Some *strengthen*, some *weaken*—like the *ascendancy* (王) and *decline* (废) of the five phases. Some *bear fruit*, some *wither*—like the *growth* (开) and *fall* (落) of grasses and trees.

*Serving the times* (役于时) yet *bound by numbers* (制于数), inevitably one cannot escape *burden*—only the Sage can avoid being burdened by *things* and stand alone above all *things*.

*Going alone, coming alone*—this is called *having alone* (独有). The one who *has alone* is called the *most noble* (至贵).

Thus, *operating* the *divine instrument* leaves *abundance*—though *things* vary in their *states*, the Sage’s *heart* remains ever unified.

**Explication:** When it is said to *go*, the moon comes; sun and moon alternate in their *illumination*—never ceasing, like the *principles* of *things* that either *move* or *follow*. Spring comes first, summer follows—seasons cycle without end, like the *principles* of *things* that either *inhale* or *exhale*.

When wood *strengthens*, water *weakens*—when fire *arises*, metal is *bound*—like the *principles* of *things* that either *strengthen* or *weaken*, as in the *ascendancy* and *decline* of the five phases. When grain *dies*, wheat *grows*—when trees *fall*, the *millet* (鞠) *blossoms*—like the *principles* of *things* that either *bear fruit* or *wither*, as in the *growth* and *fall* of grasses and trees.

From *moving* and *following* to *bearing fruit* and *withering*, all are *served by timeliness*—thus they *end with timeliness*, *bound by numbers*—thus they *exhaust with numbers*. This is why they *cycle endlessly* yet cannot escape *burden*.」

The Sage who transcends time and does not end with it,
who departs from numbers and does not exhaust with them—
what burden could there be?

The Sage understands this:
embodying the *Way*’s *non-being*, thus not burdened by *things*,
embodying the *Way*’s *virtue*, thus standing alone above all *things*.

The *Way* within me—*things* cannot pair with it.
Thus, when moving, there is no cause to move,
and so it is *solely moving*.
Thus, when coming, there is no source from which it comes,
and so it is *solely coming*.

Without bestowal, yet ever spontaneous—
this may be called the utmost nobility.

The subtlety of *non-being* lies here—
how could the *doer* fail or the *grasper* lose?

Thus, the Sage wields the *spiritual instrument* with ease.

The Sage’s heart transcends *being* and *non-being*,
unburdened by *things*—
so when *things* shift and intertwine, my *principles* do not alter.
This is why, though *things* vary in form, the heart remains one.

Therefore, the Sage rejects excess, rejects extravagance, rejects excessiveness.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
The Sage perceives the transformations of all *things*,
knows that fullness brings manifold burdens,
and so does not augment life,
does not indulge nature,
does not reach extremes,
but plays within the *measure* alone.

If so, how could the *doer* be bound or the *grasper* err?

Thus it is said:
Elaborate rituals and superficial adornments bring no benefit to governance.

**Explanatory Notes:**
*Things* flourish then decay—
thus there is change.
Fullness invites loss—
thus there are burdens.

The Sage knows that *moving* and *following*,
*inhale* and *exhale*,
*strengthen* and *weaken*,
*bear fruit* and *wither*—
these cycles do not cease.
Thus, perceiving the transformations of all *things*,
knowing the burdens of fullness,
the Sage follows their spontaneity without augmentation,
takes *moderation* as standard without excess,
preserves internally and externally without sway,
and does not indulge nature—
this is called *playing within the measure*.

To *play within the measure* is to walk in the *Unnamed*.
The *Unnamed*—this is the *Way*.
To walk in the *Way*—
how could there be the afflictions of excess or extravagance?

Guarding the source of nature,
harmonizing with the *Way* in its turns,
governing the world—
embodying *non-action*,
thus no failure in *non-action*,
aligning with the utmost transformation,
thus no loss in *grasping*—

Those who indulge in elaborate rituals and superficial adornments
are yet burdened by the afflictions of excess and extravagance—
how could they benefit governance?

**End of *Explanations on the Dao De Jing*, Volume Six**
**End of *Explanations on the Dao De Jing*, Volume Seven**

**Commentary on the *Dao De Jing* by Jiang Cheng, Student of the Imperial Academy**
**Chapter Thirty: Assisting the Sovereign with the Dao**

To assist the sovereign with the Dao is to **not** subdue the world with arms.

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
The deployment of the three armies and five weapons is the *end* of virtue.
The *end* refers to what ancient sages possessed but did not prioritize.

To assist the sovereign with the Dao is to focus on the *root* alone, hence one does not subdue the world with arms.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
Zhuangzi spoke of five *ends* in governance, and the deployment of the three armies and five weapons is the *end* of virtue. The *root* is the cultivation of spirit, heart, and method.

Arms cannot be abandoned, yet the five *ends* were possessed by the ancients.
The virtuous focus on the *root*, thus they did not prioritize them. To assist the sovereign with the Dao is to excel as a virtuous one—how could one drive the people to the brink of death and life, using coercion to subdue the world?

In days past, King Hui of Liang wished to avenge his humiliation by offering a single cup of wine to the dead of Chu. Mencius taught him that the virtuous have no rival—yet this was not by subduing the world with arms. This is the principle of *good returns*.

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
This is what Mencius meant by *"return unto yourself."*

**Explanatory Commentary:**
If one acts with goodness, others will respond in kind. If one acts with harm, others will respond likewise. This is the law of *return unto oneself*. If one takes delight in warfare and killing, what manner of retribution will they receive? Hence, *good returns*.

Where the teacher treads, brambles grow.
After great armies, famine follows.

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
The lower ranks are drained of strength, hence brambles grow.
The ruler defies the natural order, hence there are years of scarcity.
The *Odes* say:
*"To pacify the myriad states,
And reap abundant harvests."*

To pacify the myriad states is to bring harmony to the people.
When the people are harmonious, the harmony of Heaven and Earth responds in kind.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
Plowing without recklessness, weeding without destruction—this is the exertion of human effort.
Snow falls abundantly, rain nourishes the roots—this is the favor of Heaven’s timing.
Warfare is not the means to strengthen the land’s power.
To the point of draining the people’s strength, hence brambles grow.
Warfare is not the means to summon harmonious *qi*.
To the point of defying Heaven’s timing, hence there are years of scarcity.

The *Odes* say:
*"To pacify the myriad states,
And reap abundant harvests."*

To pacify the myriad states is to bring harmony to the people.
When the people are in harmony, they align with Heaven and Earth in one *qi*—
thus, when the people are harmonious, the harmony of Heaven and Earth responds.

This is a martial poem, differing from Laozi’s teachings,
to illustrate how the Sage’s intent surpasses the martial ambitions of men.
Thus, the good seek fulfillment alone,
and dare not claim strength through conquest.

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
To seek what is right in action, to achieve completion in deeds,
to expend little effort yet see great results—
this is the Dao of the Sage.
To conquer through force is the mark of a cruel virtue,
hence victory in war lies in harmony, not in numbers.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
Though undertakings differ, all seek what is right.
Though achievements differ, all seek completion.

Yet there are those who labor tirelessly yet see few results—
they do not grasp the root and the essential.

The Sage’s Dao is the foundation of the world,
and the key to governance lies here.
Thus, though effort is little, results are great.

As for displaying martial might and conquering by force,
how can this be deemed a virtuous path?

The ancient kings had ways of constant victory,
yet some turned left and right in retreat,
their campaigns failing without success—
they did not understand the Sage’s Dao.

The Sage’s Dao lies in harmonizing the people,
uniting their hearts and efforts,
so that no task is impossible,
no achievement unfulfilled,
and they stand unchallenged under Heaven—
as Leftist Master said: *"Victory lies in harmony, not in numbers."*

Mencius said: *"Geographical advantage is not as vital as the unity of the people."*
Gou Qing said: *"If the people and officers are not united,
even the likes of Tang and Wu could not triumph."*

Thus, true victory lies in harmony, not in numbers.

When success is achieved, do not boast,
do not claim glory,
do not grow arrogant,
do not act out of compulsion,
and do not force outcomes.

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
Among those who act only when compelled,
the Sage’s Dao ensures victory through moderation and righteousness,
without blame—
success is sought, and that is all.

Boasting invites decline,
self-praise yields no merit,
arrogance invites contempt.
These four pitfalls must be avoided
to achieve success without coercion.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
The Sage deliberates before acting,
and when success is attained,
it is as if responding to the Mandate of Heaven—
how could this be otherwise?
Much less so in war.

Victory through moderation brings good fortune,
righteousness brings no blame—
like the *Hexagram Nine-Two* of the *Yijing*.
To act in accordance with the natural order,
to move cautiously yet decisively,
to hunt like a skilled archer—
this is *"victory through moderation."*

To be yielding yet upright,
to guide the many with righteousness,
to speak with conviction without blame—
this is *"righteousness without blame."*

Yet the Sage does not wage war recklessly,
nor indulge in unquenchable martial ambition.
When compelled, resistance arises—
but self-boasting brings no growth,
self-praise yields no true merit,
arrogance invites disdain.
How could this be the way of the virtuous victor?

For killing the enemy is but one aspect of success—
if one achieves it without arrogance,
none dare contest their might.
If one achieves it without claiming glory,
none dare dispute their deeds.
If one achieves it without arrogance,
their victory endures unchallenged.
Thus, their achievements stand unrivaled in the world.

**On the decline of strength:**
*"Things flourish and then decay."*

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
The cycles of growth and decline are evident in summer’s length and autumn’s withering.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
Where there is spring’s nourishment and summer’s growth,
there must be autumn’s austerity and winter’s death—
the Great Transformation moves subtly.
Who can perceive its workings?
*"Things flourish and then decay"*—
this is plain to see.

This is called *"not the Dao."*

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
The Dao has no beginning or end,
and does not align with the transformations of things.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
Its beginning has no origin—
who can trace its source?
Its end has no conclusion—
who can define its limit?
From antiquity, it has always existed,
yet it transcends all transformations.
Thus, the Dao does not conform to the changes of things.
The Dao and things are fundamentally apart—
hence, *"things flourish and then decay"* is called *"not the Dao."*

*"Not the Dao"* is early decline.

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
To exist outside the Dao is to invite the contrast of flourishing and decay.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
Bound within the Great Transformation,
rigidity becomes strength,
decay becomes weakness—
all things follow this pattern,
for they exist outside the Dao.
The Dao, enduring from ancient times,
knows no decay or vigor—
how could it follow such cycles?

---
**On martial prowess (Chapter Thirty-One):**
*"Fine weapons are ominous instruments—*
things naturally abhor them,
so those who follow the Dao do not dwell upon them."*

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
Auspicious events bring omens,
but weapons are instruments of misfortune—
hence, *"ominous."*
Weapons are stored yet used in due measure,
and those who follow the Dao display virtue rather than martial might—
thus, they do not dwell upon them.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
All phenomena arise from some origin,
and auspicious signs precede blessings—
this is the natural order.
When blessings manifest in things, they are called *"omens."*
Thus, the *Yijing* states that auspicious events bring omens.
But weapons are instruments of misfortune—
what auspicious sign could precede them?
Hence, they are *"ominous."*

Still, reckless warfare is akin to sacrilege,
and though the ancient kings did not abandon it,
those who conceal their virtue do not flaunt their might,
and those who wage war without restraint leave no legacy—
thus, they do not display their armies.

As the *Commentaries* say: *"Weapons are stored yet used in due measure"*—
this refers to such conduct.

King Wu stored his arms and discarded his bows,
seeking virtuous governance to restore the Xia—
this was displaying virtue without martial show.

Moreover, martial prowess is a hidden virtue,
concealed like a crown or a wrapped foot—
it is advanced like a footstep, yet unused.
Thus, those who follow the Dao do not dwell upon it.

Hence, the gentleman values the left in peace,
but the right in war.

Weapons are ominous instruments,
not the tools of the gentleman.

**Emperor Huizong’s Annotation:**
The left embodies *yang*—generative, life-affirming—
the right embodies *yin*—destructive, punitive.
*Yang* is virtue, *yin* is punishment—
the gentleman honors virtue and fears punishment,
thus weapons are *"not the gentleman’s instrument."*

**Explanatory Commentary:**
The Great Dao is vast and unbounded,
yet it has left and right—
thus, the left is *yang*, the right is *yin*.
*Yin* and *yang* are the origins of life and death—
*yang* governs life, *yin* governs death.
Virtue governs life, hence Guanzi says *yang* is virtue,
punishment governs death, hence Guanzi says *yin* is punishment.
When virtue is complete, it ascends—
nothing can diminish it,
thus the gentleman values virtue.
When punishment is to be applied,
music is silenced—
thus the gentleman fears punishment.
Hence, ominous instruments are not the gentleman’s tools.
They are used only when compelled,
with calm resolve as the highest virtue.

Thus, it is not beautiful—
if it were beautiful, one would delight in it,
and delighting in it would mean delighting in killing people.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
To suppress tyranny and quell chaos,
one acts only when forced—
thus, it is unavoidable.
Without the intent to conquer others,
it is said that *tranquility and composure* are supreme.

Without the intent to conquer others,
warfare is not something to delight in,
thus it is not beautiful.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
Warfare is an ominous instrument—
even the sage avoids it,
yet when the sage responds to the world
to secure the people,
the tyrannical cannot but be suppressed,
and when chaos arises,
it cannot but be quelled.
When the barbarian armies come to provoke divine retribution,
warfare is mobilized at the proper time—
one cannot but go.
The deployment of the three armies and five weapons
is the lowest extension of virtue.
The sage employs them—
how could this be his original intent?
It is merely because there is no alternative.

Thus, he habitually dwells in the realm of non-contention,
yet does not dare to act first for the world—
how could he take conquest as his heart’s desire?
It is because *tranquility and composure* are held supreme.
Those who delight in killing people
shall never find fulfillment in the world.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
When the ruler cherishes *ren* (benevolence),
the world has no rival.
To secure what is perilous and profit from what is harmful,
to delight in what leads to ruin—
this is where resentment gathers
and where calamity converges.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
The benevolent have no rivals,
thus when the ruler cherishes *ren*,
the world has no rival.
If this is not understood,
then when drums clash and soldiers leap into battle,
it is as if one secures what is perilous without fear,
profits from what is harmful without caution,
and delights in what leads to ruin without shame—
how could this not invite resentment
and summon calamity?

How could one know that the ruler’s warfare
is rooted in *ren* and *yi* (righteousness),
executed through punishment and retribution,
used only when necessary,
and abandoned when peace returns—
as a means to ensure enduring stability?

For auspicious matters, the left is honored;
for inauspicious matters, the right is honored.
Thus, the *Pianjiangjun* (Vanguard General) is stationed on the left,
and the *Shangjiangjun* (Commander-in-Chief) on the right.
When occupying a position of dominance,
one observes funeral rites.
When many are slain,
one mourns them with sorrowful weeping.
When victorious in battle,
one observes funeral rites.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
The *Yijing* calls warfare *"poison for the world"*—
even if victorious, there will be those afflicted by its effects,
thus those in dominant positions and the victorious
are treated with funeral rites.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
In the *Yijing*’s *"Warfare"* hexagram:
*"With this, you poison the world,
yet the people follow you."*
The ruler’s warfare is meant to shelter the people and gather forces—
though victorious, it is like a physician gathering poisonous medicines to attack illness,
and there will inevitably be those harmed by its effects.
Thus, the *Yijing* calls warfare *"poison for the world,"*
and those in dominant positions and the victorious
are treated with funeral rites.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Two: The Dao Ever Nameless**

**Chapter Thirty-Two: The Dao Ever Nameless**

---

The *Dao* is the origin of heaven and earth—how could it be named?
**Huizong’s Annotation:**
The *Dao* is the origin of heaven and earth—how could it be named?

**Explanatory Commentary:**
The unnameable is the origin of heaven and earth.
The *Dao* is that which gives birth to heaven and earth—
thus it cannot be named.
Though *pu* (crude simplicity) is small,
nothing under heaven can subdue it.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
*Pu* (crude simplicity) is used to illustrate the complete *Dao*.
When the *Dao* manifests as form and name, the great prevails over the small.
The complete *Dao* does not depart from its essence—
small yet discerning, as Zhuangzi said:
*"Within it, the True Lord abides."*

**Explanatory Commentary:**
Zhuangzi said:
*"In harmony with desirelessness, this is called *sùpǔ* (unadulterated simplicity)."*
The *Classic* states:
*"When *pu* (crude simplicity) disperses, it becomes *qì* (vessels)."*
*Pu* illustrates the complete *Dao*—
the great lies above, few yet overcoming all things;
the small lies below, many yet overcome by things.
Only when the *Dao* manifests as form and name does it appear thus.
Yet the complete *Dao* itself does not establish any single thing—
it cannot be grasped, thus it is called *wēi* (subtle).
In perfect unity with essence, it returns to the most profound,
and can be named only as *xiǎo* (small).
This *Dao* does not cling to form,
nor is it confined to number.
The True Lord is sufficient to tower above the world—
not like those bound by square forms and burdened by size,
thus nothing under heaven dares to subdue it.

If rulers and kings could uphold it,
all things would naturally submit as guests.
**Huizong’s Annotation:**
When the *Dao* suffices as the master of all things,
all things will naturally submit as guests.

Zhuangzi said:
*"In unadulterated simplicity, the people’s nature is attained."*
To govern all things without reliance on coercion or punishment,
this is the *Dao*’s limit.

**Explanatory Commentary:**
The *Analects* say:
*"Why should not all follow this *Dao*?"*
Zhuangzi said:
*"That which moves among all things is the *Dao*."*
The *Dao* is like the root of all things,
and all things reverence the *Dao*.
If one can uphold the *Dao*,
then all things submit as guests.
Zhuangzi said:
*"In unadulterated simplicity, the people’s nature is attained."*
*Pu* is the complete *Dao*—
to embody the *Dao* is to attain the people’s nature,
and its meaning aligns precisely with this.

In the *Yijing*, it is said:
*"The sages observe the *shén* (mysterious/numenous) *Dao* in setting their teachings,
and all under heaven submit."*
Then, if one can govern all things,
what need is there for coercion or punishment?

Heaven and earth unite,
and sweet dew descends—
no one commands it,
yet all is balanced.
**Huizong’s Annotation:**
The pure and simple *Dao*,
if upheld without loss,
is not merely that all things submit as guests—
it reaches to the heavens above,
and coils around the earth below.
When the upper and lower flow in harmony with heaven and earth,
then the *Dao*’s circulation brings unity,
and all things share its beneficence.
Sweet dew is the *héqi* (harmonious *qi*) of heaven and earth.

The *Commentary* says:
*"The virtue of the emperor and king
reaches to *Tàiqīng* (Supreme Clarity),
descends to *Tàiníng* (Supreme Tranquility),
and embraces all living things—
then sweet dew descends."*

**Explanatory Commentary:**
Pure means no loss of its spirit,
simple means no admixture with other things.
The pure and simple *Dao* guards only the spirit—
if one can uphold it without loss,
then it merges with the spirit,
and the unity of its precision
harmonizes with the natural order of heaven.
How could it merely submit things?
To unite with heaven above,
then the upper merges with the *yuánhuà* (primordial transformation);
to descend with earth below,
then the lower embraces *hòudé* (profound virtue).
Spirit pervades the four directions—
above, it reaches to heaven;
below, it coils around earth.
When it flows in harmony with heaven and earth,
then the two unite in accord,
and sweet dew descends.
Which thing could fail to share in its beneficence?

The *Húguānzǐ* says:
*"The virtue of the emperor and king
reaches to *Tàiqīng* (Supreme Clarity),
descends to *Tàiníng* (Supreme Tranquility),
and embraces all living things—
then sweet dew descends."*

Heaven’s tranquility is *wúwéi* (non-action),
thus it is called *Tàiqīng* (Supreme Clarity).
To reach *Tàiqīng* is to unite with heaven above.
Earth’s tranquility is *wúwéi* (non-action),
thus it is called *Tàiníng* (Supreme Tranquility).
To descend to *Tàiníng* is to coil with earth below.
Only humans are the living souls of all things—
to embrace *wànlíng* (all living things) is to encompass all things,
thus sweet dew descends.

At first, there was no naming—
once naming was established,
one must also know when to stop.
To know when to stop is to avoid peril.
**Huizong’s Annotation:**
The sequence of the Great *Dao*:
five transformations until form and name can be raised.
When form is raised and named,
the *Dao* descends and virtue decays—
the pure is diluted, *pu* (crude simplicity) is dispersed,
and none can halt it.

The benevolent of the world
cast their eyes downward and grieve over the world’s exhaustion,
while the unvirtuous
prioritize life and death’s desires
and glut themselves on wealth and honor.
The sage is not thus.
When naming first arises,
he stands alone, his form unyielding—
how could he follow things in their transformations?
Serene and self-sufficient, dwelling with spirit and clarity—
how could this not suffice as rest?
Rather, he halts it,
and firmly grasps it.
To halt within flow is to move while remaining true—
if so, who could endanger him?

The reason why it is not in peril thus.

Compare the *Dao* in the world
to the streams and valleys uniting with the rivers and seas.

*Xuanzong* annotated: "The world, a single nature.
The *Dao* in the world, harmonizing through nature,
is like the streams and valleys uniting with the rivers and seas—
gathered by water, those alike are nourished,
those of kindred nature respond.
When the sage governs with this,
what need is there for action?
It is merely to follow nature."

*Shuyi* elaborated: "Life differs in form, yet all share one nature.
For all that live, this nature exists—
thus, the world has a single nature.
The *Dao*'s complete essence
does not depart from nature;
the sage attains its purity,
so all who possess nature
are united by it,
just as the rivers and seas
are good at descending
and all streams and torrents flow into them.
Gathered by water,
those alike are nourished,
those of kindred nature respond—
when the sage governs,
resting with hands folded,
the world is in harmony.
What need is there for action?
It is merely to transform the people
by following their nature."

Knowing people is wisdom—*Zhizhe* (Knowing People) Chapter 33.

「Huizong’s annotation states: The *Commentary* says, “Wisdom is like the eye—able to see a hundred paces away, yet unable to see its own eyelashes.” To discern the deceitful or upright in others, as one distinguishes white from black, is the work of wisdom—knowing people is all.

*Shuyi* further expounds: “Gazing upon the minute hairs of a moth in autumn, these are the most subtle of things; even at a distance of a hundred paces, a keen-eyed observer can still perceive them. Yet the eyelashes of a man are close at hand—even if one were to summon the legendary *Zhujia* (離朱, "the far-seeing") to squint and scrutinize them in broad daylight, he would not see them. So too is human wisdom: one can discern others, yet cannot truly know oneself. This is what *Han Fei* records of *Duzi* (杜子, "Master Du")—that wisdom is like the eye, able to see a hundred paces away, yet unable to see its own eyelashes.

*Mengzi* (Mencius) says: The faculty of distinguishing right from wrong is the beginning of wisdom.
*Zuozi* (苟子, "Master Zuo") says: To affirm what is right and deny what is wrong is what we call wisdom. To discern the deceitful or upright in others, as one distinguishes white from black, is to affirm what is right and deny what is wrong. Thus, wisdom as the eye can see what is external yet cannot self-examine—it can only know others.

But self-knowledge is clarity.

Huizong’s annotation states: The *Yijing* (易經, *Classic of Changes*) says, “To recover is to know oneself.” The *Commentary* says: “To inwardly gaze is what we call clarity.” Wisdom, in knowing others, becomes entangled in interaction—this is called *xinzhou* (心鬥, "mental contention"), or using one’s mind to vie with others. Yet to recover and know oneself is to remain still and return to the root—seeing oneself is all. It is the balance of heaven and earth, the illumination of all things.

*Shuyi* further expounds: To recover the subtle and discern in things, to return to the root and be still—stillness brings clarity, and there is nothing it does not penetrate. Hence, the *Yijing* says, “To recover is to know oneself.” To inwardly gaze is to see without attachment, to see others yet truly see oneself—thus, the *Commentary* says, “To inwardly gaze is what we call clarity.”

To employ wisdom in discerning the deceitful or upright in others is to uphold what is right and reject what is wrong, to look around and have all things respond—this is to engage in *xinzhou*, using an empty, unified, and tranquil mind to daily judge. The rectification of right and wrong is thus the work of knowing others, no more.

But to recover and know oneself is to inwardly observe the single nature, to remain still and return to the root, to see what others do not see and to perceive the unseen forms—thus attaining what is called *jianjian* (見見, "true seeing"). Is there anything greater than this in heaven and earth? To see is to attain its balance.

The myriad things are vast—is there anything greater than this? **Form** is their illumination.

If such is the case, then what we call *clarity* truly attains completion? Those who excel over others possess *power*, but those who conquer themselves are *strong*.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The Sage values virtue/power over force, striving to conquer oneself rather than others."* The wise deceive the foolish, the brave endure the cowardly—this is excelling over others, relying on *power* alone. To conquer one’s own selfishness, maintaining rectitude without injury—this is conquering oneself. When one goes forth, standing alone without fear; when dwelling, retreating from the world without discontent—never venturing forth without triumph, this is the source of *strength*.

The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"The foolish cannot be trusted with schemes, so the wise employ their discernment to deceive them. The cowardly cannot stand against adversity, so the brave summon their vigor to endure them—this is the valuing of force to triumph over others. To conquer one’s selfishness with a resolute mind, nurturing non-harm and preserving one’s *qi*—this is the valuing of virtue/power to conquer oneself. Only by conquering oneself does one remain unshaken when external things press upon them, unable to destroy one’s true being. Thus, going forth, one stands alone without fear; dwelling, one retreats from the world without discontent. To stand alone yet fear nothing is to be brave in *righteousness*. To retreat from the world yet feel no discontent is to find peace in *fate*. Whether one goes forth or dwells, never venturing without triumph—this is why they are called *ultimately strong*."*

Those who know sufficiency are rich. The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"To possess myriad differences is what we call richness."* The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"The myriad transformations of existence bear distinct names, yet they do not lose their essence—this is ultimate richness, where the measure of sufficiency resides in one’s self. As Zhuangzi said: 'To possess myriad differences is what we call richness.' Only those who know sufficiency roam within the *boundaries* (of the body), finding sufficiency in themselves. Thus, their head is round, their feet square, and heaven and earth take their place; their chest faces south, their back north, and yin and yang are fully embraced. All things have their limits, yet all are complete—what riches of Jin and Chu could compare? The sufficiency of those who know sufficiency is everlasting, hence the treasures of the realm gather within them."*

Those who act with *strength* possess *will*.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"To persist in self-strengthening is to uphold *will* in the Dao/Way."* The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"To exert oneself in study and inquiry broadens one’s knowledge and sharpens wisdom; to exert oneself in practicing the Dao/Way awakens virtue/power and yields great achievements. Thus, those who strive in action rely on self-strengthening, and self-strengthening is what we call having *will*. The *Daoist Classic on Virtue/Power* says: 'The Superior Sage hears the Dao/Way and moves to act upon it.' Truly, those who uphold *will* in the Dao/Way—hearing the Dao/Way and moving to act—accumulate true strength over time, persisting in self-strengthening without end. Could this be done by those without *will*? Those who do not lose their place endure long; those who conform to time and shift with things can only last temporarily. The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"If one can embody the eternal without change, steadfast in what they guard, they can then let the Dao/Way unfold in action, enduring boundlessly. In the *Book of Changes*, the *Commentary* speaks of the Sage standing firm without shifting direction, and the *Symbol* says: 'The Sage endures long in their Dao/Way.' This is precisely what is meant. If one is confined by time and shifts with things, how could they not lose their place? The aged and the young transform, and one shifts with them—could they truly not lose their place?"*

Those who die yet do not perish are *long-lived*.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Life has its root in growth, death has its return in origin; tracing the beginning and returning to the end, one understands the teachings of death and life."* The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"The Sage comprehends the Dao/Way of day and night, knowing that death is not fundamentally different from life. Thus, their form dissolves, yet their spirit does not perish, enduring alongside heaven and earth without known limit—what else could be called *long-lived*? This chapter’s meaning begins with knowing others to exhaust principles, proceeds to knowing sufficiency to fulfill nature, and concludes with not perishing to attain *fate*—thus, creation dwells within us. Who but those without past or future, entering the realm of neither death nor life, could attain this? The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"All things: life emerges from the One, death returns to the One. Life has its root in growth, death has its end in return. To trace the beginning and know the teachings of death—like Zhuangzi’s words: 'Mingled between the coarse and the fine, it transforms and gives rise to *qi*; *qi* transforms and gives rise to form, now again transforming into death.' To return to the end and know the teachings of life—like Zhuangzi’s words: 'How do I know that the dead do not regret their life? Perhaps their joy lies in the firewood’s first sprouting.' Beginning and end oppose the boundless, yet no one knows where they end—how could death and life be fundamentally different? It is merely the One, threading through all. Day and night alternate, dividing into darkness and light, and all things flow in the space between heaven and earth, serving the cycles of day and night. Day and night succeed one another, just as life and death replace each other. Those confined by time and shifting with things are destined to be overtaken by the bounds of day and night. Only the enlightened know that all is One, that death and life are but a single thread. Though they come and go with things, there is what we call 'not coming,' though they move with things, there is what we call 'not moving.' Knowing that death is not fundamentally different from life, their form undergoes myriad changes, dissolving with time, yet their true nature remains serene, their spirit unperishing. Thus, comprehending the Dao/Way of day and night, they know their independence—this is the ultimate One. Heaven endures long, earth persists forever, and together they endure without limit. Their *long-lived* nature knows no bounds."*

This chapter’s meaning begins with knowing others to exhaust principles, proceeds to knowing sufficiency to fulfill nature, and concludes with not perishing to attain *fate*—thus, creation dwells within us.

Thus, exhausting principles reveals no obstruction—hence, knowing others is to exhaust principles;
fulfilling nature leaves no desire—hence, knowing sufficiency is to fulfill nature;
realizing the essence of *fate* governs all things without constraint—hence, death without perishing is to attain *fate*.

The *Yijing* speaks of exhausting principles, fulfilling nature, and attaining *fate*—its sequence aligns with this chapter’s meaning.

When one attains *fate*, the marvels of creation arise from within oneself.
Morning clarity reveals solitude, merging in obscurity with the Dao,
transcending time’s measure, beyond the bounds of past and future,
free from form and body, untouched by life and death’s dominion.

Zhuangzi spoke of neither ancient nor modern, entering the realm of neither death nor life—this is what is meant.

---
**Chapter 34: The Great Dao Flows**

The Great Dao flows—it may turn left or right.
*Huizong’s Annotation*: Flowing without attachment, it neither loses motion nor stillness, nor exhausts coming and going—left or right, it knows no impossibility.

*Exegesis*: The interplay of yin and yang is the Dao—left is yang, right is yin. Thus, flowing without attachment, it may turn left or right.

*The Taixuan* says: "Without attachment is the sage."
Zhuangzi says: "There is left and right, yet no attachment."

Thus, it does not cling to one side, yet left and right exist.
If so, motion and stillness lie within—like yin and yang’s ebb and flow in filling and emptying, what loss can there be?
Then motion and stillness are unerring.

Coming and going lie within—like the moon’s successive illumination creating day and night, what exhaustion can there be?
Then coming and going are boundless.

Take from the left, and it is left; take from the right, and it is right—
no gate, no side, four-way expansive, yet all reach their source.
How can it be stored and not attained?

All things rely upon it to live, yet it does not refuse.
Its work done, it does not dwell upon it.

*Huizong’s Annotation*: It gives to those who depart, supplies those who seek—
all things form and transform themselves, wise and strong in their own right,
yet it does not claim their merit.
Like the four seasons: when one departs, another takes its place.
Wood thrives as water wanes—its work done, it departs.
Who would claim it as their own?

*Exegesis*: It gives to those who depart—
as Zhuangzi said, "All things depart, yet it supplies them without depletion."
It supplies those who seek—
as Zhuangzi said, "The utmost void fulfills all seeking."
Since it gives to those who depart and supplies those who seek,
yet remains untangled, life and transformation, wisdom and strength
are all entrusted to their own nature—
why claim their merit?
Like the four seasons: when *wu* emerges, *ding* retreats;
wood flourishes as water declines—
its work done, it departs.
Who would acknowledge it as their own?

It clothes all things yet is not their master—
thus, it is ever without desire, worthy of being called *small*.
All things return to it, yet it knows no master—
worthy of being called *great*.

*Huizong’s Annotation*: The Dao, in its utmost obscurity, is *small* yet distinct from things;
in its utmost transformation, it is *great* yet interacts with things.
Distinct from things, it is ever without desire;
interacting with things, all things return to it.
It covers and reveals all things yet does not display its sovereign power—
thus, it is not their master.
It stirs and guides the multitude yet no one discerns its path—
thus, it knows no master.

The Dao is not *small* or *great*—what is called *small* or *great*
are but its manifestations in things.

*Exegesis*: Its essence penetrates the spirit—*small* in its obscurity,
its radiance shines forth—*great* in its transformation.
Thus, in its utmost obscurity, it is *small* and distinct from things;
in its utmost transformation, it is *great* and interacts with things.
The Heavenly Dao ascends to the north—its time of retreat;
descends to the south—its time of departure.
Southward interaction, northward distinction—
thus, the Dao in its utmost obscurity is *small* and distinct from things,
in its utmost transformation is *great* and interacts with things.
Distinct from things, self and objects are forgotten—
thus, it is ever without desire.
Interacting with things, it yields and adapts—
thus, all things return to it.
It covers and reveals all things yet does not display its sovereign power—
thus, it is not their master (from its inherent nature).
It stirs and guides the multitude yet no one discerns its path—
thus, it knows no master (from its outward manifestation).

Moreover, the Dao covers and carves all things,
yet does not boast of its craft—
thus, in covering all things, it does not display its sovereign power.
Only by not displaying its power does it not claim mastery.
It stirs and guides to exhaust the spirit—
thus, in stirring the multitude, no one discerns its path.
Only by not revealing its traces does it remain unknown as master.

The Dao is neither *small* nor *great*—
what is called *small* or *great* is not its true constancy,
but its manifestations in things.
When it manifests in things, it is no longer the Dao beyond form.
Thus, the sage never claims *greatness*—
only then can they achieve true *greatness*.

*Huizong’s Annotation*: Their deeds cover the world yet seem not their own—
thus, their achievements are vast and abundant.
Mencius said: "To transform the great is to be sage."
To transform the great is to not be burdened by *greatness*—
thus, they achieve true *greatness*.

*Exegesis*: With solid substance and radiance, this is *great*.
*Great* not yet detached from form—
form’s *greatness* cannot transform without flaw.
Only through ceaseless change does it attain the name *sage*,
free from the burden of *greatness*.
Free from the burden of *greatness*, it does not cling to *greatness*—
thus, it achieves true *greatness*.

---
**Chapter 35: Grasping the Great Symbol**

**Chapter 35: Grasping the Great Symbol**

Holding the Great Symbol, all under Heaven comes.
*Huizong’s Annotation*: The Symbol is like the heavens in its manifestation—
without action, yet moving by fixed rhythms;
without speech, yet revealing through patterns.
The sage governs the world by resting in non-action,
by walking the silent instruction,
and the people turn to them as to parents—
thus it is said: *Holding the Great Symbol, all under Heaven comes*.

*Exegesis*: The heavens manifest the Symbol,
and the sage follows its lead.
The heavens’ manifestation is smooth yet without stagnation,
circulating endlessly without pause—
its movement is vigorous and unflagging.
What need does it have for action?

*Zhuangzi* says: *"Non-action as its action is the heaven’s way."*
*The Book of Changes* says: *"The heaven’s movement is vigorous."*
This is non-action yet moving by vigor.

The stars revolve, the clouds and rainbows appear and vanish—
its patterns are visible,
yet how does the heaven speak?

*Zhuangzi* says: *"The heaven and earth have great virtue yet do not speak."*
*The Book of Changes* says: *"Gazing upward to observe the heaven’s patterns."*
This is speechless yet revealing through patterns.

The sage’s governance of the world embodies the heaven’s transformations,
grasping the Great Symbol to show the people—
like the heaven’s manifestation,
resting in non-action,
though acting yet never crossing the boundary of non-action;
walking silent instruction,
though teaching yet never uttering the intent of speech.

Thus, the people’s turning to them
is like water flowing downward—
their love is as close as parents,
their attachment unbreakable as glue and lacquer.
Hence it is said: *Holding the Great Symbol, all under Heaven comes*.

As for the Great Symbol,
it is seen yet cannot be grasped—
the *Classics* say: *"The Great Symbol is formless."*

All come yet none are harmed.
*Huizong’s Annotation*: When yin and yang are harmonious and tranquil,
spirits and gods do not disturb,
all living things are uninjured,
ten thousand things flourish without withering,
and though the people may have knowledge,
they lack use for it—
what harm could there be?

*Exegesis*: The sage regulates heaven and earth through yin and yang,
thus yin and yang are harmonious and tranquil—
as *Liezi* says: *"Yin and yang constantly adjust."*
By the Dao they move all under Heaven,
thus spirits and gods do not disturb—
as *Liezi* says: *"The spirits have no response."*
They fulfill the needs of all living things,
and all living things cling to their homeland—
thus all living things are uninjured—
as *Liezi* says: *"Humans suffer no untimely evil."*
They nurture all ten thousand things,
and all ten thousand things find their proper place—
thus ten thousand things do not wither—
as *Liezi* says: *"Things have no flaws or diseases."*

Wondrous yet forming yin and yang,
profound yet forming spirits and gods,
abundant yet forming all living things,
dispersed yet forming ten thousand things—
none but what is properly placed.
This is called *the utmost unity*,
and thus they perpetually make the people without knowledge or desire.

Though the people may have knowledge,
they lack use for it—
this is the greatest benefit to all under Heaven,
and thus there is no harm.

Stability, peace, and flourishing.
*Huizong’s Annotation*: Stability means no fear of peril or ruin,
peace means no risk of danger or unevenness,
flourishing is when all is thorough and well-governed.

*Exegesis*: Stability is the opposite of danger,
so stability means no fear of peril or ruin—
this is *"all under Heaven is perpetually stable."*
Peace is the opposite of unevenness,
so peace means no risk of danger or unevenness—
this is *"all under Heaven is uniformly level."*
Flourishing is the opposite of stagnation,
flourishing is when blessings extend downward—
thus *The Book of Changes* says: *"Flourishing is thorough and well-governed."*

From stability to peace,
from peace to flourishing—
governance’s effects are thus,
and without holding to the ancient Dao to govern the present,
who could achieve this?

Music and bait—
passing travelers pause.
*Huizong’s Annotation*: Those who delight in sounds and flavors
are burdened by things.
Burdened by things yet unable to free themselves,
things bind them—
thus they pause.

*Exegesis*: Their nature is tied to the Five Sounds,
so the ears crave excessive tones,
and the Five Sounds confuse the ears,
making the ears dull.
Their nature is tied to the Five Flavors,
so the mouth craves extreme tastes,
and the Five Flavors—*mà* [overindulgence]—
make the mouth lose its sharpness.
Thus, those who delight in sounds and flavors
are burdened by things.

Burdened by things,
the inner and outer *wèi* [vital forces] become tangled and convoluted,
and none can untie them.
Yet those who cannot free themselves
do not know to loosen and open their paths—
things bind them.
Even passing travelers, unable to rest,
will pause for them.

The Dao’s speech is bland yet without flavor.
To look upon it is insufficient to see,
to listen to it is insufficient to hear,
to use it is never exhausted.

*Huizong’s Annotation*: What the flavor flavors has been tasted,
yet the flavor itself has never been presented—
thus it is bland yet without flavor.
What the color colors is made clear,
yet the color itself has never been displayed—
thus to look upon it is insufficient to see.
What the sound sounds has been heard,
yet the sound itself has never been emitted—
thus to listen to it is insufficient to hear.
If so, they can endure bitterness and sweetness,
can embrace depth and brightness,
can harmonize with *gōng* [first pitch] and *shāng* [second pitch],
without knowing yet without not knowing,
without ability yet without inability—
thus to use it is never exhausted.

*Exegesis*: The heavens have the Five Phases,
which transform into the Five Flavors,
beginning with bland and ending with sweet—
all are what the flavor flavors.
What the flavor flavors has been tasted,
yet the flavor itself has never been presented—
this is *"the flavor of the world,"*
as *Zhuangzi* says.
The Five Colors are made clear,
whether by probing their origins or examining their substances—
all are what the color colors.
What the color colors is made clear,
yet the color itself has never been displayed—
this is *"the noble is without color,"*
as *The Book of Changes* says.
The Five Sounds are distinguished:
clear and turbid, high and low,
reaching and returning, expansive and tight—
all are what the sound sounds.
What the sound sounds has been heard,
yet the sound itself has never been emitted—
this is *"the great sound is rare,"*
as the *Classics* say.
True speech is not beautiful,
thus the Dao’s speech is bland yet without flavor,
