yet can flavor all under Heaven’s flavors.

The Dao is without form yet perfect,
thus when seen it cannot be perceived,
yet it can manifest all under Heaven’s hues.
It has no sound, no scent,
thus when heard it cannot be detected,
yet it can produce all under Heaven’s tones.

To flavor all under Heaven’s tastes,
it can be both sweet and bitter;
to manifest all under Heaven’s colors,
it can be both dark and yellow;
to sound all under Heaven’s tones,
it can be both *gong* and *shang*.

Without taste, unseen, unheard,
it seems as though it knows nothing, can do nothing—
yet it can flavor all flavors,
can sound all sounds,
can manifest all colors.
Its operations are boundless, its uses inexhaustible,
thus it knows all, can do all.

Therefore, *Liezi* argues that form gives rise to sound and taste,
yet concludes: *"without knowing, without ability—*
*yet without not-knowing, without not-ability,"*
thus it is said: *"Its use cannot be exhausted."*

The *Transmission* says:
*"Without sound, the Five Tones resound;
without taste, the Five Flavors emerge;
without color, the Five Colors are complete.*
Is this not what is meant?

Now, the chapter on *"The Desire to Withdraw"*—the thirty-sixth.

To withdraw it, one must first fully extend it;
To weaken it, one must first fully strengthen it;
To set it in motion, one must first fully arouse it;
To take from it, one must first fully give to it.

The *Huizong* annotation states:
*"Yin and Yang illuminate, envelop, and regulate one another;
The Four Seasons succeed, generate, and destroy one another.
This is the principle of all things, the transmission of human order.
In their gathering and dispersing, their flourishing and decline,
their rise and fall, their deficiency and fullness—
often these shifts emerge from the most subtle beginnings,
yet their signs are invisible to most.
Only the sagely few, who delve into the *ji* (微妙之机),
can foresee their auspicious outcomes.
The virtuous nearly approach this understanding."*

When Yang flourishes in summer, Yin arises at *wu* (午);
When Yin congeals in winter, Yang arises at *zi* (子).

King Goujian sought to weaken Wu, yet urged it to attack Qi;
Zhi Bo sought to seize You Yu, yet gifted him a grand carriage.
Thus the Sage, seeing frost and knowing the coming of solid ice,
understands the waxing and waning of fullness and emptiness—
not fixed in their outward forms.
Hence, even the bold cannot weaken, and the wise cannot take.

The *Exegesis* explains:
*"The movement of Yin and Yang—
when one wanes, the other waxes, illuminating and enveloping,
rising and descending, regulating one another—
this is how Yin and Yang envelop, illuminate, and regulate.
The Four Seasons proceed: cold and heat alternate,
generating and destroying in succession—
the King generates, Ren conquers—
this is how the Four Seasons succeed, generate, and destroy.
Thus, all things disperse into myriad forms,
their principles unsaid, their human order transmitted without end.
When gathering reaches its peak, dispersion already stirs—
thus extension and withdrawal arise.
When flourishing peaks, decline follows—
thus weakness and strength emerge.
Whether in sudden anger or collapse, rise or fall,
whether in loss or gain, taking or giving—
these shifts begin before signs appear,
even divine vision cannot perceive their forms.
The *ji* often arises from the most subtle beginnings,
yet its portents remain unseen.
Only the Sage, discerning in the obscure,
acts brightly upon the faintest threads—
by probing deeply and mastering the *ji*,
they attain foresight of auspiciousness.
Though the virtuous may not fully grasp the *ji*,
yet if they act before chaos arises,
ordering before disorder—
they nearly approach this understanding."*

Now, when accumulated Yang brings summer’s heat,
Yang flourishes in summer—
yet *zi* (子) beauty has already waned,
and Yin is about to emerge.
When accumulated Yin brings winter’s cold,
Yin congeals in winter—
yet *wu* (午) beauty has reached its peak,
and Yang is about to arise.
As Yang Xiong said:
*"Yang does not reach its extreme before Yin does not stir,
Yin does not reach its extreme before Yang does not sprout."*
All these shifts—
withdrawal and extension, ruin and rise, alternating transformations—
are the workings of Heaven’s principle.

King Goujian sought to weaken Wu,
yet urged it to attack Qi—
to weaken it, he first strengthened it.
Zhi Bo sought to seize You Yu,
yet gifted him a grand carriage—
to take from him, he first gave to him.
Han Fei also said:
*"The Duke of Jin, intending to attack Yu,
gifted him jade and horses.
Zhi Bo, intending to seize You Yu,
gifted him a grand carriage.
Thus, to take from one, one must first give to them—
this is the intent."*

This is the way of human affairs.

「By examining Heaven’s principle and verifying human affairs,
all things conform to this truth.
This is how the Sage perceives patterns and discerns categories—
when Yin first congeals,
when frost is upon the ground and one knows the coming of solid ice.

Thus, when Heaven’s principle unfolds—
one aspect fading while another emerges,
one being full while another empties—
it does not depend on their outward forms,
but one can observe the essence of their transformations.
Though their signs may not yet appear,
their logic is clear and visible.

If so, then the withdrawal and extension of strength and weakness,
the rise and fall of ruin and prosperity,
of giving and taking, controlling and seizing—
these are self-regulating,
self-operating.
The bold dare not flaunt their vigor,
for their strength cannot weaken others;
the wise dare not deploy their cunning,
for their defenses cannot be taken.

In harmony with the Dao/Way,
Heaven’s course has already unfolded.
Only those who speak of great principles,
discuss the logic of all things,
can align with this.

This is called *subtle clarity* (微明).
Emperor Huizong annotated:
*"Before signs appear, it is subtle (微),
yet its logic is most evident (甚着)."*

Yang Xiong said in *The Taixuan* (《太玄》):
*"When water ebbs into the depths,
when wood decays from its branches,
the virtuous perceive it while the masses know not."*

The *Commentary* explains:
*"When one is in the midst of advancement,
it is manifest and easily seen;
when hidden in its unmanifest state,
it is concealed and difficult to know.
Withdrawal and extension, strength and weakness,
rise and fall, giving and taking,
controlling and seizing—
these mutually consume and flourish,
mutually rely and subdue.
Before their signs appear,
they are profound and obscure,
unseen in their progression,
concealed yet discernible by the subtle."*

From the perspective of inherent logic,
excess leads to decline,
exhaustion gives rise to rebirth,
cycling endlessly without pause.
Though hidden in no outward trace,
their inevitable course is clear and visible.
Thus, before signs appear, it is subtle (微),
yet their logic is most evident (甚着).

Yangzi composed *The Taixuan* (《太玄》), stating:
*"When water ebbs into the depths,
when wood decays from its branches,
the virtuous perceive it while the masses know not."*

The masses seek its description,
the virtuous grasp its logic.
Hence, the virtuous perceive it while the masses know not—
the masses differ from the virtuous in this.

Softness overcomes hardness,
weakness overcomes strength.

Emperor Huizong annotated:
*"Only the Sage can accumulate many small unconquerables
into a great unconquerable."*

The *Classics* say:
*"Nothing in the world is softer or weaker than water,
yet nothing surpasses it in overcoming hardness and strength."*

In *Zhuangzi’s Outer Chapters* (《庄子·外篇》),
discussing the mutual regard of the Great Snake and the Wind,
it is said:
*"Point at me, and I will overcome you;
press against me, and I will overcome you—
yet in breaking great trees and toppling great houses,
only I can do it."*

The *Commentary* explains:
*"In terms of personal conduct,
hardness and strength suffice to overcome softness and weakness;
in terms of the Dao/Way,
softness and weakness suffice to overcome hardness and strength.
Softness overcomes hardness,
weakness overcomes strength—
this is the Dao/Way’s logic.
Accumulate softness, and it becomes hardness;
accumulate weakness, and it becomes strength.
To seek hardness, one must guard it with softness;
to seek strength, one must preserve it with weakness.
Is this not the Dao/Way of perpetual victory?
This is what *Zhuangzi* meant when he said:
*'Only the Sage can accumulate many small unconquerables
into a great unconquerable.'*

Moreover, descending from the Dao/Way,
those closest to it are like water—
its nature is fluid and yielding,
making it the world’s ultimate softness,
yet able to overcome the world’s ultimate hardness.
As the *Classics* say:
*'Nothing in the world is softer or weaker than water,
yet nothing surpasses it in overcoming hardness and strength.'*

And so it is with the Wind—
when it enters through the *xun* (巽, southeast) direction,
its use is unparalleled in bending all things.
It too overcomes hardness and strength with softness and weakness.

In *Zhuangzi’s Outer Chapters* (《庄子·外篇》),
it is said of the Snake and the Wind:
*'Point at me, and I will overcome you;
press against me, and I will overcome you—
yet in breaking great trees and toppling great houses,
only I can do it.'*

Pointing at me, pressing against me, overcoming me—
this is the softness and weakness.
Breaking great trees, toppling great houses—
this is the overcoming of hardness and strength.

The *Transmission* (《传》) says:
*"Softness is the Dao/Way’s hardness,
weakness is the Dao/Way’s strength."*

Fish cannot be detached from the depths,
and the state’s sharpest tools
should not be displayed to the people.

Emperor Huizong annotated:
*"The depths are where fish conceal their bodies.
Sharp tools are the ruler’s ultimate authority,
by which all things are governed.
A fish that can swallow a boat,
if stranded and losing its water,
even ants can torment it—
thus it cannot be detached from the depths.
When the ruler shows favor,
subjects exploit its influence;
when the ruler inflicts punishment,
subjects wield its power.
Favor and punishment are the means of governance—
yet even these should not be displayed,
how much less the Dao/Way of governance itself?"*

The Sage wields sharp tools yet does not display them,
not for their strength,
but for the subtle Dao/Way within them.

One can exhaust the seas without gaining fame for wisdom,
command all things without earning credit for valor,
yet never rely on victory over others
to attain the Dao/Way of perpetual triumph.

Yang opens while Yin closes,
transformation endless.
Guiding the ministers,
moving the world—
none can fathom it.
Thus, they are governed without being governed.

Authority lies with the sovereign,
the key with the ruler—
and the world is in order.

The *Commentary* explains:
*"The depths are where water flows back upon itself,
the profound and unfathomable.
Fish hide and escape, submerged in the depths—
thus the depths are where fish conceal their bodies.
Sharp tools are the ruler’s supreme power,
by which all things are controlled.
Hence, authority lies above,
and subjects are governed by the ruler—
sharp tools are the state’s means of control.
Yet fish in water are like people in the Dao/Way:
one cannot for a moment depart from the Dao/Way,
so fish cannot for a moment lose their water.
A fish that can swallow a boat,
if stranded and losing its water,
even ants can torment it—
this is because it was detached from the depths.
Favor and punishment are visible incentives for the world;
when the ruler personally employs them,
the ministers fear his power and submit to his authority.
If favor is not bestowed with precision,
if punishment is not applied with thoroughness—
this is the ruler showing favor,
and the ministers will exploit its influence."*

When punishment of evil is not thorough,
this is the ruler showing punishment,
and the ministers will exploit its power.

Rewards and punishments are the tools of governance,
yet they must not be openly displayed—
how much more so the Dao/Way itself?
One may make people follow it,
but one must never let them comprehend it.

Han Fei also said:
Rewards and punishments are the state’s sharp tools.
When the ruler displays favor,
ministers will diminish it to claim virtue;
when the ruler displays punishment,
ministers will seize it to claim authority.
This is the same principle.

Thus, the state’s sharp tools
must never be openly shown.

The Sage wields these tools without revealing them,
not by brute force,
but by standing in the unfathomable,
acting in the unseen—
there lies a wondrous Dao within,
by which the divine instruments move without constraint,
and great things are achieved without loss.

One can exhaust the realm’s resources without exerting intelligence or strength—
not because intelligence or strength are absent,
but because their application is beyond naming.

One can command the realm’s submission without displaying valor—
not because valor is absent,
but because its deployment escapes the world’s gaze.

Thus, it is not a matter of deliberate conquest,
yet victory is ever secured.
Not fixated on conquering,
one attains the constant Dao of victory.

The expansion and contraction of the Dao’s attainment,
its opening with the Yang and closing with the Yin—
as the *Transmission* says:
*"With the Yin, close together;
with the Yang, open together."*

To know the divine’s workings,
and transform endlessly—
as the *Transmission* says:
*"Manifest in transformation."*

With this, one governs ministers and moves the world,
yet none can fathom it;
one controls others without being controlled—
who else could hold victory so?

This has no other source:
it lies in the ruler above.
Rectify the root, and all things align;
grasp the essential, and all things are governed.

Without deliberate governance,
the world is already governed.

---
**Chapter Thirty-Seven: The Dao’s Constant Non-Action**

*The Dao acts without doing, yet nothing remains undone.*

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
Silent and unmoving,
yet responsive—
thus the world is naturally transformed.

**Explanatory Notes:**
The Dao has essence and function—
non-action is its essence,
yet its function is boundless action.

If one seeks the Dao through exclusive non-action,
one drowns in emptiness,
losing the Dao’s essence.
If one seeks the Dao through exclusive action,
one stagnates in form,
losing the Dao’s function.

Only in silent stillness,
non-action without neglecting action,
responding yet remaining in non-action—
only then does the Dao’s utmost subtlety
leave no trace behind.

If lords and kings could uphold this,
all things would transform of their own accord.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
Just as water reflects form without effort,
so too the Dao’s contact with the world
requires no contrivance—
yet nothing can escape its shaping.

When lords and kings govern by the Dao,
they enter the realm of non-action,
yet emerge from it to transform all things,
as if by nature’s own doing—
unknown to those transformed,
hence *"self-transformation."*

**Explanatory Notes:**
A clear mirror reveals no dust;
still water reflects even eyebrows.
Water and mirrors respond without storing—
thus the *Transmission* says:
*"Like water reflecting form,
it requires no contrivance,
yet nothing can escape its shaping."*

How is the Dao’s response different?

Those who attain this
become emperors above,
kings below—
thus, if lords and kings uphold the Dao,
all things will transform of their own accord.

Before undertaking deeds or establishing enterprises—
this is the king’s Dao.
Moderating conduct and adhering to measure—
this is the lord’s duty.
Thus, lords and kings
are the pivot upon which all things depend.

If they truly uphold the Dao to govern the times,
their rule over the world and state will be effortless.

The Dao’s true essence governs the self;
its remnants govern the state;
its extensions govern the world.

Only by embodying the Dao’s non-being,
responding to the world’s manifestations,
intending action yet never acting,
intending non-action yet never ceasing—
entering the realm of non-action,
yet emerging from non-action—
thus transforming all things,
all is fulfilled without omission,
all is measured without exhaustion,
from sentient beings to the insentient—
none fail to be moved in perception,
and their transformation is balanced.

Like *qi* flowing through the cosmos,
great transformation moves subtly,
all things arise and proliferate,
as if by nature’s own doing—
unknown to those who act,
this is self-transformation.
What more is there to it?

When transformation stirs to action,
I shall govern it with the nameless Primordial.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
Mencius said:
*"Those who wait for King Wen to rise before acting
are merely common folk."*
People follow their ruler—
when transformation stirs to action,
it departs from the Dao to seek goodness,
risks virtue to act,
and strays from nature to follow desire—
this is insufficient to stabilize the world.

Only the Dao, nameless and undifferentiated,
can restrain such stirrings.
Thus, the Sage saves the erring with loyalty.

**Explanatory Notes:**
The Sage always aids others,
never abandoning them—
those transformed by the Dao
all rise anew.

Thus Mencius said:
*"Those who wait for King Wen to rise before acting
are merely common folk."*
The noble’s virtue is like wind,
the commoner’s like grass—
grass bends with the wind,
so people follow their ruler.

The ruler’s transformation of the people
is like molten metal shaped by the caster,
or clay molded by the potter.

The violent and bold become compliant;
the perverse and self-serving become upright;
the arrogant and constrained become harmonized—
yet even this is not without some artificiality.

When transformation stirs to action,
it departs from the Dao to seek goodness—
the Dao’s completeness is impaired;
it risks virtue to act—
the virtue’s essence may falter.

The Dao has neither goodness nor non-goodness;
if one clings to goodness,
it becomes disjointed and severed.

The highest virtue is without virtue,
when virtue is perfected as action,
then it becomes difficult and perilous.

The Dao and virtue are both innate nature (*xìng*),
while goodness and action are the nature’s manifestation in the heart.
To depart from the Dao for goodness,
to risk virtue for action—
this is to abandon nature and follow the heart.

If one seeks to govern the world with the heart,
then heart-to-heart recognition is insufficient to govern the world.

Only the Dao, nameless and undifferentiated,
remains undispersed, thus the Sage abides in stillness.
Nameless and without substance,
it resides in the void of things—
only the Dao gathers the void,
the Great Primordial, nameless.

What is beneath form is called *qì* (instrumental),
the Dao transcends form and is not confined by *qì*,
thus it remains undifferentiated and undispersed.

The nameless Primordial is the complete essence of the Dao—
with this, the Sage abides, and who could suffer from deficiency or excess?

The nameless Primordial also does not desire.

**Emperor Xuanzong’s Annotation:**
The Master’s non-action lies in the slightest inclination of things;
ancient Daoist techniques reside here as well.

Though few can embody the fullness of Heaven and Earth,
the Master thus does not desire—
this is why Laozi dispels confusion and resolves delusion.

**Explanatory Notes:**
*"Clustering blocks, accumulating dust—*
though non-action may appear so,
it is not without principle.
Thus, the Master’s non-action still lies in the slightest inclination of things,
and ancient Daoist techniques reside here as well."

This reflects the debate between the two schools,
raised in inquiry to Master Taigong.

Nonetheless, the Dao is ever in non-action yet nothing remains undone,
Heaven and Earth act through non-action.
The nameless Primordial is like the Master’s non-action—
if one acts without action, few can embody Heaven and Earth’s perfection,
thus the Master does not desire.

Laozi dispels the obscurity of words
and resolves the later ages’ confusion—
is this not his insight?

With non-desire comes stillness,
and the world will naturally align itself.

**Emperor Xuanzong’s Annotation:**
*"Water still becomes level and balanced—
the great artisan takes this as his model."*

With non-desire in stillness,
one does not stray from correctness—
first, one corrects oneself,
thus the world will naturally align itself.

*The Classic of Changes* says:
*"The Creative’s Dao is transformation and change,
each rectifying its nature and destiny."*

The Creative’s Dao manifests in transformation and change—
this is non-action yet nothing remains undone.
Each rectifying its nature and destiny—
this is non-desire in stillness,
and the world will naturally align itself.

To govern the world with the Dao,
until each rectifies its nature and destiny—
this is called governance at its utmost.

**Explanatory Notes:**
*"The noble observes great waters necessarily,
for the highest goodness resembles water,
nearly embodying the Dao."*

Water, though shifting in square, round, curve, and straight,
varies without constancy—
yet when still, it is level and balanced;
the great artisan takes this as his model—
*"The unmoved is level,"*
and *"The ruler’s measure must be balanced."*

The Dao’s essence is thus—
ancient worthies took water as their model.
Water still becomes level and balanced—
this is non-desire in stillness,
and the great artisan takes this as his model,
thus the world will naturally align itself.

With non-desire in stillness, it remains one and unchanging,
never straying from correctness.
Never straying from correctness while first correcting oneself—
this is correcting oneself so that things correct themselves,
thus the world will naturally align itself.

As Zhuangzi said:
*"Only the Sage can correct life to correct all lives,
only the Still can still all stillness—*
this is the same intent."

*The Classic of Changes* says:
*"The Creative’s Dao is transformation and change,
each rectifying its nature and destiny."*

Sudden, formless revolution is called *change*;
gradual, form-adapted transformation is called *change*.
The Creative’s Dao appears in transformation and change—
this is non-action yet nothing remains undone.
Preserving the form while safeguarding the spirit,
each has its measure—this is *nature*,
and continuity without interruption—this is *destiny*.
All things rectifying their nature and destiny—
this is non-desire in stillness,
and the world will naturally align itself.

This chapter begins with *"The Dao is ever in non-action yet nothing remains undone,"*
and ends with *"the world will naturally align itself."*
The effects of governance are complete and exhaustive—
nothing further can be added.
This is called governance at its utmost.

**End of *Explanatory Notes on the Dao-De Jing*, Volume Seven.**

**#1** *"No contesting"*—this sentence likely has a missing passage.
**#2** *"Not straying from correctness"*—*"then"* (*zé*) may be a corruption of *"its"* (*qí*).

**Volume Eight of *Explanatory Notes on the Dao-De Jing*,**
by Jiang Cheng, Student of the Imperial Academy.

**Volume Eight of *Explanatory Notes on the Dao-De Jing* (continued)**

Huizong’s annotation states: *"The Dao has no fixed form; virtue has completion and deficiency. Aligning with the Dao means there is no virtue to be named, while distinguishing from virtue means there is virtue to be discerned."* The *Ren-Yi-Li-Zhi* [仁義禮智], according to their measure, are received, and according to the times, they are applied—this is virtue alone. Those who embody the Dao differ from this, hence they are placed in the lower scriptures.

The *Explanatory Notes* state: *"Adaptability is the Dao; thus the Dao cannot be sought in fixed ways. The Dao beyond form is called the Dao; thus the Dao cannot be sought in fixed forms."* The Dao is present in all things: look upward and it is in the heights, look downward and it is in the depths, strive forward and it is in the front, cast aside and it is in the rear. It has no gate, no side path—its boundless radiance extends in all four directions. This is its formlessness. It can be yielding yet firm, soft yet hard, short yet long, round yet square; precious yet empty. Gazing upon it all day, one cannot see it—this is its formlessness.

If the Dao has no fixed form, how can there be completion and deficiency in virtue? If virtue has a fixed form, how can there not be completion and deficiency in it? Virtue has high and low; only by elevating it can one ascend to its completion, otherwise it will be deficient. Virtue has small and great; only by cultivating it can one progress to its completion, otherwise it will be deficient.

Zhuangzi said: *"When virtue is complete, this is called ‘establishment’*; thus virtue indeed has completion."* Again he said: *"The proper may be deficient*; thus virtue indeed has deficiency."* Viewed from their unity, it aligns with the Dao, with no virtue to be named—Yang Xiong’s *"unity is wholeness"*, Zhuangzi’s *"virtue encompasses the singularity of the Dao"*. Viewed from their distinction, it separates from virtue, with virtue to be discerned—Yang Xiong’s *"separation is dispersion"*, as the scripture says: *"When the Great Dao is abandoned, there is Ren and Yi"* [大道廢有仁義].

Ren is the proper conduct for humanity, applied as fitting; Li is the way to enact it; Zhi is the way to know it. The worthy discern its vastness, the unworthy attend to its minutiae. The benevolent see it and call it *Ren*, the wise see it and call it *Zhi*—each receives according to their measure, and applies it according to the times. This is virtue alone.

But the Dao is otherwise: it universally encompasses all without distinction, flows ceaselessly and fills all without lack, bestows benefits and achieves completion without ever exhausting itself. Who could speak of completion or deficiency in the Dao?

Those who embody the Dao differ from virtue in this way, hence virtue is placed in the lower scriptures.

**Chapter Thirty-Eight: "The Upper Virtue Does Not Virtue"**
The upper virtue does not virtue, hence it has virtue. Huizong’s annotation states: *"When things are granted life, this is called virtue. If all receive it equally, it is silently in accord with the Dao. When one transgresses, they do not repent; when one is appropriate, they do not feel self-satisfied—this is called ‘not virtue’."* When Confucius did not dwell upon his sainthood yet acted in the manner of the sage, this was his way of having virtue.

The *Explanatory Notes* state: *"Speaking of its origin, life is not virtue unless clarified. Speaking of its completion, life is the radiance of virtue."* All things within the realm of existence have life, and the reason they live is that they obtain the One. What is the One? Virtue nearly arises from it—Zhuangzi’s *"the One from which all things arise, the One yet unformed"*. When things are granted life, this is virtue. If nature is equal and virtue is shared, then all receive it equally. If virtue is in accord with the Dao, then it silently aligns with the Dao.

Only by knowing nature and reflecting upon virtue, when virtue reaches its initial state, does one move according to principle and act according to the times—this is transgression. Not transgressing by choice, but because principle does not allow otherwise—what is there to repent? When one is appropriate, it is not by choice, but because principle does not allow otherwise—thus one does not feel self-satisfied. The True Person’s virtue is such; this is called ‘not virtue’.

If otherwise, then reckless and wild behavior, stepping boldly upon the Great Square and moving without restraint, circulating within the central rites—this can be called upper virtue? In the past, Confucius, though endowed by Heaven with the potential for sainthood, when answering Gongxi Hua’s question, said: *"If it were about sainthood and virtue, how could I dare?"* When answering Zigong’s question, he said: *"Sainthood, I cannot achieve; I do not dwell upon my sainthood."* Yet when times were clear, he was clear; when times were demanding, he took responsibility; when times were harmonious, he was harmonious. Gathering the great achievements of the Three Sages, Mencius regarded this as the time of sainthood, and said: *"Since the birth of the people, there has been none like Master Kong."* His virtue is evident.

The lower virtue does not lose virtue, hence it lacks virtue. Huizong’s annotation states: *"Recognizing and possessing virtue, one is selfish and loses the Dao—what virtue remains?"*

The *Explanatory Notes* state: *"The Dao within me has never had a fixed form. If one claims possession of the Dao, the Dao will abandon you. Thus, recognizing and possessing virtue are all delusions."* Moreover, to cultivate oneself for private gain and to abandon private gain for the public good—this is what the Dao does for the public, yet to claim possession of the Dao is selfish. Only the selfless can achieve their private gain; how could selfishness not lose the Dao?

To desire not to lose virtue yet to lack virtue—what else is this but lower virtue?

**The Upper Virtue Acts Without Action, Yet Nothing Remains Unacted**
Huizong’s annotation states: *"Without thought yet attaining, without effort yet accurate, without movement yet arriving—this is upper virtue."*

The *Explanatory Notes* state: *"One nature has awareness, piercing through the boundless—this is ‘without thought yet attaining’. Employing it without strain, naturally in accord with the Dao—this is ‘without effort yet accurate’. Never exerting force, yet nowhere does it fail to be preserved—this is ‘without movement yet arriving’. If such, then acting is non-acting, and non-acting emerges from acting. The peak of virtue cannot be surpassed—thus it is called upper virtue."*

**The Lower Virtue Acts With Action, Yet Nothing Remains Unacted**
Huizong’s annotation states: *"Without thought, one does not attain; without effort, one is not accurate; without movement, one does not arrive—this is lower virtue."*

**The Virtue of High and Low—Thus the Sage and the Wise Are Distinguished**

Virtue has its high and low—is this not how the sage and the wise are divided?

To transcend the body and abandon wisdom, to attain the Great Unity—
Ren, righteousness, ritual, and wisdom are to be simplified, yet cannot be discarded,
and so there is no need for them.
To bend and adjust ritual and music,
to sigh and lament over ren and righteousness,
to soothe the hearts of the people—
this is to gain what others gain,
yet not to possess it oneself,
and so there is need for them.

*The Explanatory Notes* state:
When sincerity illuminates, it is called the Dao.
When illumination returns to sincerity, it is called teaching.
To attain without thought, to hit without effort, to arrive without movement—
this is sincerity illuminating.
To think and thus attain, not to think and thus not attain;
to exert effort and thus hit, not to exert effort and thus miss;
to act and thus arrive, not to act and thus fail—
this is illumination returning to sincerity.
Sincerity illuminating is the work of the sage,
illumination returning to sincerity is the work of the wise.
Thus virtue has its high and low—
is this not how the sage and the wise are divided?

Unburdened by the body, unconstrained by wisdom,
yet transcending the body and abandoning wisdom—
the body cannot obstruct,
wisdom has its own penetration,
and thus one attains the Great Unity.
To forget ren and righteousness,
to forget ritual and music—
ren, righteousness, ritual, and wisdom are to be simplified, yet cannot be discarded,
and yet there was never anything to begin with—
this is called *without need for them*—
this is upper virtue.

To make music soft and refined, to make ritual precise and orderly,
yet to bend and adjust ritual and music,
to make ren pliant and righteousness compliant,
to sigh and lament over ren and righteousness—
to use these to soothe the hearts of the people,
sufficient only to unsettle the hearts of the people—
this is to gain what others gain,
yet not to possess it oneself—
this is called *with need for them*—
this is lower virtue.

**Upper Ren Acts Yet Has No Need for Action**
Huizong’s annotation states:
*"The sage is born with it—
seven times he covered the world, yet did not seek personal gain,
and so there is no need for it."*

*The Explanatory Notes* state:
Zhuangzi said: *"Great ren is not ren."*
What is called "not ren" is not without ren—
it is able to ren yet does not use ren as ren.
Thus there is *easy ren* and *utilitarian ren*.
Easy ren follows naturally without need for action,
utilitarian ren uses ren for gain and acts accordingly.
Mencius said: *"The sage is born with it."*
Easy ren is not the same as acting in ren.
Thus ren covers the world without exclusion—
this is called upper ren.
Zhuangzi spoke of the sage resting in what is at ease,
not resting in what is not at ease,
and the *Book of Documents* praised Emperor Yao’s virtue as *"tranquil and tranquil"*—
this is upper ren acting yet having no need for action.

**Upper Righteousness Acts Yet Has Need for Action**
Huizong’s annotation states:
*"To array enemies and measure suitability—this is righteousness,
to establish oneself and govern affairs—how can one avoid action?"*

*The Explanatory Notes* state:
Righteousness in discernment is arraying enemies,
righteousness in adaptation is measuring suitability—
Yang Xiong’s *"arraying enemies and measuring suitability"* refers to this.
Mencius said: *"If a person can fill the space between ‘you’ and ‘I’ with righteousness,
then righteousness cannot be exhausted in its application."*
Thus righteousness certainly establishes oneself.
Yuzi said: *"To respond to change with righteousness,
then righteousness certainly governs affairs."*
Because it establishes oneself, the *Explanation of Characters* says: *"Righteousness is ‘I.’"*
Because it governs affairs, the *Book of Documents* says: *"To govern affairs with righteousness."*
To establish oneself yet not forget oneself,
to govern affairs yet not abandon affairs—
all these involve action,
and is this not upper righteousness acting yet having need for action?

The *Book of Songs* speaks of *"the wildfowl and cranes coming to the ancestral hall"*—
yet follows with *"blessings and prosperity come with them"*—
those who come are welcomed because of their righteousness.

**Upper Ritual Acts Yet Finds No Response**
Huizong’s annotation states:
*"Ritual is for exchanging with others,
for demonstrating to people,
for regulating and adorning ren and righteousness—
its uses are many.
If no one initiates giving and returning,
and giving is exhausted yet no one responds,
then resentment and contention arise,
and disorderly changes emerge.
In the Spring and Autumn period,
a single unanswered word,
a single unreciprocated bow—
two states would fight to the bones over it,
and yet this was still to restrain the lesser disorder.
The sage is thick in ren yet thin in righteousness,
and ritual is merely what is trodden upon, not what is dwelled in.
Thus upper ren aligns with virtue,
upper righteousness has need for action,
upper ritual finds no response."*

*The Explanatory Notes* state:
To distinguish is to use arms,
to exchange is to use the *dou* vessel—
ritual’s use with guests and hosts is when the *dou* is employed—
thus ritual is for exchanging with others.
Ascending and descending, circling and bowing—
ritual’s presence in ceremony is an external performance—
thus ritual is for demonstrating to people.
The way to establish a person is through ren and righteousness—
ren’s substance lies in filial piety,
righteousness’s substance lies in following elders—
ritual’s substance is to regulate and adorn these two—
thus ritual regulates and adorns ren and righteousness.
The *Rites* number three hundred,
the *Analects on Ritual* number three thousand—
from solemn rites to joyous rites,
from rites for heavenly spirits to rites for human ghosts—
its uses are many.
In summary, it is about giving and returning first—
the *Records* say: *"The highest virtue values virtue,
the next values giving and returning."*
Yet ritual values reciprocity—
to give without returning,
to return without giving—
all are unritual.
When giving is exhausted yet no one responds,
then resentment and contention arise—
this is inevitable.
In the Spring and Autumn period,
a single unanswered word,
a single unreciprocated bow—
two states would fight to the bones over it—
this was because action was met with no response.
Thus to restrain the lesser disorder by rolling up one’s sleeves and persisting.
The sage knew this—
what is close yet cannot be too broadly applied is ren,
so virtue cannot be too thick in ren.
What is distant yet cannot be too narrowly occupied is righteousness,
so the Dao cannot be too thin in righteousness.
As for ritual—
to depart and not linger,
to pass and not dwell—
it is merely what is trodden upon, not what is dwelled in.

Thus it is that the proper footwear for advancing—
the ritual of what is trodden upon—
is so because it is lowly yet what all tread upon,
and thus ritual is what is walked upon, not dwelled in.

The *Yijing* takes heaven above and marsh below as "trodden upon,"
and says, "Trodden upon, yet not dwelled in"—
this is why it is so.

The ancient Perfect Ones did not know where ritual would lead,
yet they forgot themselves in the Dao and its arts—
this is because of this.

Thus, supreme virtue aligns with power yet does nothing,
supreme righteousness has something to establish itself upon,
supreme ritual has those who do not respond to it—
yet it cannot escape its burdens.

Therefore, one loses the Dao and then attains virtue,
loses virtue and then attains ren,
loses ren and then attains righteousness,
loses righteousness and then attains ritual.

Ritual, then, is the thinning of loyalty and trust—
and the origin of chaos.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
The Dao cannot be attained, so one loses the Dao and then attains virtue.
Virtue cannot be hoarded, so one loses virtue and then attains ren.
Ren can be cultivated, and when cultivated, it approaches righteousness—
so one loses ren and then attains righteousness.
Righteousness can be diminished, and when diminished, it is adorned with ritual—
so one loses righteousness and then attains ritual.

As for ritual, the farther it strays from the Dao, the more it loses.
All things do not flourish simultaneously—
this is yin and yang.
They take from one another—
this is威 (awe) and德 (virtue).
Where substance is thick, appearance is thin—
this is the ritual of father and son.

Thus observed, the more elaborate the ritual,
the more surely substance decays.
When substance decays, deceit follows,
and contention and chaos arise—
hence, ritual is the thinning of loyalty and trust,
and the origin of chaos.

**Explanation of Meaning:**
The Dao is ever without action, thus without form—
without form, it is not "that."
Hence, it cannot be attained.
Yet the Dao is the revered foundation of virtue—
so one loses the Dao and then attains virtue.
Virtue, once obtained, is within oneself—
within oneself, there is nothing external—
hence, it cannot be hoarded.
Yet virtue encompasses all that can be done as ren—
so one loses virtue and then attains ren.
Virtue is without action, but ren carries the heart of love and benefit—
thus ren can be cultivated.
Yet when cultivated, it approaches righteousness—
so one loses ren and then attains righteousness.
Righteousness, when thick in substance, must be set in proper measure—
thus it can be diminished.
When diminished, it is adorned with ritual—
so one loses righteousness and then attains ritual.

From losing the Dao to ritual,
each descent is lower,
the farther from the Dao,
the more is lost.
For action brings deceit,
non-action brings truth—
thus their difference is so great.

Non-action, then, is the root of all things,
and the reason virtue is supreme—
this is so.

Moreover, the ebb and flow never cease,
kings rise and fall in succession,
things do not flourish simultaneously—
this is yin and yang.
When yang prevails, yin withdraws in listening,
when yin prevails, yang hides in concealment.
What is diminished here must overflow there—
this is awe and virtue.
To govern with virtue, awe is not the priority;
to rule with awe, virtue comes afterward—
this is the natural order, unchangeable.

Thus, the ritual of father and son,
though thin in appearance, is thick in substance—
as Zhuangzi said:
*"Dressing like a market peddler’s feet,
he excuses himself with fright,
his brother with a nurse’s voice,
and with great respect for his elders—*
this is the meaning.

Thus observed, elaborate ritual decays in substance—
like a tree not yet flourishing, its root must already be weak.
When substance decays, deceit follows,
and contention and chaos arise—
how then could it be substantial yet unknown as loyal?
How could it be proper yet unknown as trustworthy?

Given how ritual fosters deceit in this way,
one must abandon the false and take the true,
and extinguish it.

The *Records* say that loyal and trustworthy persons can learn ritual—
this is because it values its root.

**Premature Knowledge** is the flower of the Dao,
and the beginning of folly.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
The Dao descends and manifests,
manifests and gives rise to wisdom.
Wisdom, when used as a chisel,
sharpened and applied,
wears out the spirit and recklessly measures—
this is premature knowledge.
Premature knowledge follows superficiality and forgets the root—
thus it is the flower of the Dao.
The heart labors, and wisdom grows more constrained—
thus it is the beginning of folly.
Though it often succeeds, this is why Confucius despised Zigong.

**Explanation of Meaning:**
Ren, righteousness, ritual, and wisdom—
none are outside virtue.
The Dao descends and manifests,
manifests and gives rise to wisdom.
Though wisdom branches from the Dao,
it is still rooted in the Dao.
Yet what Confucius despised in wisdom
was its inability to act without interference—
this is using wisdom as a chisel.
How much more so when probing the nature of things,
sharpening its grasp,
wearing out the spirit in shallow depths,
recklessly measuring—
this is premature knowledge,
the flower of the Dao, not its substance.
Though it may seem plausible, it is not so.
For minor knowledge harms virtue,
lost knowledge harms the Dao—
but premature knowledge,
boasting cleverness and skill,
already follows superficiality and forgets the root,
not knowing to nourish the source of wisdom
while frugally expending it—
though it has not yet reached harming virtue or the Dao,
it is still not the true Dao.

If so, then deceit arises,
the heart labors daily, growing more clumsy,
and wisdom is constrained—
this is the nature of premature knowledge,
and why it is the beginning of folly.
Though it often succeeds, this is why Confucius despised Zigong.

Confucius said:
*"Do not anticipate deceit,
do not recklessly measure the untrustworthy."*
Yet this was the foresight of the virtuous—
how much more so the wise?

Thus, the superior person dwells in substance, not in superficiality,
resides in truth, not in its adornment—
and so abandons the false to take the true.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
What is "there" is what the Dao rejects.
What is "here" is what the Dao upholds.
What the Dao upholds is thick, not thin,
substantial, not superficial—
unless one’s wisdom is sufficient to know oneself,
returning to one’s original nature
without drifting into the habits of things—
who could do this?

The *Yijing* says:
*"Thickly restore, without regret—
self-examination in the center."*
Thick—this is the utmost of substance.
A person born with substance is nature itself.

To restore one’s nature is simply to dwell in substance—
this is how the superior person fulfills the Dao and preserves virtue.

**Exegesis:**
To feign with propriety or contrive with cleverness
are both distant from the Dao, belonging to "there."

The highest virtue acts without action yet lacks nothing,
the highest benevolence acts yet lacks nothing—
both are not far from the Dao, belonging to "here."

What belongs to "there" pursues trifles and forgets the root,
thus the Dao rejects it.
What belongs to "here" returns to substance and fortifies thinness,
strives for truth and abandons adornment,
thus the Dao upholds it.

What the Dao upholds is thick yet not thin,
substantial yet without adornment—
only those who see goodness clearly and wield firm resolve
can know themselves with sufficient wisdom,
refining their nature to return to virtue
without drifting into the habits of things.
Thus they abandon the falsehoods of contrived action
and take the truth of non-action.
The *Yijing* says:
*"Thickly restore, without regret—
self-examination in the center."*
Thick—this is the utmost of substance.
If we speak of *dunren* (thick benevolence),
it means benevolence is the utmost substance.
If we speak of *dunhua* (thick transformation),
it means transformation is the utmost substance.
Only when people are born with substance,
following nature’s shifts,
does one’s birth substance become the nature of Heaven.
If one truly restores this utmost substance,
all actions will lead to auspiciousness—
who would have cause for regret or blame?
This is the foundation of restoration,
explaining *"thickly restore, without regret."*
Unless wisdom suffices to know oneself,
one cannot attain this.
Thus the symbol speaks of self-examination in the center,
while Confucius said: *"Restore to know oneself."*
This is how the superior person fulfills the Dao and preserves virtue—
its foundation lies here.

**On the Chapter "The Ancient Who Obtained Oneness" (Thirty-Ninth):**
The ancient who obtained oneness—
Heaven obtained oneness and became clear,
Earth obtained oneness and became tranquil,
The spirit obtained oneness and became numinous,
The valley obtained oneness and became full,
All things obtained oneness and were born,
The sovereign obtained oneness and became the rectifier of the world—
this is the attainment of oneness.

**Huizong’s Annotation:**
Zhuangzi said: *"To penetrate to oneness is to complete all things."*
Attaining oneness means not being dual,
embracing oneness means not departing,
guarding oneness means not shifting.
To know oneness is to know all things;
not to know oneness is to know nothing.
The ancient who obtained oneness
embodied the utmost precision of the world—
nothing could pair with it.
Thus, firmly established above,
it was pure and unmixed;
precipitously grounded below,
it was still and unchanging.
In the most profound darkness, formless—
this is the spirit, which, obtaining oneness, does not grow dim.
In the utmost emptiness, responsive—
this is the valley, which, obtaining oneness, does not exhaust.
All things transform from essence into form,
thus obtaining oneness to be born.
The sovereign rules alone over the multitude,
thus obtaining oneness to rectify the world.
From Heaven and Earth down to the sovereign,
though high and low differ in station,
dark and bright vary in dispersion—
Heaven’s clarity, Earth’s tranquility,
the sovereign’s rectification of the world
are not sought externally but attained through oneness alone.
Thus it is said: *"This is the attainment of oneness."*

**Exegesis:**
The Dao, obscure in non-being,
is hidden in the unformed oneness;
the Dao, descending into being,
is divided among the myriad differences.
Within the undifferentiated,
the unformed gives rise to the formed—
the Two Principles are thus established,
the myriad things thus dispersed.
In darkness, the spirit;
in brightness, humanity—
all converge in oneness.
What is this oneness?
It is the number of essence.
All things within the realm,
governed by fixed numbers,
do not originate outside this oneness.
The *Great Correlation*’s number is fifty,
its use forty-nine—
one must always leave one empty.
The Nine Categories begin with the Five Phases,
yet the Five Phases must be one—
saying *"water is one"* means
it originates from the beginning of fixed numbers,
and thus one can act through the myriad.
The Six Wings and Four Openings—
its operation is nowhere absent.
Zhuangzi said: *"To penetrate to oneness is to complete all things."*
Is this not that virtue encompasses the Dao’s oneness?
To obtain this oneness and align with it,
to extend it in action—
its use is boundless.
Spirit and essence arise from the Dao,
form originates from essence—
when a person is born,
spirit gathers with essence, the likeness is complete,
and essence remains whole, not borrowed from without.
If oneness flows into artifice,
true essence is swiftly lost.
To associate with others is to obstruct the heart.
If one wishes to preserve one’s form without disturbing one’s essence,
what path leads to this?
It lies in attaining oneness to focus upon it,
embracing oneness to preserve it,
guarding oneness to solidify it—
then form is whole, essence is restored,
and one becomes one with Heaven.
Essence and spirit merge and do not separate.

The *Yijing* speaks of attaining oneness:
*"To attain oneness is to concentrate one’s will—
thus not dual."*
The Classic says: *"To embrace oneness, can one not depart?"*
To embrace oneness is to hold fast without slipping—
thus not departing.
Zhuangzi said: *"I guard my oneness to dwell in harmony."*
To guard oneness is to remain still and focused—
thus not shifting.
To know these three is to obtain oneness.
Yet obtaining it is not difficult;
to know that oneness is difficult.
To know oneness is to know all things—
this is *"the few obtain it."*
Not to know oneness is to pair with it—
all remain within oneness.
Thus the *Qian* (Creative) Dao seems difficult,
firmly established above yet ever easy—
easy, yet pure and unmixed.
The *Yijing* on *Qian* says: *"Strong, healthy, central, pure, precise"—
is this not Heaven obtaining oneness to become clear?*
The *Kun* (Receptive) Dao seems complex,
precipitous yet ever simple—
simple, yet still and unchanging.
The *Yijing* on *Kun* says: *"Utterly still, virtue complete"—
is this not Earth obtaining oneness to become tranquil?
The unfathomable, the unrecognizable—
this is the spirit.
Though the spirit is most profound,
calamity and fortune follow their kinds most clearly—
this is because obtaining oneness does not lead to dimness.

「Not to grasp nor to resist—
the most void yet ever responsive—
this is the *Gu* (Valley).

The *Gu* is utterly void,
yet its vast and subtle echoes answer without distinction—
this is because obtaining oneness brings forth boundless resonance.

Heaven and Earth receive the *Qian* (Creative) essence to become *Yin* and *Yang*,
*Yin* and *Yang* focus the essence to form the four seasons,
the four seasons disperse the essence to create myriad things,
and all things transform essence into form—
this is rooted in the *Gu* alone.
Thus, obtaining oneness brings forth life.

Only the void can respond to the real,
only stillness can govern movement,
only oneness can traverse all things.
Rulers and kings, who alone command the multitude,
embody this principle—
thus, obtaining oneness, they uphold the *Zheng* (Rectification) of the world.

From Heaven and Earth down to rulers and kings,
high and low occupy distinct positions,
dark and bright diverge in their forms—
these are the three *Ji* (Extremes).
Though each has its own established nature,
they share the functions of the three *Cai* (Talents),
yet remain distinct in their operations.

Trace their origins—
why Heaven is clear, why Earth is tranquil,
why rulers and kings uphold the *Zheng* (Rectification) of the world—
could they be separate from oneness?

This oneness,
founded in inherent stability,
harmonized with natural law,
resides within the utmost stillness,
where its essence is most true.
It is achieved through oneness alone—
no external pursuit is needed.
This is the *"Zhi Yi"* (Attaining Oneness),
is it not?

Heaven, without clarity, would fear collapse,
Earth, without tranquility, would fear eruption,
The spirit, without vitality, would fear exhaustion,
The *Gu* (Valley), without fullness, would fear depletion,
All things, without life, would fear annihilation,
Rulers and kings, without *Zheng* (Rectification) and noble virtue, would fear ruin.

The *Huizong* (Emperor Huizong) annotated:
Heaven’s duty is to nurture and cover,
Earth’s duty is to shape and bear.
If Heaven collapses, how can it cover?
If Earth erupts, how can it bear?

The spirit depends on humanity to manifest—
if it falters, there is no display.
The *Gu* (Valley) receives without hoarding—
if it is exhausted, none can respond.

When gathered, essence and *qi* become things—
life arises from obtaining oneness.
When dispersed, wandering spirits become transformations—
annihilation follows the loss of oneness.
Only through *Zheng* (Rectification) can one govern all changes,
constantly attaining oneness,
thus upholding all beings,
transcending and standing alone above all things—
its enduring and prominent influence is evident.

To seek nobility without peril,
this lies in the principle alone.

Hence, nobility roots in humility,
height begins with the lowly.

The *Huizong* annotated:
Humility is what nobility relies upon for stability,
the lowly is what height originates from.

Common folk see only the superficial,
while sages discern the foundation.
Common folk observe only the accomplished,
while sages perceive the subtle beginnings—
thus they always attain oneness.

The *Shuyi* (Explanatory Notes) say:
Nobility and humility have fixed roles,
height and low have defined positions—
these are immutable distinctions.

Yet nobility must root in humility,
for humility is what nobility depends upon for stability—
the lowly among the people are the source of its foundation.
Height must begin with the lowly,
for the lowly are what height arises from—
the humble origins of the *Tai* (Altar) and *Lai* (Sacrificial Platform) are the basis for establishing *Taiping* (Great Peace).

Examine the principles of things,
and none defy this pattern—
how much more so in human affairs?

Common folk chase fleeting trends,
yet the farther they stray from the Dao,
the more they see only the superficial.

Sages discern the foundation,
embodying the Dao’s void,
free from arrogance and excess.

Clinging to past traces,
they fail to grasp where true insight lies—
thus they see only the accomplished.

Sages perceive the subtle,
penetrating its essence,
untainted by boastful words—
thus they always attain oneness,
even amid ten thousand changes, remaining unharmed.

The *Shijing* (Classic of Poetry) sings of *Tianbao* (Heaven’s Protection),
meaning rulers know how to humble themselves,
thus completing their governance,
and ministers return praise to their superiors—
this too is nobility rooted in humility,
height founded in the lowly.
This is why the foundation endures without limit.

Examine the end of its composition,
and one sees sages ruling by the Dao,
guiding the times—
preventing fullness from reaching excess and failing,
elevation from peaking and then declining,
completion from crumbling,
and flourishing from decaying,
thus preserving the world’s order.

Therefore, rulers and kings call themselves *"Gu Gua Bu Gu"* (Lonely, Weak, and Not Valley-like)—
is this not their humility as foundation?
Is it not?

The *Huizong* annotated:
*"Gu Gua Bu Gu"* (Lonely, Weak, and Not Valley-like)
are names of humility,
yet rulers and kings use them as titles—
knowing their roots alone.

Thus rulers and kings bear noble virtue without peril—
is this not through this principle?

The *Shuyi* (Explanatory Notes) say:
Rulers and kings alone command the multitude,
so their comprehensive governance is universal.
Their virtue nurtures the world,
and their administration is thoroughly benevolent.
Their titles reflect unique distinctions,
yet they use *"Gu Gua Bu Gu"* (Lonely, Weak, and Not Valley-like)—
the names of humility—
to embody their modest, yielding, and self-effacing nature,
not claiming authority for themselves.
Could this be done by those who do not know the foundation?

Duke Zeng said:
*"Cōngmíng shèngzhì"* (Wisdom and sagely insight),
they guard with *"yú"* (foolishness);」

「Their achievements benefit the land,
yet they preserve them with humility.
These are all the Dao of the sage’s modesty—
only rulers and kings know to govern themselves thus,
and so they endure noble virtue for ten thousand generations without losing it.
Is it not true that humility is not the root of evil?

Thus they attain many praises yet no true praise.
The *Huizong* (Emperor Gaozong) notes:
*"If one elevates oneself to overcome others,
if one claims nobility to degrade others,
if one is strong yet does not know to yield with gentleness,
if one is bright yet does not know to temper with darkness—
seeking praise from the world while their governance earns frequent commendation—
then exaggerated praise will gather swiftly,
and baseless slander will follow soon after,
and thus they earn no true praise."*

The *Shuyi* (Explanatory Notes) say:
*"Not by others’ lowliness do we raise ourselves,
then will people gladly exalt us;
Not by others’ degradation do we claim our nobility,
then will people honor and revere us.
To elevate oneself to overcome others—
this is artificial pride, what use is there in such elevation?
To claim nobility by degrading others—
this is leveraging power, what use is there in such nobility?
Gentleness overcomes rigidity—
this is the natural order;
rigidity yet unaware of yielding to gentleness—
this is knowing only extension, not knowing retreat.
Brightness without blinding—
this is the Dao’s return;
brightness yet unaware of tempering with darkness—
this is knowing only display, not knowing subtlety.
With this, they boast of the world’s flaws,
though it may suffice to sell their name and earn frequent commendation,
it is merely a violation of the Dao to provoke it.
If so, then unearned praise will gather swiftly,
and baseless slander will follow soon after—
how could they escape Mencius’ scorn?
Seeking praise like this—
then their reputation floats above true merit, what difference does it make?
When the channels are full, their drying can be awaited,
and praise will soon be followed by blame—
how can it endure long?
To call this *no true praise*—
is this not fitting?

They do not wish to be *glittering like jade*,
nor *rugged like stone*.
The *Huizong* notes:
*"Jade is noble, stone is base,
yet both are fixed and unchanging."*

The sage rides the times and follows nature,
unhindered in their course,
with all change yet the heart ever steady—
this is the true attainment of *Oneness*.
Thus they cannot be fixed as noble or base.
Mencius said:
*"What I detest in those who cling to Oneness,
is that they cling to one thing while abandoning the hundred.*
They do not wish to be *glittering like jade*,
nor *rugged like stone*—
only the sage who knows the transformations can reach this,
and this is called *Supreme Virtue*."*

The *Shuyi* say:
*"Virtue has essence and function,
with all change contained within it.
Wondrously observing its essence—
all myriad things can be gathered into Oneness;
broadly observing its function—
with Oneness, all myriad things can be enacted.
Adjusting and responding to it—
no action is unfit,
flowing through the mechanisms of emergence and return,
circulating in the uses of transformation—
this is the true Oneness,
serene and alone in its existence.
How could it be like the *glittering jade* that is noble yet cannot be base,
or the *rugged stone* that is base yet cannot be noble,
bound to a fixed essence,
grasped yet unchanging?
The sage’s movement is like water,
skilled in timing yet never lost,
avoiding obstacles yet never blocked,
adapting to square and round, straight and bent—
responding to change without constancy,
how could there be any hindrance?
The deeper the inquiry, the farther the exploration,
though it encompasses myriad transformations,
it remains ever true to the One—
this is the true attainment of Oneness.
Thus they cannot be fixed as noble or base,
for Oneness knows no nobility or baseness.
Laozi, having clarified the meaning of Oneness,
further warned those who cling to one-sidedness,
lest they be blinded by a single perspective,
unable to encompass all and ignorant of ultimate truth—
rigid like jade and stone,
stuck and unyielding.
Thus he reiterated his teaching.
Mencius said: *‘What I detest in those who cling to Oneness,
is that they cling to one thing while abandoning the hundred.’*
This is exactly his intent.
Thus they do not wish to be *glittering like jade*,
nor *rugged like stone*—
only the sage who is vast and transformative can reach this.
The *Yijing* says: *‘To exhaust the spirit and know the transformations,
this is the height of virtue.’*
The sage’s way of knowing the transformations—
is also through *non-action* and *non-inaction*,
and this is called *Supreme Virtue*."*

**Chapter 40: *Fǎn* (Return) of the *Yijing* (Changes)**
*The *Fǎn* (Return) is the Dao’s movement—
the *weak* is the Dao’s use.
All things under heaven are born of *having*,
and *having* is born of *non-having*.
The *Huizong* notes:
*"The principles of all under heaven—
movement and stillness interdepend,
strength and weakness complement each other.
The myriad things, each returning to their root,
then already past yet return again,
restoring to the utmost stillness.
Yet, when they are stirred and thus connect with all under heaven,
then movement is none other than myself,
and thus it is said: *‘The *Fǎn* (Return) is the Dao’s movement.’*
Gentleness overcomes rigidity,
weakness overcomes strength—
the Dao’s wondrous use lies here.
Zhuangzi said: *‘The accumulation of many small non-victories becomes a great victory,
only the sage can do this,’*
and thus it is said: *‘The *weak* is the Dao’s use.’*
The seasons’ course—
they gather and store in winter,
only to flourish and renew in spring.
Water’s nature is utmost gentleness,
yet in attacking rigidity and strength,
none can surpass it—
is this not what is meant?
Thus *having* and *non-having* generate each other,
like a cycle—
so *non-having* gives rise to movement,
and *having* reaches its limit and returns to *non-having*,
like the *Yin* and *Yang* of the *Yijing*’s *Xī* (The Last) hexagram,
yet they cannot be said to negate each other.
Those who are obscured by *non-action*,
drowned in *partial action*—
how could this be the Dao?"

The *Shuyi* say:
*"Movement arises from stillness established,
and all under heaven’s moving lines return to stillness;
strength arises from weakness accumulated,
and all under heaven’s strong lines must gather from weakness.
Thus movement and stillness interdepend,
strength and weakness complement each other—
this is the natural order.
The myriad things’ birth—
they flow in countless forms,
like the swift and the fleeting,
yet in their end,
they shed their superficiality and return to their true nature,
restoring to their dwelling place.
The myriad things, each returning to their root—
this is the order of nature.
Thus when they are already past,
they trend toward the path of movement’s emergence,
and when they return to their source,
they restore to the realm of utmost stillness.
Being able to settle and respond,
stirred yet naturally connecting with all under heaven,
while movement is none other than myself—
this is how *Return* establishes movement,
and thus it is said: *‘The *Fǎn* (Return) is the Dao’s movement.’*"*

The soft is the Dao’s strength—
thus soft overcomes hard.
The weak is the Dao’s power—
thus weak overcomes strength.
The soft and weak—
this is the Dao’s *wu* (non-being).
Non-being as a function—
thus the Dao’s function lies here.

Yet weakness is the Dao’s root,
and guarding the root means
self-mastery alone.
Thus without defeat,
one attains the Dao of constant victory.
As Zhuangzi said:
*"Accumulating countless small non-victories
becomes great victory."*
Only the Sage can do this—
precisely this is meant.
Thus it is said: *‘The weak is the Dao’s function.’*

The *Yijing* says:
*"Stopped yet yielding, movement never ceases—*
*is this not what is meant?"*

Observe the four seasons:
If winter’s enclosure is not firm,
spring’s growth will not flourish.
Thus one must withdraw and store in winter
before luxuriance blooms in spring.

Observe water’s nature:
It receives filth and grime,
yet abides in softness and weakness,
uncontending—
and none can surpass it in overcoming hardness.
How different is this?

Now, all things generate through form,
yet the formed cannot form
what forms it.
Falling into *you* (being),
the formed is called *wu* (non-being).
Non-being does not abolish being,
thus it manifests in the east and south;
things do not remain in being,
thus they bend toward the west and north in *wu*.
Being and non-being generate each other,
like a cycle—
so with non-being’s movement,
non-being generates being,
and being, reaching its limit,
must return to non-being.
Like east and west,
though their directions differ,
neither can be omitted.

Those drowned in the Dao’s stillness,
like Ji Zhen’s *"doing nothing,"*
or those blinded by the Dao’s movement,
like Jiezi’s *"sometimes acting,"*
are they truly the Dao?

---
**Chapter 41: The Superior Person Hears the Dao**

The superior person hears the Dao
and diligently practices it.

*Huizong’s Annotation:*
The scholar who aspires to the Dao—
the superior person, through sincere and unceasing effort,
accumulates strength over time.

*Exegesis:*
Tools and vessels are mere instruments;
thus neither the high nor low defy them.
The scholar is one whose talent reaches upward—
what is above form is called the Dao.
If his talent ascends, his aspiration toward the Dao is clear.
The so-called superior person
is one who excels as a scholar
and holds high his resolve.
Thus when he hears the Dao,
his devotion is unwavering,
his faith profound.
Daily he knows what he lacks,
and with unflagging effort, he acts—
beginning as a scholar,
ending as a Sage.
As Master Kong said:
*"True accumulation of strength over time."*
He dwells in ease,
guards with firmness,
studies as if never enough,
and only ceases with death.
Never does he wish to rest midday—
as the *Records* say:
*"The utmost sincerity never ceases."*
Like Yanzi, who heard the Dao
and never grew weary—
is this not *"diligently practicing it"*?

The mediocre person hears the Dao,
as if it exists, as if it does not.

*Huizong’s Annotation:*
The mediocre person harbors doubt,
and doubt divides his resolve.
Thus toward the Dao,
he is sometimes drawn,
sometimes repelled.

*Exegesis:*
Above the common man,
one may speak of the superior.
The mediocre person is not of superior wisdom,
so when he hears the Dao,
his heart is not steadfast.
He sees the Dao with doubt,
thus doubts arise.
His resolve is divided,
his spirit congealed.
The mediocre person cannot be taught the superior,
for his heart is not steadfast
and he harbors doubt.
Doubt obstructs him—
sometimes he clings,
sometimes he drifts.
His resolve is divided,
yet he cannot unite.
Like Zixia, who exited to see
the splendor of the world and took delight,
then entered to hear
the Master’s teaching and found joy—
is this not *"as if it exists, as if it does not"*?

The inferior person hears the Dao
and laughs—
not to laugh is not to grasp the Dao.

*Huizong’s Annotation:*
The inferior person lacks faith to uphold it,
and wisdom to comprehend it,
thus he laughs.
The Dao has no form, color, sound, or taste to grasp—
it is far removed
from what the ears, eyes, nose, and mouth desire.
Zhuangzi said:
*"Great sounds do not enter the vulgar ear,
profound words do not dwell in the common heart."*

*Exegesis:*
Music and bait may halt travelers,
but the Dao, when spoken,
is as insipid as non-being.
The inferior person, lacking lofty vision,
aligns with the vulgar.
His convictions are unsteady,
his faith insufficient to guard.
His sight is narrow,
his wisdom inadequate to illuminate.
Thus he scorns the Dao as shameful
and laughs at it.
There is a boundary between them—
his perception is burdened by things,
not the Dao;
this one’s hearing unites with the Dao,
not things.
The Dao is not transmitted through form, color, sound, or taste—
it is far removed
from what the ears, eyes, nose, and mouth desire.
The Dao and things are thus unequal,
bounded by this division.
Hence the shallow may laugh at it.
If the Dao were like things,
burdened by traces,
it could be cast to the masses’ desires—
then the Dao would be small.
Thus not to laugh is not to grasp the Dao.

Zhuangzi said:
*"Great sounds do not enter the vulgar ear,
profound words do not dwell in the common heart."*
When sound reaches subtlety,
few know its harmony;
when words reach subtlety,
few understand them.
The Dao, at its subtlest,
is like great sound and profound speech—
thus the scholar laughs at it.
The *Classic* says:
*"Few know me—
thus I am precious."*
The meaning is the same.

Thus the *Jianyan* says:
*"Illuminating the Dao is like darkness—"*

*Huizong’s Annotation:*
If the sun and moon
shine upon the earth,
this is illumination.
Yet their wisdom is vast yet unshown,
their light borrowed yet not dazzling—
thus it seems like darkness.

*Exegesis:*
The *Yijing* says:
*"The sun and moon push each other,
and illumination arises."*
Master Kong said:
*"In Heaven, none are brighter than the sun and moon."*
The sun shines by day,
the moon by night.
When they hang high in the sky
and illuminate the earth below,
this is illumination.
The Dao’s brilliance is easy to see,
yet it is demonstrated without secrecy—
like the sun and moon’s light upon the earth,
this is *"illuminating the Dao."*

The *Classic* further states:
*"Yet the essence of the Way is profound and unfathomable,
the ultimate of the Way is dim and silent.
Wisdom and strategy are not employed—
its boundless virtue emerges without sound,
as if lost yet still present,
borrowing its radiance yet not dazzling—
thus it seems like darkness.*

*The Way is smooth like simplicity—*
*Huizong’s Annotation:*
*"Though their paths differ, their destination is the same;
one principle governs a hundred deliberations."*

*Exegesis:*
*Smooth* means *easy*—easy is without peril.
*Smooth* also means *constant*—constant is without change.
If there is no peril and no change,
then the Way is singular indeed—
as the *Classic* says: *"The Great Way is most easy."*

Though the Way is singular,
yet all things arise from it,
all things move by it—
as if not singular yet truly singular.

For when threads combine in purity,
they become without distinction,
distinction leads to separation and non-unity.

The Way’s application, though split into myriad branches,
its return is the same;
though dispersed in myriad forms,
its essence is singular—
as the *Systematic Discourse* says:
*"Divergent paths yet the same return,
a hundred deliberations yet one principle."*

*Advancing the Way is like retreat—*
*Huizong’s Annotation:*
*"Yan Yuan used retreat as advancement,
Zhuangzi called it 'Sitting in Forgetting.'"*

*Exegesis:*
*To study is to exhaust principle—*
thus it is called *gain*.
*To cultivate the Way is to deplete the self—*
thus it is called *loss*.
To study and gain each day
is to regard advancement as retreat.
To cultivate the Way and lose each day
is to regard retreat as advancement.

The Master Kong praised Yanzi saying:
*"I see his advancement—
I have not yet seen his cessation."*
Thus Yanzi had already advanced in the Way.

Yet at first he forgot benevolence and righteousness,
then he forgot ritual and music,
and finally reached 'Sitting in Forgetting.'
From his own account,
it seemed like retreat—
yet abandoning form and discarding wisdom,
uniting with the Great Permeation,
this retreat was truly advancement.

Thus Yang Xiong said:
*"In ancient times, Yan Yuan used retreat as advancement—
few could match him,
and Zhuangzi praised his 'Sitting in Forgetting.'"*

*The highest virtue is like a valley—*
*Huizong’s Annotation:*
*"Empty yet responsive,
responsive yet inexhaustible,
empty yet receptive,
receptive yet not hoarding."*

*The Classic* says: *"To be the valley of all under Heaven,
constant virtue is sufficient."*

*Exegesis:*
Zhuangzi said: *"Virtue encompasses the Way."*
He also said: *"Virtue encompasses the Way’s singularity."*

The Way is neither in being nor in non-being,
neither being nor non-being—
only emptiness.
Virtue is likewise.

Thus like a valley’s emptiness yet responsiveness,
responsive yet inexhaustible—
as the *Book of Documents* says: *"If virtue is ample, the body is sufficient."*
Like a valley’s emptiness yet receptivity,
receptive yet not hoarding—
as Zhuangzi said: *"Virtue contains nothing it does not embrace."*

*The Classic* says: *"To be the valley of all under Heaven,
constant virtue is sufficient."*
Is this not so?

*The most brilliant is like humiliation—"*

「The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"To purify and remove all obscurity, one sees no flaw—this is the state of great brightness. To dwell where others abhor, hence it appears as humiliation."*

*The Exegesis* says: *"The Classic states: ‘Bright and clear, penetrating and insightful.’ Zhuangzi said: ‘With this, one clarifies the heart.’ Thus, great brightness is the Way’s manifestation as flawless. How could one not see any flaw without purifying and removing external attachments, without possessing? How could one not recognize the profound and subtle principles without gazing into the depths and silently knowing them? Only when one is flawless can they stand apart from the common yet not be opposed by it, be an eccentric yet not be excluded by others—like water, dwelling where others abhor, hence it appears as humiliation. This is what Zhuangzi recorded of Laozi’s Dao/Way and techniques: ‘Know its brightness, preserve its humiliation.’*

*"Ample virtue appears insufficient—*
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Virtue is all-embracing yet does not consider itself excessive, hence it appears insufficient."*

*The Exegesis* says: *"The highest virtue does not claim virtue, hence it has virtue; the lowest virtue does not lose virtue, hence it lacks virtue. When virtue reaches the point of embracing all without exclusion, it is indeed broad. Yet if one considers it as having excess, then it is not broad. Only when one does not consider it as having excess, does its embrace of all become truly boundless. Thus, it appears as insufficient.*

*"The Autumn Waters rise in season, yet the River Warden is pleased—*
*The Exegesis* says: *"The River Warden, though pleased, is mocked by those of great insight, for he does not know the vastness of the Eastern Sea, believing all beauty to be within himself. This is how one who lacks virtue is laughed at by the wise."*

*"Established virtue appears as theft—*
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The Sage deliberates to act yet does not seek success. Though they act with deliberation, success is inevitable, yet this does not mean they seek it. Thus, their established virtue seems like theft, for they act without intent."*

*The Exegesis* says: *"The Sage’s engagement with the world is without contrivance. Only without contrivance can they act in matters of creation and enterprise, all arising from necessity—what Zhuangzi called ‘not compelled by necessity in affairs.’ Yet even emperors and kings achieve results without deliberate action. Though they deliberate to act, they do not aim for success, yet each action succeeds—this is their established virtue, seemingly like theft, for they act without intent.*

*"The *Analects* say: ‘If one does not abandon the old, the people will not be negligent.’ Negligence leads to superficiality and thinness, yet because they act without intent, it appears as theft.*

*"Pure essence seems like mud—*
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Should one not say: ‘Though polished, it does not gleam’? Though rubbed, it does not dull. Should one not say: ‘Though white, it is not stained’? Though dyed, it is not blackened."*

*The Exegesis* says: *"At first, cinnabar and mineral pigments are vivid, but over time, they fade—fading means change. The True Person’s application of the heart cuts off the root of benevolence yet does not wither it, enriches the source of wisdom yet restrains its expression. In their perception, there is no favoritism; in their hearing, no excess. Their *qi* is full yet without deficiency, finding their own fulfillment without relying on others. Though they distinguish themselves from things, they remain in true clarity while discerning illusion. Though they are stirred, they remain tranquil—this is what is meant by ‘Though polished, it does not gleam.’ Though rubbed, it does not dull.*

*"Immediate purity upon staining—*
*This is what is meant by ‘Though white, it is not blackened.’ Thus, it appears as mud.*

*"The Great Square has no corners—*
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The Great Square is the square of the boundless. Though it has form, it does not sever, hence it has no corners."*

*The Exegesis* says: *"Yin perceives the Spirit’s presence in Yin yet not in Yang; Yang perceives the Spirit’s presence in Yang yet not in Yin. Yin and Yang are unfathomable, and the Spirit cannot be sought in any fixed form. Thus, in the realm of supreme clarity, at the origin of utmost Yang, entering the gate of profound obscurity, at the origin of utmost Yin—this is how the Way has form that can be pointed to. Though the Way has form that can be pointed to, it adapts flexibly to all things, leaving no trace of rigid distinction. It responds spontaneously without fixed patterns—this is the square of the boundless, differing from virtue that has corners. Hence, though it has form, it does not sever.*

*"The *Book of Odes* says: ‘Solemn and dignified, the bearing of virtue.’ Thus, virtue’s corners can be known. Zhuangzi said: ‘No gates, no sides—four-way openness, vast and boundless.’ Thus, the Way’s corners cannot be known. The great vessel takes time to mature—*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The great vessel is the vessel of the boundless. It neither adds to life nor aids in growth, hence it matures late."*

*The Exegesis* says: *"Where there is form, there is name; where there is division, there is limitation—this is the vessel. Yet there is a vessel that transcends form and enters awareness, creating shape while existing beyond measure, standing in the unfathomable, moving without trace, uniquely surpassing all images and precedents—this is the vessel of the boundless. The vessel of the boundless is the Way. The Way cannot be advanced abruptly, nor can one take it without following its natural progression. Hence, it matures late.*

*"Yang accumulates into heat, Yin accumulates into cold—*
*This is not achieved in a single day. Could it be that one adds to life to bring about auspiciousness, or plucks the millet to aid its growth? The great sound is rare in voice—*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"It moves without form, yet responds when touched—hence its voice is rare."*

*The Exegesis* says: *"The source of sound is evident, yet the sound itself has never been emitted—this is what is meant by ‘sound is the great sound.’ Though the blind musician Jian was to listen all night with his head bowed and ears straining, he would not hear its voice, hence it is called rare. Rare implies a broad yet questioning resonance, not something heard by the ears—this is what Zhuangzi meant by ‘moving without form.’ Though unheard, its clarity and turbidity, high and low, shouts and sighs, all respond when touched, and the five tones cannot but resound in their proper order—this is sound, and it is indeed great. The Classic says: ‘Listening yet not hearing is called rare.’"*
