*The Huainanzi* says: *"There is no sound, yet the Five Tones resound within it. Is this not what is meant by the great sound that is rare in hearing?"*

*The Great Image is formless.*
Huizong’s annotation: *"It rests in the profound and obscure, yet when viewed, it cannot be seen—hence it is formless."*
*The Exegesis* says: *"That which shapes form is real, yet the shaping itself has never taken place—this is what is meant by the shaping that is the Great Image. Even if Li Zhu were to gaze at midday, wiping his eyes and raising his brows in expectation, he would not see its form, hence it is called the Great Image. For what is seen is called an image; yet when there is seeing, to say it is formless is to speak of the form that does not manifest. This is what Zhuangzi meant by dwelling in the profound and obscure. Though unseen, all things that have form arise from it—its role as an image is indeed great. The Classic says: ‘The image of the formless.’*
*The Huainanzi* says: *"There is no form, yet from it all forms arise. Is this not what is meant by the Great Image being formless?"*

*The Dao is hidden and nameless.*
Only the Dao is good at lending and completing.
Huizong’s annotation: *"From the Illuminated Dao to the Great Image, all are the Dao. The subtlety of the Dao cannot be sought with intellect nor pursued with form—it is hidden, and yet one cannot make it clear."*

The Sage attains the Dao, thus giving without depletion, responding without exhaustion, and completing all things without claiming possession—merely resting therein. The essence of the Dao is hidden in its namelessness, yet its function is to lend and complete effortlessly. Thus, with diligent practice, one arrives at the formless and rests in the untransformed. The remaining matters suffice for imperial governance. *The Transmission* says: *"Learning begins with becoming a scholar and ends with becoming a Sage."*

*The Exegesis* says: *"The Dao is nowhere without being present, manifesting as essence when in essence, as function when in function—nameless and without substance, yet never separate from named and substantial things. Thus, from the Illuminated Dao to the Great Image, though their names differ, all are the Dao. Yet the utmost subtlety of the Dao is profound and obscure—though not separate from named things, it cannot be sought with intellect. The utmost extremity of the Dao is hazy and silent—though not separate from material things, it cannot be pursued with form. It is indeed hidden. Though one may force a name upon it, the original source of the Dao resists naming, for the Dao is hidden and nameless."*

The Sage attains the Dao, using ultimate emptiness to govern all things and ultimate substance to move all things. Thus, the more one gives to others, the more one possesses; the more one responds, the more one is fulfilled. As the foundation for all things and the reliance of transformation, the Sage rescues all without abandoning any, nurtures all without discarding any. When things come with sensation, the Sage aligns with them in natural harmony, yet never neglects; when things depart, the Sage rests with them in tranquility, yet never burdens. Those who dwell elsewhere take for themselves and receive, yet ultimately return to their origin. Those who dwell here achieve without taking, yet fundamentally incur no cost. All things—small or great—are completed in their proper measure, without claiming possession, simply resting therein.

The principles of the world: *"The departing will depart, yet ultimately return; the deceased will die, yet ultimately revive."* The Dao lends to things, ending where it begins, ceasing yet never ending—hence it is called *qiě* (thus). The essence of the Dao is hidden in its namelessness, yet its function is to lend and complete effortlessly—this lies in diligent practice alone.

If one truly accumulates strength over time, one arrives at the formless and merges with the Dao, resting in the untransformed and enduring through ages eternal. The remaining matters suffice for governing the state and rectifying the world—its surplus is ample for imperial achievements. What is within us is not merely sufficient but abundant.

This section begins with the Superior Person hearing the Dao and practicing diligently, concluding that the Dao lends and completes effortlessly. Thus, from lower learning to higher attainment, those who excel as scholars all follow this path. Even Master Gu said: *"Learning begins with becoming a scholar and ends with becoming a Sage."* This is what is meant.

*End of Volume Eight of the Exegesis on the Dao De Jing*
*Volume Nine of the Exegesis on the Dao De Jing*

**Volume Nine of the Exegesis on the Dao De Jing (continued)**

*Chapter 42: Dao Gives Rise to One*

**Dao gives rise to One.**
Emperor Huizong’s annotation: At the beginning of all things, there is the indeterminate (無), which is nameless; this is where One originates.
The Exegesis states: There is the *Taiyi* (太易) and there is the *Tai’chu* (太初).
The *Taiyi* is the state before *qi* (氣) manifests.
The *Tai’chu* is the first emergence of *qi*.
When it is called the *Tai’chu*, it is because it lies within the realm of the indistinct and formless, when the Dao transforms into *qi*—at that moment, not yet a single thing exists. Vast and empty, it is but indeterminate (無) alone. Thus, before the *Tai’chu*, there was nothing at all—this is the origin of indeterminacy (有無).
As Zhuangzi said, *"indeterminacy"* is what is called *wu* (無), for indeed, there was nothing.
Once it is said to be *wu* (無), then it is *wu’ming* (無名)—nameless.
Moreover, Heaven has no fixed name for things; things arise and their names follow naturally. When their forms are complete and their numbers established, then their abundance or scarcity can be named.
In its state of *wu* (無), not yet a single thing exists, and not yet a single thing, then it is not something that can be sought through appearance, form, sound, or color. Who could name it?
The *Jing* (經) says, *"The nameless is the beginning of Heaven and Earth,"* and again, *"The Dao is ever nameless."*
Thus, the nameless indeterminacy (有無) is the essence of the Dao. The Dao’s essence is fundamentally indeterminate (無), yet it never neglects the determinate (有). Therefore, when it moves, it does not generate indeterminacy (無) but generates the determinate (有), manifesting its signs. When it shifts from indeterminacy to determinacy, numbers begin to establish themselves. Thus, the origin of One is rooted in the *Tai’chu*—this is the Dao descending and emerging.
If the Dao remains indeterminate (無), then it returns to the undifferentiated (浑沦), when *qi* has not yet appeared, and One cannot be obtained. This is what is meant by *"At the beginning of all things, there is indeterminacy (有無), and indeterminacy is nameless (無名)."* This is where One originates—just as Liezi said, *"The easy transforms and becomes One."*

**One gives rise to Two.**
Emperor Huizong’s annotation: Heaven is One, Earth is Two; water emerges, and fire follows; when the essence is complete, the spirit arises.
The Exegesis states: Heaven is noble, Earth is humble; *Yin* (乾) and *Kun* (坤) are thus defined, and so it is that there is Heaven and Earth, establishing differences between the high and low.
*Yin* (乾) is Heaven, its number is odd.
*Kun* (坤) is Earth, its number is paired.
Thus, the *Yijing* (易) refers to Heaven as One and Earth as Two. There is Heaven before there is Earth, and so Earth is Two following Heaven.
Water is deep and empty; it takes form through the solid, and distinction comes later. It is represented in the *Hexagram* (卦) as *Kàn* (坎), which contains One. Hence, One is water.
Fire is restless and swift; it takes form through stillness, and unity comes later. It is represented in the *Hexagram* (卦) as *Lí* (離), which encompasses Two. Hence, Two is fire.
From the Dao descending, water is nearly of the Dao, yet within water there is fire, succeeding in turn; thus, water is One, and fire is Two following it.
Heaven, One, gives rise to water, which in things is the *jing* (精).
Earth, Two, gives rise to fire, which in things is the *shen* (神).
Only the most refined essence in the world can become the most refined spirit in the world; thus, when a person is born, the *jing* (精) gathers the *shen* (神), and the body takes form.
The *jing* (精) is the foundation of the body; when the *jing* is complete, the *shen* reigns supreme. If one guards it and does not lose it, the *shen* and *jing* become one.
Therefore, one who does not depart from the *jing* is called a *shen’ren* (神人)—this is what is meant by *"when the essence is complete, the spirit arises."*

**Two gives rise to Three.**
Emperor Huizong’s annotation: One combines with speech to become Two; Two combines with One to become Three.
The Exegesis states: Numbers begin with One, are established in Two, and completed in Three.
One is odd; alone, it does not generate.
Two is paired; alone, it does not complete.
One odd and One paired, and yet Three arises.
When traced to its source, the Dao’s great origin is singular and without counterpart. Though myriad things exist, they are all of One with me.
Once it is called One, it cannot lack speech, so One is one thing, and speech is another thing—thus, One and speech become Two.
One is odd, Two is paired; odd and paired generate each other, so there is Two when there is Three—thus, Two and One become Three.
From indeterminacy to determinacy, until it reaches Three, it is then gathered to extend toward the infinite—this is the natural progression of numbers, which cannot be altered.

**Three gives rise to the myriad things.**
Emperor Huizong’s annotation: Heaven marks One at the North, Earth marks Two at the South, and the human takes form in Three. When the Three Principles are complete, the myriad phenomena are divided.
The myriad things are called "ten thousand" (万) by number; from this point onward, they cannot be counted exhaustively.
The Exegesis states: Once the undifferentiated is divided, the light and pure gather upward to form the *yang* (陽), and its number is odd, so Heaven marks One at the North.
The heavy and turbid descend and congeal to form the *yin* (陰), and its number is paired, so Earth marks Two at the South.
Yet Heaven governs the original *qi*, and Earth governs the original form; there must be one who governs the original awareness.
Heaven’s role is to give birth and cover; Earth’s role is to bear and load; there must be one who takes charge of teaching and transforming.
Thus, Heaven and Earth establish their positions, and the human takes form within them.
When the Three Principles are complete, those rooted in Heaven belong to the high and associate upward—Heaven is clear and disperses.
Those rooted in Earth belong to the low and associate downward—Earth is turbid and gathers.
The distinct clustering and separation of kinds, the varied states of flight, movement, plants, and growth—
all arise in their ordered ranks, and the myriad phenomena are divided.
Count all the myriad things in the world, and none do not arise from this; hence, the *Yijing* says, *"Heaven and Earth exist, and then the myriad things are born."*
From Three, the myriad things arise; from this point onward, even the most skilled cannot count them exhaustively.
Yet the myriad things are called "ten thousand" (万) because their multitude is named and spoken of—
how could one enumerate their teeming, tangled confusion? Can it be exhausted?

**The myriad things bear *yin* and embrace *yang*, and the harmonizing *qi* unites them.**
Emperor Huizong’s annotation: *Yin* is still and quiet; the myriad things bear it, hence the Superior Person says, *"Enter and rest."*
*Yang* is fluid and accomplished; the myriad things embrace it, hence the Sage says, *"Face the bright and govern."*
There must be the balance between *yin* and *yang*, and this is the harmonizing *qi*.

Zhuangzi says: *"The utmost *yang* blazes resplendent; the utmost *yin* stands solemn and grave.*
*Solemn and grave* issues from Heaven, while *resplendent* arises from Earth. The two interact to form harmony, and thus the myriad things are born."

**Exegesis says:** The luminous function of Heaven, manifesting all things, is called *yang*; the obscure, formless, and unfathomable is called *yin*. *Yang* moves and emits, while *yin* remains still and contracts, hence *yin* is still and quiet. *Yang* warms and flourishes, while *yin* congeals and restrains, hence *yang* is fluid and accomplished.

Since *yin* is still and quiet, the myriad things that follow in its wake—all that is born—bear *yin* and face north. Since *yang* is fluid and accomplished, the myriad things that precede it—all that is born—embrace *yang* and turn south.

Because the myriad things bear *yin*, the Superior Person follows the principle of *yin*, hence he retires at dusk to rest, for night is the peak of *yin*—as the *Yijing* says: *"The Superior Person, at the approach of evening, enters to rest in repose."* Because the myriad things embrace *yang*, the Sage faces the bright and governs, for day is the peak of *yang*—as the *Analects* say: *"With reverence and rectitude, he faces south."*

Without *yin* and *yang*, there would be no harmonizing *qi*. The harmonizing *qi* takes Heaven’s One as its root and Heaven’s Five as its pivot. Without the harmonizing *qi*, there would be no supreme harmony—harmony that does not lean toward *yang* nor cling to *yin*, but remains in the balance of *yin* and *yang*.

Zhuangzi says: *"The utmost *yang* blazes resplendent; the utmost *yin* stands solemn and grave."* Indeed, *yang* is bright while *yin* is obscure; *resplendent* ascends to the heights of supreme luminosity, the origin of the utmost *yang*; *yang* gives birth while *yin* destroys—*solemn and grave* enters the gates of profound darkness, the origin of the utmost *yin*.

*Solemn and grave* issues from Heaven, and Heaven’s *qi* descends; *resplendent* arises from Earth, and Earth’s *qi* ascends. Heaven and Earth intermingle in vaporous transformation, and the myriad things refine into purity. The two interact to form harmony, and thus the myriad things are born.

Yang Xiong also said: *"Heaven and Earth interact, and the myriad things are born."*

What people detest—being solitary, orphaned, and impoverished—is what kings and lords take as their title.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** Things that are not exalted do not perish; affairs that are not at their peak do not decline. This is the operation of *yin* and *yang*, the principle of all things.

Ebb and flow, fullness and emptiness, proceed together without losing harmony—this is the way of the Sage alone. Thus, though solitary, orphaned, and impoverished are what people detest, kings and lords take them as their title. When things reach their extreme, they return; when they are full, they diminish—this is how one remains above without peril.

**Exegesis says:** When things ascend to their peak, they descend; hence the high are brought low. When things reach their fullness, they decline; hence the abundant are diminished. Whether refined into *yin* and *yang* or dispersed as affairs and things, none escape this law: things that are not exalted do not perish; affairs that are not at their peak do not decline. This is the operation of *yin* and *yang*, the principle of all things.

The Sage delves into origins and knows subtleties, thus he consumes when it is time to consume, rests when it is time to rest. He follows the ebb and flow of fullness and emptiness, preserves the great harmony, and moves with the times without deviation. Hence, though solitary, orphaned, and impoverished are names of lowly things, he takes them as his title without shame.

For the trends of the world, extremes must necessarily decline, but he guards his attainment without loss, knowing when to return at the peak. Though fullness must inevitably overflow, he holds his abundance without excess, knowing when to diminish at the point of saturation. Thus does he long endure in wealth and nobility, remaining above without peril?

Therefore, things may be diminished to benefit, or benefited to diminish. **Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** When trees shed their leaves, their roots are fertilized—thus, diminution brings benefit. When the moon is full, it must be eclipsed—thus, addition brings diminution. Heaven and Earth follow the ebb and flow of fullness and emptiness; how much more so for humanity?

Thus, though solitary, orphaned, and impoverished are what people detest, kings and lords take them as their title. Though it seems self-diminishing, it is the way to achieve benefit and maintain noble elevation.

What people teach is also what I teach. The overbearing and violent do not meet a good end. I shall make them my teaching father.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** To subdue the weak with strength, to conquer the gentle with rigidity—this is what people teach. What I teach is different.

The overbearing and violent have *yang* within them and love contention; thus, they have the way of death. The wise observe this and take it as a warning, hence I shall make them my teaching father.

**Exegesis says:** In terms of affairs, the weak cannot resist the strong, nor can the gentle overcome the rigid—thus, to subdue the weak with strength, to conquer the gentle with rigidity, is what people teach.

In terms of the *Dao*, rigidity and strength dwell below, while gentleness and weakness dwell above—indeed, the gentle can overcome the rigid. If so, then my teaching is certainly different from that of others.

Though my teaching differs from others’, what others teach is also my principle—only that what others teach pertains to affairs, while my teaching pertains to the *Dao*. Yet in using strength and weakness as lessons, they are one.

For the rigid and strong are doomed to perish, while the overbearing and violent have *yang* within them and love contention—thus, they have the way of death. The wise observe this and take it as a warning: *"The wicked serve as a resource for the virtuous. When one sees the wicked, one must solemnly reflect upon oneself."*

「Is this not the very principle of my teaching? *Chapter Forty-Three: The Supreme Gentleness of the World*

The supreme gentleness of the world can conquer the supreme rigidity of the world.

The *Huizong* annotation states: *"The rigid will be destroyed, the sharp will be blunted. Only the Sage can accumulate the small to achieve the great victory."*

The *Shuyi* commentary explains: *"The world has ways of constant victory and constant defeat. The way of constant victory lies in gentleness, while the way of constant defeat lies in rigidity.*

Indeed, the way of constant victory lies in gentleness—this is why the ancient *Dao*’s true and magnanimous masters must embody meekness and humility as their outward expression.

To manifest as meek and humble, they root themselves in depth, having no need for rigidity, for rigidity invites destruction. They adhere to restraint, having no need for sharpness, for sharpness invites blunting. Thus, they do not engage with rigidity or sharpness, so others always take the lead, while they alone take the rear. Others always grasp what is solid, while they alone embrace what is empty. They yield to the overbearing and follow the winding paths of others, conforming to the flow of things like water in waves. In their emptiness and stillness, they never falter—this is what is meant by the supreme gentleness of the world.

When it comes to governing all things and directing myriad movements, responding to the boundless, and drawing upon resources without depletion, turning the heavens and earth yet no one perceives its vigor, subduing the realm yet no one attributes it to force—this is also the supreme gentleness of the world, unstoppable in its conquest.

Zhuang Zhou’s discussion of accumulating small advantages to achieve great victory—only the Sage can do this—truly demonstrates profound insight into this principle.

Observe the wind as it moves through the boundless void: it points at me and I conquer it; it adds to me and I conquer it. When it comes to stirring and swaying all things, none dare resist its command. Water, dwelling in gentleness, flows east when guided eastward, flows west when guided westward. Yet when it attacks rigidity and strength, none can precede it—this too exemplifies the idea of accumulating small to achieve great victory. Who would say the supreme gentleness of the world cannot conquer the supreme rigidity of the world?

There is no entering the boundless void.

The *Huizong* annotation states: *"In the *Zhuangzi·Outer Chapters*, it discusses how the *Kui* snake and the wind and the eyes of the *Mu* target each other’s vulnerabilities, yet concludes with the eyes of the *Mu* targeting the heart. The foot’s movement is limited by distance, the eye’s sight by subtlety, but only the spirit is boundless in its reach."*

*"In the *Inner Chapters*, it speaks of the master of life-nurturing, and how much more so the butcher Ding’s art of dismembering an ox."*

*"Ding* is the yin aspect of fire and the manifestation of the spirit. Thus, his blade moves with ease, as if playing without effort."*

If so, then to enter the boundless void—is this not the domain of one who exhausts the formless and wanders without trace? Who else could grasp this?

The *Shuyi* commentary explains: *"When things are constrained by form and *qi*, they are always limited by these very things. The *Kui* snake and the wind and the *Mu*’s eyes are each vulnerable in their own way, yet none can perceive the subtle, hidden movements of the great transformation, which unfolds silently in the depths, without action yet naturally so. They are enslaved by the craft of creation, yet cannot escape their inherent flaws or excesses.*

The *Zhuangzi·Outer Chapters* thus discusses how the *Kui* snake and the wind and the *Mu*’s eyes target each other’s vulnerabilities, yet concludes with the eyes of the *Mu* targeting the heart—all are constrained by their own limitations.

The foot’s movement is confined by near and far, its strength by reach; the eye’s gaze is limited by fine and coarse, its clarity by scope. But the spirit is profound and formless, its operations boundless—swift yet not hasty, reaching yet not forced. Its uses are wondrous, its transmission without limit, yet it never stagnates in form or body.

The spirit’s boundlessness is but emptiness itself. Through emptiness, it finds utility in both entering and exiting—thus, it is without limit and cannot be contained. Is this not precisely what is meant by having no entry into the boundless void?

In the *Inner Chapters*, it speaks of the master of life-nurturing, and how much more so the butcher Ding’s art of dismembering an ox. All things arise through their forms, yet the spirit governs them. The butcher’s task is to harmonize, thus nurturing life.

The earth gives birth to fire, which in humans becomes the spirit. *Ding*, the yin aspect of fire, is the manifestation of the spirit.

When the spirit is employed, the butcher Ding’s art of dismembering an ox is realized. And when one encounters things with the spirit rather than the eye, halting the senses while the spirit acts freely—then the mind illuminates itself, and all *Dao*’s principles are attained. He splits great joints, guides great cavities, yet never falters, his blade moving with effortless grace. The ox is dismembered with ease, its pieces falling apart as if by themselves.

Even the metaphor of the butcher’s blade—though thin and entering gaps—can still meet resistance. But what of one who has no form entering the boundless void? Then, the six orifices and four limbs are fully open, clear and penetrating, with no obstruction or hindrance at all. Only one who exhausts the formless, attains boundlessness, and leaves no trace of the *Dao* could achieve this.

Thus, one understands the benefits of *wuwei*—non-action.

The *Huizong* annotation states: *"The gentleness overcomes rigidity, the void encompasses the having, and the wondrous uses of the *Dao* truly lie here. Abandoning action spares the body’s labor, neglecting life preserves the essence’s integrity—this is why it is beneficial."*

The *Shuyi* commentary explains: *"The *Dao* is ever in non-action yet never fails to act. Non-action is the true, still essence of the *Dao*, unmoving in its nature. Never failing to act is the wondrous operation of the *Dao*, responding to all things without effort.

Only in its non-action yet never failing to act does the *Dao* achieve victory through gentleness, and encompass the having through the void. Its wondrous uses truly lie here.

Gentleness overcoming rigidity—this is what is meant by accumulating in gentleness to achieve rigidity. The void encompassing the having—this is what is meant by non-action generating the void, and the void giving birth to the having.

Its uses are thus wondrous—how could one delight in forced action and labor? The operations of the *Dao* are measured and harmonious, emerging and responding as needed. Though myriad transformations arise, the principle of non-action remains unchanged.

If non-action is embraced, what need is there to abandon action? Why should one labor in vain?」

**生奚足遗？**
Why should life be abandoned?

**弃事则形不劳而全，遗生则精不亏而复。**
To abandon worldly affairs preserves the body unwearied and whole, while to abandon life exhausts the vital essence and leaves it depleted.

**形全精复，与天为一，则其有益孰大於是？**
When the body is whole and the essence restored, one aligns with the Heavenly Way—what greater benefit could there be?

**不言之教，无为之益，天下希及之矣。**
The teaching of non-speech, the benefit of non-action—few in the world attain to these.

**徽宗注曰：**
*The Emperor Huizong’s annotation:*
**不言之教，设之以神；**
The teaching of non-speech is established through the numinous.

**无为之益，不亏其真。**
The benefit of non-action does not diminish its authenticity.

**圣人以此抱朴而天下宾，无为而万物化，故及之者希。**
The Sage embraces simplicity and thus the world comes as guest; through non-action, all things transform—few attain to this.

**疏义曰：**
*Explanatory commentary:*
**言有当愆，孰若不言之教？**
When words falter, what surpasses the teaching of non-speech?

**妙通心术而设之以神，《易》所谓以神道设教，而天下服者，此也。**
To subtly penetrate the mind’s workings and establish it through the numinous—this is what the *Yijing* means by teaching through the Way of the numinous, thereby winning the world’s submission.

**为有成亏，孰若无为之益？**
Action brings completion and loss—what surpasses the benefit of non-action?

**得於自然而不亏其真，庄子所谓无益损乎其真者，此也。**
To gain from spontaneity without diminishing authenticity—this is what Zhuangzi meant by not harming one’s true nature.

**盖天地有大美而不言，则知不言之教固神矣。**
Heaven and Earth possess supreme beauty yet speak not—thus the teaching of non-speech is indeed numinous.

**天地以无为而清宁，则知无为之益固真矣。**
Heaven and Earth achieve tranquility through non-action—thus the benefit of non-action is indeed authentic.

**圣人以天地为宗，故以此抱朴而天下宾，无为而万物化。**
The Sage takes Heaven and Earth as model, thus embracing simplicity and welcoming the world, acting in non-action while all things transform.

**朴者，道之全体，惟道能总摄群有，所谓守小朴而物自宾也。**
Simplicity is the complete essence of the *Dao*; only the *Dao* can encompass all things—this is what is meant by preserving the small simplicity and letting things come as guests.

**无为者，道之自然，惟道能干旋化枢，所谓我无为而民自化也。**
Non-action is the *Dao*’s spontaneity; only the *Dao* can pivot and transform the world—this is what is meant by my non-action while the people spontaneously transform.

**然则不言之教，无为之益，非体无尽道者不能知此。**
Thus, the teaching of non-speech and the benefit of non-action—only those who embody the boundless *Dao* can comprehend these.

**圣人者道之极，故天下希及之。**

*The Sage is the pinnacle of the Dao—few in the world attain to this.*

---
**名与身章第四十四**

**名与身孰亲？**
Between name and body, which is closer?

**身与货孰多？**
Between body and wealth, which is greater?

**徽宗注曰：**
*The Emperor Huizong’s annotation:*
**两臂重於天下，则名与身孰亲？**
If two arms weigh heavier than the world, then between name and body, which is closer?

**生者岂特隋珠之重哉，则身与货孰多？**
If life is not merely as precious as the Duke of Sui’s pearl, then between body and wealth, which is greater?

**至愿在我，名非所亲也；**
When ultimate desire resides within, name is not what is cherished.

**至富在我，货非所多也。**
When ultimate abundance resides within, wealth is not what is excessive.

**惟不知亲疏多寡之辨，而残生损性，以身为徇，若伯夷死名于首阳之下，盗跖死利于东陵之上，岂不惑哉。**
Only those who fail to discern closeness, distance, abundance, or scarcity will harm life and diminish nature, sacrificing the body for name—as Bo Yi starved to death for reputation beneath Chief Yang’s slopes, or Zhi the Robber died for gain at Dongling’s heights—how bewildered they are!

**达生之情，而不务生之所无以为，此有道者之所以异乎俗也。**
To understand life’s essence and not pursue what life cannot provide—this is how the Daoist differs from the common.

**疏义曰：**
*Explanatory commentary:*
**务内观者取足於身，诚其身者真乐内融，则一身之中，众美咸具，何往而不自适。**
Those who cultivate inner observation find sufficiency in the body; those who sincerely embody it experience true joy within—thus within one body, all virtues are complete, and where could one not find self-contentment?

**世之昧者，不能定乎内外之分，辨乎真伪之归，乃矜搅外务，见得忘形，汲汲於名，以危其身，殊不知两臂重於天下，身亦重於两臂，名与身孰亲？**
The ignorant of the world cannot distinguish between inner and outer, nor discern true from false—thus they boast of external affairs, seeing gain while forgetting the body, eagerly pursuing name and endangering themselves. How could they know that two arms weigh heavier than the world, and the body heavier than two arms? Between name and body, which is closer?

**孳孳为利，以害其生，殊不知生固重於隋侯之珠，利固轻於千仞之雀，身与货孰多？**
Striving ceaselessly for profit harms life—how could they know that life is heavier than the Duke of Sui’s pearl, and profit lighter than a thousand *ren* sparrow? Between body and wealth, which is greater?

**惟不介意於傥来而以守身为本，然后能自适其性分，以道为重矣。**
Only by not fixating on transient gains and taking body-preservation as foundation can one naturally align with one’s nature and hold the *Dao* as paramount.

**列御寇居郑圃四十年，人无识者，未闻其干於名。**
Lie Yukou dwelled in Zheng’s fields for forty years; no one recognized him, nor did he seek fame.

**颜回乐箪瓢陋巷，人不堪其忧，未闻其累於货。**
Yan Hui took joy in a gourd and bowl in a humble lane; though others could not endure his poverty, he was never burdened by wealth.

**所重者，道而已。**
What they valued was the *Dao* alone.

**是以至愿名誉并焉，至富国财并焉。**
Thus, ultimate desire encompasses both name and reputation, while ultimate abundance encompasses both state and wealth.

**至愿在我，则不急於人知，名非所亲也。**
When ultimate desire resides within, one is not hasty for others’ recognition—name is not what is cherished.

**至富在我，则万物皆备，货非所多也。**
When ultimate abundance resides within, all things are complete—wealth is not what is excessive.

**於此而不能明亲疏多寡之辨，其为智亦疏矣。**
To fail to discern closeness, distance, abundance, or scarcity here—such wisdom is indeed coarse.

**故有见生於可欲，乃残生伤性，以身为徇而忘其真，如伯夷见名之可欲，饿于首阳之下是也，盗跖见利之可欲，暴于束陵之上是也，此皆昧於至理，惑而不能解也。**
Thus, when life is seen as desirable, one harms life and injures nature, sacrificing the body while forgetting its true essence—as Bo Yi, seeing name as desirable, starved beneath Chief Yang’s slopes, or Zhi the Robber, seeing profit as desirable, perished at Shulei’s heights. These are all ignorant of ultimate truth, bewildered and unable to awaken.

**惟达生之情，不务生之所无以为。**
Only by understanding life’s essence and not pursuing what life cannot provide—

**生之所无以为者，非生之所待而生也，此有道者所以能保身全生而异於世俗者欤？**
What life cannot provide is not what sustains life—this is how the Daoist preserves the body and ensures life while differing from the common.

**得与亡孰病？**
Between gain and loss, which is more harmful?

**徽宗注曰：**
*The Emperor Huizong’s annotation:*
**烈士徇名，食夫徇利，其所得者，名与货，而其亡也，乃无名之朴，不赀之躯，病孰甚焉？**
The heroic sacrifice name, the gluttonous sacrifice profit—what they gain is name and wealth, yet what they lose is nameless simplicity and a body without treasure. Which is more harmful?

**疏义曰：**
*Explanatory commentary:*
**求诸性分之内者，有益於得；**
To seek within one’s nature’s scope benefits gain;

**求诸性分之外者，无益於得。**
To seek beyond one’s nature’s scope benefits gain not.

**有益於得，则其得也裕然而有余；**
When gain is beneficial, its acquisition is abundant and overflowing;

**无益於得，则其得也歉然常不足。**
When gain is unbeneficial, its acquisition is meager and always insufficient.

**夫惟不足，故烈士徇名，则累於名高而不知名公器也，不可多取。**
It is because of insufficiency that the heroic sacrifice name, burdened by high reputation, fail to recognize that name is a public instrument, not to be hoarded.

**黄夫徇利，则累於利厚而不知货恶其弃於地也，不必为己。**
The gluttonous sacrifice profit, burdened by excessive gain, fail to recognize that wealth is detestable when abandoned on the ground, not to be hoarded for oneself.

**盖万物之理相为消长，有得则亡或继之，故诸所病。**
For the principles of all things involve mutual growth and decline—gain is often followed by loss, hence the afflictions.

**然其得之也，是岂真得之哉？**
Yet what they gain—is this truly gain?

**名与货皆傥来之寄耳，寄之其来不可图，其去不可止。**
Name and wealth are but transient lodgings—when they come, one cannot foresee them; when they depart, one cannot stop them.

**而其亡也，乃至於损德塞性，苦身疾作，丧无名之朴而失其至真，忘不赀之躯而舍其至贵，则其病大矣。**
Yet what they lose is to damage virtue and obstruct nature, toil the body and hasten illness, to abandon nameless simplicity and lose its utmost authenticity, to forget the priceless body and forsake its utmost worth—then their affliction is great indeed.

**是故甚爱必大费，多藏必厚亡。**
Thus, excessive love brings great expenditure, and excessive hoarding brings great loss.

「黄夫慕外，则累於利而忘其真；徇物过度，则损德而失其至。 无慕於外，则心固而不费；无累於物，则性全而不失。

**徽宗注曰：无慕於外，则啬而不费。**
*Emperor Huizong’s annotation:* Without coveting the external, one becomes frugal yet unspent.

**无累於物，则守而不失。**
Without being burdened by things, one preserves yet does not lose.

**取予之相权，积散之相代，其至可必，若循环然，岂可长久。**
The interplay of taking and giving, of accumulation and dispersal, is certain in its unfolding—like the cycles of nature, how can it endure long?

**疏义曰：重内而不重外，然后能无慕於外；**
*The Explanatory Commentary:* To value the inner while not the outer—only then can one avoid coveting the external;

**见性而不见物，然后能无累於物。**
To perceive nature while not seeing things—only then can one avoid being burdened by them.

**不生外慕，则无多欲之逐，故啬而不费，非至於甚爱必大费者也。**
Without external craving, there is no pursuit of excessive desires, hence frugality yet no waste—this is not the case where extreme love brings great expenditure.

**不为物累，则无食积之忧，故守而不失，非至於多藏必厚亡者也。**
Without being burdened by things, there is no anxiety from overindulgence, hence preservation yet no loss—this is not the case where excessive hoarding brings great ruin.

**虽然利之在天下，民咸用之，无所不通，有取斯有予，其出入常相权，有积斯有散，其盈虚常相代，乃必至之理。**
Though benefit pervades the world, all people employ it, and it flows everywhere—there is taking, then there is giving; its ebb and flow are ever balanced, its fullness and emptiness ever alternate—this is the inevitable Dao.

**势若循环之不能自已，又乌可恃为长久而认为己有哉？**
Its momentum is like the unceasing cycles of nature—how then can one arrogantly claim it as one’s own enduring possession?

**虽执之使留，且自冥冥中去矣，惜夫世俗莫之悟。**
Though one clings to it to make it stay, it departs from the unseen depths—alas, the common folk do not comprehend this.

**经曰：金玉满堂，莫之能守。**
*The Classic says:* Gold and jade fill the hall, yet none can keep them.

**知足不辱，**
Knowing contentment brings no disgrace,

**徽宗注曰：处乎不淫之度，何辱之有？**
*Emperor Huizong’s annotation:* To abide in the measure of non-excess, what disgrace can there be?

**疏义曰：一性之真，万善充足，道之尊，德之贵，莫之爵而常自然。**
*The Explanatory Commentary:* The singular truth of nature suffuses all virtues—its Dao is exalted, its virtue/power noble, yet it requires no titles and remains ever spontaneous.

**不假势物而无所歉，则亦处乎不淫之度而已。**
Without relying on external things, lacking nothing, one simply abides in the measure of non-excess.

**盖不淫之度者，不皦不昧，适与之当而不过也。**
For the measure of non-excess is neither excessive nor deficient—it harmonizes with the appropriate and does not overstep.

**以此安其性命之情，则真君高世，良贵在我。**
By this, one settles the sentiments of life and nature—then the True Sovereign transcends the world, and true nobility resides within oneself.

**所以为义荣者，物无以尚之，又何辱之有哉？**
Thus, what is deemed honorable in virtue is beyond comparison with worldly things—what disgrace can there be?

**知止不殆，**
Knowing cessation brings no peril,

**徽宗注曰：游乎万物之所终始，故无危殆之患。**
*Emperor Huizong’s annotation:* To wander in the ultimate origins and endings of all things—thus, no peril of danger befalls.

**疏义曰：域於流动之机者，一息不停。**
*The Explanatory Commentary:* Bound within the mechanism of flux, it never ceases for a moment.

**固有默使之者，若有机缄而不得已，其运转而不能自止。**
There is an unseen force that governs it—like hidden springs that cannot be restrained, its motion turns yet cannot be halted.

**虽行之於无止，而不知有真止者。**
Though it moves without end, one does not know of true cessation.

**存势物之徒，不能徐观一性，镒渊静不迁之宗，乃与物为偶，相刃相靡，**
Those who cling to transient things cannot leisurely observe the singular nature—*the Dao of the boundless depths, tranquil and unchanging*—instead, they align with things, clashing and eroding like blades.

**其行尽如驰而莫之能止，且将菁然痕役而不知其所归，岂不危其身耶？**
Their actions rush like galloping steeds, unable to be stopped, yet they labor in vain and do not know their end—does this not peril their very being?

**真人则不然，超然独立乎形器之上，与造物者进，与无终始者为友，**
*The True Person* is otherwise—transcending and standing alone above form and matter, advancing with the Creator, consorting with the Eternal.

**盖将造乎不形而止乎无所化，故能游乎万物之所终始，虚静之中默与道会，**
For they craft the formless and cease at the unchanging—thus, they wander in the ultimate origins and endings of all things, and in emptiness and tranquility, silently converge with the Dao.

**其天守全，其神无却，物无自入而莫之能伤，又何危殆之患哉？**
Their natural defenses are intact, their spirit unobstructed—things cannot intrude of their own accord, nor cause harm—what peril of danger can there be?

**老氏於道常无名亦曰知止所以不殆，盖非通乎物之所造者，不能与此。**
*Master Laozi* speaks of the Dao as ever nameless, yet also calls it knowing cessation so as not to perish—only those who penetrate the mechanisms of things can align with this.

**可以长久。**
Thus, it can endure eternally.

**徽宗注曰：物有聚散，性无古今，世之人以物易性，故好名而徇利，**
*Emperor Huizong’s annotation:* Things gather and disperse, yet nature knows no past or future—the people of the world mistake things for nature, hence they pursue fame and yield to profit,

**名辱而身危。**
Fame brings disgrace, and the body faces peril.

**圣人尽性而足，天下至大也，而不以害其生，故可以长久而与天地并。**
The Sage fulfills nature and finds sufficiency—this is the utmost greatness under heaven—yet does not harm their life, thus they can endure eternally alongside Heaven and Earth.

**疏义曰：即理以观物，则无常之分，有积有做。**
*The Explanatory Commentary:* To perceive things through the Dao of transformation, one sees their impermanent nature—there is accumulation, then there is dispersal.

**即妙以观性，则真常湛专，无古无今。**
To perceive nature through its subtlety, one beholds its eternal truth—unmoved, undivided, without past or future.

**以性逐物则性与物俱驰，以性辨物则物无自而入。**
To chase things with nature is for nature and things to rush together; to discern things through nature is for things to have no power to intrude.

**世之人昧於至理，驰其形性，港之万物，以易所固有，**
The people of the world are blind to ultimate truth—they exhaust their form and nature, ensnared by all things, replacing what they inherently possess,

**於是好名者困於志，徇利者忘其真，**
Thus, those who love fame are ensnared by ambition, and those who yield to profit forget their true essence,

**至於名辱而罔觉，身危而莫顾，交战於利害之域，何可长也。**
To the point where fame brings disgrace yet they remain unaware, their body faces peril yet they do not heed—engaged in battle within the realm of profit and harm, how can they endure long?

**圣人尽性而足，故能贵爱其身，不以害其生，**
The Sage fulfills nature and finds sufficiency, hence they cherish and esteem their body, not harming their life—

**三万岁而一成纯，与天地相为长久。**
Three ten-thousands of years pass in perfect unity, enduring alongside Heaven and Earth.

**大成若缺，其用不弊。**
*Great Completion, though seemingly incomplete*—its application is without exhaustion.

**徽宗注曰：域中有四大，道居一焉。**
*Emperor Huizong’s annotation:* Within the realm are the Four Great Elements, yet the Dao dwells in unity among them.

**体道之全，故可洛於大。**
To embody the Dao in its entirety—thus, one can dwell in the great.

**无成与亏，是谓大成。**
Without completion or deficiency, this is called *Great Completion*.

**不有其成，故若缺。**
Without its outward completion, it seems incomplete.

**知化合变，而不以故自持，故其用不弊。**
To know the transformations of change yet not cling rigidly to any fixed form—thus, its application is without depletion.

**此孔子所以集大成而为圣之时。**
This is how Confucius gathered *Great Completion* and became Sage.

**疏义曰：道未始有封，强为之名曰大。**
*The Explanatory Commentary:* The Dao has never been confined—yet it is named the "Great" by convention.

**既谓之大，则未离方体，寓於域中，而居四大之一焉。**
Though called "Great," it remains within the square and round, dwelling in the realm as one of the Four Great Elements.

**盖道覆载万物者也，天无不覆，地无不载，**
For the Dao is the one that encompasses and nurtures all things—Heaven covers nothing it does not, Earth bears nothing it does not,

**圣人位乎两间，体道之全以配天地，故皆名於大。**
The Sage stands between Heaven and Earth, embodying the Dao fully to harmonize with them—thus, they are named the "Great."

**则其由是道以出应天下，**
Then, through this Dao, they emerge to respond to the world,

**天造地设，发越显着，以彰制作之妙，其成也可谓大矣。**
Heaven and Earth crafting, their expression surpassing and manifest—thus, their completion can be called *Great*.

**然而道无成亏，乌至而倪小大？**
Yet the Dao knows no completion or deficiency—how can one measure it as small or great?

**世之人徒知圣人以道之绪余土直致天下之大利，**
The people of the world merely know that the Sage employs the remnants of the Dao to directly achieve the world’s greatest benefits,

**成天下之大顺，丰功茂烈，巍乎其成，**
Completing the world’s grand harmony, with abundant achievements and towering accomplishments—majestic in their completion,

**而不知道之妙用，本於精神心术之微。**
Yet they do not perceive the Dao’s subtle applications, rooted in the essence of spirit and the refinement of heart-mind.

**果且有成与亏乎哉？**
Does it truly have completion or deficiency?

**果且无成与亏乎哉？**
Does it truly lack completion and deficiency?

**惟其无成与亏，所以谓之大成。」**
Only because it lacks completion and deficiency is it called *Great Completion*.

The principles of all things reach their peak and then decay—
when deeds are accomplished, they return in cycles;
when names are attained, they incur loss.
Only by not clinging to completion can one achieve it without decay.

Thus, the divine person has no deeds—
not because they lack deeds, but because they do not dwell on them when accomplished;
the Sage has no name—
not because they lack a name, but because they do not rely on it when attained.
By abandoning deeds and names and returning them to the common people,
they are called *as if incomplete*.

*Great Completion* is *as if incomplete*—
not only does it not cling to its own completion,
but it also reveals its understanding of transformation and adaptation,
without rigidly adhering to the old ways.

The *Classic of Changes* says:
*"Those who know the Dao of transformation—
do they not know the workings of the numinous?"*

The numinous has no fixed form—
without fixed form, it transforms and nurtures to exhaust its changes,
and through change, it harmonizes to exhaust its benefits.
How could it be bound by the old and constant,
unable to adapt to the ebb and flow?

Thus, its use is inexhaustible.

Confucius gathered the essence of *clearing, harmonizing, and fulfilling*—
the very moment of his sagehood—
and this is why he attained it.

To *gather completion* is to strike like metal and resonate like jade—
to strike like metal is to begin with order;
to resonate like jade is to perfect that order.

The three Sages’ established ways—
this is what is meant by *resonating like jade*.
To hold fast to one principle without change
is only to achieve its end.

But when one strikes like metal and resonates like jade,
it is because they understand transformation and adaptation—
thus able to complete both beginning and end.
Hence, it is said: *Great indeed is Confucius.*

*Great fullness is as if empty*—
its use is boundless.

The *Commentary of Xiuzong* says:
*"Filling all without limit, suffusing all existence—
this is great fullness.
Empty to respond to things, void yet applied—
thus its giving never ceases, its use is boundless.
A wise merchant hides his wealth as if empty,
and great virtue appears as if simple."*

The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"The true essence of the Dao encompasses the six extremes,
vast yet containing all things without trace.
The subtle applications of the Dao flow through the six voids,
broad yet reaching everywhere without obstruction.
The Sage, attaining the Dao’s completeness,
fills all without limit, covering all things unseen,
suffusing all existence, animating the masses—
yet those who seek it find it inexhaustible,
its fullness so profound it cannot be measured.
Yet if fullness cannot yield to emptiness,
it cannot respond to things.
Only by the Dao’s emptiness meeting all things
can one apply it like a deep spring, flowing as needed.

Thus, filling it does not overflow,
drawing from it does not deplete,
giving it does not exhaust—
the more one gives to others, the more one possesses;
the more one shares, the more one has.
Its use is boundless—
hence, *great fullness is as if empty*.

Though it has accumulation, it is insufficient.
Though it has no hoarding, it overflows.
The perfected one has no accumulation—
only emptiness—
thus those who preserve this Dao do not desire fullness.

A wise merchant hides his wealth as if empty,
great virtue appears as if simple—
this is why Laozi spoke thus to Confucius.

To have as if not having,
to be real as if empty—
this is the scholar’s ultimate endeavor,
just as the wise merchant hides his wealth.

*Great whiteness is as if stained*,
great virtue appears insufficient—
here lies the Sage’s emptiness and the gentleman’s demeanor.

From this, one can infer:
*Great fullness is as if empty*.

*Great straightness is as if bent*—
The *Commentary of Xiuzong* says:
*"Following things’ transformations, yielding and winding,
not forcing one’s will—
thus it appears bent."*

The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"The Sage empties themselves to move through the world,
adapting to every situation—
nowhere does this fail to be straight,
for it follows things’ transformations.
Thus, winding yet unharmed,
curving yet unerring,
yielding to strength, following turns—
never rigidly self-assertive.
Though winding and turning,
its alignment is beyond control—
this is *great straightness*, appearing bent.

It is said, *not forcing one’s will*—
this is the meaning, is it not?

Zhuangzi in *Human World* says:
*'The inwardly straight align with Heaven,
the outwardly curved align with humanity.'*
For the world of humanity is to respond to others.
If inwardly aligned with humanity,
one cannot stand apart—
thus, outwardly curved—
and *great straightness is as if bent* becomes clear.*

*Great skill is as if clumsy*—
The *Commentary of Xiuzong* says:
*"Shaping things’ forms—
round, square, curved, straight—
their artistry unseen,
thus it appears clumsy."*

The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"The Sage’s ultimate emptiness fulfills all demands,
yet their skill is hidden—
like Nature shaping forms without effort.
Thus, round and square yet bound by compass and square,
curved and straight yet never straying from the plumb line—
forms manifest, colors shine,
yet no trace of the true master remains.
Though round, square, curved, or straight,
each exhausts its art—
yet *great skill* is beyond measure.
It is said, *their artistry unseen*—
this is the meaning, is it not?

Zhuangzi in *Great Master* says:
*'Treading Heaven and Earth,
carving all forms—
yet not considered skilled.'*
For the *Great Master* is the source of all things.
Since all things take their source,
they give birth without possession—
thus, not considered skilled—
and *great skill is as if clumsy* becomes clear.*

*Great eloquence is as if inarticulate*—
The *Commentary of Xiuzong* says:
*"Speech without words—
this is called great eloquence.
Hui Shi’s many arguments—
his skill is small indeed."*

The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"Utmost speech transcends speech,
achieved through forgetting speech—
then one attains speech’s perfection.
The demand is for *eloquence without words*—
is this not the meaning of ultimate speech?

Zhuangzi says:
*'Great eloquence does not utter a single word.'"*

「Indeed, only by not speaking does one achieve *great eloquence*.
The Sage does not take *skill in argument* as true ability;
deeply cultivating the silent understanding,
where ultimate truth is preserved,
one illuminates without words,
requiring no aid from mere vocal sounds—
thus, it seems *as if clumsy*.
Hui Shi’s many arguments
are but the narrow confines of a skilled debater,
branching and convoluted yet failing to grasp the essence—
how could they ever speak of the *extreme*?
His *Dao* is flawed, and his words never harmonize;
he contradicts without purity,
yet claims his eloquence is vast—
his labor is no greater than a gnat’s or a horsefly’s,
so his *eloquence is small indeed*.
Who knows the *eloquence without words*?

*Agitation overcomes cold*,
*tranquility overcomes heat*,
yet *serene stillness* is the true rectitude for all under Heaven.

Xiuzong’s *Commentary* says:
*"Yang’s movement brings agitation,
thus it overcomes cold.
Yin’s restraint brings tranquility,
thus it overcomes heat.
Both extremes lean toward Yang or Yin,
yet neither aligns with the *Middle*—
one is already ensnared by things,
already embroiled in strife with movement—
how could they ever rectify all under Heaven?
Only by abandoning agitation that overcomes cold,
and tranquility that overcomes heat,
does one remain untainted and clear—
embracing the *Spirit* in stillness,
then all under Heaven will naturally find rectitude.
The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"All things bear Yin yet embrace Yang,
and the *intermingling qi* harmonizes them.
Humans receive the *central qi* of Heaven and Earth,
and inherit the *intermingling qi* of their balance.
When one moves, agitation arises—
it issues from Yang,
and its heat is like blazing fire,
thus it overcomes cold.
When one ceases, stillness arises—
it settles in Yin,
and its cold is like frozen ice,
thus it overcomes heat.
Since both extremes suffer from Yang or Yin’s afflictions,
one clings to Yang in joy,
and to Yin in anger,
yet neither finds satisfaction in the *Middle*—
this is not the *Dao* of *‘moving yet all in accord’*.
If so, one is already ensnared by things,
sinking into the realm of chaotic flux,
already embroiled in strife with movement—
waging war in the paths of gain and loss,
where Yang and Yin become bandits,
and thus cannot rectify all under Heaven.
Only by abandoning agitation that overcomes cold,
and ensuring Yang does not disperse,
and tranquility that overcomes heat,
and ensuring Yin does not grow dense,
can one achieve *serene stillness* as the true rectitude for all under Heaven.
For *untainted clarity* is the utmost of *‘pure and serene’*,
as in *‘how clear it is’*!
To *embrace the Spirit* in stillness
is the utmost of *‘serene stillness’*,
as in *‘motionless and tranquil’*!
If one must be tranquil and clear,
then the *form* is upright and its *shadow* must be straight—
all under Heaven will not *await* rectitude,
yet rectitude will naturally arise.
Laozi, speaking of the *Dao*’s *non-action*, also says:
*‘By not desiring, one attains tranquility,
then all under Heaven will naturally find rectitude.’*
To call this *non-action*,
it is but *‘dull and unadorned’*—
seeking agitation that overcomes cold,
or tranquility that overcomes heat,
there is none indeed.

**Chapter 46: The Way of the World**

When the *Dao/Way* prevails in the world,
warhorses are turned back to manure the fields.

Xiuzong’s *Commentary* says:
*"Those who govern the world with the *Dao*
let the people each take joy in their occupations,
with no strife—
they merely manure their fields.
The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"When the *Dao* is practiced in the world,
the people do not *corrupt* their nature;
when the world is *nurtured* with the *Dao*,
the people do not *shift* their virtue.
If the people do not *corrupt* their nature,
and do not *shift* their virtue,
then they till the soil and eat,
weave and wear,
hold their infants to their chests and play,
pound their bellies and wander—
each takes joy in their occupation,
with no boasting or coveting of others’ desires.
They but exert themselves in their roots,
toil in the southern fields,
and manure their plots—
even if they possess swift horses that chase the wind or lightning,
they will turn them back and not use them—
only those who know the *Dao* of the world can understand this.

When the *Dao* does not prevail in the world,
warhorses are born in the suburbs.

Xiuzong’s *Commentary* says:
*"The strong oppress the weak,
the many tyrannize the few—
even borders cannot be upheld."

The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"How can humans gather in groups? By *division*.
How can *division* be implemented? By *righteousness*.
*Righteousness* with *division* brings harmony,
and harmony brings unity.
Groups without *division* lead to strife,
and strife brings chaos.
The strong rely on force,
sometimes even oppressing the weak.
The many rely on numbers,
sometimes even tyrannizing the few.
They fight over land,
killing until the fields are filled—
warhorses are born in the suburbs,
and borders cannot be upheld—
this is because they do not know
to govern the world with the *Dao*."

No greater crime exists than *desiring what is forbidden*—
Xiuzong’s *Commentary* says:
*"If one does not see what is forbidden to desire,
the heart will not be disordered.
Human desires can reach the point
where they endanger life and virtue to contest them—
this is where crime begins."*

The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"The heart is naturally pure and clear,
yet desires do not sprout without provocation.
Things entice from without,
and emotions give rise to desires.
The *will* is the *qi*’s leader,
and *qi* is the *body*’s fulfillment.
If one uses the *will* to command the *qi*,
and engages with things without restraint,
then the *qi* will grow exhausted,
and the heart will begin to disorder.
Thus, if one does not see what is forbidden to desire,
the heart will not be disordered.
The ignorant masses are unaware of this.
Those burdened by fame will see the *desire for reputation*,
and those burdened by profit will see the *desire for gain*—
struggles of gain and loss wage war in the breast,
reaching the point where they endanger life and virtue to contest them—
greed, corruption, deceit, and falsehood
all arise for selfish ends—
this is how crime begins."*

No greater calamity exists than *not knowing contentment*—
Xiuzong’s *Commentary* says:
*"Moderation brings blessing,
excess brings calamity.
Knowing contentment brings no shame—
what calamity could there be?"*

The *Explanatory Notes* say:
*"When Yang is bright and clear, the state of Jin is rich—*this* brings blessing.
When Yin is dark and obscured, the state of Zhou retreats—*this* brings calamity.
Thus, blessing thrives in *‘spreading’*,
while calamity arises in *‘withdrawing’*.
Yet blessing and calamity are neighbors,
and calamity and blessing rely on each other—
so moderation brings blessing,
while excess brings calamity.
The *Classics* say:
*‘No blessing is greater than having no calamity.’*
This is why.
If one does not know contentment
and strives for ever more gain,
then even noble families will have *spirits* lurking in their halls—
what calamity is greater than this?
Only by abiding in the *measure of non-corruption*
does one know contentment without shame—
what calamity could there be?"

No greater fault exists than *desiring to obtain*—
Xiuzong’s *Commentary* says:
*"To desire and then obtain
is what people blame."*」

As the valley empties and responds,
it responds yet does not cling.
The one who follows the Dao is not without desire—
desire lies in *not* indulging it.

If one exchanges oneself for things,
sees gain and forgets form,
and cannot let public virtue overcome private desire,
this is what people reject—
what greater fault is there?

Thus, the sufficiency of *knowing sufficiency*
is always sufficient.

Xiuzong’s *Commentary* says:
*"When people see what is desirable,
they do not know sufficiency.
Not knowing sufficiency leads to desiring to obtain,
and desiring to obtain sparks contention—
calamity and chaos arise,
even to the point where warhorses march at the city’s gates.

Yet if one knows sufficiency
and each abides in their nature’s allotted portion,
applying no cleverness or artifice—
merely using food and drink—
what contention could there be?"*

**Chapter Forty-Seven: Not Leaving the Door**

Not leaving the door, one knows the world;
not peering through the window, one sees the Way of Heaven.

Xiuzong’s *Commentary* says:
*"Though the world is vast, the Sage knows it through wisdom;
though the Way of Heaven is distant, the Sage perceives it with the heart.
Wisdom encompasses all things—
nothing is too far to examine—
so there is no need to leave the door.
The heart, subtle in its divine clarity,
penetrates even the most hidden—
so there is no need to peer through the window.
Zhuangzi said:
‘His swiftness between bowing and raising
reaches twice beyond the four seas.’
This is how the Sage operates secretly and transforms alone."*

*Explanatory Notes* say:
*"Though the multitude of living beings is vast
and worldly affairs are complex,
the world, though great, cannot hide its nature—
this is ‘knowing it through wisdom,’
for one discerns it by principle.
The Way of Heaven is boundless and formless—
yet if one gazes at the formless,
the Way of Heaven, though distant, shares its mystery—
this is ‘seeing it with the heart,’
for one perceives it in its intangibility.
The Dao descends and gives rise to wisdom;
the Dao ascends and gives rise to spirit.
Wisdom and spirit are one—
the Sage’s spirit, boundless in its operation,
enters and exits without limit,
so wisdom and spirit together
can encompass all things—
though distant as the eight extremes,
nothing escapes their grasp.
How could one need to leave the door to know the world?
Zhuangzi said:
‘Great wisdom observes the distant;
near wisdom encompasses all things.’
The Dao-heart is subtle—
grasping it is impossible—
it permeates Heaven and permeates Earth,
so the heart unites with the Dao.
The Dao’s great root precedes Heaven and Earth,
moving yet accumulating nothing—
the heart, empty and gathering the Dao,
thus subtly manifests the divine—
though hidden in the formless,
nothing remains unseen.
How could one need to peer through the window to see the Way of Heaven?
Yang Xiong said:
‘Heaven and Earth are divine yet unfathomable,
yet the heart’s subtlety would fathom them—
this is how it is.’

Though one’s perception is boundless,
wisdom is great;
though one’s insight penetrates the hidden,
the heart is divine.
Wisdom knows nothing unknown,
the heart sees nothing unseen—
both arise from the same source of emptiness and stillness,
vast and penetrating,
so myriad transformations and changes
have no end.
The Six Perfections and Four Openings
are all within them.
Their mysterious operation begins here—
this is why the Sage, as Zhuangzi said,
discusses the human heart’s swiftness between bowing and raising,
reaching twice beyond the four seas—
only through such transformation could this be possible.
Thus the Sage operates secretly and transforms alone.

The *Liezi* says:
‘The skilled transformer’s Dao is subtle yet harmonious in agreement.
Subtle operation reveals the mystery of transformation,
as if a true sovereign moves without trace;
the foundation of solitary transformation
is like motion yet unable to stop itself.
From this, we see:
The Sage does not leave the door to know the world,
nor peer through the window to see the Way of Heaven—
how could this merely be attained through clever reasoning or subtle techniques?
The farther one goes,
the less one knows.’*

Xiuzong’s *Commentary* says:
*"Does this mean one has glimpsed the heart of Heaven and Earth?
Taking from the near—within the body—
all principles are complete.
Seeking in yin and yang, in measurements,
one only drifts farther from the Dao,
knowing less and less."*

*Explanatory Notes* say:
*The *Yijing* speaks of *‘seeing the emotions of Heaven and Earth’* in *Great Strength*
and *‘seeing the heart of Heaven and Earth’* in *Repetition*.
*Great Strength* is vast yet interacts with things;
*Repetition* is subtle yet distinguishes itself from things.
Only because it distinguishes itself from things
does it, when all things act together,
follow the path of emergence and return.
I observe that movement must return to stillness,
and emergence must return to repetition—
thus, I perceive the heart of Heaven and Earth.
Heaven and Earth are too vast to measure in an instant,
but if one makes the heart empty,
merging it with the boundless,
then the heart of Heaven and Earth *is* my heart.
What makes *Repetition* noble
is its stillness, not its movement and emergence."*

「Just by observing this, one realizes that the Dao/Way is near at hand and need not be sought from afar—
it is sufficient to take it from oneself.

Within a single body, all things are fully present;
one who practices inner observation finds everything already complete—
the most profound traces of the universe are all here.

If one can attain emptiness, hold fast to stillness,
and gather them together with the mind,
then the Dao/Way will dwell within you—
why wait to seek it far away?

To seek it through *yin-yang* (阴阳),
the Dao/Way may never leave *yin-yang*,
yet *yin-yang* cannot fully contain it;
to seek it through *measurements and calculations* (度数),
the Dao/Way may be abundant in *measurements and calculations*,
yet *measurements and calculations* cannot exhaust it.

Some may take five years yet fail to grasp it,
others twelve years yet still not attain it—
the farther one strays from the Dao/Way,
the less one truly knows.

The Dao/Way is like a great road,
bright and easily seen—
how difficult can it be to know?
The trouble lies only in not seeking it.

If one turns inward to seek within oneself,
then without form, the mind becomes complete—
one will advance beyond the mere breadth of knowledge,
and what fear is there of knowing too little?

Thus, the Sage does not act yet knows,
does not look yet names,
does not do yet achieves.
Emperor *Huìzōng* (徽宗) commented:
*With my understanding, I know the world—*
this is called *not acting yet knowing* (不行而知).
*With the mind of the Five, I perceive the Dao/Way—*
this is called *not looking yet naming* (不见而名).
If one *does not act yet knows*,
and *does not look yet names*,
then what need is there for action?
The Sage stands firm, and success comes naturally—
this is called *not doing yet achieving* (不为而成).

The *Commentary* says:
*To act and then know—*
the places one’s feet reach are near,
and one cannot perceive what lies far;
*to see and then name—*
what the eyes can grasp is shallow,
and one cannot illuminate what is hidden.
Only by *knowing the world with my understanding*
can one transcend and recognize the distant—
sufficient to penetrate the principles of the world,
even without stepping outside one’s door,
this is called *not acting yet knowing*.
Only by *perceiving the Dao/Way with my mind*
can one gaze deeply into the mysteries—
sufficient to explore the subtleties of the Dao/Way,
even without peering through a window,
this is called *not looking yet naming*.
If one *does not act yet knows*,
and *does not look yet names*,
then the vastness of the world,
the remoteness of the Dao/Way,
have never been a concern—
one follows nature effortlessly, without favor or bias,
discarding knowledge and artifice while aligning with the natural order—
what need is there for action?

By dwelling in *non-action* (无为之事),
the world will naturally achieve its functions,
thus the Sage skillfully employs subtle methods
and alone attains the clarity of the *Zhāokuàng* (昭旷).
This is indeed the Dao/Way of their own.

The world cannot grasp or know it—
perhaps they only see the Sage’s firm success.

Did *Zhuāngzǐ* (庄子) not say:
*The Emperor-King achieves without action,
and the world is transformed*—
is this not the meaning of *not doing yet achieving*?

*Chapter Forty-Eight: The Daily Progress of Learning*
*Learning is a daily advancement,*」

「The Emperor Huizong annotates: *Learning seeks to attain the Dao/Way*, beginning with the cultivation of the scholar and culminating in the perfection of the Sage. With daily progress, the Dao accumulates within oneself. Confucius spoke to Yan Yuan, saying: *"I see his advancement."*

The *Cushui* (疏义) explains: *The Dao cannot be directly attained, yet there are things said to be attainable—only learning can achieve this.* Indeed, learning encompasses both the *Tian Dao* (天道, Heavenly Dao) and the *Ren Dao* (人道, Human Dao). One cultivates the desired virtues until they reach the ineffable divine, and practices filial piety until it manifests as the Sage’s Heavenly Dao—this is what the *Yǔ* (语) refers to as *"the scholar attains the Dao through learning,"* and what *Mòzǐ* (苟子) means by *"beginning as a scholar, ending as a Sage."* Only when one earnestly pursues learning will they see daily gains and forget their past ignorance, monthly progress and retain their acquired knowledge. Though daily calculations may seem insufficient, yearly reflections will yield abundance, and the Dao will naturally reside within you. This is truly *"daily advancement, the Dao accumulating within oneself."*

Yan’s son knew to emulate the lofty virtue of the *Jian Gao* (坚高), yet forgot the labor of striving upward. When asked about *ren* (仁), he diligently sought to practice it, and upon hearing its merits, he wholeheartedly embraced them without hesitation. Thus, Confucius said of him: *"Daily I see his advancement."* Is this not fitting?

*The Dao/Way is attained through daily diminishment.*
The Emperor Huizong annotates: *To attain the Dao is to abandon one’s physical form, discard sharp intellect, sever from the body and forget worldly affairs—thus it is called "diminishment."*

*Lian Boyu* (连伯玉) reached sixty years and yet transformed sixty times. The *Cushui* explains: *If one knows that the body is not truly one’s own possession but merely a borrowed shape from Heaven and Earth, then they will not be burdened by the body and thus abandon physical form.* If one understands that their lifespan is finite while knowledge is boundless, then they will not cling to cleverness and thus discard sharp intellect. By severing from the body, the body cannot hinder; by forgetting worldly knowledge, the mind is unconstrained. Neither internal fluctuations nor external conformity exist—when engaged in affairs, not a single hair’s worth is spared, yet all worldly concerns vanish. Thus, it is called *"diminishment."*

*Zhuāngzǐ* said: *"Lian Boyu reached sixty years and yet transformed sixty times."* This means that with each passing year, he underwent transformation until, at sixty, he had transformed sixty times—truly one who forgets the passage of years and aligns with the natural *Hua* (化, transformation) of humanity.

Observing Lian Boyu’s conduct, it is clear that the Master wished to minimize his faults yet could not fully achieve it—thus, *"reaching sixty years and yet transforming sixty times"* is understandable.

*Diminish again, and again diminish,* until one attains *non-action* (无为) yet *achieves all* (无不为).

The Emperor Huizong annotates: *Learning exhausts principles and encompasses all things; the Dao fulfills one’s nature and reaches the ineffable.* To diminish again and again is to arrive at *non-being* (未始有). *Non-being* is *non-action*. In stillness, one remains unmoved—this is *non-action*. Yet one responds to all things and naturally harmonizes with the world’s patterns—this is *achieving all*.

*To attain stillness within oneself is the Dao of inner Sagehood; to act and engage with the world is the work of outer Kingship.* Both originate from this principle, hence *Zhuāngzǐ* says: *"Stillness leads to Sagehood, movement leads to Kingship."* This is followed by *non-action* (无为) to achieve *Zhēn* (尊, reverence).

Thus, those who take possession of the world often rely on *non-action* (无事). But once they engage in worldly affairs, they are no longer sufficient to possess the world.

The Emperor Huizong annotates: *The world is a great thing. Those who possess great things cannot be possessed by them; if one is possessed by things yet transcends them, then they can possess all things.* Those who take possession of the world often rely on *non-action*—this is clear.

*The world is a divine instrument; it cannot be forced.* Those who force it ruin it, and those who cling to it lose it. Hence, once they engage in worldly affairs, they are no longer sufficient to possess the world.

The Sage embodies the Dao’s truth to govern themselves. The rise of an Emperor-King and the establishment of a King’s realm both occur when they respond to necessity. Even if they forget the world, the world will naturally submit to them—just as *Tàiwáng Zhí Fù* (太王直父) avoided harm from the *Dí* (狄) people, did not let nourishment injure his life, nor let profit burden his form, and thus established his kingdom beneath the *Qíshān* (岐山).

The *Cushui* explains: *If one is confined by things, there must be something beyond them to govern them. If one is enslaved by instruments, there must be something beyond instruments to command them.* Though the *Liùhé* (六合, the six directions—Heaven, Earth, and the four cardinal points) may seem vast, they are still confined by things. What transcends them is the Dao. Though instruments may seem powerful, they are still enslaved by them. What transcends instruments is the Dao. To embody the Dao is to have no worldly affairs—thus, their use of the world is abundant. This is what *Zhuāngzǐ* meant by *"the world is a great thing; those who possess great things cannot be possessed by them, yet they possess all things."* Hence, it is clear that those who take possession of the world often rely on *non-action*.

If one does not embody the Dao, they will cling to affairs and thus be insufficient in their use of the world. The *Classics* say: *"The world is a divine instrument; it cannot be forced. Those who force it ruin it, and those who cling to it lose it."* This is the case.

The Sage governs themselves with the truth of the Dao. The rise of an Emperor-King and the establishment of a King’s realm both occur when they respond to necessity. Even if they forget the world, the world will naturally submit to them—just as *Tàiwáng Zhí Fù* did not let nourishment injure his life, nor let profit burden his form, and thus avoided the *Dí* (狄) people’s calamity, establishing his kingdom beneath the *Qíshān*.

*End of Volume Nine of the *Zhēn Dàodé* (真道德) Annotated Classic.*
*Beginning of Volume Ten of the *Zhēn Dàodé* (真道德) Annotated Classic.*」

**Volume Ten of the *Zhēn Dàodé* (真道德) Annotated Classic**

**Commentary by the Scholar Jiang Cheng on Chapter Forty-Nine: "The Sage Has No Fixed Heart"**

The Sage has no fixed heart, yet takes the hearts of the people as their own.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: The heart of the Sage is the luminous reflection of all things.

Empty yet able to receive, still yet able to respond—
like a mirror reflecting forms, taking all their beauty and ugliness,
like a valley echoing sounds, embracing all their vastness and minuteness—
how could there be a fixed heart?

The commentary observes all things and knows their nature,
following the people and nothing more.
This is what is meant by "taking the hearts of the people as their own."

Zhuangzi said: The lowly cannot be ignored, for they are the people.

The commentary explains: The heart is empty and unified, still and responsive.
Only emptiness can give rise to substance,
only stillness can embrace movement.
Emptiness and stillness are the root of all things.

The Sage penetrates the truth of all things and upholds their root,
thus encompassing all without discrimination,
and perceiving the four quarters with luminous knowledge.
Zhuangzi speaks of the Sage’s heart as the luminous reflection of all things,
meaning that emptiness allows for reception,
stillness allows for response—
thus, there is no fixed heart.

It is like a mirror:
not inclining nor welcoming,
yet all beauty and ugliness are revealed,
no preference nor selection made.

It is like a valley:
receiving and responding,
all vastness and minuteness arrive,
no rejection nor favor—
how could there be a fixed heart?

Though the *Yì* warns against establishing a heart that does not waver (*wùdài*), and Mencius says that not wavering over one’s possessions is a virtue, the Sage here speaks of no wavering heart—why?

The Dao’s meaning is that wavering arises from clinging to change,
while the Sage’s "no fixed heart" is not the same as the *Yì*’s warning against wavering.
The commentary observes all things and knows their nature,
following the people and nothing more—
thus, their "no fixed heart" is the very means by which they attain true constancy.

Beholding the Way of Heaven,
its seeing and hearing come from the people,
its wisdom and awe come from the people—
for it has no heart of its own.

The Sage has no heart,
only aligning with Heaven—
thus Zhuangzi says: The lowly cannot be ignored, for they are the people.

The virtuous, I treat as virtuous;
the non-virtuous, I also treat as virtuous—
then virtue is complete.
The trustworthy, I trust;
the untrustworthy, I also trust—
then trust is complete.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: The virtuous and non-virtuous contradict each other,
the sincere and insincere mock each other—
common people’s emotions naturally create distinctions of good and bad,
truth and falsehood. How could this be virtue?

Virtue as virtue reveals that all actions are without exception virtuous,
so even the non-virtuous are treated as virtuous;
virtue as trust reveals that all emotions are without exception trustworthy,
so even the untrustworthy are treated as trustworthy.
True and false are both forgotten,
right and wrong are unified—
this is the person of complete virtue.

This is how Shun regarded the *Xiàng* (象),
sincerely trusting and rejoicing in it,
not regarding it as false—
thus the Sage does not distinguish between truth and falsehood.

While the Sage is in the world, their words are but clamor,
yet they muddy the hearts of the people.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: When in the world,
the Sage shares in the people’s joys and sorrows,
even if they have no fixed heart,
they cannot fail to take heed.

Thus, in governing themselves,
they are cautious and vigilant,
never at ease or idle.
In governing the world,
they equalize virtue and non-virtue,
unify trust and insincerity,
forgetting praise and blame,
and blending all into one.

The commentary explains: Knowing this and discerning it is how the Sage governs themselves;
awakening to this and merging with it is how the Sage governs others.

Thus, they demand much of themselves with thoroughness,
yet treat others with lightness and restraint.
When in the world,
they are ever watchful,
sharing in the people’s joys and sorrows,
cautious and vigilant in their warnings,
diligent in their prudence—
though they have no fixed heart,
they dare not neglect it.
Thus, even in rest, they are not at rest,
cautious and vigilant, never idle.

When governing the world,
they respond to all things,
merging beauty with ugliness,
committing right and wrong to their proper courses,
equalizing virtue and non-virtue,
unifying trust and insincerity,
embracing all the strange and wondrous—
the Dao flows as one,
and they forget praise and blame,
merging all into simplicity.

The people all focus their ears and eyes upon them,
yet the Sage treats them as children.
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: Heaven’s vision comes from the people’s vision,
Heaven’s hearing comes from the people’s hearing—
thus the Sage takes the people as their heart.
The Sage acts, and all things observe them—
thus the people all focus their ears and eyes upon them.
The people rely solely on the Sage’s vision and hearing,
so the Sage is the parent of the people.

With tender care and compassion,
like protecting an infant,
they cover all beneath Heaven with benevolence.

The commentary explains: Though Heaven is high, its oversight is humble;
though Heaven is distant, its scrutiny is near—
for the Way of Heaven has no heart,
only taking all things as its heart.

Thus, the Sage never uses their own eyes,
yet through the people’s vision sees all things—
never uses their own ears,
yet through the people’s hearing hears all things.
Only Heaven is clear and wise,
only the Sage is timely and authoritative,
yet they simply take the people’s heart as their own heart.

Taking the people’s heart as their own heart,
they harbor no private thoughts yet grasp the people’s hearts,
so their actions are manifest—
like the great sun rising in the east,
those with eyes and limbs await its light to complete their tasks.
Who among the people would not focus their ears and eyes upon it?
This is what the *Yijing* says:
*"The Sage acts, and all things observe them."*

The Great Vision is above,
while the lower vision brings forth transformation—
they observe the rites and respond,
they listen to the songs and move accordingly,
so the people rely solely on the Sage’s vision and hearing.

The Sage, as the parent of the people,
treats them with love, fearing only that it may not be sufficient,
and protects them, fearing only that they may be harmed—
nurturing and fostering them,
guarding and sustaining them,
their virtue is profound indeed.

Yet the Sage’s care for the people
is not limited to that of a parent,
so their tender compassion and protective embrace,
like shielding an infant,
cannot be exhausted.

The Sage’s governance and transformation
cover all beneath Heaven—
this is why.

**Chapter Fifty: Life and Death**

Life and death,
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
All things emerge from the *machine* and return to the *machine*.
When Heaven’s *machine* expands, life begins;
when Heaven’s *machine* contracts, death ends.
The living are the initial workings of *Zao-hua*,
the dead are the transformations of *Yin-Yang*.

The commentary explains:
There is no motion without something arising,
nor anything lasting without returning to nothingness—
the interplay of existence and nonexistence
is like a mechanism that cannot act of its own accord.

All distinctions of kind and group,
all variations in movement and stillness,
are thus governed by this principle—
so all things emerge from the *machine* and return to the *machine*.

Moreover, when things arise,
they do so swiftly and with urgency,
without motion that does not alter,
nor time that does not pass.
From nonexistence to existence,
then the *void* transforms into *spirit*,
*spirit* into *qi*,
*qi* into *form*—
Heaven’s *machine* expands, and with it life begins.

From existence back to nonexistence,
then *form* transforms into *qi*,
*qi* into *spirit*,
*spirit* into *void*—
Heaven’s *machine* contracts, and with it death ends.

Yet life is the beginning of death,
and death is the completion of life—
what dies here may yet live there,
for life and death are inconstant,
and none know their measure.
Thus, the living are the initial workings of *Zao-hua*,
and the dead are the transformations of *Yin-Yang*.

The initial workings of *Zao-hua* are called *refined qi forming matter*,
the transformations of *Yin-Yang* are called *wandering souls*.
