Those who follow life are thirteen in kind,
those who follow death are thirteen in kind.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
Those who follow life and death
are bound by the mechanisms of emergence and return,
and thus cannot escape their fate.

The commentary explains:
When the Great Dao is obscured,
it splits into many paths.
Those who delight in life are burdened by form,
yet they do not realize that the body is not their own—
they are ensnared by the Dao’s motion,
leaning on the force of *Yin-Yang* to become followers of life.

Those who seek stillness forget their bodies,
yet they do not grasp the form of non-form—
they are drowned in the Dao’s tranquility,
halted in decay, and become followers of death.

To follow life and death,
one is already confined to the mechanisms of emergence and return,
and thus cannot escape the bonds of life and death—
both are incomplete aspects of the Dao.

The numbers begin at One,
are established at Two,
and perfected at Three.
Heaven and Earth combine to form Ten,
so Three is the completion of numbers,
and Ten is their fullness.

Those who follow life and death
pursue their own desires,
establishing their own methods,
and using numbers to justify them—
thus, in their Ten, they attain only Three,
for those confined to the mechanisms of emergence and return
cannot fully escape the cycles of *Yin-Yang*.

The people’s lives,
when they stir toward death’s domain, are also thirteen in kind.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
They blame fate while abandoning principle,
forget life while pursuing gain—
when the people’s lives stir toward death’s domain,
what choice is there between life and death?

The commentary explains:
Life is not my own,
so those who blame fate are wrong.
Thus, those who understand the nature of life
do not labor for what life cannot provide—
if one even blames fate while abandoning principle,
there are those who retain their form yet lose their life.

Life is the radiance of virtue,
so those who forget life are also wrong.
Thus, those who honor life
do not burden their bodies with profit—
if one even forgets life while pursuing gain,
there are those with excess yet whose bodies are not nourished.

The common people are deeply confused and foolish,
not enlightened nor discerning—
they stumble blindly forward,
floundering in their vitality,
revering those who cherish life with great depth yet unaware of abandoning it,
forgetting life while harming their nature, yet unaware of preserving it,
seeking alms from the kitchen and begging at the walls—
they stir toward death’s domain,
though their forms may not yet perish, their hearts are already dead.

Zhuangzi called them *Xiao-ren*,
Yangzi called them *Ye-ren*—
these are they.

Though they are called "not dead," what benefit is it?
Then their lives, what choice is there between life and death?

Why so?
Because of their excessive attachment to life.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
Those who follow life delight in life yet burden their forms,
those who follow death seek stillness yet forget their bodies.
When they stir toward death’s domain,
the crippled and the lame are everywhere,
and the prisons and punishments are in sight—
these are all those who do not know
that the body is a great affliction,
that life is limited,
and who err in their excessive reverence for living.

The ancient ones who attained the Dao
did not let wealth and honor harm their bodies,
nor did poverty and lowliness burden them with gain—
they did not rejoice in longevity,
nor lament in early death,
they awoke to solitude and saw the One,
thus they could transcend past and present
and enter the realm of neither life nor death.

The commentary explains:
The Dao is not made clear
because the wise overreach it,
and the unworthy fall short.
Those who follow life and death,
the wise are those who overreach,
and the unworthy are those who fall short—
those who stir toward death’s domain
are the unworthy who fall short.

Those who cling to life are drowned in the delusion of what compels them,
unable to forget the years and lose themselves,
ever rejoicing in life while burdening their forms.

Those who cling to death are blinded by the notion of nothingness,
unable to cherish life while preserving their forms,
ever turning toward emptiness and forgetting the body.

The debates of these two schools
each grasp one side—
examining them to their own liking,
though they do not encompass all,
they are but one-sided scholars,
thus they cannot instill fear in the people.

Those who court death’s domain
are akin to those who fail to nurture life.

For those who suffer internal punishments,
the yin and yang energies harm them;
for those who suffer external punishments,
metal and wood weapons assail them.

Only those who flee external punishments
are the ones who face the gallows in succession,
who fill the prisons in sight,
and who fall into the nets of crime in great numbers—
these are all those who do not know
that the body is a great exhaustion,
that life is limited,
and who cling too deeply to living.

The ancient ones who attained the Dao were different from these:
they knew that things abound while the form does not sustain them,
thus wealth and honor did not harm life through excessive nourishment—
different from those who devour life while defying nature;
they knew that the form does not perish while life fades,
thus poverty and lowliness did not burden the form with gain—
different from those who forget life while pursuing profit.

They knew of that which is neither ever dead nor ever born,
thus they did not rejoice in longevity nor align with those who cling to life;
they knew that what endures in me is unforgotten,
thus they did not lament early death nor align with those who cling to death.

With insight clear and vision penetrating,
they stand alone above all things,
nothing can pair with them—
they exist before Heaven and Earth without beginning,
after Heaven and Earth without end,
spanning antiquity and the present while ever enduring.
How could this be the excessive reverence for life?

As Zhuangzi said:
*"Awakening to solitude and seeing the One,
thus they could transcend past and present
and enter the realm of neither life nor death."*
This is how they attained it.

It is said that those who master nurturing life
walk on land without fear of rhinoceros or tiger,
enter battle without armor or weapons.

The rhinoceros has no place to thrust its horn,
the tiger has no place to strike with its claws,
weapons have no place to lodge their blades.
Why is this?
Because they have no place of death.

The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:

Those who master nurturing life preserve their form intact and restore their essence, uniting with the Dao and thus safeguarding their natural defenses. Their spirit is unshaken, their hidden movements unimpeded, their tread upon fire unburning, their passage above all things unperturbed. Thus, they trample upon creatures without breaking, and none can harm them.

The *Yijing* says:
*"To traverse the Dao of day and night,
and enter the mechanisms of life and death—
this is the way of all things."*

To know the teachings of death and life,
and transcend the creations of things—
this is the realm of the Perfect Sage alone.

The *Cishu* explains:
*"To exist in the formless yet know the form’s formlessness—
thus the form remains whole.
To unsettle not the essence yet refine it ever deeper—
thus the essence is restored.*

When form is whole and unharmed,
and essence is restored and unlost,
then they abandon human contrivance
and align with the natural Dao.
How could one know that Heaven is not human,
or human is not Heaven?
Indeed, they unite with the Dao.

Thus, they attain completeness from Heaven,
and their natural defenses remain whole.
Their spirit is unshaken,
their hidden movements unimpeded—
solid things cannot obstruct them,
fire cannot burn them,
their passage above all things is unperturbed—
height cannot endanger them,
and the terrors of death and life do not enter their hearts.
Thus, they trample upon creatures without fear,
and none can harm them.

Therefore, they do not encounter rhinoceros or tiger,
and wild beasts cannot harm them.
They do not wear armor or wield weapons,
and blades have no place to strike them—
this is indeed the realm of no-death."

The *Yijing* says:
*"To traverse the Dao of day and night,
and know the alternating dark and bright.
Day and night succeed one another,
embracing yin and cradling yang,
bound by the mechanisms of emergence and return—
none can escape this.*

Like the owl, bright in night and dim in day,
or the kestrel, bright in day and dim in night—
this is not because day and night differ in nature,
but because they fail to traverse the Dao of day and night
and thus do not know their true essence.

All things that do not grasp the Dao
are thus bound.

Only the Perfect Sage comprehends the principles of all things,
knows the teachings of death and life,
and transcends the creations of things.
Thus, they guide all things without direction,
command all things without response,
participate in ten thousand years as one pure whole—
what weight could death and life possibly hold?"

This is called the realm of no-death—
is it not so?

**Chapter Fifty: "The Dao Gives Birth"**

The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:
*"The Dao acts without action yet accomplishes all things.
All things flourish and grow,
yet all arise from non-action."*

The *Cishu* explains:
The *Yijing* says:
*"Silent and unmoving,
yet responsive and thus harmonizing all under Heaven—
this is the Dao."*

Silent and unmoving—
this is the essence of the Dao,
hence its constant non-action.
Responsive and thus harmonizing—
this is the function of the Dao,
hence its non-action yet all-accomplishing.

Though myriad things arise and flourish,
their origins trace back to the Dao—
this is what is meant by:
*"All things flourish and grow,
yet all arise from non-action."*

As Zhuangzi says:
*"When the two non-actions unite,
all things transform—
is this not so?"*

**The Dao nurtures virtue/power,**
The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:
*"When things attain life through it, this is virtue/power."*

The *Cishu* explains:
*"Life is the radiance of virtue/power.
Before things take form, virtue/power is unseen.
Once life emerges, each attains its due—
thus, when speaking of life, it is called virtue/power,
for virtue/power is what grants it."*

As Zhuangzi says:
*"When things attain life through it, this is virtue/power—
thus, the Dao nurtures virtue/power."*

**The Dao shapes form,**
The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:
*"When movement is restrained and things are born,
and life takes shape with its principles established—
this is form."*

The *Cishu* explains:
*"When yin is still and tranquil,
this is the restraint—yin itself.
When yang moves and emits,
this is the movement—yang itself.
Yang alone cannot generate,
so mere restraint is insufficient to birth things.
Yin alone cannot complete,
so restraint without movement is also insufficient to birth things.
Only when yin and yang combine in restraint and movement
do things take form.
Once life emerges, its principles are complete—
is this not the Dao shaping form?"*

**The Dao completes its essence.**
The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:
*"When form and substance are complete,
their inherent tendencies manifest—
long and short taking shape,
high and low inclining—
this is their natural essence."*

The *Cishu* explains:
*"Bound by things, form and substance are established.
Once form and substance are complete,
their inherent tendencies manifest.
As with the crane’s long neck,
or the duck’s short—
naturally taking shape;
as with Heaven’s natural height,
or Earth’s natural low—
naturally inclining.
Is this not when form and substance are complete,
and their inherent tendencies manifest?"*

Thus, all things do not fail to revere the Dao
and honor virtue/power.

The Emperor Huizong’s commentary states:
*"All things first turn to the Dao.
What is completed and elevated is virtue/power.
To revere is to overcome and diminish things,
to honor is to ensure none can degrade them.
Mencius said:
*'What Zhao Meng holds noble,
Zhao Meng can also degrade—
this is not virtue/power.'*"

The *Cishu* explains:
*"The Dao is boundless in its application,
sometimes not filling—
deep as the origin of all things,
thus all things first turn to it as the Dao.
Virtue/power is completed and elevated,
art is completed and lowered.
Thus, what is completed and elevated is virtue/power.
Opposing virtue/power, the Dao is revered,
and virtue/power is honored.
Only reverence can overcome and diminish things—
as Zhuangzi said:
*'Though Heaven and Earth are vast,
they do not depart from within.'*
Opposing things, virtue/power is honored,
and things are degraded—
as Zhuangzi said:
*'The most noble titles and ranks are thus attained.'*

Mencius said:
*'What Zhao Meng holds noble,
Zhao Meng can also degrade—
this is not true nobility,
for it lies in things, not virtue/power.'*

But virtue/power—
this is what each person holds noble within themselves,
unable to degrade.
Thus, it is the noblest under Heaven."

The *Transmission* says:
*"Only those who possess it are truly noble."*

The reverence of the Dao,
the nobility of virtue/power—
none bestow them,
yet they abide eternally natural.

Huizong’s annotation:
*"Things decay with time,
forces topple with time,
but the True Sovereign stands above the age—
true nobility lies within oneself,
not borrowed from things or forces,
yet ever remains as it is."*

Exposition:
Some stir when acted upon,
some cease when restrained—these are *things*.
Some vie in length and shortness,
some tilt in high and low—these are *forces*.
Time and fate are boundless,
yet things and forces are finite.
If confined by things,
how could decay be avoided?
If adorned by forces,
how could toppling be averted?

Only the True Sovereign stands above the age—
no thing can humble him,
true nobility lies within,
not gained through others,
not borrowed from things.
Thus, he knows no decay,
for he does not rely on things,
and knows no toppling,
for he does not rely on forces.
This is why he transcends past and present,
ever remaining as he is.

Thus, the Dao begets and nurtures,
lengthens and matures,
completes and ripens,
nourishes and shelters.

Huizong’s annotation:
*"Speaking separately, there are distinctions between virtue/power and things/forces;
speaking together, all arise from the Dao.
The Dao is the hidden essence of all things.
When all things transform and emerge, the Dao is born with them;
when all things withdraw and store, the Dao completes with them.
It emerges from *Zhen* [Thunder],
is perfected in *Gen* [Mountain],
is nurtured by *Kun* [Earth],
is sheltered by *Qian* [Heaven].
Yang and yin interact,
the Eight Trigrams resonate—
as if bound by an unseen mechanism,
the Dao pervades it all."*

Exposition:
From the Dao’s begetting to forces’ completion—
speaking separately, the four stages differ,
but speaking together, all are encompassed by the Dao.
Thus, though Heaven and Earth are vast,
each has its hidden essence,
yet the Dao generates both Heaven and Earth.
Though people are countless,
each has its hidden essence,
yet humanity is shaped by the Dao—
this is why the Dao is the hidden essence of all things.

When spring’s breath awakens and ten thousand grasses sprout,
this is the transformation of all things—
the Dao, as Heaven’s humanity, emerges with them without hesitation.
When autumn arrives and all treasures mature,
this is the withdrawal of all things—
the Dao, as humanity’s Heaven, enters with them without resistance.

Even as thunder stirs them into *Zhen*,
*Gen* restrains them into completion,
*Kun* conceals them into nurturance,
*Qian* governs them into shelter—
yang and yin interact,
generating the Six Children and completing transformations,
the Eight Trigrams resonate,
cycling sun and moon to produce cold and heat.
All things thus take form,
all flourishing things thus emerge and retreat—
as if bound by an unseen mechanism,
who could command this?
It is the Dao that pervades it all.

The Master Lao treats life, growth, nurturing, maturation, and covering as all returning to the Dao.
This is simply a comprehensive way of speaking.

To generate without possessing,
to act without claiming,
to grow without controlling—
this is called the **Mysterious Virtue**.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
To generate is to manifest through movement and emergence,
to act is to embody through transformation and change,
to grow is to manifest through unification and singularity—
when the Dao descends into the world,
it resides in **virtue** alone.

Yet to generate without possessing its work,
to act without claiming its ability,
to grow without seeing its meticulous craft—
unless one’s **virtue** is profound yet subtle,
who could comprehend this?

Thus it is called the **Mysterious Virtue**.
The annotation explains:
Zhuangzi says, *"The luminous arises from the obscure"*—
this implies returning to the hidden to reach the manifest,
so there is the meaning of "manifesting through emergence."

The *Yijing* says, *"Transformation and change are called 'acting'*"*—
acting means to create and never cease,
so there is the meaning of "embodying through change."

The Classic says, *"The Sage employs it to become an official and leader"*—
to lead is to hold noble rank and gain the people’s trust,
so there is the meaning of "manifesting through unification."

These three aspects—
when the Dao descends into the world,
they reside in **virtue** alone.

The one who generates does so without ownership,
the one who transforms does so without attachment—
no desire for gain,
this is called **generating without possessing its work**.

The one who harmonizes all things does so without seeking righteousness,
the one whose benevolence extends to all ages does so without claiming virtue—
no pride or self-aggrandizement,
this is called **acting without claiming its ability**.

The one who covers and sustains Heaven and Earth,
who carves and shapes all forms—
without displaying the skill of their craft,
this is called **growing without seeing its meticulous craft**.

Unless one’s **virtue** is profound yet subtle,
who could comprehend this?

Thus it is called the **Mysterious Virtue**.
Because the profound is subtle yet small.

---

The world has a **beginning**, which is called the **Mother of the World**.
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
The **beginning** without name is Heaven and Earth,
the **Mother** with name is all things—
both the **beginning** and the **Mother** are the Dao.
From the **beginning** of *qi*, it is called **beginning**,
from the **generation** of all things, it is called **Mother**—
to have a **beginning** is to be able to generate.

The annotation explains:
Before Heaven and Earth were born, the Dao was there.
The Dao is always nameless,
so it is called the **beginning** without name.
When the Dao descends into the realm,
Heaven and Earth are the greatest—
only after Heaven and Earth exist do all things arise,
so it is called the **Mother** with name.
The Classic says, *"To know the ancient origins is called 'the Dao’s principle'*"*,
and *"It can serve as the **Mother of the World**'*"*—
thus, the **beginning** and the **Mother** are both names for the Dao.

The **beginning** refers to the **origin of *qi***,
the **generation** of all things is called *Yijing* (change),
and it is called the **Mother of the World**—
this is spoken of from the perspective of generation.
These two arise from the same source but have different names,
both called the Dao—
so the words **beginning** and **Mother** are like fishhooks.

Without a **beginning**, it enters into non-generation;
with a **beginning**, it can generate.
To call it the **Mother of the World** is to say it lies in having a **beginning**.

Once one obtains its **Mother**, one knows its **children**.
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
The Dao can **mother** all things and name them,
so things are its **children**.
Those who understand the Dao also understand all things,
so by obtaining its **Mother**, one knows its **children**.

The annotation explains:
The Classic says, *"The Dao generates them"*—
thus the Dao can **mother** all things and name them.
All things arise from the Dao,
so the Dao is their **Mother**, and things are its **children**.
Those who do not understand the Dao cannot harmonize all things;
those who understand the Dao can harmonize all things.
If one truly knows the Dao,
then the principles of all things are known without needing recognition—
so by obtaining its **Mother**, one knows its **children**.
Once one knows its **children**, one again guards its **Mother**,
and thus one’s life is never in peril.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
Excessive knowledge leads to guarding it with restraint,
excessive observation leads to guarding it with precision—
to exhaust the principles of things without being burdened by them,
to reach the Dao’s full extent without losing its subtlety,
then one’s use of emergence and retreat is advantageous,
and one’s coming and going never ends—
one can preserve life, fulfill one’s years,
and avoid danger and peril.

The annotation explains:
The Dao does not desire mixture;
mixture leads to excess,
excess leads to disturbance—
so by understanding unity, all affairs are complete.
Thus, the Dao’s essence is not in complexity;
the more one hears and sees,
the less one can rely on simplicity.

The Classic says, *"Fewer things, the more one gains"*—
Mencius says, *"Returning to the concise is the way of simplicity."*
By this, one exhausts the principles of things—
then one’s broad vision sits in clarity without being burdened by them;
by this, one reaches the Dao’s full extent—
then though things are chaotic and myriad, one does not lose its subtlety.
Thus one can emerge or retreat without disadvantage,
come or go without exhausting the Dao.
To drift in ease, to rest in comfort—
the way of preserving life and fulfilling years is achieved,
and there should be no danger or peril.

Though few in knowledge, one lacks restraint;
few in observation, one lacks precision—
so one must first know its **children**,
then one can guard its **Mother**.

Block the **Treasure of *Yui***,
close its **Gate**,
and one’s life will never be weary.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
*Yui* means delight,
**Gate** means exit—
when things entice from without,
the heart delights within.
Eyes, ears, nose, and mouth—
the spirit and intelligence emerge from them.
Guard your inner self,
close your outer senses—
do not take pleasure in harmonizing things,
and things cannot lure you—
then one delights in Heaven and finds self-satisfaction.
Who would toil endlessly for things?

The **spirit and intelligence** emerge from them—this is so.

Only knowing **harmony**,
one joyfully communicates without losing oneself to *Yui*.
With **inner restraint**,
one repels the myriad directions,
presenting them before yet never entering their embrace;
with **outer closure**,
one remains still and unmoved,
not taking pleasure in harmonizing things,
not seeing what is desirable—
then things cannot lure one.

If so, one finds delight in **Dao** without worry,
self-satisfaction in self-satisfaction—
who would toil endlessly for things?
This is called **eternal stillness**.

Open its *Yui*,
complete its affairs—
then **eternal stillness** is lost forever.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
To foolishly perceive what is pleasing,
to engage with it and thus be drawn—
then one’s life is spent in ceaseless strife,
yet never sees success.

The *Cushui* says:
*Yui* means delight—
when its *Yui* is closed, things cannot lure;
when its *Yui* is opened, one foolishly perceives what is desirable.
If things cannot lure,
one knows to restrain the inner self,
to close the outer senses.
If one foolishly perceives what is desirable,
then one takes pleasure in harmonizing things,
and things gain the power to lure.
If such is the case,
one clashes with things in battle,
one is worn down by them in transformation—
how could one know delight in **Dao** and self-satisfaction,
and not take things as one’s concern?

To toil endlessly like this,
seeking success through deeds,
yet never seeing it—
then one is lost in confusion, unaware of return,
entrapped and unable to retreat,
losing one’s nature greatly—
this is called **eternal stillness** undone.

To see what is small is to be clear,
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
Smallness is the **mystery of the Dao**.
To see the **mystery of the Dao**,
is to know oneself—
thus there is no lack of clarity.

The *Cushui* says:
To return to smallness and discern through things,
then smallness is the **Dao’s return to non-action**.
What is called seeing smallness,
is to see the **mystery of the Dao** alone.
To see the **mystery of the Dao**,
is to know oneself.
Only by knowing oneself,
is there no unknown—
as the *Classic* says:
*"To know oneself is to be clear."*

To guard softness is to be strong,
The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
Softness is the **root of the Dao**.
To guard the **root of the Dao**,
is to conquer oneself—
thus there is no unvanquished.
The *Cushui* says:
The utmost softness conquers the utmost firmness in the world—
thus softness is the **root of the Dao**.
What is called guarding softness,
is to conquer one’s selfish desires.
To conquer one’s selfish desires,
is to conquer oneself.
Only by conquering oneself,
is there no unvanquished—
as the *Classic* says:
*"To conquer oneself is to be strong."*

To employ its **light**,
and return to its **clarity**.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
**Clarity** is the essence of **light**.
**Light** is the function of **clarity**.
When the Sage responds to the world,
from essence arises function—
then radiance spreads as **light**,
and when function is withdrawn to essence—
then wisdom penetrates as **clarity**.
To display **ren** outwardly,
to conceal it in action—
like the sun and moon:
all things are illuminated and clear,
yet never diminished—
this is how one manifests the **virtue of spirit and clarity**.

The *Cushui* says:
**Clarity** and **light**,
different names, same reality—
from essence arises function,
then **clarity** disperses as **light**,
so **light** is the function of **clarity**.
When function is withdrawn to essence,
then **light** returns to **clarity**,
so **clarity** is the essence of **light**.
When the Sage responds to the world,
**clarity** emerges as **light**,
then **light** shines upon the four quarters—
this is called:
*"From essence arises function,
then radiance spreads as **light**."*
When **light** returns to **clarity**,
then its **clarity** ascends—
this is called:
*"Function is withdrawn to essence,
then wisdom penetrates as **clarity**."*
Thus, to employ its **light**,
though **ren** may sometimes be displayed,
to return to its **clarity**,
while **light** is naturally concealed—
like the sun and moon adorned in the sky,
illuminating all things—
the **clarity** of the sun and moon
is never truly diminished.
The *Yijing* speaks of *"manifesting the **virtue of spirit and clarity**"*—
this is why.

Without abandoning the body to misfortune,
this is called **embracing constancy**.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
The transformation of things is without constancy.
Only those who return to **command** abandon things and distance themselves from people,
returning to **clarity** without joining the transformation of things—
thus they embody constancy without harm,
differing from those who scheme with form to create **light**.

The *Cushui* says:
The **spirit** transforms into decay,
decay transforms back into **spirit**—
this is the **transformation of things** without constancy.
But if one returns to **command**, it is not so:
abandoning wisdom and returning to simplicity,
one distances oneself from things and people,
returning to **stillness and clarity** while regaining **clarity**—
enduring through ages yet unchanging,
transcending all forms without alteration—
how could one join the transformation of things?
To know **command** within oneself,
as when spring and summer return to autumn and winter,
embracing nature while preserving life—
this is why one embodies constancy without harm.
Then how different is this
from those who scheme with form to create **light**,
and thus must avoid them like a recluse!

*"Let me stand firm with knowledge of the fifth-thirty-third,*
walking the **Great Dao**,
only **generosity** do I fear.*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation says:
The **Dao** rejects excess and extravagance—
excess leads to licentiousness in **virtue**,
extravagance leads to wastefulness in nature—
**generosity** is the fault.
**Firmness** is smallness discerning through things.
To stand firm and discern through things,
while inwardly knowing oneself—
then one’s roots are deep and foundation solid,
taking sufficiency in one’s own body—
thus one fears only **generosity**.

The *Cushui* says:
Excess and extravagance,
the **Dao** rejects them—
thus the *Classic* says:
*"The Sage rejects excess and extravagance."*
Excess leads to licentiousness in **virtue**,
not the *"inner preservation while outer senses remain undisturbed."*
Extravagance leads to wastefulness in nature,
not the *"inner simplicity walking the **Dao of the Nameless**."*
Excess and extravagance,
are not the **Dao of frugality and generosity**.
To be small and sidelong is not **firmness**—
**firmness** is not its nature,
but to discern and assist.
This is the meaning of **firmness**—
thus **firmness** is smallness discerning through things.
To stand firm and discern through things,
one does not take pleasure in harmonizing things—
thus one is not lured by things,
while inwardly knowing oneself.
If so,
then one’s nature returns to itself,
one’s form is preserved whole—
deep roots and solid foundation are achieved.
Indeed, **nature** is the root of form,
and form is its foundation.

With deep roots and firm foundations,
one returns to guarding its **Mother**—
*indulging in virtue* and *excessive nature*?
How could this be the **Dao**?

Only the **inner observation** is cultivated,
not the **outer pursuits**—
those who suffice within themselves
have truly attained it.

*"Only giving is feared,"*
for it differs from those who *aspire to temporal gain*.
The **Great Dao** is most level,
yet people prefer shortcuts.

*Huizong’s Commentary* says:
*"The Dao is level, while shortcuts are swift—
to seek swiftness for near-term success
is only to distance oneself from the Dao."*

*Explanatory Notes* say:
*"If the Dao is like a great highway,
then the Dao is level indeed.
To walk without shortcuts,
then shortcuts are swift indeed.*
*The **I Ching** says: "Dwelling long in the Dao brings harmony to all under heaven,"*
*and the **Book of Poetry** speaks of the Zhou King’s longevity through virtuous governance—*
*yet to seek swiftness for near-term success*
*is only to distance oneself from the Dao.*

The court is *excessively formal*,
the fields are *neglected and overgrown*,
the granaries are *emptied*—
*Huizong’s Commentary* says:
*"To honor the worthy and employ the capable,
to achieve governance in the court,
yet to neglect diligent farming and accumulating wealth,
to fail in fostering a prosperous people—*
this is to *pursue trifles while abandoning the fundamentals*,
not a **Dao** that can endure."*

*Explanatory Notes* say:
*"To rectify the court is to rectify all officials—*
the court is the *source of governance*.
Once the people are flourishing,
what further need is there?
To say *‘wealth’*,
the prosperity of the people is the *foundation of governance*—*
to achieve the **Dao of governance**,
one must first establish the *foundation of governance*.
Thus, Shun’s commissioning of **Jie** was followed by **Ben’s** instruction to sow seeds,
and Mencius spoke of not *disrupting agricultural seasons* before exhorting filial piety and fraternal duty.
*Farming is the fundamental basis of the state.*
The reason kings can endure long and prosper is because they *root themselves in this **Dao***.
Yet to honor the worthy and employ the capable,
to achieve governance in the court,
yet to neglect diligent farming and accumulating wealth,
to fail in fostering a prosperous people—
this is to *pursue trifles while abandoning the fundamentals*,
not a **Dao** that can endure."*

To *adorn oneself in fine attire*,
to *wear sharp blades at one’s side*,
to *disdain plain food*,
while *abundant wealth* is held as honor—
this is *robber’s display*,
not the **Dao**!

*Huizong’s Commentary* says:
*"Those within the **inner simplicity** walk the **Dao of the Nameless**,
those outside it *aspire to temporal gain*.
To walk the **Dao of the Nameless**,
one fears only *excessive giving*—
to *aspire to temporal gain*,
one adorns oneself in fine attire,
wears sharp blades at one’s side,
disdains plain food,
and holds abundant wealth as honor,
while insufficiency is shame—
*consumption and extravagance extinguish righteousness*,
*arrogance and indulgence lead to false display*—
how could this be the **Dao**?"*

*Explanatory Notes* say:
*"Those within the **inner simplicity***
are those who *embody it within themselves*.
*Those outside it*
are those who *pursue it externally*.
*The **Nameless Dao***
is the *subtle essence of the **Dao***.
*Temporal gain*
depends on *external things*.
Those within the **inner simplicity** walk the **Dao of the Nameless**,
thus *suffice within themselves* and fear only *excessive giving*.
Those outside it *aspire to temporal gain*,
thus find pleasure in *harmonizing with things*—
adorn themselves in fine attire,
wear sharp blades at one’s side,
disdain plain food,
and hold abundant wealth as honor,
while insufficiency is shame.
To *adorn oneself in fine attire* and *disdain plain food*,
is to *shame coarse garments and coarse food*—
yet this is not enough to discuss.
To *wear sharp blades at one’s side*,
is to *fail in understanding that tranquility is supreme*.
To hold abundant wealth as honor,
while insufficiency is shame—
this is not *‘I am content whether in poverty or wealth’*,
but *‘I find joy in both abundance and scarcity’*.
If so,
*consumption and extravagance extinguish righteousness*,
*arrogance and indulgence lead to false display*—
how could this be the **Dao**?"

**Chapter 54: The Good at Establishing Do Not Pull Up the Stems**
The good at establishing do not pull up the stems—
*Huizong’s Commentary* says:
*"Establishing the **Central Dao** unites all above and below,
thus one does not pull up the stems."*

*Explanatory Notes* say:
*"The **Central** is the *great foundation of all under heaven*.
Only by *fully aligning with the **Central Dao***
can one *stand upright in the two realms* (of heaven and earth),
*unbiased and harmonious*,
*penetrating and unifying all above and below*—
this is to *establish the **Central Dao***.
To *establish the **Central Dao***
is to *uphold the **Dao** within oneself*,
thus one does not pull up the stems.

*The **Classic of Documents** says: "Establish the **Central Dao** among the people."*
Only by *employing the **Central Dao** among the people*
can they *never forget it*.
The good at establishing do not pull up the stems—
this is clear.

The good at embracing do not lose their hold—
*Huizong’s Commentary* says:
*"Embracing **Oneness** to respond to all transformations,
thus one does not lose their hold."*

*Explanatory Notes* say:
*"**Oneness** is the *most subtle essence of all under heaven*.
Only by *conforming to the **Dao** of **Oneness***
can one *vastly transcend all movements*,
*emerge to interact with things*,
*meet all transformations with ease*—
this is to *embrace **Oneness** while never losing their hold*.
To *embrace **Oneness***
is to *never lose it*,
thus one does not lose their hold.

*The **Classic** says: "Can one embrace **Oneness** without departing from it?"*
Only by *embracing **Oneness** and never departing from its **subtle essence***
does the **subtle essence** merge with the **spirit**—
the good at embracing do not lose their hold—
this is clear.

Descendants continue the sacrifices without ceasing—
*Huizong’s Commentary* says:
*"To establish the **Central Dao** without straying from the **Dao**,
to embrace **Oneness** without departing from its **subtle essence**—*
if so,
how could one’s influence be limited to a single state,
or their virtue confined to one generation?
Their transmission will be *far-reaching indeed*."*

*Explanatory Notes* say:
*"Mencius said: ‘Standing in the **Central Dao**,’
and ‘to establish the **Central Dao** without straying from the **Dao**’
is to *respond to all under heaven with subtle application*.
Zhuangzi said: ‘The **perfect penetration of **Oneness***,’
and ‘to embrace **Oneness** without departing from its **subtle essence***
is to *merge with the **most subtle essence of all under heaven***.
*Subtle application is inexhaustible*,
*permeating all ages without flaw*;
*The **most subtle essence** flows universally*,
*transforming all things without limit*.
If so,
their *virtue reaches the four seas*,
their *benefits extend to future generations*.
Their *application is ever-widening*—
not merely confined to a single state,
but *spread universally across all under heaven*.
Their *duration is everlasting*—
not merely confined to one generation,
but *passing down through all future ages*.
Descendants preserve the sacrifices
through *countless generations*—
their transmission will be *far-reaching indeed*,
how could it not be *trustworthy*?"

To cultivate it in oneself,
its **virtue/power** becomes *true*;
To cultivate it in the family,
its **virtue/power** becomes *excessive*;
To cultivate it in the village,
its **virtue/power** becomes *long-lasting*;
To cultivate it in the state,
its **virtue/power** becomes *abundant*;
To cultivate it in all under heaven,
its **virtue/power** becomes *universal*.

*Huizong’s Commentary* says:
*"To cultivate it in oneself,
its **virtue/power** becomes *true*—*
this is the **Dao**’s truth in governing oneself.
To cultivate it in the family,
its **virtue/power** becomes *excessive*—*
this is the **Dao**’s remnants in governing others."*

「To cultivate it in the state,
its **virtue/power** becomes *abundant*—*
to cultivate it throughout all under heaven,
its **virtue/power** becomes *universal*—*
this is the **Dao**’s *straightforwardness* (土直) in governing the states and all under heaven.

The farther one’s cultivation extends,
the broader one’s **virtue/power** spreads—
all within oneself is its *true* essence,
while outward manifestations are but its *remnants*.
Hence, *excessive* (余) follows *true*,
*abundant* follows *long-lasting* (长),
and these belong to the **Dao**’s external unfoldings.

*Cushui* (疏义) explains:
*"The noble one’s practice begins by cultivating oneself,
and thus all under heaven is at peace.
First, one cultivates oneself;
last, one cultivates all under heaven.
From inner to outer,
from near to far—
this is the sequence of cultivating **virtue/power**.

To cultivate it in oneself,
its **virtue/power** becomes *true*—*
all things are fully present within me,
turn inward and be sincere,
and there is no greater joy.
*Possession* (具) refers to the utmost sincerity,
thus cultivating oneself yields *true* **virtue/power**,
as said: *the **Dao**’s truth in governing oneself.*

The *Classic of Documents* (《书》) states:
*"The **Dao** accumulates in one’s person."*
Thus, how one governs oneself through the **Dao**’s *truth* is clear.

To cultivate it in the family,
its **virtue/power** becomes *excessive*—*
to cultivate it in the village,
its **virtue/power** becomes *long-lasting*—*
for once **virtue/power** is sufficient within oneself,
ordering the family leaves ample room,
extending it to the village ensures it endures without ceasing—
as said: *the **Dao**’s remnants in governing others.*

Master Zhuang (庄子) said:
*"The **Dao** acts upon all things;
the highest governance of others is through *affairs* (事)."*
Thus, how one governs others through the **Dao**’s *remnants* is clear.

To cultivate it in the state,
its **virtue/power** becomes *abundant*—*
to cultivate it throughout all under heaven,
its **virtue/power** becomes *universal*—*
for once **virtue/power** is shared with others,
applying it to the realm brings fullness and sufficiency,
extending it to all under heaven covers all broadly—
as said: *the **Dao**’s *straightforwardness* in governing states and all under heaven.*

Master Yang (杨子) said:
*"Though all under heaven is vast,
governing it lies in the **Dao**."*
Thus, how one governs states and all under heaven through the **Dao**’s *straightforwardness* is clear.

The farther one’s cultivation extends,
the more **virtue/power**’s resonance endures—
reaching the utmost distance,
as said: *"The near can be extended through this."*

The broader one’s **virtue/power** spreads,
the more *profound virtue* (盛德) is called *new*—*
reaching boundless scope,
as said: *"The extent of **virtue/power**’s reach."*

All within oneself is its *true* essence,
thus *true* lies in the inner,
to receive from Heaven.
Outward manifestations are but its *remnants*,
thus *remnants* lie in the outer,
to propagate afar—
hence, *excessive* follows *true*,
*abundant* follows *long-lasting*,
and these belong to the **Dao**’s external unfoldings.

Indeed, the **Dao** is what all things follow;
**virtue/power** is the **Dao** within oneself—
*excessive*, *long-lasting*, *abundant*, *universal*—
all are the **Dao** manifesting as **virtue/power**,
thus they belong to the **Dao**’s external unfoldings.

Yet the Sage, through the **Dao**,
governs oneself without straying from *truth*—
even when cultivating all under heaven,
only the **Dao**’s *remnants* and *straightforwardness* emerge in response.
The reason it is *true* is that it neither harms nor benefits—
the Sage values *truth* precisely for this.

Thus, one observes oneself through oneself,
one’s family through one’s family,
one’s village through one’s village,
one’s state through one’s state,
and all under heaven through all under heaven.

*Huizong’s Commentary* (徽宗注) says:
*"All things are fully present within me,
turn inward and be sincere,
and there is no greater joy—
thus observing oneself through oneself brings self-governance,
and this applies by analogy."*

All under heaven has its *constant nature* (常然),
and all things arise from it,
all find fulfillment in it—
by extending the *constant nature* of my heart
to all under heaven,
those of like kind respond without losing their *constant nature*.

Thus, one governs all under heaven without actively governing—
for *observation* (观) requires
that the higher observe the lower,
then the lower may observe the higher.
Observing from above to below,
as in the *I Ching*’s *"central rectitude in observing all under heaven"*—
observing from below to above,
as in the *Rites of Zhou*’s *"commanding all the people to observe the governance’s symbols"*—
the observation of higher and lower is thus.

If the higher and lower observe in this way,
then following them becomes effortless.

As the *Records* (《记》) say:
*"First cultivate oneself, then harmonize the family,
harmonize the family, then govern the state,
govern the state, then bring peace to all under heaven—*
this aligns perfectly with the above.

How do I know all under heaven is thus?
Through this.

*Huizong’s Commentary* says:
*"The simple and easy reveal all under heaven’s principles."*

*Cushui* (疏义) explains:
*"The *trigram Qian* (乾) is firm and constant,
its **Dao** seemingly difficult,
yet it shows people the ever-easy.
The *trigram Kun* (坤) is receptive and yielding,
its **Dao** seemingly complex,
yet it shows people the ever-simple.
Qian and Kun reveal the simple and easy to people,
thus the governance’s symbols are observable—
easy to know, simple to follow.
Easy to know without fearing what is hard to know,
simple to follow without fearing what is hard to follow—
then though the talents and principles of all under heaven
are hidden in the utmost traces,
they can be penetrated and understood without remainder,
what need is there to seek elsewhere?
From this perspective,
one sees that the Sage’s practice is the most restrained,
yet its application is the most expansive—
achieved through the simple and easy,
thus it is said: *‘Observe Heaven and Earth, and you will see the Sage.’*
The chapter *"Thick with Virtue"* (含德之厚), the fifty-fifth.*」

**Thick with Virtue** compares to the infant.

The **Huizong Commentary** says: Only when a person’s virtue is profound does their nature not shift with things. Thick with virtue, they do not stray with things, thus their *qi* is concentrated and their will is singular.

Mencius said: The noble person does not lose the heart of an infant.

The **Exegesis** says: Who among those born with innate nature does not cherish this virtuous excellence? Yet when they interact with others, few can uphold it.

Only when virtue is profound does one speak of its root in nature. If one strays with things, then nature is submerged in artifice.

Only those thick with virtue do not see strange things and stray from them. Not straying with things, their *qi* is concentrated and their will is singular.

Thus **Liezi** said: In infancy, *qi* is concentrated and will is singular—this is the utmost harmony. Things cannot harm them, and no virtue surpasses them.

*Qi* is empty and awaits things; when concentrated, it is still and unmixed—this aligns with the *Classic*’s “concentrating *qi* to govern softness.”

Will is where the mind directs itself; when singular, it is unified and not divided—this aligns with Confucius’ “as one whose will is singular.”

When *qi* is concentrated and will is singular, constant virtue does not depart, and one returns to infancy—thus Mencius praised the noble person who does not lose the heart of an infant.

Indeed, fulfilling one’s nature is the noble person’s affair. To fulfill one’s nature is to be substantial yet not lose the source of emptiness, to act yet not lose the basis of stillness—this is the infant’s purity without deficiency, truth without artifice. What need for choice?

Thus the great person dwells in profundity, not shallowness. Poisonous creatures do not sting, fierce beasts do not seize, snatching birds do not strike.

The **Huizong Commentary** says: Those thick with virtue have no place for worry to enter, no room for perverse *qi* to attack—thus nothing can harm them.

**Zhuangzi** said: If a person can empty themselves to engage with the world, who can harm them?

The **Exegesis** says: The world values the infant because they are still and unchanging, pure and unadulterated, free from the fear of misfortune, unburdened by perverse *qi*.

Thick with virtue compares to the infant—thus worry cannot enter, perverse *qi* cannot attack. Worry comes from without, hence “enter.” Perverse *qi* seizes gaps, hence “attack.” If worry cannot enter, their virtue is whole; if perverse *qi* cannot attack, their spirit is unharmed.

If so, calamity does not come, fortune does not arrive—unmoved by things’ gains and harms, thus nothing can harm them. If nothing can harm them, they have nothing to oppose, reaching the utmost emptiness.

**Zhuangzi** said: If a person can empty themselves to wander in the world, who can harm them? Indeed, the utmost emptiness leaves no opening for things—like an empty boat struck by currents, even those with narrow minds do not anger.

If a person attains emptiness to wander among worldly affairs, yielding and conforming like flowing water, who can harm them? This is how the perfected one’s mind works like a mirror—neither grasping nor resisting, responding without storing, thus able to overcome things without harm. This too is emptiness.

Bones are weak, sinews supple, yet their grip is firm. They do not know the union of male and female yet are aroused—this is the utmost *jing*.

The **Huizong Commentary** says: When virtue is whole, form is whole, hence bones are weak, sinews supple, yet their grip is firm. When form is whole, spirit is whole, hence they do not know the union of male and female yet are aroused. The utmost *jing* can enter the spirit—**Zhuangzi** said: The Sage values *jing*.

The **Exegesis** says: Life is the radiance of virtue; to preserve virtue is to preserve form—this is “virtue whole, form whole.” The body is the dwelling of the spirit; to preserve form is to preserve spirit—this is “form whole, spirit whole.”

When virtue is whole, form is whole, hence bones are weak, sinews supple, yet their grip is firm—because their form is generated without exhaustion, they do not know where to take hold, yet their grip is firm. This differs from laboring the form without rest, leading to exhaustion.

When form is whole, spirit is whole, hence they do not know the union of male and female yet are aroused—because their spirit is whole and unharmed, they do not know what to unite with, yet they are aroused. This differs from overusing *jing* without cessation, leading to depletion.

Without the utmost *jing*, who could achieve this? Only the utmost *jing* in the world can merge with the utmost spirit of the world. The utmost *jing* can enter the spirit, thus *jing* and spirit merge without separation.

**Zhuangzi** said: The Sage values *jing*—because the singular *jing* harmonizes with the natural order. If a person harmonizes with the natural order, they are also the natural order itself. This is why the Sage values *jing*.

When **Zhuangzi** discusses nurturing the spirit, his words touch on valuing *jing*—is this not because spirit and *jing* arise from the Dao, and form originates from *jing*? Is there not truth in this?

To cry all day yet the voice does not grow hoarse—this is the utmost harmony.

The **Huizong Commentary** says: Attaining singularity is called *jing*; *jing* means virtue is whole and spirit is unharmed. Pure *qi* achieves harmony, thus harmony means *qi* is whole and the voice does not grow hoarse.

A person’s life receives *jing* from Heaven’s One to become the source of wisdom, and harmony from Heaven’s Five to become the foundation of trust. When perfected, they can enter the spirit, can restore their vital command.

But those who lose the heart of an infant—*jing* wavers and is not preserved, *qi* erupts and is not pure, they abandon their innate nature, submerging it in myriad things. Is this not tragic?

The **Exegesis** says: All things transform through *jing* and take form; they receive One to live. Concentrating One is called *jing*. Attaining singularity means not being divided—this aligns with the *Yijing*’s “speaking of attaining singularity.”

*Jing* is the utmost rectitude of Heaven’s virtue; preserving *jing* means virtue is whole and spirit is unharmed—this is “their Heavenly Guard is whole, their spirit has no flaws.”

All things bear yin and embrace yang; obtaining the balance of yin and yang, pure *qi* achieves harmony.

The harmonizing *qi* interflows and achieves balance—this aligns with Zhuangzi’s account of the harmonious *qi* as the essence of humanity.

Balance, when expressed, is always measured and harmonious. When balance is attained, *qi* is whole and the throat does not constrict—this is what is meant by: *"The elder brother cries all day but his throat never constricts; this is the ultimate of harmony."*

Human life begins when *jing* is received from Heaven’s One, becoming the root of wisdom, and when balance is attained from Heaven’s Five, becoming the foundation of sincerity.

Indeed, *jing* is what the One generates; it is received from Heaven’s One as water, corresponding to the North in direction, and thus becomes the root of wisdom. Balance is what *qi* attains; it is received from Heaven’s Five as earth, residing centrally in position, and thus becomes the foundation of sincerity.

When humans are born, they embody the five phases, from Heaven’s One to Heaven’s Five, and the numbers of their formation are complete. If one truly preserves the One and dwells in balance, and further cultivates the self, they may live a thousand two hundred years without ever decaying in form.

When they attain this state, can they not enter the spirit and restore their vital command?

To enter the spirit is to attain the ineffable—this is what is meant by *"the profound essence entering the spirit."*

To restore the vital command is to return to stillness—this is what is meant by *"stillness is called restoring the vital command."*

Yet the world’s people lose their infantile heart because their *jing* wavers and is not preserved, their *qi* erupts and is not pure. How much more could they know of not letting their *jing* waver, preserving it and not losing it, and not letting their *qi* erupt, making it pure and unharmed?

Instead, they abandon their innate nature to harm their life, submerging themselves in myriad things to drown their desires. Their self-abandonment is so extreme—is this not tragic?

**Knowing harmony is constancy.**
Huizong’s annotation: *"The preservation of pure qi, with vital command governed within, form transforms while nature does not perish."*

The **Exegesis** says: *"Balance is the great harmony with all things."*

Guan Yin’s discourse on the Perfect One, who walks above all things without fear, states: *"This is the preservation of pure qi."*

If one can preserve their *qi*, making it pure and unadulterated, then its gentle harmony is evident.

Only when harmony is perfected can one traverse the beginning and end of all things, and communicate with the creation of all things—this is what is meant by *"vital command governed within, form transforms while nature does not perish."*

When vital command is governed within, creation originates from oneself, enduring through all ages and eternally existing.

When form transforms while nature does not perish, one nature remains constant, enduring myriad forms without change, encompassing Heaven and Earth, inheriting the primal *qi*—how could this not be called constancy?

**Knowing constancy is clarity.**
Huizong’s annotation: *"Clarity suffices to perceive the Dao because it knows nature does not perish."*

The **Exegesis** says: *"The *Transmission* says: Inner vision is called clarity."*

Clarity suffices to perceive the Dao—this is beyond the reach of mere sight. Above form, one gazes with spirit alone, still and returning to the origin, awakening to see the singular. Then one knows that nature abides within, eternally fixed since antiquity.

As in the *Yijing*: *"Nurturing nature, nature abides—this is the gateway to Dao and virtue."*

Because nature abides, its abiding is the gateway to Dao and virtue, then Heaven and Earth are born from it—even Heaven and Earth depend on this to be born. How could clarity not suffice to perceive the Dao, knowing that nature does not perish?

**Augmenting life brings omens.**
Huizong’s annotation: *"Omens are the presages of things; the principle of generating life, when augmented, becomes superfluous—fortune and misfortune are yet undetermined."*

The **Exegesis** says: *"Divine omens manifest as signs; knowing how to respond, omens are the presages of things. Fortune and misfortune are already foretold—how could one escape their response?"*

Moreover, the generation of things is complete in its own principle. To augment it only adds excess, like hastening the growth of seedlings—this is to encourage what is already complete. These are all instances of humans aiding Heaven, yet fortune and misfortune remain undetermined.

Only by following nature without augmentation, without letting likes and dislikes internally harm the body, can one attain Heaven’s fullest bestowal.

**The heart directs *qi* as strength.**
Huizong’s annotation: *"When the body aligns with the heart, and the heart aligns with *qi*, then *qi* is harmonious and does not erupt. The stumbling and rushing are *qi*—yet the heart truly compels this strength."*

To contend with things in this way is not the path of self-mastery.

The **Exegesis** says: *"The body is the heart’s dwelling."*

When the body aligns with the heart, all actions of the four limbs are rooted in the heart. The heart is the sovereign of *qi*.

When the heart aligns with *qi*, then cultivating the mind in stillness allows *qi* to merge with emptiness. Kang Changzi said: *"If the body aligns with the heart and the heart aligns with *qi*, then in the way of nurturing *qi*, one knows its foundation."*

Only when *qi* is properly nurtured can it be harmonious and not erupt, achieving ultimate softness.

Now, the stumbling and rushing—when walking, they do not conform to Xia Wu’s measured steps, and when moving, they do not conform to the graceful *Shao Hao*—all are the result of erupting *qi*.

If stumbling and rushing persist, even if the leader of *qi* is at fault, it is truly the heart that compels it—this is what is called *"strength."* To contend with things in this way means joy aligns with yang and anger aligns with yin, yet this is not the path of self-mastery. How could people know that harmony and softness suffice to pacify things, making them unable to harm?

**When things flourish, they age—this is called not the Dao.**
Huizong’s annotation: *"The Dao knows no past or future, yet things have flourishing and aging, strength has its time of weakness, and prosperity has its time of decline. They are enslaved by time and governed by fate—how could this be the Dao?"*

The **Exegesis** says: *"There are those who live, and those who give life; there are those with form, and those who shape form. The Dao knows no past or future, so giving life never ends, and shaping form never ceases. This is what Zhuangzi meant by *self-rooted, self-originating, eternally fixed since antiquity.*"*

「When things have phases of flourishing and aging, then there is the *Dao of life and death*—this is what Liezi meant by *the great transformation* where youth is followed by vigor, then old age and decrepitude.

Only in their transformation from youth to vigor, from vigor to old age, do the principles of strength and weakness, rise and decline manifest. Bound by the *造物* [Creator’s] silent decree, they cannot escape—thus strength has its time of weakness, and one must consider softness; prosperity has its time of decline, and one must reflect on repose.

They are enslaved by time as *overflowing and deficient*, constrained by fate as *abundant and scarce*—how could this be the Dao?

The Dao is *not the Dao* from the very beginning. Huizong’s annotation: *"The Dao has never been exhausted, yet the people’s confusion persists, saying it is so since ancient times."*

The **Exegesis** says: *"The *Wuji* [Non-Extreme] contains no *Wuji*, the *Wujin* [Non-Exhaustion] contains no *Wujin*—thus the Dao is never exhausted.

Those who lose their nature in worldly affairs see only things, not the Dao. As form transforms, their minds are equally bewildered, and they never understand it their whole lives. The people’s confusion has indeed been long-standing—how could they know the Dao has endured eternally, without ever perishing, throughout all time?

*Laozi’s True Scripture of the Dao and Its Virtue—Exegesis, Volume Ten concluded. Volume Eleven begins.*」

The scholar Jiang Cheng of the Imperial Academy expounds:

**Chapter Fifty-Six: The Wise Do Not Speak**
*"The wise do not speak; those who speak do not know."*

Huizong’s annotation: *"The Dao has no questions; questions have no answers. Thus, those who know the Dao remain silent and intuitively grasp it, having no need to speak."*

Yiqié questioned Wangni, yet through four inquiries and four unknowns, his words exhausted themselves, and he grew distant from the Dao.

**Exegesis:**
What can be articulated is but the coarse nature of things.
What can be intuited is the refined essence of things.
The Dao’s supreme refinement is profound and inscrutable—though one might wish to speak of it, words fail to capture it, hence there are no questions.

For there is an entrance to the Dao, one may question it.
But the Dao itself has no entrance—who can question it?
For there are questions, one may respond to them.
But the Dao itself has no questions—who can respond to it?
Thus, those who seek answers to questions about the Dao do not know the Dao.
The sage explains this by saying:
*"The Dao can be received by spirit but not transmitted through speech;
it can be intuitively grasped by the heart but not sought through outward traces."*
So, the ultimate speech transcends speech,
and attains the mystery of silence—
one must remain silent and intuitively grasp it.

If one sees the Dao with the eyes, it remains stored within—
how can it be contained by sound?
What need then for speech?

The *Yijing* speaks of *"silent and it is accomplished"*,
Liezi speaks of *"silent and it is attained"*—
this is the very meaning.

In the past, when Yiqié questioned Wangni,
he first asked of what all things are the same—
Wangni did not know.
Then he asked of knowing what one does not know—
Wangni did not know.
Next he asked of things having no knowledge—
Wangni did not know.
Finally, he asked of knowing not being ignorance and ignorance not being knowing—
Wangni did not know.
Four questions, four unknowns.

Not that he did not know—
rather, knowing was shallow, ignorance profound;
knowing was external, ignorance internal—
his unknown was the true knowing.

But those who cling to words,
entangled in endless speech, cannot escape confusion—
who knows that the Dao cannot be spoken of,
and yet speech is inevitable?

Block its *fuè* [receptive/outward aspect],
close its entrance—

Huizong’s annotation: *"Dull the senses and preserve the spirit; withdraw and hide in secrecy."*

**Exegesis:**
For those whose outward aspect is confused,
one cannot grasp them through excessive speech
to lead them to the inward essence;
for those whose inward aspect is confused,
one cannot entangle them in convoluted speech
to lead them to the outward essence.
Block its *fuè*, then there is no confusion of inward and outward,
and the harmonious joy flows freely without losing its receptivity.

In sleep, the *hun* [ethereal soul] mingles;
in waking, the form opens.
Engaging with others brings discord—
it is called *"fighting with the heart"*—
close its entrance, then there is no peril of heart-fighting.
The well-closed cannot be opened without a key.

This is what Zhuangzi called *"dull the senses and preserve the spirit,"*
and what the *Yijing* called *"withdraw and hide in secrecy."*

For dulling the senses brings clarity to the Dao’s inscrutable depth,
unlured by things, one can thus embrace the spirit in stillness
and merge with it—
this is called *"blocking its fuè."*

Hiding in secrecy returns to the origin of nature,
where nothing enters of its own accord—
one can thus conceal within heaven and merge with heaven—
this is called *"closing its entrance."*

Guangchengzi said:
*"Guard your inward self,
seal your outward self,
dull its sharpness,
unravel its confusion."*

Huizong’s annotation: *"Take depth as root,
take restraint as measure."*

**Exegesis:**
Sharpness is like fire’s form—
it cannot avoid injury,
but if dulled, it will not harm.
Confusion is like tangled threads—
it cannot avoid disorder,
but if unraveled, it will not be disordered.
This is what Zhuangzi called *"take depth as root,
take restraint as measure."*

For depth aligns with the *Yijing*’s *"ultimate depth and probing the subtle"*—
taking depth as root means one does not follow superficial trends,
interacting with things without harming them,
and things cannot harm one—
this is called *"dull its sharpness."*

Restraint aligns with Mencius’ *"guard restraint while applying breadth"*—
taking restraint as measure means one does not drown the heart in excess,
though myriad affairs unfold before one,
yet cannot enter to merge—
this is called *"unravel its confusion."*

Harmonize its radiance,
align with its dust—

Huizong’s annotation: *"Follow things with flexibility,
and conform to their currents."*

**Exegesis:**
Harmonize refers to its non-defiance;
align refers to its non-separation.
Harmonizing its radiance means radiance without dazzle—
not like the formed and polished that produces light.
Aligning with its dust means great unity with things—
not like those who withdraw from the world and differ from customs.
This is Zhuangzi’s *"follow things with flexibility and conform to their currents."*

Only by shifting with the times,
and rising or falling with the world,
flexing and yielding without clashing with things,
never setting oneself apart in rigid distinction,
can one attain this.

This is called *Xuántóng* [Mysterious Unity].

Huizong’s annotation: *"The Dao returns to the utmost profound,
and unites with the utmost singular—
the utmost profound is called Mysterious,
the singular is called Unity.
Mysterious Unity means all things and I,
yet choosing among them is impossible—
how could one secretly entangle oneself in distinctions of near and far,
benefit and harm,
nobility and lowliness?"*

**Exegesis:**
Entering the inscrutable gate,
concealing the Dao without revealing it—
this is returning to the utmost profound.
Merging in the primal chaos,
gathering all into one Dao—
this is uniting with the utmost singular.
Returning to the utmost profound,
it dwells in subtle transformations—
small yet wondrous—
hence the term *Mysterious*.
Uniting with the utmost singular,
it encompasses all distinctions—
different yet unified—
hence the term *Unity*.

Only when the small merges with things,
obtaining their singularity and aligning with it,
does one know that things are inherently without things,
and I am not truly I—
when things and self are both forgotten,
all things and I become one,
and choosing among them is impossible—
how could one secretly entangle oneself in near and far,
benefit and harm,
nobility and lowliness?

For all distinctions are originally non-existent—
one cannot attain to be near,
one cannot attain to be distant,
one cannot attain to benefit,
one cannot attain to harm,
one cannot attain to nobility,
one cannot attain to lowliness—
thus one is noble for all under heaven.

Huizong’s annotation: *"The world’s people attack each other with love and hate,
and thus have attitudes of nearness and distance.
Emotions and pretense influence each other,
and thus distinctions of benefit and harm arise.
Actions vie for advantage,
and thus divisions of nobility and lowliness emerge.
These fluctuate endlessly,
never reaching an end—
how can they suffice to be noble for all under heaven?"*

Those who know the Dao transcend words,
those who transcend words extinguish love and hatred,
forget emotions and pretense,
abandon gain and loss,
and merge in mystery with the One Nature within—
thus their nobility is complete,
and all under heaven is forgotten,
hence they are noble for all under heaven.

**Cui’s Annotation:** The passions of the world
create their own likeness and difference,
their own right and wrong,
their own gain and loss.
When aligned with oneself, one loves it;
when divergent from oneself, one hates it—
love and hatred clash, giving rise to nearness and distance.
To depart from passion is wrong,
to depart from pretense is right—
passion and pretense intertwine, giving rise to perceptions of benefit and harm.
What one possesses is lowly;
what one loses, one mourns—
what one gains at the moment is noble, and one rejoices in it—
gain and loss vie for advantage, creating distinctions of nobility and lowliness.
These distinctions arise and vanish,
shift and alternate,
ebbing and flowing without end—
how far they are from the Dao!
How can they suffice to be noble for all under heaven?

But the Dao is that which all under heaven do not fail to be noble in.
Those who know the Dao understand this,
penetrate the true nature of things,
and preserve their root.
They transcend words and cast off the burden of speech,
love what is not loved,
hate what is not hated—
and thus extinguish love and hatred.
Thus they have no extreme nearness,
no extreme distance—
nearness and distance cannot be attained.
Passion is not true,
pretense is not false—
and thus they forget emotions and pretense.
Thus they do not cling to benefit,
nor resist harm—
benefit and harm cannot be attained.
There is no use in what is used,
what is used is not truly useful—
and thus they depart from gain and loss.
Thus nobility and lowliness are not in them—
nobility and lowliness do not belong to themselves.
Only knowing the One Nature’s truth,
they do not see the world’s pretense—
their nobility is complete,
all under heaven is forgotten,
hence they are noble for all under heaven.

**Chapter Fifty-Seven: Governing with Rectitude**
To govern with rectitude,
to employ the extraordinary in warfare,
and to take all under heaven with non-action.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** Rectitude is the Dao’s constancy,
the extraordinary is the Dao’s variation,
non-action is the Dao’s truth.
The nation is settled by rectitude,
victory in war comes from the extraordinary,
the Dao’s truth is without private interference.
Following things in their natural course,
all under heaven is governed.

**Cui’s Annotation:** Uprightness is rectitude;
rectitude is to abide in the One.
Only by abiding in the One
does one preserve the eternal constancy—
thus rectitude is the Dao’s constancy.
Rectitude again becomes extraordinary;
the extraordinary is to oppose rectitude.
Only by opposing rectitude
does one abandon the old constancy—
thus the extraordinary is the Dao’s variation.
Truth varies with things,
yet never lacks—
truth abides within,
and thus one does not externally engage in affairs—
thus non-action is the Dao’s truth.
What is first in governing the nation?
Only to abide in the One.
The nation is settled by rectitude—
this is what Mencius said: *"One rectitude and the nation is settled."*
To hold victory has its method,
strategic planning is all—
this is what Zhuangzi said: *"Continuous and cautious, unmeasurable and unconquerable."*
Truth is the utmost of sincerity;
only when one is empty of worldly concerns
can truth be preserved—
thus the Dao’s truth is without private interference.
Without private interference,
one does not contrive pretense for others,
and simply follows things in their natural course.
If so, then without deliberate governance,
all under heaven is governed.
The Classic says: *"Thus those who take all under heaven,
always do so with non-action."*

How do I know this is so?
The world abounds in prohibitions,
yet the people grow ever poorer.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** The people are not difficult to gather—
love them and they become close,
benefit them and they come.
But if one governs with what they hate,
they scatter.
Now without hearts of love and benefit,
yet with many prohibitions and strict scrutiny,
the people will scatter to the four quarters—
thus the people grow ever poorer.

**Cui’s Annotation:** To gain all under heaven has its method,
to gain its people is to gain all under heaven.
To gain its people has its method,
to gain their hearts is to gain the people.
For indeed, to love and benefit them
is how one gains their hearts.
To impose what they hate
is to lose their hearts.
This one loves them because I love them—
thus they love me as a parent.
This one benefits them because I benefit them—
thus they carry their children and come to me.
If one oppresses them to satisfy one’s desires,
exploits them to sustain oneself,
and imposes what they hate,
then they will all take each other’s hands and depart.
Thus without hearts of love and benefit,
yet indulging in cruelty to assert one’s power,
with many prohibitions and harsh scrutiny to claim one’s wisdom,
one cannot enrich the people with governance—
the people will scatter to the four quarters,
and the common folk will barely suffice.
This is called *"overflowing above and leaking below"*—
who knows the way of governance being dim and murky,
while its people are pure and simple?

When people abound in clever tools,
the state grows increasingly corrupt.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** Those who have clever devices must have clever schemes,
those who have clever schemes must have clever hearts.
Clever hearts arise, and then purity and simplicity are incomplete—
sometimes deceiving the ruler to oppose the Dao.

**Cui’s Annotation:** If one makes measures and weights to regulate them,
they will also use measures and weights to steal.
If one makes scales and balances to weigh them,
they will also use scales and balances to steal—
thus breaking measures and weights,
the people will not contend.
Yet those who possess clever devices
must have clever schemes,
and those who have clever schemes
must have clever hearts—
this is why the *I Ching* speaks of the "Old Man of Han Yin."
Indeed, the *I Ching* says:
*"The creative (乾) is known by its ease,
the receptive (坤) by its simplicity,
and thus the principles of all under heaven are attained."*
Once clever devices are made,
clever schemes will inevitably take form,
and once clever schemes take form,
clever hearts will inevitably arise.
When clever hearts arise,
what was once empty becomes filled,
what was once unified becomes scattered—
the so-called *"without mixing with anything, pure and unstained"*
may well be diminished—
this is why purity and simplicity are incomplete.
One will see cleverness and deceit clash,
artifice and pretense flourish daily—
sometimes deceiving the ruler to oppose the Dao—
this is why the Sage acts,
yet pardons all under heaven,
to remove this calamity.

When people rely too much on cleverness,
strange things will proliferate.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"If cleverness prevails, people will turn to frivolous pursuits,
and thus strange garments and odd instruments will emerge to disrupt customs."*

The *Zhuangzi*’s commentary explains:
*"If one man does not plow,
the whole world will suffer from his hunger;
therefore, the ancient sages taught the people
to avoid useless labor that harms productive work,
and to value practical goods over rare ones,
lest frivolous pursuits harm agriculture."*

In the age of supreme virtue,
the people are simple and unadorned,
knowing only the daily necessities of food and drink—
who among them would have the skill for elaborate cleverness?

Indeed, if cleverness prevails,
people will turn to frivolous pursuits,
and thus strange garments and odd instruments will emerge to disrupt customs.

The ancient rulers governed by this principle:
whenever strange garments or odd instruments were sold in the marketplace
or brought into the palace,
there were fixed punishments to uphold the essential
and suppress the frivolous,
so that the people might return
to the honest and steadfast customs of old.

As laws and decrees grow stricter,
thieves and robbers multiply.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"If strictness becomes excessive,
there will inevitably arise hearts that defy it."*

The *Zhuangzi*’s commentary explains:
*"To observe the deep fish is an ill omen,
to outwit the hidden is to invite calamity;
thus, once laws are established, deceit arises,
and once decrees are issued, trickery flourishes.*
If one merely taxes a few bushels of grain
and multiplies laws to oppress the people,
one will not stop theft—
for theft will persist.
This is what *Zhuangzi* meant when he said:
*'If strictness becomes excessive,
there will inevitably arise hearts that defy it.'*
This is the truth of it.

The people, being simple and unknowing,
if treated gently will submit,
if oppressed will seek vengeance—
unless the ruler governs with benevolence,
who would willingly reform and avoid crime?

Thus, the Sage governs through policy,
aligns through punishment,
and the people escape punishment yet feel no shame.

Hence, the Sage declares:
*"I act without action, and the people transform themselves."*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"Heaven governs without action and remains clear,
Earth governs without action and remains tranquil;
when these two—without action—harmonize,
all things naturally transform.
The Sage follows Heaven and Earth alone,
so the people reform without knowing who guides them."*

The *Zhuangzi*’s commentary explains:
*"Heaven’s movement is without action,
yet its clarity is pure and unadulterated—
it nurtures life and covers all without fail.
Earth’s dwelling is without action,
yet its stillness is unchanging—
it bears form and sustains all without omission.
Pure and unadulterated clarity is evident,
tranquil stillness is evident—
all this comes from attaining the Way of non-action.
Heaven and Earth blend in their non-action,
and all things transform and arise.
Observing Heaven and Earth reveals the Sage—
what need is there for action?
The Sage merely follows Heaven and Earth.*

Thus, the violent and fierce transform into the yielding,
those who bend to private interests transform into the public-spirited,
and all who harbor criminal intent
are led to pursue their own solitary ambition—
the people daily reform,
yet do not know who guides them.

*"I cherish stillness, and the people align themselves."*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"The balance’s interaction with form
requires no contrivance of skill,
yet straight and round, curved and right cannot escape it—
how much more so does it need hooks, cords, or measuring tools
before things can be made straight?"*

The *Zhuangzi*’s commentary explains:
*"If the balance is clear, dust and filth cannot cling;
if the water is still, even whiskers and eyebrows are reflected.
The balance and the water achieve this
because they remain singular and unchanging,
steady and responsive in nature.
The *Classic* says:
*'The balance’s interaction with form
requires no contrivance of skill,
yet straight and round, curved and right cannot escape it'—*
this speaks of its stillness.
The Sage’s use of the heart
is like this:
he guards stillness with unwavering devotion,
yet does not let movement disrupt his nature—
simply following his natural course,
undisturbed and unruffled.
How much more so does he need hooks, cords, or measuring tools
before things can be made straight?"*

Thus, the Way of governance values clarity and stillness,
and the people naturally align themselves.
The *Classic* states:
*"Clarity and stillness are the rectification for all under heaven."*

*"I act without interference, and the people grow rich."*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"The world is originally without interference—
it is only the vulgar who disturb it.
If one does not disturb it,
the people will naturally grow rich."*

The *Zhuangzi*’s commentary explains:
*"To govern a great nation is like cooking a small fresh fish—
if one overcooks it, the fish spoils;
if one overgoverns the people, they become confused.
The Sage pardons all under heaven,
forgetting distinctions in Dao/Way and skill,
like fish forgetting the rivers and lakes—
then there is no interference,
and life flourishes in order.
Is not the Way of enriching the people
found in this absence of interference?
Yet the world is originally without interference—
it is only the vulgar who disturb it.
Those who later alter the ancient ways
and disrupt the established order to invite disaster
do not understand the Way of enriching the people."*

*"I have no desires, and the people return to simplicity."*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"If one does not prize the virtuous,
the people will not vie for them;
if one does not value hard-to-obtain goods,
the people will not become thieves—
this aligns with having no desires,
and thus the people’s nature is preserved."*

The *Zhuangzi*’s commentary explains:
*"To promote the virtuous leads the people to compete;
