thus, by not prizing the virtuous,
the people do not vie for them.
Hard-to-obtain goods provoke obstruction;
thus, by not valuing hard-to-obtain goods,
the people do not become thieves.
If one does not prize the virtuous
and does not value goods,
then the people see nothing to desire.
The Sage’s use of the heart
aligns with having no desires—
this is called *substance-simplicity*,
and substance-simplicity preserves the people’s nature.

*Substance-simplicity* is the complete embodiment of the Dao,
before it disperses into things.
If the people return to their simplicity,
they see the Dao and not things,
and what they see surpasses what they merely observe.
If one cannot abandon desires and dispel longings,
one will still be ensnared by things—
one will still be bound by the threads of artificiality,
one will still be enslaved by the schemes of others—
then the people lose their simplicity,
drowning in human artifice—
how could they ever restore the ancient winds of virtue?"

The *Laozi* wrote:
*"Do not see what is desirable,
so that the heart does not become disturbed."*
*"No greater crime than desiring what is desirable."*
*"Fewer private concerns and lesser desires—
for desires harm nature,
and they must be dispelled."*

The *Mencius* said:
*"When it comes to being human,
those with many desires,
even if they have some virtue,
are few indeed."*

Its governance is obscure and profound—
the fifty-eighth chapter—
its governance is obscure and profound...

In governing with leniency, the people know of it,
yet they lack the joy of exultation.

The *Commentary* explains:
"The world has innate *virtue/power* (*德*), and each *virtue/power* has its natural *qi* (*性*).
It cannot be forced—
to force it is to contrive (*伪*),
it cannot be disturbed—
to disturb it brings anxiety.
Only by letting *qi* unfold without excess,
and by granting leniency so that *virtue/power* does not stray,
can one bring the whole world to a realm of tranquil simplicity (*澹汉*).
Then the people will all say, 'He governs us naturally,'
as the *Laozi* described when he said,
'*The people know of it* yet it brings them no exultation.'

When the people know of it,
their *qi* does not grow more vital,
their *virtue/power* does not diminish—
relaxed and harmonious, without exultation,
yet they still hold praise beyond measure.

Did the *Mencius* say:
'*The people of a king are pure and bright* (*皡皡如也*).
Is this not the governance of tranquil simplicity?'

As for the people being sincere (*淳淳*):
The *Huizong* annotation says:
'*Seeing simplicity and embracing naturalness, few private concerns and lesser desires.*'

The *Commentary* explains:
'*Simplicity and naturalness* (*素朴*) are the people’s innate *qi*.
To see and embrace it,
then purity and clarity remain unadulterated,
stillness and unity unchanging,
and they reach the point of *illuminating clarity* (*明白入素*),
where governance is effortless and naturalness restored.

*Private desires* (*私欲*) are the people’s emotions.
To have few private concerns and lesser desires,
then they conquer themselves in victory,
excel in nurturing the heart,
and reach the point of *turning from self for the public* (*背私为公*),
where they no longer see what is desirable.
No greater sincerity than this.

As for governance being *clear and strict* (*察察*),

*Huizong* annotation says:
"To issue simple orders and enforce them strictly—like combing hair after loosening it, or counting grains before cooking—
this is to take petty matters as brilliance (*窃窃然以苛为明*), the very essence of *clear and strict* governance."

The *Commentary* explains:
"The key to governance lies in the *Dao*, which must not be burdensome.
The Sage governs through the *Dao* in the world,
by assuming the utmost *emptiness* (*至虚*) to welcome the myriad forms of reality,
and by embodying the utmost *stillness* (*至静*) to receive the ceaseless movements of life—
thus achieving simplicity yet effectiveness.
They never resort to issuing simple orders and enforcing them strictly,
nor take petty matters as brilliance.

Indeed, issuing simple orders and enforcing them strictly
exhausts the spirit in trivial affairs,
and wearies the mind in insignificant matters.
To take petty matters as brilliance,
how can this suffice to benefit the world?

Thus, the *Dao* of governance values *clarity and stillness* (*清静*), letting the people regulate themselves.
When the people are *confused and restless* (*缺缺*),

*Huizong* annotation says:
"To elevate the worthy brings strife among the people (*举贤则民相轧*),
to entrust the clever leads to theft among the people (*任知则民相盗*),
thus there is no *full virtue* (*全德*)."

The *Commentary* explains:
"The Sage’s governance always ensures the people have no knowledge nor desires.
To elevate the worthy, the people will vie and compete (*跨跂而相轧*),
unable to settle their *fate’s distinctions* (*性命之分*);
to entrust the clever, the people will covet and plunder (*觊觎而相盗*),
unable to quiet their *fate’s emotions* (*性命之情*).

This is the peril of knowledge and desire (*有知有欲之为患*),
and the reason the people are led astray by external things (*迁於物*).
When led astray by external things,
it is insufficient to enrich the people,
thus there is no *full virtue*.

*Zhuangzi* says:
"*Full virtue* preserves the body (*德全者形全*),
and failing to preserve *full virtue*,
nourishing the body cannot sustain life—
this is what is meant by *the body’s essence being greatly depleted* (*形精大亏*),’
is it not the state of the people being *confused and restless* (*缺缺*)?

*Misfortune* and *fortune* are interdependent (*祸兮福所倚，福兮祸所伏*),
who knows their limits?

*Huizong* annotation says:
"*Illuminating clarity* (*昭昭*) arises from *obscurity* (*冥冥*),
and *order* (*有伦*) emerges from *formlessness* (*无形*).
*Virtue* and *wisdom* guard against *intellect*,
and their essence lies in *sickness and health* (*疢疾*).
In households of *high clarity* (*高明之家*),
spirits survey their halls (*鬼瞰其室*),
for they know time cannot be halted,
ranks cannot be fixed,
and beginnings and endings cannot be rigidly predetermined—
thus, the interdependence of *misfortune* and *fortune*,
what constancy can there be?

The *Commentary* explains:
Within *emptiness* and *stillness* (*虚静*),
the Great Transformation subtly shifts (*大化密移*),
from hidden to revealed (*由隐而之显*),
from nonexistence to manifestation (*自无而适有*).
If one lacks the true Sovereign yet fails to discern its signs (*不得其朕*),
then *illuminating clarity* arises from *obscurity*,
and *order* emerges from *formlessness*.

The *spirit* (*精神*) in its manifestation is *illuminating clarity*,
yet the *Dao*’s ultimate truth remains *formless*;
the *essence* (*精*) of things, once fully realized, is *formless*,
but their *order* (*有伦*) in completion reveals *form*.

The principles of *misfortune* and *fortune*
are concealed in the depths of existence,
responding like echoes and shadows (*应若影响*),
or the silent operation of the Transformative Mechanism (*化机*).
Only those who *penetrate* (*达者*) know
that *benefit* can generate *harm* (*利足以生害*),
and *joy* can breed *sorrow* (*乐足以生忧*).
Thus, they can apply *intellect* (*智*) at the outset,
before chaos has taken root (*未奔沈之初*),
and illuminate the path
amidst the faint and fleeting (*忽眇绵*),
so that *spontaneity* (*诞先*) may ascend
to the realm of effortless virtue (*无难之地*).

The *Mencius* warns of *breathing with caution* (*虑息之戒*), saying:
"*Virtue* and *wisdom* shield against *intellect*,
and their essence lies in *sickness and health* (*德慧卫智，存乎疢疾*),"
while *Yang Xiong*’s *Self-Parody* (*解嘲论*) states:
"*High clarity* households are watched by spirits (*高明之家，鬼瞰其室*),"
both penetrating this very truth.

Indeed, *virtue* and *wisdom* shield against *intellect*—
this can be called *clear and sagacious* (*明哲*),
yet they still hold *sickness and health* (*疢疾*) as their core,
for the *gentleman* must anticipate calamities and prepare defenses (*思患而豫防*).

*High clarity* households can be free of worry (*无虞*),
yet they must speak of spirits watching their halls (*鬼瞰其室*),
for spirits bring harm to the overfilled and fortune to the modest (*害盈而福谦*).

Observing this,
one must know that time cannot be halted (*时无止*),
ranks cannot be fixed (*分无常*),
and beginnings and endings cannot be rigidly predetermined (*终始无故*),
for this is the path to true understanding.

To know time cannot be halted,
one remains distant yet unconstrained (*遥而不闷*),
gathering without strain (*掇而不跂*);
to know ranks cannot be fixed,
one discerns excess and deficiency (*察乎盈虚*),
and neither gain nor loss can perturb the heart (*得失不足以撄其心*);
to know beginnings and endings cannot be rigidly predetermined,
one comprehends the way of *effortless spontaneity* (*坦涂*),
and neither death nor life can burden the heart (*死生不足以累其心*).

When time cannot be halted,
one passes through without lingering;
when ranks cannot be fixed,
one adapts without clinging;
when beginnings and endings cannot be rigidly predetermined,
one never finds fault—
thus, *misfortune* and *fortune* are interdependent,
mutually consuming and growing (*相为消长*),
what constancy can there be?

It is said that *fortune* is the beginning of *misfortune*,
and *misfortune* is the stage for *fortune*—
this is because their constancy is ever-changing, never fixed (*无常未始有极*).

What of *right* and *wrong*?
*Huizong* annotation says:
"If those who are *same* as me and you (*我与若*) are *right*,
then *winter* is *right* (*正冬*).
If they are *same* as me and you,
how can they be *right*?
If those who are *different* from me and you are *right*,
then *right* them.
If they are *different* from me and you,
how can they be *right*?
Then who can be the *ultimate right* for the world?"

The *Commentary* explains:
The world’s *right* and *wrong* (*是非*)
are nothing but the *same* and *different* between me and you (*我与若相为同异*).
If those who are *same* as me and you are *right*,
then *right* them.
Yet if they are *same* as me and you,
then both are *right*—
how can they be *right*?

If those who are *different* from me and you are *right*,
then *right* them.
Yet if they are *different* from me and you,
then both are *wrong*—
how can they be *right*?

If neither me nor you can truly know each other,
then who can know the *ultimate right* of the world?

If one cannot discern the *ultimate right*,
then their *body* knows only comfort and ease (*安佚*),
but not the *right* place to be (*正处*);
their *mouth* knows only coarse and fine tastes (*刍豢*),
but not the *right* flavor (*正味*);
their *eyes* know only beautiful colors (*美色*),
but not the *right* hues (*正色*).
This is the failure to attain *right* knowledge (*正知*).

Thus, the path of *right* and *wrong*,
how can I know its true distinctions?

*Right* becomes *strange* (*奇*),
*good* becomes *disastrous* (*祆*).
The people’s confusion (*迷*)
has been long since established.

「Emperor Huizong annotated: The world is but one *qi* in flow. What was *right* today may be *wrong* tomorrow; what was once *united* may later be *disjointed*. The divine and the foul, the fragrant and the putrid, arise in perpetual succession—thus, the *strange* and the *right* intergenerate, the *disastrous* and the *good* transform in cycles, all as the natural unfolding of one *qi*.

The world’s formation has endured long, yet minor confusions invert directions—turning east to west, facing the *Dark Mountain* yet failing to see it; great confusions distort nature—mistaking the nonexistent for the real, abandoning the *Mysterious Pearl* yet never seeking it. The habit of self-interest prevails, blinding them to the *curved* and the *straight*; they fail to grasp the *inverted* and *upright* interplay of calamity and fortune. Moreover, they scrutinize with rigid precision, yet only deepen their bewilderment—unresponsive, unenlightened. How then can the people return to their true essence?

Thus, Laozi composed the *Political Confusion* chapter, intending to guide the people back to simplicity and purity, to return to their true nature.

Hence, the Sage remains *square* yet does not cleave: Emperor Huizong annotated: *Square* means steadfast in discernment, yet the *Great Square* has no corners—still yet fluid, leaving no trace of rigid distinctions. The *Cushioned Explanation* states: Those bound by *square* forms cling to fixed views, differing from customs; this is the “steadfast in discernment” of which we speak. *Steadfast* here aligns with the *I Ching*’s “interposed” (*jiè*), as in *interposed* between stones. The *Great Square* has no bounds—no south nor north, its vastness dissolving in all directions, sufficient to answer the *unbounded* Way. It is not like those who cling to *square* forms, treating them as mere *vessels*.

The Sage remains *still* yet does not stagnate: This mirrors the *Great Stillness* above, untouched by fluctuations, like water settled in repose. The master carpenter takes its form as law, leaving no path of rigid distinctions—is this not what Confucius called “first stabilizing, then responding”?

The Sage remains *honest* yet does not excess: Emperor Huizong annotated: *Honest* yet excessive breeds arrogance; radiant yet blinding invites ruin. The Sage holds *straightness* within yet bends without, wielding light yet returning to clarity—only the Sage can do so.

The people’s confusion lies in treating *square* as *right*—like the *Child Mo* who clings to the center, or the *Middle Son* who holds to rigid virtue. They know extension yet not contraction, prominence yet not subtlety, and thus lose the Sage’s *Great Harmony*, boasting of superficial customs while neglecting the whole. How can they rectify the world?

What then is the Sage’s method to rectify the world? Simply this.

The Sage remains *clean* yet does not exclude: Emperor Huizong annotated: *Clean* yet excluding leads to harshness; the *Great Honest* is unobstructed, pure yet receptive, without rigid judgment. The *Cushioned Explanation* states: Those who guard their *cleanliness* with caution often pride themselves in self-purification—this is the “pride in self-purification” of which we speak. *Pride* here aligns with the *Analects*’ “the ancient *pride*” in *clean* conduct. The *Great Honest* is unobstructed, fully content without further demands, serene in self-sufficiency, rejecting the laborious and difficult. It is not like those who cling to *clean* yet fail to trust—rather, it is like the *Analects*’ “victorious yet unharmed.”

The Sage remains *bright* yet does not dazzle.»

「The *Cushioned Explanation* states: If one is rigid in self-righteousness and calls it *straightforwardness*, then *straightforwardness* will inevitably indulge one’s emotions, giving rise to the arrogance of trampling others. Only by abandoning the burdens of extreme rectitude and avoiding the faults of crookedness and strain can one achieve *straightforwardness* without indulgence. When applied to things, it moves flexibly and adaptably, never clashing with the world, and thus the arrogance of trampling others does not arise.

If one takes delight in pleasing the crowd and calls it *brightness*, then *brightness* will inevitably dazzle with outward display, inviting the calamity of boasting. Only by discarding the superficial brilliance of contrived appearances and the ostentatious glare of insincere flattery can one attain *brightness* without dazzle. When its radiance is used to illuminate action, it follows the times and conforms to things, never forcing the heart’s accord, and thus the calamity of boasting does not arise. This is how one remains *straightforward* inwardly yet *flexible* outwardly, wielding its *brightness* while restoring its *clarity*—this is the unique way of the Sage.

Indeed, inward *straightforwardness* is what aligns one with the *Dao* of Heaven. Outward *flexibility* is what harmonizes one with humanity. Only the fully *flexible* and *crooked* can achieve *straightness*—this is the Sage’s virtue. Wielding its *brightness* reveals all *Ren* (benevolence). Restoring its *clarity* conceals the subtleties of application. Only those who follow the manifest yet grasp the hidden, and who discern the pivotal yet remain in the mysterious, can achieve this.

For the Sage alone can harmonize with Heaven, humanity, and the divine *clarity*. Their virtue cannot be matched by others. As for the people’s confusion, it has long been so. Those who cling to *rigid* principles as truth, like **Zǐ Mò** (子莫) who grasped the *Middle*, cannot adapt with expedience and thus discard the hundred for the one. Those who prize *upright* conduct as truth, like **Zhòngzǐ** (仲子) who upheld *purity*, cannot fulfill its broader scope and thus stain themselves while disrupting harmony. To yield is *crookedness*; to advance is *extension*. Knowing *extension* yet not *crookedness*, one is fixed in rigidity and cannot harmonize with the natural flow—how then can one know *crookedness* and *extension* in mutual accord, as in the *Book of Changes*’ “beneficial for use, secure in abiding”?

The *hidden* is the *profound*; the *manifest* is the *displayed*. Knowing only the *displayed* yet not the *profound*, one is trapped in superficiality and cannot inherit its *clarity*—how then can one know the *profound* and the *displayed*, as in the *Book of Changes*’ “discerning the few, this is the divine”?

**Zǐ Mò**’s grasp of the *Middle*, **Zhòngzǐ**’s rigid *purity*, knowing only *extension* yet not *crookedness*, knowing only the *displayed* yet not the *profound*—all these are blinded by private bias, narrow and incomplete. They strain their intentions and boast of their conduct to flaunt the flaws of the age, losing the Sage’s full completeness. They do not see the pure and complete essence of Heaven, Earth, and the ancient worthies—how can they rectify the world?

This is the failure to rectify oneself while presuming to rectify others—clearly, they do not comprehend what is possible. The Sage’s way of rectifying the world is simply this: *rigid* yet not divisive, *upright* yet not rigidly harsh, *straightforward* yet not indulgent, *bright* yet not dazzling.

The Sage embodies the complete beauty of the *Dao*, holding fast to the *One* as the model for the world. By extending this, rectifying the world’s true essence becomes a mere formality. This is how **Great Shun** could rectify all beings, achieving governance through *non-action*.

**Chapter 59: Rectifying Humanity and Serving Heaven**

Rectifying humanity and serving Heaven—nothing surpasses *frugality*.

Emperor Huizong annotated: *Hearing* (聪明) and *intelligence* (智识) are Heaven’s gifts. *Action* (动) and *rest** (静), *thought* (思), and *deliberation* (虑) are human affairs. Adjusting the measures of *action* and *rest*, moderating the burdens of *thought* and *deliberation*—this is how one rectifies humanity. Not exhausting the power of *hearing* nor fully depleting the capacity of *intelligence*—this is how one serves Heaven. This is called *frugality*.

The *One* of Heaven resides in the *zàng* organs (脏), with the *kidneys* (肾) as its domain. Established in the *unyielding field* (不贷之圃), when *intelligence* is abundant and *frugality* flows, humanity is rectified and Heaven’s *principle* is attained.

The *Cushioned Explanation* states: To know what Heaven does and what humanity does—this is the ultimate. Thus, in attaining the natural, there is no action not of Heaven; in observing expedience, there is no action not of humanity. *Hearing* and *intelligence* arise from the natural and are perfected by Heaven, hence **Han Fei** said: *Hearing* and *intelligence* are Heaven’s gifts. *Action* and *rest*, *thought* and *deliberation*, emerge from expedience and are rooted in humanity, hence **Han Fei** said: *Action* and *rest*, *thought* and *deliberation* are human affairs. To rectify humanity and serve Heaven, one must first prioritize *frugality*—for *action* and *rest* have their constants, the true *principle*. What one thinks and deliberates is the ultimate *Dao*. Adjusting the measures of *action* and *rest* ensures that *action* occurs only at its proper time. Moderating the burdens of *thought* and *deliberation* leads to a tranquil, ever-serene state. Observing expedience in human affairs—this is *frugality* in its essence.

To abandon *hearing* is to align with the *Great Harmony* (大通). To discard *intelligence* is to follow Heaven’s *principle*. Not exhausting the power of *hearing* allows one to *withdraw sight* and *return hearing* (收视反听). Not fully depleting the capacity of *intelligence* enables one to *revert to simplicity* and *return to purity* (还淳复朴). Attaining the natural in Heaven’s affairs—this too is *frugality*.

Indeed, the *One* of Heaven generates *water* (水), which in humanity is *essence* (精). The *kidneys* are the *zàng* organ that houses *essence*. The *One* of Heaven resides in the *zàng*, its origin rooted in the *unyielding field*—hence the *kidneys* are its domain. Yet it does not depart from *essence*, for this is the way of the *Divine Person* (神人). Thus, in rectifying humanity and serving Heaven, nothing surpasses *frugality*.

Established in the *unyielding field*, one fears only excess. When *intelligence* is abundant and *frugality* flows, it does not waste itself in virtue. By this means, humanity is rectified and Heaven’s *principle* is attained. Only through *frugality* does one achieve early restoration.

Emperor Huizong annotated: Restoration comes late when one awakens from confusion. If one seeks restoration before confusion sets in, *frugality* does not waste itself in human nature—thus, restoration comes early.

The *Cushioned Explanation* states: To attain one’s *nature* (性) is to perpetuate life endlessly; to lose one’s *nature* is to fail in perpetuation and reach exhaustion. To return to one’s original *nature* and restore its essence, one must value swift restoration. Restoration after confusion is far removed from the original *nature*—this is the people’s long-standing confusion, the cause of their late awakening.»

「Observe the thunder within the earth—it is *Fù* (復) in the *Hexagram*, yet the first line of nine states: *"No regret, supreme auspiciousness."* But the top line of six becomes ominous. For the first line’s *Fù* is near, while the top line’s *Fù* comes only after confusion has taken hold.

The *Hexagram of Fù* (比) likewise: the first line speaks of *"There is another,"* auspicious; the sixth line declares *"Fù without a head,"* ominous. This is because *Fù* and *Bǐ* (比) prefer to act early.

To practice *frugality* is to not waste one’s *nature* (性), and when *nature* is yet near its source, restoration comes early. Early restoration is called *accumulating virtue* (重积德).

Emperor Huizong annotated: *Fù* (復) is the root of virtue. When one restores through self-awareness, the Dao within us grows daily and ever renews.

The *Cushioned Explanation* states: Virtue is attainment. Attainment from one’s *nature* is what is meant. To observe *Fù* (復) in the midst of the *common multitude* (芸芸), to *Fù* (復) at the very moment of *adversity* (挠挠), is to never stray from one’s *nature*. Thus, the *Book of Changes* says: *Fù* (復), the root of virtue.

If one can restore to its root, then *nature* is cultivated and virtue is perfected, and wisdom will never fail to illuminate. Thus, the *Book of Changes* again says: *Fù* (復) with self-awareness. Therefore, when one does not waste one’s *nature* and restores early, virtue grows daily and becomes vast.

The *Classic of Documents* (《书》) says: Virtue is renewed daily, and again renewed daily. *Accumulating virtue* (重积德) means none can overcome it.

Emperor Huizong annotated: To conquer is to prevail. To govern all things and command the myriad movements without any that cannot be overcome—this is virtue alone.

The *Cushioned Explanation* states: When virtue is complete within oneself, one does not seek to conquer others but prevails over all. Thus, within oneself it is abundant, and when bestowed upon others, there is still surplus. In its operation and response, it is boundless and universally applicable, its radiance extending to the four quarters and reaching both above and below. Who can know its limits?

Mencius said: Virtue’s teachings overflow abundantly, permeating the four seas. If none can know its limits, one can have a kingdom. Emperor Huizong annotated: When virtue is fully realized and boundless, its application to the world is effortless. How much more so for a single kingdom?

The *Cushioned Explanation* states: The true Dao begins with governing oneself; its excess is applied to the state, and its fullness extends to the world. When virtue reaches the stage of *Fù* (復) in its original state, it is boundless and pure. This is what Zhuangzi called *exhausting the whole to achieve unity* (周尽一体). Though the world is vast, it lies within one’s grasp, so its application to the world is effortless. How much more so for a small kingdom?

The mother of having a kingdom can endure forever. Emperor Huizong annotated: The Dao is the mother of all things, and those who possess the Dao will never falter throughout the ages.

The *Cushioned Explanation* states: The world has a beginning, and this beginning is the mother of the world. Thus, the Dao is the mother of all things. From formed existence to formlessness, from having a mind to being mindless, all arise from it. Hence, it is called the mother.

The mother of having a kingdom is the possession of the Dao. Those who attain the Dao transcend time, enduring forever without flaw—what is this if not longevity and eternal vision?

Emperor Huizong annotated: The Dao is the mother of all things, and all things are its offspring. *Nature* (性) is the root of form, and form is its foundation. Knowing its offspring and guarding its mother, one will not perish in life. Thus, one can endure forever. A deep root secures its foundation; restoring *nature* preserves form. Aligned with Heaven and Earth, one attains eternal life. Sharing light with the sun and moon, one attains eternal vision. When all things transform while I alone remain, this is called the Dao.

The *Cushioned Explanation* states: There are beings, and there are beings that give rise to all things. The former are things; the latter is the Dao. The text says: *"The named is the mother of all things."* Zhuangzi said: *"All things flourish and grow from non-action."* Thus, the Dao is the mother of all things, and all things are its offspring.

There are forms, and there are forms that give rise to forms. Forms are the physical; forms that give rise to forms are *nature* (性). Zhuangzi said: *"Nature is the essence of life."* Liuzi said: *"Form is the vessel of life."* Thus, *nature* is the root of form, and form is its foundation.

The mother of having a kingdom is the one who knows its offspring and guards its mother. Guarding its mother means uniting with the Dao, enduring through all ages and remaining constant. Thus, one will not perish in life and can endure forever.

Now, take the growth of grasses and trees: deep roots secure their foundations, just as in humans, restoring *nature* preserves form. Only by preserving form can one ensure life’s vitality without exhaustion, like grasses and trees rooted in the earth, enduring without end. Thus, aligned with Heaven and Earth, one attains eternal life, and wisdom never ceases, like the sun and moon sharing their light, one attains eternal vision.

When all things transform while I alone remain, this is to transcend form and not transform with it, to depart from fate and not perish with it—this is called the Dao.

Chapter Sixty of *The Great Learning* (《记》):
To govern a great kingdom, one must act as if cooking a small fish.」

「Huizong’s annotation states: When dealing with the multitude and often disturbing them, success will be rare. When storing great treasures and frequently moving them, many will be damaged. When cooking a small fish and constantly stirring it, it will be ruined. When ruling a great state and frequently altering its laws, confusion will arise. Therefore, the Dao of governance values clarity and stillness, and the people will naturally settle.

The *Cushui* commentary explains: If a ruler favors strictness, all affairs will be meticulous; if he favors meticulousness, all affairs will become chaotic. Hence, the ruler’s Dao lies in governing with singular focus—if he knows how to govern one thing properly, then all other matters will follow in order. Thus, it is natural that dealing with the multitude and often disturbing them yields little success, and storing great treasures while frequently moving them leads to many damages.

Indeed, the world has its fixed ways: to follow them brings order, to disrupt them brings worry. Only by not disrupting Heaven’s natural course and not opposing the inclinations of things can one establish laws that are firm and unchanging, avoiding the flaw of issuing morning decrees and evening reversals. Then, the world will be well-governed. Just as with cooking fish—if one constantly stirs it, it will spoil—so too, ruling a great state by frequently altering its laws will surely bring confusion.

*The Classic of Transmission* says: If the people trust in their laws, they will be obedient. This is the virtue of ancient sages in lawmaking: their decrees were steadfast as metal and stone, reliable as the four seasons, and never changed.

Therefore, by remaining unadulterated and clear, embracing the Way with tranquility, the people will have no need for governance and will settle naturally. Hence, the Dao of governance values clarity and stillness, and the people will naturally settle. Who would willingly multiply laws and edicts to burden the masses?

Those who govern the world with the Way, their spirits are not malevolent. Huizong’s annotation states: The sage is the Way’s sovereign over all people and things. When compelled to govern the world, the best course is *wuwei* (non-action). The Way is ever in non-action, yet through non-action, all things are governed. Those who rely on others to act will also find stability and rest, none rising to cause misfortune, hence it is said that their spirits are not malevolent.

The *Cushui* commentary explains: The *Book of Poetry* says, *"A hundred spirits obey you."* The *Book of Documents* says, *"Only the primordial sovereign acts as parent and mother to the people."* Zhuangzi says, *"The sage is the pivot upon which all things hinge."* Thus, the sage is indeed the Way’s sovereign over all people and things. Yet, it is easy for me to forget the world, but difficult for the world to forget me. The reason the sage emerges to guide the world is but a dwelling in necessity, a temporary abode.

When compelled to govern the world, what else is there to do but maintain reverence and uprightness? Then, through non-action alone, the people’s lives will be at peace, and they will transform naturally. Thus, none will remain ungoverned. Those who rely on others to act and yet claim to be the Way’s sovereign will find their own support and ease, all settling into stability and rest, none rising to cause misfortune—like the *Book of Poetry* says, *"The ancestral spirits come to rest, their fragrance lingering."* Hence, it is said that their spirits are not malevolent.

It is not that their spirits are not malevolent, but that the Way’s spirit does not harm the people. It is not that the Way’s spirit does not harm the people, but that the sage also does not harm the people. When the two do not harm each other, their virtue reciprocally returns.

Huizong’s annotation states: Those who govern the world with the Way do nothing yet everything is naturally so—there are no wars of aggression, no punishments of slaughter. This is what is meant by "not harming the people."

In such times, the Way’s spirit and the people’s spirit do not harm each other, and virtue reciprocally returns. The Way’s spirit has no need to manifest its divine influence, but only bestows abundant blessings, hence it is said that the Way’s spirit does not harm the people. The people need not exert their cleverness, for their daily lives are but sustenance and nourishment—how could there be any harm?

The *Cushui* commentary explains: The Way is ever in non-action yet accomplishes all. If one governs the world with the Way, one follows its inherent nature without imposing artificial disruptions—this is what is meant by *"doing nothing yet everything is naturally so."* Protecting the people as a father, viewing them as a mother, never resorting to arms—thus there is no calamity of war. Punishments are set aside and unused—thus there are no executions. The Way’s spirit and the people’s spirit do not harm each other, and the bright are without human censure, the hidden without ghostly blame. Then virtue reciprocally returns, for the Way’s spirit has no need to manifest its divine influence, but only bestows abundant blessings, as the *Book of Poetry* says, *"The Way’s spirit descends in sorrow."* The people need not exert their cleverness, for their daily lives are but sustenance and nourishment—like the *Book of Poetry* says, *"The people are simple."* How could there be any harm?

Chapter Sixty-One of *The Classic of Transmission*:
Great states are the lower reaches of the rivers...」

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"No one lacks the inclination toward the high, yet those who pursue the high often stumble."* The rivers and seas become the sovereigns of all valleys because they excel in yielding. The *Cunyi* commentary explains: *"A nine-tiered tower begins with piled earth; to ascend high, one must start from below. Knowing that the high depends on the low as its foundation, one avoids stumbling by not elevating without a base."* Merely desiring the high while neglecting the low is to love the high without attaining it—how could one not stumble?

Water does not accumulate without forming depths; rivers and seas become great by receiving all streams. Thus, they reign over all valleys, and waters pay homage to them because they excel in yielding. Then, is it not the case that a state’s greatness lies in its yielding, and its governance follows accordingly?

The meeting place of all under Heaven, the feminine of all under Heaven—
the feminine always overcomes the masculine through stillness,
and stillness is the way of yielding.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"While all others rely on strength to contend, I alone dwell in the realm of gentle stillness and non-contention—who then can overcome me?"* This is the Dao of harmonizing with all under Heaven. The *Classic* says: *"Knowing the masculine, one holds to the feminine, thus becoming the valley of all under Heaven."*

The *Cunyi* commentary explains: *"From the perspective of things, only the low can support, only the yielding can prevail. This is the inherent principle: those who rely on fierceness and exploit others, thinking to rise above, merely court ruin. The constant way of victory lies in yielding; the constant way of defeat lies in rigidity."*

All under Heaven rely on strength to contend, yet the Sage adopts weakness, humility, and yielding as his standard. Dwelling in stillness and non-contention, he retreats only to lead, withdraws only to endure. Though he does not seek to overcome others, none can overcome him—this is the Dao of harmonizing with all under Heaven. The *Classic* says: *"Knowing the masculine, one holds to the feminine, thus becoming the valley of all under Heaven."* Indeed, knowing the way to overcome, yet dwelling in yielding stillness, all things must yield to it—because of its stillness.

Thus, when great states yield to small states, they take the small states;
when small states yield to great states, they take the great states.
Hence, sometimes one yields to take, sometimes one yields and takes.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"To gather, one must first disperse; to take, one must first give."*

The *Cunyi* commentary explains: *"There is contraction and expansion, fullness and emptiness—this is not only human affairs but the natural order of Heaven. Only those who perceive these subtle shifts beforehand know that to gather, one must first disperse, understanding that contraction and expansion mutually consume each other; to take, one must first give, knowing that fullness and emptiness follow as beginning and end."*

Thus, great states yield to small states with the affairs of greatness, and small states yield to great states with the affairs of smallness—each attains what it desires. Then, to rise above others, one must speak of yielding, yet the gentleman does not wish to rise above many—precisely for this reason.

Great states seek only to nurture others, small states seek only to serve others—both attain what they desire, hence the great should yield.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The Dao of Heaven is yielding yet illuminates all; thus it treads without failing. The Dao of Earth is low yet ascends; thus it sustains Heaven."* Human affairs follow Earth, Earth follows Heaven—hence the great should yield.

The *Cunyi* commentary explains: *"The Dao of Heaven yields yet nourishes all, its light covering without bound. The Dao of Earth is low yet ascends, sustaining the seasons of Heaven."* Practicing the way of humility and openness, even Heaven and Earth do so—how much more so for humanity? Emperor Yao shone with sincere reverence, his virtue reaching to the four quarters; Emperor Shun ascended with gentle humility, his profound virtue heard in Heaven. All this is but following the ways of Heaven and Earth.

The Three Powers occupy distinct positions, yet their ways are one—
thus the Sage does not exalt himself, following Earth,
and so achieves effortless success for all under Heaven.
Earth does not exalt itself, following Heaven,
and so nurtures all things without striving.
Thus, not exalting oneself is the way to achieve greatness—
this is the constant truth.

Hence, the great should yield—is it not so?

*The Commentary on the Dao De Jing* concludes, Volume Eleven.
*The Commentary on the Dao De Jing* begins, Volume Twelve.

**The Scholar Jiang Cheng’s Commentary on the *Dao De Jing* – Chapter Sixty-Two: *The Dao as the Hidden Essence of All Things***

The Dao is the hidden essence of all things.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation:
The hidden essence of Heaven lies in the west-north, where the vital essence condenses.
The hidden essence of Earth lies in the Yellow Springs, where the spirit-forms conceal themselves.
The hidden essence of humanity lies in thought and deliberation, where the supreme spirit is nurtured.

Heaven, Earth, and humanity each possess their hidden essences, yet all are encompassed by the Dao.

The Dao is difficult to exhaust, difficult to fathom, difficult to measure, and difficult to recognize—thus it is the hidden essence of all things.

If the Dao is the hidden essence of all things, then are not all things the manifest expression of the Dao?

*The Commentary* states:
The Dao’s hidden nature is unfathomably deep—like the innermost chamber of a hall, sealed in profound secrecy, or like the depths of the Qi River, concealed and locked away. All that is perverse and manifest yet enters into concealment possesses its hidden essence. In the *Taiyuan* [section], the Three Models are discussed precisely to speak of their hidden depths.

Heaven’s hidden essence lies in the unseen—its west-north corresponds to the *Kan* hexagram, returning to the utmost stillness, where the vital essence condenses. When the vital essence condenses, the movement of *qi* becomes subtle and concealed; when the sun meets the moon, it is obscured—thus it is said: *The hidden essence of Heaven lies in the west-north, where the vital essence condenses.*

Earth’s hidden essence lies in the formless—its Yellow Springs are unfathomably deep, plunged into the utmost darkness, where the spirit-forms conceal themselves. When the spirit-forms conceal themselves, the *yang* energy germinates in secret, hiding its form—thus it is said: *The hidden essence of Earth lies in the Yellow Springs, where the spirit-forms conceal themselves.*

Humanity’s hidden essence lies in the heart and abdomen—thought and deliberation reside within humanity, bending and rising to embrace the four seas, drifting yet weaving through all directions, where the supreme spirit is nurtured. When the supreme spirit is nurtured, it becomes wondrous and boundless, beyond measure—thus it is said: *The hidden essence of humanity lies in thought and deliberation, where the supreme spirit is nurtured.*

The Three Powers occupy distinct positions, yet each has its hidden essence. Though Heaven possesses its hidden essence, the Dao gives rise to Heaven; though Earth possesses its hidden essence, the Dao gives rise to Earth; though humanity possesses its hidden essence, humanity abides within the Dao. Thus all are encompassed by the Dao—because the Dao cannot be sought through form or number.

The Three Powers have not yet departed from form and number, yet the Dao cannot be sought through form and number. It may be called difficult to exhaust and difficult to fathom, yet it has never been confined; difficult to measure and difficult to recognize, yet its mysteries cannot be glimpsed.

The virtuous lend and yet fulfill—its transformation is subtle and profound. The Great Dao is formless, giving birth to Heaven and Earth; the Great Dao is nameless, nourishing all things—thus it is the hidden essence of all things.

If the Dao is the hidden essence of all things, then from the nonexistent arises the existent, and all things, though manifest, cannot evade it—are they not the manifest expression of the Dao?

**The Treasure of the Virtuous Person**

Emperor Huizong’s annotation:
To practice virtue is to accumulate goodness until nature is perfected, and the divine clarity is naturally attained. The Sage follows this path.

*The Commentary* states:
There are those who treasure material things—jewels and jade, as spoken of in the *Transmissions*.
There are those who treasure the Dao—such as this statement on the treasure of the virtuous person.

The virtuous person does not treasure material things; their treasure lies in the Dao. Thus, from humanity they ascend to Heaven, from virtue they reach sainthood, and without entering they attain self-possession.

For the Dao is the interplay of *yin* and *yang*. What follows it is virtue; what fulfills it is nature. The virtuous person knows that virtue arises from the Dao and is perfected in nature, and thus their unadorned simplicity can be practiced and realized.

When practiced, it becomes boundless in its goodness, and in its realization, it never falters. Able to exhaust their nature, they can assist in the transformation and nourishment of all things, aligning with the divine.

From the accumulation of desired goodness to the fulfillment of perfect beauty—this is the accumulation of virtue into nature.
From the radiance of great progress to the attainment of the ineffable divine—this is the divine clarity naturally attained.

If the Sage’s heart follows this path, then they do not stray from the Dao.

What treasure can surpass this?

**What the Non-Virtuous Preserve**

Emperor Huizong’s annotation:
To act contrary to the Dao is to invite harm; to follow its principles is to remain whole. To act without restraint is to invite perversity; to still one’s nature is to remain upright—thus one can preserve the body.

*The Commentary* states:
The Dao acts without action yet accomplishes all things.
If one acts contrary to its all-encompassing nature, there will be no harm.
If one acts without restraint, there will be no perversity.

To act contrary to the Dao without harm—one must abandon cleverness and artifice, follow its principles without deviation, and thus guard their wholeness, so that nothing can harm them. This is called *sincerity made whole and returned to its origin.*

To act without restraint yet remain upright—one must preserve their true nature in emptiness, still their essence without disturbance, and thus attain their uprightness, so that perversity cannot enter. This is called *the four extremes undisturbed, the heart then upright.*

To follow its principles and remain whole, to still one’s nature and remain upright—no matter what the world offers, it cannot exchange their守. Thus they can preserve their body without fear of ruin, and this is as it should be.

The *Yijing* says: *To exhaust principle, to exhaust nature, and thus to attain fate.*
To follow its principles is to exhaust principle;
To exhaust one’s nature is to exhaust nature;
To preserve the body is to attain fate.

What the non-virtuous preserve is no different from this.

Fine words can be sold in the market,
Noble deeds can be bestowed upon others.
What is there to abandon in humanity’s failings?

Emperor Huizong’s annotation:
Words are like waves—deeds are like the loss of substance.
Neither is what the Dao values.

When words are beautiful and pleasing, when deeds are noble and worthy of emulation, they can still be sold in the market, or bestowed upon others, and people will submit to them. How much more so when one embodies the Dao’s hidden essence, encompassing all without distinction?

Thus, what is there to abandon in humanity’s failings?

*The Commentary* states:
The heart, when dwelling in emptiness and stillness, is virtuous. When it manifests in speech, it becomes the waves of wind—when it ceases, it becomes substance, and when it appears in action, it becomes the loss of substance.

Words are like waves—deeds are like the loss of substance.
Both are lost in superficiality and are not what the Dao values.

Yet when speech is harmonious and the people are content, then beautiful words that please are still sufficient to—

「Reverence, caution, awe, and propriety—these are the norms for the people. When actions are noble and worthy of emulation, they are still sufficient to bestow upon others, and how much more so when one embodies the Dao’s hidden essence?

Only when one embodies the Dao’s hidden essence does one nurture and manifest the myriad transformations, encompass all wonders, move like the heavens in their operation—covering all without partiality—and hold like the sea in its capacity—containing all without omission. Though they may appear diverse and conflicting, all converge in the singular principle of the Dao. What then is there to distinguish?

Thus, what is there to abandon in humanity’s imperfections?

*The Classic of Poetry* says:
*"Is this not my great sage?*
*What is there in others that he does not embrace?"*

Therefore, only those who embody the Dao’s hidden essence can truly fulfill the universal principle of inclusivity. Hence, establishing the Son of Heaven and appointing the Three Exalted Ministers—though they may offer jade discs and four-horse chariots to attract the worthy—is not as effective as simply cultivating this Dao to refine oneself. The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:
*"The virtue of a gentleman lies in cultivating his own person to achieve peace in the realm. The Son of Heaven and the Three Exalted Ministers, though they use jade discs and four-horse chariots to summon the worthy and gain their service, fail to prioritize the cultivation of the Dao within themselves. Thus, they forsake themselves for others and lose the path of self-governance. ‘Not as effective as cultivating this Dao’ means seeking virtue within oneself alone. Where the Dao resides, the Sage holds it in reverence, and thus the people follow it as they return to the marketplace."*

*The Commentary* states:
*"Though the world is vast, governance lies in the Dao; though the four seas are distant, governance lies in the heart. What is the greatest of guardianship? Guardianship of one’s person is greatest. When that which is guarded is most refined, then its application becomes most expansive. The ancients who spoke of governance began with self-discipline, their thoughts reaching far even in proximity. This is achieved through the cultivation of one’s own person. As for governing the realm, it is because the gentleman’s self-cultivation brings peace to the world. Though the Son of Heaven and the Three Exalted Ministers are esteemed for their rank, if they govern the people through the Dao, the realm will naturally submit. Even if they use jade discs and four-horse chariots to attract the worthy and gain their service, making them a glory for the state, yet they do not prioritize the cultivation of the Dao within themselves, they cannot maintain true guardianship. Without true guardianship, they abandon themselves for others and lose the path of self-governance.*

*Yangzi said: ‘First govern oneself, then govern others—that is the mark of a great vessel.’*
*‘Not as effective as cultivating this Dao’ means prioritizing self-governance.*

*What is called self-governance is not external influence; it is seeking within oneself alone. If one seeks within oneself alone, then the use of others is but an extension of oneself. In ancient times, Emperor Yao achieved clarity and virtue, bringing the common folk to harmony in times of change. King Wen of Zhou was born with sagely wisdom, ascending to the shore of governance and ruling his state with ease. Each attained this through the same means.*

*Though the Dao’s true essence lies in self-cultivation, its subtle extensions govern the state, and its fundamental principles govern the world—yet the key is to understand the Dao. Hence, where the Dao resides, the Sage holds it in reverence, and thus the people follow it as they return to the marketplace. The Sage is the parent of the people. When the Sage holds the Dao in reverence above, the people follow it below. If they grasp the Dao with the tenacity of a dog or the steadfastness of an elephant, will not the world turn toward them? Why was this Dao so esteemed in antiquity? Was it not because one seeks and attains it, and even the guilty are absolved? Hence, it is most precious for the world.*

*The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:*
*‘To seek is to attain—this seeking is within oneself.’*
*Ancient people sought the Dao in the principles of yin and yang and the rhythms of fate, yet did not attain it. This was because they sought externally. Can the malevolent be transformed into the virtuous, and the foolish into the wise? This is how the guilty are absolved. The Dao’s benevolent teachings are such, hence its preciousness for the world.*

*The Classic of Changes says:*
*‘There is nothing in the world that is not esteemed—except the Dao.’*
*The Commentary states:*
*‘The heart is the sovereign of the Dao, so the Dao is never separate from me. The Dao cannot be sought through emotions, so it is never obstructed by external things. Since the Dao is never separate from me, one must seek it within oneself to attain true attainment—because seeking lies within me. Since the Dao is never obstructed by external things, one must seek it through the principles of yin and yang and the rhythms of fate. Seeking it for five years without attaining it, seeking it through yin and yang for twelve years without attaining it—this is because seeking lies externally. Seeking within oneself is beneficial for attainment; seeking externally is not. Thus, those who seek but do not attain do so because the Dao is near, yet they seek it far. But the virtuous seek and attain it by seeking within themselves alone. The Dao within oneself contains all beauties; people only suffer from not seeking it. If one truly aligns the heart with the Dao, then the malevolent can be transformed into the virtuous, virtue cultivated unconsciously, and there will be no fault of misconduct. The foolish can become wise, understanding the principles and awakening to them, without the confusion of divergent paths. This is how the guilty are absolved. The Dao’s benevolent salvation of humanity is such, hence it cannot be cheapened—it is most precious for the world. ‘There is nothing in the world that is not esteemed—except the Dao’ means it is not esteemed by external titles, yet its natural reverence is ever-present.*

**Chapter Sixty-Three: On Non-Action**
*To practice non-action (wuwei), to act without clinging to action (shi-wu-shi), to savor without attachment to flavor (wei-wu-wei).*

*The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states:*
*The Dao’s essence is without fabrication, hence non-action (wuwei); without form, hence without clinging to action (shi-wu-shi); without desire, hence without attachment to flavor (wei-wu-wei). The Sage responds to things as they are, yet embodies the Dao’s essence of non-fabrication in these three aspects.*

*The Commentary states:*
*The Dao’s subtlety lies in its non-existence; it cannot be sought through physical form. Yet when speaking of its grand completeness, we provisionally call it ‘essence.’ What is called the Dao’s essence is like Zhuangzi’s description of the ancient people’s great framework. Since the Dao’s essence is subtly non-existent, its stillness and motionlessness are non-fabrication (wuzuo). Non-fabrication means non-action (wuwei), and non-action means the utmost action is without fabrication. The formless form is without clinging to form (wuxiang). Without clinging to form means without clinging to action (shi-wu-shi), and without clinging to action means no burden of fabrication or action. The tranquil sufficiency is without desire (wuyu). Without desire means without attachment to flavor (wei-wu-wei), and without attachment to flavor means those who crave flavor have never truly presented themselves."*

「Engaging in affairs and undertaking deeds—though they are accomplished, they are said to be "action," yet their origin lies in *wuwei* [non-action].
The ears, eyes, nose, and mouth each have their functions—though they manifest form, their operations arise from *wushì* [non-affairs].
The taste of the river is universally desired—though it is called "desire," its essence comes from *wuwèi* [non-flavor].
Thus, one sees how the marvelous application of the Dao unfolds.

The Sage embodies the true *wu* [non-being] while perpetually manifesting *you* [being], and manifests the marvelous *yòng* [function] while perpetually returning to *wu* [non-being].
Therefore, in responding to the *you* of things, it is as if the *wu* naturally transforms into *you*.
In embodying the *wu* of the Dao, it reaches the utmost *wu* to fulfill its *you*.
Through these three aspects, the Dao’s essence and function are indeed manifest.

**Great and small, much and little:**
Huizong’s annotation: "Great and small" refers to form, "much and little" to quantity. The measure of things is boundless, beyond enumeration.
If something is great yet not excessive, small yet not deficient, then the distinctions between resentment and gratitude—who can discern them?

The Sage unifies all things in the One *wu*, thus achieving the utmost greatness.
The *Cushui* explains:
From the perspective of things, they appear in diverse forms—whether the void or the great marsh, the minutest hair or a horse’s body—differing in shape and confined by form, varying in number and constrained by quantity.
From the perspective of the Dao, all things are one. Though the Northern Sea is vast, it is unknown whether it is "great yet not excessive"; though the Jīng River’s flow is small, it is unknown whether it is "small yet not deficient." Since the measure of things is boundless, it cannot be enumerated.
Thus, resentment may have its distinctions, but gratitude arises from the heart. Though these two may be instruments of rectification, they are not the Dao of rectification itself.
Acting without expectation of return, the Sage harmonizes with all things—how then can distinctions be seen?

The Sage, through the emptiness of the Dao, receives all the manifold realities of the world, never causing harm to others, never hoarding benevolence and grace. Thus, unifying all things in the One *wu*, gathering all smallness into unity, achieving the utmost greatness—spanning heaven and earth, vast beyond containment.
Therefore, the Emperor and King take heaven and earth as their ancestral model, and this is the pinnacle of the central realm.

**Repaying resentment with virtue:**
Huizong’s annotation: Titles and rewards are insufficient as incentives, punishments and shame are insufficient as deterrents—then what resentment remains? What is valued is virtue alone.
The *Cushui* explains:
One who embodies complete virtue gains the world’s praise yet remains indifferent, unaffected by the rewards of crowns and honors—thus, titles and rewards are insufficient as incentives.
One who embodies complete virtue faces the world’s censure yet remains unshaken, fearing neither the executioner’s axe nor the blade—thus, punishments and shame are insufficient as deterrents.
When honor and disgrace arise, they neither benefit nor harm their true nature. Thus, distinctions of right and wrong cannot be divided, and distinctions of greatness and smallness cannot be enumerated. At the utmost division, there is ultimately no division—then what resentment remains?
Only one of supreme virtue can achieve this. Thus, what is truly valued is virtue alone.

For the Yang is virtue, the Yin is resentment. Repaying resentment with virtue means merging into the indistinct, flowing without obstruction—this is called "responding to resentment without resentment," and it is the highest expression of virtue.

**Addressing difficulties in their ease, achieving greatness in the minute:**
The world’s difficult matters must begin in ease, the world’s great affairs must begin in the minute.
Huizong’s annotation:
A thousand-zhang dike collapses from the burrow of an ant;
A hundred-foot hall burns from a crack’s smoke.
White Gui’s dike-walking seals the burrows, thus avoiding water’s calamity.
The aged man’s fire-watching seals the gaps, thus avoiding fire’s disaster.
Worldly affairs always originate in the most trivial matters, and by the time they reach their end, they become impossible to counter—thus, the Sage acts early.

The *Cushui* explains:
*The Classic* says, "Do not let it spread," for once it spreads, it is difficult to counter—thus, those who plan far ahead do not neglect what is easy.
*The Scripture* says, "Do it before it exists," for those who know the few must examine what is minute.
A thousand-zhang dike may be called a river’s defense, yet if the ant’s burrow—though small—is not stopped, the trickle will not be dammed, and it will become a river.
A hundred-foot hall may be called a secure dwelling, yet if the crack’s smoke—though faint—is not heeded, the flicker will not be extinguished, and the flames will rage—what then?
White Gui’s dike-walking must seal the burrows, thus avoiding water’s calamity.
The aged man’s fire-watching must seal the gaps, thus avoiding fire’s disaster.
Yet the enlightened sage acts brilliantly in the fleeting and subtle, applying wisdom before matters become overwhelming. Each time this is observed, it echoes Han Fei’s teachings.

Thus, it is seen: the world’s affairs always originate in the most trivial matters.
*The Odes* say, "On auspicious days, be cautious of the minute," because affairs arise from the most trivial matters.
By the time they reach their end, they become impossible to counter.
*The Changes* say, "In anticipating calamities, take preventive measures," because their end is difficult to counter.
The Sage’s wisdom penetrates to the divine, and thus acts early—knowing this alone. This aligns with the Master’s saying, "Planning ahead in advance is called foresight."

Thus, the Sage never seeks the great directly, and so achieves the utmost greatness.
Huizong’s annotation: "Acting in the small, thus achieving the great."
The *Cushui* explains:
When the earth is but a clod, it becomes a field;
When water is but a ladleful, it becomes a river.
Thus, acting in the small allows one to achieve the great—this is the natural order.
The Sage deliberates in undertaking affairs, ensuring each success, thus bringing about great governance—also by acting in the small.

*The Odes* praise the governance of Wen and Wu, accumulating the *Xiao Ya* to form the *Da Ya*—its beginning was small, its accomplishment great, thus achieving internal and external governance, beginning with diligent concern.

Thus, if one waits until chaos has already taken form before seeking to govern it, is this not too late?

Mencius cited *The Odes: Owl* (《鸱鸮》) to say:
*"Before the heavens darken with rain, mend the thatched doors and windows of the mulberry grove.*
*This is to make known the state’s policies and laws when there is still leisure—*
*seeking to govern before the crisis arises.*
*How could it compare to waiting for the great cold before seeking warm garments?"*

Thus, the Sage takes this poem as the *Dao/Way* of foresight.

Therefore, the affairs of the world—what seems small can be done, and what seems great cannot be undone once neglected.

He who makes light of promises will lack trustworthiness; he who undertakes many things lightly will face many difficulties. Hence, the Sage proceeds with caution, and thus ultimately faces no difficulties.

Huizong’s annotation:
*"Calamity is often hidden in the subtle, erupting when people overlook it.*
*The Sage’s response to the world is always to be vigilant in the subtle and never negligent,*
*so there is never any reckless action,*
*and thus no insurmountable calamity arises.*
*This is all in accordance with the principle of *wu* (無)—non-doing, non-action."*

The *Cushui* explains:
*"Fire arises from wood; if the disaster is inevitable, then calamity is indeed hidden in the subtle.*
*If one treats it lightly, it is unwise, and thus calamity erupts when people overlook it.*
*Mencius said that blessings and misfortunes are all self-sought—*
*this is to say, do not neglect the subtle.*

*The Sage’s response to the world is to share in the people’s afflictions.*
*His wisdom suffices to comprehend the principles of things,*
*his foresight suffices to perceive omens early,*
*and he deliberates before signs appear,*
*always cautious in the subtle, considering beginnings with the same care as endings,*
*and never overlooking what others neglect.*
*Thus, there is never any reckless action,*
*and thus no insurmountable calamity arises.*

*The Records* say:
*'Better to have one’s own grudges than to break a promise without cause.'*
*Without reckless promises, words will align with actions.*

*The Commentaries* say:
*'If one deems it easy, difficulty will soon follow.'*
*Without undertaking many things lightly, every action will lead to auspicious outcomes.*

*Thus, navigating the world with this approach—*
*adapting flexibly and appropriately—*
*one will ultimately face no difficulties.*

*Yet this is not because one delights in omniscience,*
*but because one embodies the *wu* of *wuwei*—*
*non-action, non-taste,*
*the *Dao/Way* of nothingness.*

*Where the *Dao/Way* resides, who can pose difficulty?*
*The final stanza of *The Teal and Heron* speaks of no future calamity—*
*this aligns with the same meaning.*

*Chapter Sixty-Four of *The Ease of Holding Firm*:*
*'What is firm is easy to hold,*
*what is yet unmanifest is easy to plan,*
*what is fragile is easy to dissolve,*
*what is subtle is easy to disperse.*
*Act before it exists,*
*govern before it is disordered.*

Huizong’s annotation:
*'Firmness is the opposite of danger,*
*the unmanifest is the opposite of what is already formed,*
*the fragile is the opposite of hardness,*
*the subtle is the opposite of what is manifest.*
*To hold firmness is to avoid danger,*
*to plan before the unmanifest is to prevent its formation.*

*The Sage’s foresight—*
*to dissolve fragility before it hardens,*
*to disperse the subtle before it takes hold—*
*this is the near-miss of the worthy.*

*Chariots racing on steep paths,*
*boats navigating treacherous currents—*
*the Sage has no need for such cleverness.*
*Cleverness is applied before the race or the sinking,*
*this is what is meant by*
*'acting before it exists,*
*governing before it is disordered.'*

The *Cushui* explains:
*'Matters hidden in non-existence all follow natural principles;*
*once they manifest, they all follow inevitable mechanisms.*
*The principles are deeply hidden—*
*only the most divine can penetrate them;*
*the mechanisms are subtly triggered—*
*only the most wise can foresee them.*

*'Firmness is the opposite of danger—*
*where there is firmness, there must be danger.*
*The unmanifest is the opposite of what is formed—*
*if unmanifest, its form will vanish,*
*this is the principle of non-existence.*
*The fragile is the opposite of hardness—*
*hardness has already taken its substance.*
*The subtle is the opposite of what is manifest—*
*manifestation has already revealed its beginning,*
*this is the reality of existence.*

*'To hold firmness is to avoid danger,*
*thus one can preserve the state.*
*To plan before the unmanifest is to prevent calamity.*

*The Sage’s foresight—*
*is it not divine?*
*Knowing the subtle and the manifest,*
*acting clearly in the fleeting and obscure,*
*divine insight into the future.*

*To dissolve fragility before it hardens—*
*this is why one must be cautious at the first frost.*
*To disperse the subtle before it takes hold—*
*this is why one must examine the minutest details.*

*The worthy’s near-miss—*
*their wisdom, though not divine,*
*is close to perfection when reflecting,*
*and does not stray when erring,*
*discerning early what the wise later grasp.*

*Indeed, omens are the subtle movements,*
*the precursors of auspiciousness.*
*Seeing the second line of *Yù* (《豫》):*
*'In accordance with principle, one awakens—*
*this is the Sage’s foresight.'*

*The first line of *Fù* (《复》):*
*'Form takes shape, then one awakens—*
*this is the worthy’s near-miss.'*

*Only because they know omens*
*do they not ultimately say, 'This is auspicious.'*
*Only because they nearly miss*
*do they have no regret,*
*and thus how could there be calamities of racing chariots or sinking boats?*

*Indeed, chariots are for ascending steep paths,*
*boats are for crossing difficulties—*
*racing chariots, sinking boats,*
*the Sage has no need for such cleverness.*

*Yang Xiong, in response to a question,*
*spoke of applying cleverness before the race or sinking.*

*Thus, to act before it exists,*
*to govern before it is disordered—*
*this brings ultimate safety,*
*surpassing even the most perilous paths or the need for oars.*

*A tree of embrace grows from the minutest sprout;*
*a nine-tiered tower rises from a pile of earth;*
*a thousand-*li journey begins beneath one’s feet.*

Huizong’s annotation:
*'All formed things—*
*the great must grow from the small,*
*the high must be founded on the low,*
*the distant must begin near.*
*What is simple in its beginning*
*will inevitably become vast in its completion.*

*The Sage, seeing the beginning, contemplates the end,*
*observing the finger, knows the path.*
*Thus, he does not become the first to invite blessings,*
*nor the first to invite calamity.*
*He deliberates before undertaking affairs,*
*ensuring each success.'*

The *Cushui* explains:
*'What is formed arises from what is formless;*
*all that is constrained by form*
*must progress from the subtle to the manifest—*
*thus, the great must grow from the small.*

*To seek the axe’s handle,*
*one must begin with the uncut millimeter;*
*that a tree of embrace grows from the minutest sprout is evident."*

If ascending must begin from below,
then the high must indeed be founded on the low.
Lofty mountains rise from the unceasing accumulation of soil—
a nine-tiered terrace begins with the same.
From near to far, then the distant must begin near.
To reach a thousand *li*, one accumulates at every step without pause—
a thousand-*li* journey begins beneath one’s feet.

These three truths:
Their beginnings are simple,
their origins small,
yet their completion is vast,
their end great—
such is their nature.

All things have a root and branch,
all affairs have an end and a beginning.
The Sage, seeing the beginning, contemplates the end,
thus seeking its ultimate fulfillment;
observing the finger, he knows its path,
thus grasping its destined course.

Contemplating near and far,
in silence aligning with the Way,
he does not become the first to invite blessings—
for blessings do not then come—
nor the first to invite calamity—
for calamities do not then arrive.
He acts in accordance with the time,
moves in harmony with the Way,
deliberates before undertaking affairs,
and ensures each success—
responding only to what is felt.

When the affairs of the world press upon him,
and he might decline them without action,
even the most trivial matters contain what must be done—
yet to do them when they are easy?
He must first deliberate before acting,
lest he act only when it is no longer easy.

Only by not acting when it is easy
do the affairs he undertakes
inevitably achieve completion—
truly, his successes are grand!

To act is to court failure;
to grasp is to court loss.
Thus the Sage does nothing,
and so knows no failure;
he does not grasp,
and so knows no loss.

*The Cushui* explains:
The Sage does not engage in affairs,
and so knows no failure.
He does not cling to fixed ways,
and so knows no loss.
The ignorant, however, set measures and cling to them,
ever drifting farther from the Way—
how could they avoid failure and loss?

The principles of the world
may be followed, not forced;
trusted, not seized.
To act seeking completion
is to ensure failure;
to grasp seeking possession
is to ensure loss.

The Sage embodies the Way’s emptiness,
remaining still and doing nothing—
not engaging in affairs,
thus bearing no responsibility for action,
and so knows no failure.
He adapts without clinging,
not holding fast to old ways—
thus bearing no burden of past actions,
and so knows no loss.

Yet the ignorant do not know
that time never stops,
that circumstances never remain fixed—
they set measures and cling to them,
blinded by one perspective,
unaware of the whole.
How different is this from carving a boat to seek a sword,
or gluing a pillar to tune a zither?
No wonder they drift ever farther from the Way,
unable to embrace change—
how few escape failure and loss!

Thus the common folk,
when nearly complete in their endeavors,
often fail.
*The Cushui* explains:
To halt midway is to never reach the peak;
to abandon halfway is to never find the path.
Though they begin with diligence,
they end in slackness—
this is the nature of the common heart,
and so their affairs often fail just as they near completion.

*The Commentary* says:
*"He who can reach the hill with his feet*
*must follow the path to its end—*
*to halt midway is to reach nothing.*
*Though the road be winding, it leads to all under Heaven—*
*to abandon it halfway is to find no destination."*

Confucius spoke of *"what I do not do for myself,"*
meaning this very truth—
not only for learning,
but for all undertakings.
If their resolve falters in what they guard,
if their strength wears in what they pursue,
beginning with diligence but ending in slackness,
how could their affairs succeed?

At first, they take joy in fleeting moments,
yet soon grow weary of endurance—
the common heart is easily swayed by things.
To begin with vigor and end in neglect
is the way of all the common folk.
Only by stopping not to advance,
abandoning halfway to draw their own limits,
do their affairs often fail just as they near completion.

Mencius sighed for those who act,
comparing them to one who digs a well nine *ren* deep
yet never reaches the spring.
To treat the end as carefully as the beginning
is to know no failure in affairs.

*The Cushui* explains:
*"None begin without end,*
*few complete what they start.*
*To make the end like the beginning,*
*time itself renews—*
*what affair, thus treated, would not succeed?"*

*The Commentary* says:
King Cheng admonished his ministers,
saying, *"Let your ambition be as vast as your resolve,*
*your deeds as broad as your actions."*
This means:
Within, exhaust your heart—this is resolve;
without, exhaust your strength—this is diligence.
*"None begin without end,*
*few complete what they start,"*
for when the heart grows slack
and strength grows weary,
how could one not tire before completion?

Yi Yin said:
*"To make the end like the beginning,*
*time itself renews—*
*thus, with unceasing sincerity,*
*one attends to its ultimate fulfillment.*
*To let time renew itself,*
*one acts without weariness,*
*even in rest, never resting."*

Applying this to affairs,
they must be brought to order before ceasing—
what undertaking, thus treated, would not succeed?
This is what is meant by:
*"To treat the end as carefully as the beginning*
*is to know no failure in affairs."*

*The Classic* says:
*"Only the Sage has both beginning and end."*
Thus the Sage desires not to desire,
values not the hard-to-obtain treasures;
learns not to learn,
restoring what others have overstepped.

*The Cushui* explains:
Those who desire profit
exchange themselves for things,
drowning in worldly pursuits.
Those who pursue learning
drown in endless knowledge,
losing its true path.
How could this lead to *wuwei*?

The Sage does not burden himself with profit,
desiring only not to desire—
nurturing self and others equally,
stopping only when fully nourished,
thus not valuing hard-to-obtain treasures,
not letting others extinguish the Way.

Learning lies in not learning,
merely weaving and brightening the light—
thus restoring what others have overstepped.
When the Way is obscured,
even the worthy err—
how much more the common folk?
To restore their errors and return to nature
is how the absolute learner knows no worry,
only joy.

*The Commentary* says:
Zhuangzi spoke of those who indulge in wealth and honor,
exchanging themselves for things,
thus lost in worldly song.
Liezi warned that scholars perish through endless pursuits,
drowning in knowledge without foundation.

「Is it not sufficient to attain *wuwei*?

The so-called *wuwei* holds nothing dearer than *xu* [emptiness], nothing more skillful than *jing* [stillness], and nothing else.

To exchange oneself for things is to lose oneself in them—
already galloping with the restless, one does not know
that stillness lies in motion itself.

To drown oneself in knowledge is to make the heart
branched and doubtful—
already obstructed by the concrete, one does not know
that diminishing the concrete brings *xu* [emptiness].

Thus, it is not sufficient to attain *wuwei*.

The Sage does not act so.

He does not burden his form with profit,
for his seeking lies within—
what he desires is internal, not external,
and desire arises from *not* desiring.

To share profit brings delight,
to share sustenance brings ease—
not clinging to the gains of one age as private possession,
but ensuring all under heaven are nourished,
which aligns with Zhuangzi’s words:
*"nurturing self and others equally,
stopping only when fully nourished."*

Thus, he does not value hard-to-obtain treasures,
nor let others extinguish the Way—
he simply abandons human contrivance.

What he learns lies in the heart,
not in external pursuit,
and learning is in *not* learning.

Following nature’s inherent course,
practicing until mastery,
he does not augment true virtue with fragmented schemes,
but aims for morning clarity to behold the singular—
this is what the *Shijing* [Book of Songs] calls
*"learning lies in weaving and brightening the light"*—
thus restoring what others have overstepped.

For those who exhaust skill and seek rare treasures,
these are the hard-to-obtain things.
The Sage does not value them,
for he wishes the people not to stray after things—
thus can it be said:
*"I have no desires, yet the people find sufficiency."*

To abandon the root and chase the superficial
is the error of the common folk.
The Sage restores their errors,
not to punish them but to return them to the Way—
thus can it be said:
*"He is always good at saving people,
yet never abandons them."*

For the Way, when obscured,
even the worthy err—
the wise among the worthy still have their *guo* [oversteps],
how much more the common folk?

To restore their errors and return them to nature
is how the heart is cultivated and virtue is restored,
virtue reaches perfection and aligns with its original state,
approaching the vision of the Dao and transcending learning—
abiding in nature’s inherent course,
without fixed purpose yet ever joyful,
this is how the absolute learner knows no worry.

If the son of Yan [Preyan] discards *renyi* [benevolence and righteousness], *gongyue* [rites and music],
yet finds joy in his *danpiao* [coarse meal and gourd], *zhuoru* [wild plants and coarse rice],
is this not akin to the Sage’s joy in forgetting worries?

To aid the natural unfolding of all things,
yet not dare to act.

*The Commentary* of Xuanzong [Huang] says:
*"Heaven is high, earth is low,
all things are dispersed and distinct—
how could one force them?
Their growth and change are nature’s own.
To aid their natural unfolding,
is how their nature is perfected.
To act is to ruin them—
thus the Sage does not dare to act.
This is how the Sage relies on Dao/Way’s transformation,
not on clever schemes."*

*The Commentary* says:
*"Heaven moves above,
not creating yet transforming,
earth rests below,
not growing yet nurturing—
all things flourish between heaven and earth,
like motion or stillness,
sprouting in varied forms—
this is what is meant by
*‘Heaven is high, earth is low,
all things are dispersed and distinct.’*

The Sage praises heaven and earth’s transformation and nurturing,
and all things that follow their Way—
how could one force them?
Their birth, growth, and change in form and color,
their intelligence and capabilities waxing or waning—
these are nature’s own.
To aid their natural unfolding,
is not to promote their growth,
nor to urge their completion,
but to let them be as they are,
entrusting them to their own course—
thus their nature is perfected.

Yet heaven acts without action and remains clear,
earth acts without action and remains tranquil,
all things follow their roles—
each thrives from *wuwei* [non-action],
so what aids their nature
is not by human force or cleverness?

To act is to ruin them,
and even worse, to invite the loss of *zhong* [augmentation].
Thus the Sage does not dare to act.
This is how the Sage relies on Dao/Way’s transformation,
not on clever schemes.

Relying on Dao/Way’s transformation,
he follows things’ natural course without private favor—
not clinging to cleverness,
he discards knowledge and habit,
conforming to heaven’s principles—
thus, without action, all things transform.

As for those who carve *zhu* [bamboo or wood],
three years to make one leaf—
then few are the things with leaves,
how can they speak of Dao/Way’s transformation?

**Chapter 65: Ancient Masters of the Dao**
The ancient masters who excelled in the Dao
did not seek to enlighten the people—
they sought only to bewilder them.

*The Commentary* of Xuanzong [Huang] says:
*"The people can be led by the Way,
but cannot be made to know it—
the ancient masters of the Dao
led them by the Way alone.
To reverse their natural state,
the Way can be carried and followed together—
no need for clever schemes,
thus it is called *bewilderment* [愚, *yu*].

Since the Three Dynasties [Xia, Shang, Zhou],
those who expound *wuwei* [non-action] and *tianran* [natural ease]
do not know how to handle *wuwei*’s affairs,
those who delight in *hentian* [artificial harmony]
do not know how to practice *unspoken teaching* [不言之教],
yet they claim to enlighten the people,
calling it *governance* [治],
but which chaos is greater than this?"*

*The Commentary* says:
