*"The Sage’s Way is present in the world,
its benevolence is evident in its adaptability and completion—
thus the people can be led by it.
Its skill and depth are hidden in its application—
thus it cannot be made known.
The *Yijing* [Book of Changes] says:
*‘The common folk say “it works,” yet do not know why.’*
Mencius says:
*‘Many follow it all their lives,
yet do not know its Way.’*

The ancient masters of the Dao
each attained this principle,
yet they said:
*‘I bewilder the people’s hearts—
simple and pure,
the vulgar are clear and bright,
I alone seem muddled.’*
Pushing this to transform the people,
none would fail to follow it.

Obtained through observation and feeling,
reversing their natural state,
and restoring the heart’s original nature—
this is how they bewildered them.

Bewilderment leads to the Way,
the Way can be carried and followed together—
thus there is no need for clever schemes.

For the world has its natural course,
crooked and straight need no plumb line,
round and square need no compass—
adhesion does not require glue,
binding does not require ropes—
forgetting the Way’s artifice,
discarding the heart of cleverness,
this is how one does not lose its natural state.

Unless one is truly skilled in the Dao,
how can one reach this?

Since the Three Dynasties,
those who expound *wuwei* and *tianran*
do not know how to handle *wuwei*’s affairs,
those who delight in *hentian*
do not know how to practice *unspoken teaching*—
yet they claim to enlighten the people,
calling it *governance*,
but which chaos is greater than this?"*

Bending and curving the rites and music,
to rectify the world’s form yet harm it—
this is to await the plumb line and compass before achieving rectitude.

Ah, extolling benevolence and righteousness,
to soothe the world’s heart yet err in pedantry—
this is to rely on cords and glue before achieving firmness.

If such is the case, then the natural course is lost.
To claim enlightenment of the people,
calling it governance—
what greater chaos is there than this?

Zhuangzi said:
*"I have heard of nurturing the world,
but never of governing it."*

For to enlighten and govern the people
is not the way to govern the world.
Only by nurturing them does one see
the people daily incline toward the realm of peace and ease,
living in settled stillness—
why then speak of governing the world?
With such a heart of nurturing,
how could one say there is governance of the world?

The difficulty in governing lies in their excess of cleverness.
Emperor Xuanzong annotated:
*"When the world is often in great disorder,
the fault lies in their love of knowledge."*

The *Cushui* commentary explains:
*"If one’s knowledge of bows, crossbows, and arrows is excessive,
then winged and soaring creatures cannot ascend high.
If one’s knowledge of nets and traps is excessive,
then scaled and swimming creatures cannot dive deep.
If one’s knowledge of axes, saws, and tools is excessive,
then those who walk with calloused feet cannot tread in the abyss.
If this is true of things, how much more so for people?
Thus, the more one knows of deceitful schemes,
the more the common folk are bewildered by them."*

Zhuang Zhou, by examining the principles of things to verify human affairs, understands that all things arise from the **Dao/Way**, yet cannot be disturbed by cleverness. Thus, he says that when the world often falls into great disorder, the fault lies in their love of knowledge.

The **sage’s governance** always keeps the people without knowledge or desire, allowing the **Dao/Way’s** empty tranquility to emerge as the natural response for all under heaven—what need is there for cleverness to suffice?

Therefore, those who speak well of governance say:
In discussing the foundation of **Great Peace (太平)**, they declare that **schemes and stratagems are not employed (智谋不用)**.
In speaking of the marvelous transformation of the **Dao/Way (道化)**, they say that it does not rely on **cleverness and skill (不恃智巧)**.
Is this not to say that the essentials of governance lie in **knowing the Dao/Way (知道)** rather than in loving cleverness?

Hence, **governing with cleverness** is the ruin of the state.
Emperor Xuanzong annotated:
*"When laws are promulgated, deceit flourishes; when decrees are issued, fraud arises."*

The **Cushui commentary** explains:
Zhuangzi says: **"The wise are instruments of contention (争之器)."**
If cleverness arises from contention, then the people will pursue profit and become **artful in schemes (机巧)**.
What is meant by **"laws are promulgated"** is that without laws, there is nothing adequate to bind them.
If severe prohibitions are established by law, yet there are still those who **defy and transgress (抵冒)**, then the hearts of deceit and malice cannot be unified.
What is meant by **"decrees are issued"** is that without decrees, there is nothing adequate to command them.
When decrees are issued, only obedience follows—yet if there are still those who **pretend compliance (面革)**, then the emotions of deceit and falsehood cannot be stopped.
The **Classics** say: *"The more laws and decrees are multiplied, the more thieves and robbers will appear."*
Thus, deceit and fraud are evident—**governing with cleverness** is the ruin of the state.
**Not governing with cleverness** is the blessing of the state.
Emperor Xuanzong annotated:
*"Burn the symbols and shatter the seals, and the people will be simple and unsophisticated.
Abandon the measures and break the balances, and the people will not contend."*

The **Cushui commentary** explains:
Zhuangzi says: **"The Dao/Way is the public standard (为之公)."**
If the **Dao/Way** is made the public standard, then the people will naturally **trust without constraint (不约而自孚)**.
What is meant by **"burn the symbols and shatter the seals"** is not that they are literally burned and broken—rather, by **establishing trust (信信)**, the people become simple and unsophisticated, and symbols and seals are no longer relied upon.
What is meant by **"abandon the measures and break the balances"** is not that they are literally discarded and snapped—rather, by **acting with fairness (平平)**, the people do not contend, and measures and balances are of no use.
The **Classics** say: *"If I love tranquility, the people will naturally be upright.
If I have no desires, the people will naturally be content."*
When the people are upright and content, their simplicity and lack of contention are clear—**not governing with cleverness** is the blessing of the state.
Understanding these two principles is the **model (楷式)**.

Emperor Xuanzong annotated:
*"Understanding these two principles means knowing how to govern the state.
Knowing how to govern the state, the people will follow and emulate them as the model."*

The **Cushui commentary** explains:
*"The form is correct, then its reflection is correct;
The source is clear, then the stream is clear—
this is the natural correspondence (自然之符)."*
The **sage**, standing above the people and things of the world,
**upright in form and clear in source (端表澄源)**,
governs without action while the world transforms—
it is only a matter of **employing cleverness or not employing cleverness (用智与不用智)****.
Understanding these two principles means knowing that the responsibility of governance lies in **clarity and tranquility (清静)**, with no need for cleverness.

If one does not govern with cleverness,
then those who listen to the **sage’s voice (听唱)** and observe his **rites and conduct (视仪)**,
will derive their **model (效法)** from the **Dao/Way’s** manifestations and responses—
who would not follow and emulate them as the model?

**Model (则)** is like the **standard for making a standard (作则之则)**—
because it has a standard, the people follow the standard.
**Emulation (象)** is like the **pendant for hanging a pendant (垂象之象)**—
because it has an emulation, the people emulate it.
Only because there is a model and emulation,
the people follow and emulate them as the model.

If, for instance, Master Guanzhi posed the question:
*"Why does water round the basin, yet the basin itself is round?
Why does water square the vessel, yet the vessel itself is square?"*
His intent aligns with this.

**Constantly knowing the model (常智楷式)** is called **Mysterious Virtue (玄德)**.
**Mysterious Virtue** is profound, vast, and contrary to things.
Emperor Xuanzong annotated:
*"Mysterious (玄) is the color of Heaven.
If one constantly knows the model without using cleverness,
then one unites with Heaven’s virtue—
profound beyond measure,
vast beyond limit,
standing alone above all things,
with nothing to pair with."*
Thus, it is said: **"contrary to things (与物反)."**

The **Cushui commentary** explains:
*"The subtle and minute is called Mysterious (玄).
Mysterious is the color of Heaven.
If one constantly knows the model without using cleverness,
then one takes **Embracing Unity (抱一)** as the model for all under Heaven.
If so, then abandoning cleverness and fixed habits,
following Heaven’s principles,
one should naturally unite with Heaven’s virtue—
silent and odorless,
profound beyond measure,
boundless and undivided,
vast beyond limit,
standing alone above all things,
with nothing to pair with—
this is what Master Liezi called **the Doubtful One (疑独)**,
hence it is said: **‘contrary to things.’**
Unless one **enters the Dao/Way (入)** and **discerns things (辨物)**,
who can unite with Heaven’s virtue?
Then one reaches **Great Harmony (大顺)."**

Emperor Xuanzong annotated:
*"Harmony (顺) is Heaven’s principle.
To reach **Great Harmony** means
abandoning cleverness and fixed habits,
following Heaven’s principle alone."*
Zhuangzi says:
*"To unite with Heaven and Earth,
its union is **profound and vast (缗缗)**,
as if **stupid and befuddled (若愚若昏)**—
this is called **Mysterious Virtue (玄德)**,
in accord with **Great Harmony (大顺)."**
Only by being **as if stupid and befuddled (若愚若昏)**,
can one abandon cleverness.

The **Cushui commentary** explains:
In the *Yijing*’s **"Yù (豫)"** it says:
*"Heaven and Earth move with Harmony (顺动),
so the sun and moon do not pass their bounds,
and the four seasons do not err."*
This is **Harmony as Heaven’s principle**—
from its **nature (体)**, **Qián (乾)** is the most robust,
yet from its **principle (理)**, **Qián (乾)** is easily known—
hence it is said: *"The simple principles of all under Heaven are easily attained (易简天下之理得),"
and this is why **Harmony** is Heaven’s principle.*

To reach **Great Harmony** means
abandoning the cleverness that makes one **artificial (使然)**,
and aligning with the **natural principles (自然之至理)**—
by **not knowing and not recognizing (不识不知)**,
following the **sage’s model (帝之则)** alone.
This is what is meant by:
*"Abandoning cleverness and fixed habits,
following Heaven’s principle."*

Zhuang Zhou wrote in the *"Heaven and Earth (天地)"* chapter,
discussing **nature (性)**, **cultivation (修)**, and **inverted virtue (反德)**—
when **virtue (德)** reaches its peak,
it is in accord with the **initial state (初有)**:
*"To unite with Heaven and Earth,
its union is **profound and vast (缗缗)**,
as if **stupid and befuddled (若愚若昏)**—
this is called **Mysterious Virtue (玄德)**,
in accord with **Great Harmony (大顺)."**
Between Heaven and Earth,
**there is emptiness yet no bending (虚而不屈)**,
**movement yet only expansion (动而愈出)**—
when united, it **communicates with Heaven and Earth (通於天地)**,
and when in accord, it is **only emptiness (同乃虚)****.

**Profound and vast (缗缗)** unite naturally—
not like firewood stacked and burning,
nor from any **known perception or discernment (所知见)**.
This is their union.

「When **stupid and befuddled (若愚)**, the mind becomes **profoundly silent (冥心)** and without knowledge,
when **befuddled (若昏)**, the eyes are **stuck and unmoving (胶目)** and without sight—
without knowledge and without sight,
this is called **Mysterious Virtue (玄德)**.

When **virtue (德)** reaches **Mysterious Virtue (玄)**,
then **nature (性)** and Heaven accord naturally—
without striving or meeting others,
and thus in accord with **Great Harmony (大顺)**.

Only because they are **stupid and befuddled (若愚若昏)**,
can they **abandon discernment (去智)**;
Only because they **abandon discernment (去智)**,
can they **return to virtue (原於德)** and **fulfill the Heavenly (成於天)**.

Zhuangzi, in the opening of the *"Heaven and Earth (天地)"* chapter,
speaks only of **Heavenly Virtue (天德)**,
and its meaning aligns with this.

**Chapter 66: "The King of All Valleys (江海为百谷王)"**
The reason **Jiānghǎi (江海)** can be the **King of All Valleys (百谷王)** is that it **excels in descending (善下)**,
thus able to be the **King of All Valleys (百谷王)**.

The **Huizong (徽宗)** annotation states:
*"When one undertakes deeds and establishes achievements,
and compares them to the highest (一上比者),
that is the **King (王)**."*

The **King (王)** carries the meaning of **returning and drawing (归往)**—
if a ruler **descends to those below (下下)**,
then the people **return to him (归之)**,
as water flows downward.

The **Cushioned Explanation (疏义)** states:
*"The Sage speaks of **virtue (德)**,
the **King (王)** speaks of **achievements (业)**—
these are the deeds and achievements
that make one a **King (王)**.
The **King (王)** is the **Lord (公)**,
and the **Lord (公)** is Heaven—
this is the comparison to the highest (一上比),
so one becomes a **King (王)**.
The **King (王)** carries the meaning of **returning and drawing (归往)**—
this is the **King of the people (下民之王)**.
The reason the people **turn toward and attach themselves (向往而亲附)** is that he **receives them humbly (容而下之)**,
possessing the **Way of descending to those below (下下之道)**.
Thus, those nearby **sing and rejoice (讴歌而乐)**,
and those far away **exert themselves to approach (竭蹶而趋)**,
like water flowing downward,
abundantly and irresistibly (沛然莫之能御).
The **Scripture (经)** says:
*"The Dao in the world is like the rivers and valleys to Jianghai.
The people’s **returning and drawing (归往)**—
is it not similar?"*
Yet, the Sage’s sequence states *"the ruler descends to those below (君能下下)"*,
and the *"Book of Poetry (诗)"* says *"there is none who does not comply (无不尔哉)"*—
does this not follow the **Way of knowing how to descend (知善下之道)"**?

Thus, the **Sage (圣人)** desires to **elevate others (上人)**,
yet speaks of **descending (言下之)**;
desires to **go before others (先人)**,
yet **follows in body (以其身后之)**.
Thus, the **Sage (圣人)**,
though **in the highest position (处上)**,
does not make others **heavy (人不重)**,
though **in the forefront (处前)**,
does not make others **harmed (人不害)**—
thus the world **rejoices to push him forward (乐推)** and does not **weary (厌)**.

The **Huizong (徽宗)** annotation states:
*"The *Yijing (易)* in the *Tún (屯)* hexagram’s first line says:
*'With the noble descending to the base, great is the people’s compliance (以贵下贱，大得民也).'*
He who **gains the people (得其民者)**,
**gains their hearts (得其心也)**.
When **in the highest position (处上)** but others do not **heavy (不重)**,
then they **follow him lightly (从之也轻)**.
When **in the forefront (处前)** but others do not **harmed (不害)**,
then many **benefit from him (利之者众)**.
If such, there is no thought that does not **comply (不服)**,
so there is no **wearying (不厌)**.
The *Yijing (易)* says:
*'The people comply with his ability (百姓与能).'*

The **Cushioned Explanation (疏义)** states:
*"The noble **takes the base as its foundation (以贱为本)**,
so the *Yijing (易)* in the *Tún (屯)* hexagram says:
*'With the noble descending to the base, great is the people’s compliance (以贵下贱，大得民也).'*
**Cloud and thunder gather (云雷并作)**,
forming the *Tún (屯)* hexagram—
this is the time when Heaven **created the ten thousand things (天造)** in **primordial chaos (草昧)**.
The **first line (初九)** represents the **governing ruler (经纶之君)**,
who can **cultivate himself through humility (能以谦自牧)**,
forgetting his **nobility (忘其贵)** and descending to those below,
thus becoming what the people **seek (民之所求)**—
so he **greatly gains the people (能大得民)**.
He who **gains the people (得其民者)**,
**gains their hearts (得其心也)**.
He who **gains their hearts (得其心者)**,
is this not beyond what **teachings and exhortations (教诰)** can command?
Because he **speaks of descending (以其言下之)**,
when **in the highest position (处上)** others do not **heavy (不重)**—
they all have **a heart to revere and wear (愿戴之心)**,
and thus **follow him lightly (从之也轻)**,
as Mencius says: *'Those who follow him are like returning to the market (从之者如归市).'*
Because he **descends in body (以其身下之)**,
when **in the forefront (处前)** others do not **harmed (不害)**—
they all have **a sincere desire to attach themselves (乐附之诚)**,
and thus many **benefit from him (利之者众)**,
as Zhuangzi says: *'Many benefit from benevolence and righteousness (利仁义者众).'*
Thus, the people from all four directions
do not fail to **love him as their parents (亲之若父母)**,
**cherish him as orchids and lily (爱之若芝兰)**,
without a thought that does not **comply (无思不服)**,
gained through the heart,
and **rejoicing to push him forward without hesitation (悦乐推而不耿)**.
The *Yijing (易)* says:
*'The people comply with his ability (百姓与能).'*

Indeed, Heaven and Earth **establish their positions (设位)**,
and the **Sage fulfills their abilities (成能)**—
with the **Sage fulfilling their abilities (圣人之成能)**,
the people **comply with him (与之)**,
then **love and praise him (亲誉)** is most fitting,
and there is no time when they **do not grow weary (而斁)**.

Because he **does not contend (不争)**,
the world **cannot contend with him (莫能与之争)**.

The **Huizong (徽宗)** annotation states:
*"He **practices virtue (行贤)** yet **abandons his own virtuous conduct (去自贤之行)**—
how could one not **love him (不爱哉)**?"

The **Cushioned Explanation (疏义)** states:
*"If one **practices virtue toward others (以贤临人)**,
there has never been one who **gains others (得人者)**.
If one **practices virtue toward others from below (以贤下人)**,
there has never been one who **does not gain others (不得人者)**.
If **practicing virtue toward others from below (以贤下人)** can **gain others (得人)**,
how much more so if one **practices virtue (行贤)** yet **abandons his own virtuous conduct (无自贤之行)**?
It is naturally fitting for him to be **loved by others (为人之所爱)**,
and thus **Yangzi (阳子)** has these words.

Indeed, **practicing virtue yet abandoning his own virtuous conduct (行贤而无自贤之行)**,
then he can **dwell in the realm of non-contention (处乎不争之地)**.
**Going securely without being loved (安往而不爱)**,
then **things cannot contend with him (物不能与之争)**.

The world all says of **Chapter 67**:
*"The world all says my Dao is **great yet seems unorthodox (大似不肖)**—
only because it is **great (大)**,
does it seem **unorthodox (不肖)**.
If it were **orthodox (肖)** for long,
then it would be **petty (细)** indeed."*

The **Huizong (徽宗)** annotation states:
*"The **orthodox (肖)** clings to things—**small (小)**;
the **clung to by things (为物所肖)** is **great (大)**.
The Dao **envelops and nurtures all things (覆载万物)**—
how **vast (大)** it is!
Thus it seems **unorthodox (不肖)**.
If it were **orthodox (肖)**,
then the Dao would have things outside it—
how could it be **great (大)**?"*

The **Cushioned Explanation (疏义)** states:
*"The **orthodox (肖)** imitates others—**its form is small (其体小)**,
so the **imitator (肖物者)** is **small (小)**.
The **great (大)** is what others **follow (人之所因)**,
so the **followed by things (为物所肖者)** is **great (大)**.
Swiftly did the **seventy disciples (七十子)** imitate **Zhòngní (仲尼)**—
through the **seventy disciples (七十子)** observing **Zhòngní (仲尼)**,
then **small and great (小大)** can certainly be known.
Yet the **Sage’s ability to fulfill the great (圣人所以能成其大)** is because he **obtains the root of the Dao (得道之本宗)**.

The Dao **envelops and nurtures all things (覆载万物)**—
it **takes the Dao as the origin of all things (以道为万物祖)**,
so there are **ten thousand differences (万不同)**,
all arising from it.
Heaven and Earth, though **great (大)**,
and the **autumn hair (秋毫)**, though **small (小)**,
all do not **transcend the Dao’s enveloping and nurturing (不外於覆载之内)**.
*"How vast it is!" (洋洋乎大哉)*—
this speaks of its **all-encompassing embrace (无不该徧)**,
and **broad capacity (广乎能容)**.
Being **clung to by things yet not clinging to things (为物所肖而非肖物)**,
thus it seems **unorthodox (不肖)**.
If it were **orthodox (肖)**,
then the Dao would have things outside it—
how could it be called **great (大)**?
Outside the Dao,
what things are there?
Even if one **forcibly names it (强为之名)**,
it is still not **great (大)**.
If one **speaks of it as unbegun (未始有)** without **opposing terms (对言之)**,
then it is certainly **not to be debated (固不可以议)**.*

I hold three treasures, treasures to be cherished.
Huizong’s annotation: Different from the common world’s perception, yet guarded without loss, these are the treasures I hold.
Exegesis: The Sage differs from the virtuous; viewing the Sage through the eyes of the virtuous still reveals distinctions—how much more so with the vulgar’s perspective? Their divergence is profound indeed.
The so-called three treasures: In the vulgar world, they are obscured by private views, mistakenly deemed small. Yet when viewed through principle, they are what constitutes the great.
To differ from the vulgar’s perception and guard them without loss—then one embraces them well without abandoning them, never for a moment separated. This may truly be called cherishing and holding them.

The Confucian scholar does not treasure gold and jade but holds loyalty and trust as treasures, thus knowing that my three treasures lie within, not without, and must be held to their utmost.

Alas, the vulgar man treasures his treasures yet fails to treasure their essence, making them burdens upon himself. Hence, those who treasure pearls and jade invite calamity upon themselves.

**First: Benevolence.**
Huizong’s annotation: Benevolence loves all things; it is the substance of virtue.
Exegesis: Benevolence takes compassion for all as its heart; virtue takes love for humanity as its foundation. Thus, filial piety begins with a father’s benevolence, and feasting demonstrates benevolent kindness. Ultimately, all stem from love—how could benevolence not be the substance of virtue?
Laozi spoke of Dao and virtue, rejecting virtue yet treasuring this; he did not reject it but sought to clarify virtue’s essence.

**Second: Frugality.**
Huizong’s annotation: Frugality ensures sufficiency; it is the moderation of ritual.
Exegesis: Frugality is the common virtue; ritual values appropriate use. Thus, frugality without ritual leads to universal scarcity, while frugality with ritual is fitting. Ultimately, all honor sufficiency—how could frugality not be the moderation of ritual?
Confucius said, *"Rather be frugal than extravagant"*—he did not mean miserliness but sought to clarify ritual’s moderation.

**Third: Not daring to lead the world.**
Huizong’s annotation: To lead is to seek victory over others, to value strength over virtue.
Exegesis: There is a constant way of victory called softness, and a constant way of defeat called rigidity.
Softness does not seek victory over others; it manifests as yielding, weakness, and humility, yet none can contend with it.
Rigidity seeks victory over others; though it strives to excel, how can it always excel?
If one can be rigid yet not soft, leading to seek victory over others, then one values strength over virtue.
Strength is the beginning of contention; valuing strength means favoring bravery, and things will oppose it—like Zhuangzi’s *"using cleverness to contend with strength."*
Humility is the handle of virtue; valuing virtue means softness and compliance, and none can demean it—like the *Yijing*’s *"Heaven, Earth, humanity, and spirits all take from humility."*
Then, those who seek victory over others value strength over virtue—how can they compare to those who do not seek victory, valuing virtue over strength?
To not value strength is to know not to dare lead the world, to humble oneself and advance in virtue. Thus, restrained in bearing, it becomes the corner of virtue.

Benevolence, therefore, brings courage.
Huizong’s annotation: King Wen viewed the people as wounded; his anger settled the world’s people.
Exegesis: The virtuous must be courageous; thus, those who love others abhor harm to them.
The virtuous must have authority; thus, constant virtue suffices to establish martial affairs.
King Wen cherished the common people, benefiting widows and orphans. Mencius called his care for the people like tending to wounds—truly benevolent.
When his wrath flared, he disciplined his troops and achieved martial triumphs, conquering the exalted. His anger settled the world’s people—his courage was unmatched.
Thus, benevolence brings courage; this is evident.

Frugality, therefore, brings breadth.
Huizong’s annotation: Store in winter, and abundance will flourish in spring.
Even Heaven and Earth cannot always indulge in excess; how much more so for humanity?
Exegesis: The *Yijing* says: *"Kān is concealment and withdrawal."*
Kān corresponds to the north in direction and winter in season, where all things retreat—thus, concealment is spoken of in winter.
It also says: *"Zhèn is flourishing and abundance."*
Zhèn corresponds to the east in direction and spring in season, where all things emerge—thus, abundance is spoken of in spring.
Only by concealing in winter does abundance come in spring. The movement of qi and the principles of all things have their own times of waxing and waning, their own measures of fullness and contraction. Heaven and Earth provide for all without ever indulging, transform into all things without ever wasting—thus even they cannot always indulge in excess. It is because they do not indulge in excess that they achieve such breadth. Examined in human affairs, it is also that frugality brings breadth.

Not daring to lead the world, therefore, completes the vessel and makes it long-lasting.
Huizong’s annotation: The Dao does not contend yet triumphs—this is Heaven’s way. The Dao’s exaltation makes it the longest-lasting vessel.
Exegesis: Heaven is the parent of all things, transforming and nurturing, silently revolving in the boundless void. All things move and act through its mechanisms; those that emerge, it aids without hesitation; those that return to their roots, it guides without obstruction. It follows things’ natural course, aligning with their inherent principles.
Yet those confined by the numbers of birth and death all receive their mandates here, unable to escape its keys—thus, the Dao does not contend yet triumphs.
The Dao’s exaltation is first among all things, and none can demean it; hence, it is the longest-lasting vessel.

---

「Laozi states: *The sage employs the Way of the feminine*—when used, it becomes the foundation for leadership.
Huizong’s annotation: The sage comprehends the Dao’s boundless nature, dwelling in the world as though in a realm of non-contention, and none dare contend with him. The Great Dao already flows—how could it not be that which the world follows first, thus attaining the status of the longest-lasting vessel?

Now, abandoning the Way of the feminine yet clinging to the masculine, abandoning frugality yet seeking extravagance, abandoning humility yet aspiring to preeminence—this is the path of death.

Huizong’s annotation: The world’s people know that courage suffices to overcome others, yet do not know that compassion alone can foster true courage. They know that generosity can dazzle the multitude, yet do not know that restraint alone can sustain true abundance. They know that leadership is esteemed, yet do not know that yielding precedence is the essential key. Thus, they are but the followers of brute strength, and their way is one of death.

Exegesis: The Three Treasures are the true essence of nature, not contrivances of human artifice. Only when one forgets both self and object can they truly treasure and uphold them. The world’s people, however, chase after superficial truths while abandoning the genuine, blinded to ultimate principles. They oppose objects with their own ego, clashing at every turn, knowing only that courage suffices to overcome others, boasting in strength, yet unaware that compassion alone can forge invincibility—*as the scriptures say: "The seven virtues make one unstoppable."* They know that generosity can dazzle the multitude, indulging in ostentation, yet fail to grasp that restraint alone can ensure true abundance—*as the sages say: "Use it without exhausting it."* They know that leadership is esteemed, striving to excel in ability, yet do not understand that yielding precedence is the true path—*as the scriptures say: "He who yields precedence is first among men."* These are but the followers of rigidity and force; their way is one of death. Hence it is said: *The obstinate die the death of the strong.*

In ancient times, Zhuangzi spoke of the *Perfect Person* (博大真人) and said: *"He is always expansive toward things, never diminished by others—thus, compassion is known."* *"He moves with ease yet never wastes—taking moderation as his standard—thus, frugality is known."* *"While others take precedence, he alone takes last—thus, he does not dare to lead the world first."* If Laozi were such a man, he could truly be said to embody these teachings, not only manifesting them in words but also demonstrating them in deed. Yet the world’s people pick up these fragments and call them *rigidity and strength*, risking their own lives without reflection. Truly, they are the *blinded and deluded masses.*

Compassion, when wielded like a shield, becomes invincible. When held as a fortress, it becomes unshakable.

Huizong’s annotation: The virtuous man is unstoppable in the world—thus, compassion makes him invincible in battle. The people love their ruler as hands and feet defend the head, as children protect their parents and elder siblings—dying for him without hesitation. Thus, compassion makes him unshakable in defense.

Exegesis: Mencius said: *"When a ruler loves virtue, the world has no rival."* That is, the virtuous man is unstoppable in the world, for the people’s affection lies in virtue. The virtuous man, without rival, can thus awaken great benefit and align with the great harmony. The people’s return to virtue is like water flowing downward—inevitable. Thus, compassion makes him invincible in battle, achieving total victory. The *Book of Documents* says: *"The people have no constant grievance, only grievance when virtue is absent."* Xunzi said: *"The people love their ruler as hands and feet defend the head, as children protect their parents and elder siblings."* Therefore, when the people’s loyalty is rooted in virtue, compassion makes him unshakable in defense.

In ancient times, King Tang of Shang conquered through kindness and virtue, and thus his courageous deeds were celebrated, none daring to refuse his offerings. This was the virtuous man’s unstoppable way in the world. King Tai of Zhou had boundless virtue, so the people of Qin followed him as though returning to their marketplace. This was the people’s love for their ruler. Those above, with virtue and benevolence, are unstoppable; those below, with sincere and willing hearts, love their ruler. Thus, compassion makes him invincible in battle, unshakable in defense.

Compassion as a treasure—how could its value be slight? Heaven, when it would save and protect, does so with compassion.

Huizong’s annotation: Those who aspire to virtue are drawn by Heaven’s favor. Those who reject virtue are stripped by Heaven’s judgment. Thus, Heaven saves and protects through compassion alone—this is why compassion is first among the Three Treasures.

Exegesis: Those who exhaust their hearts know their nature. Those who know their nature know Heaven. Virtue is rooted in the inherent nature of all beings, a mandate from Heaven. Only by embodying virtue can one fulfill their nature; only by fulfilling their nature can one align with Heaven. Thus, those who aspire to virtue are drawn by Heaven’s favor—*as the scriptures say: "Nurture the good, bless the benevolent, bestow a hundred blessings upon those who do good."* Those who reject virtue are stripped by Heaven’s judgment—*as the scriptures say: "Overturn the wicked, punish the licentious, bring a hundred calamities upon those who do evil."* Therefore, those who follow the Dao are good, and those who lead with goodness are virtuous. Heaven’s Way is impartial, always rewarding the good. It saves them to make them secure, protects them to make them steadfast—all because of their goodness in compassion. This is why compassion is first among the Three Treasures, and also why virtue is the overarching name for all goodness, the consummation of humanity’s path. None can surpass the love of virtue.

*The Great Learning of the True Classic (道德真经疏义) concludes Volume XII, begins Volume XIII.*

The *Great Learning of the True Classic* (《道德真经疏义》), Volume XIII, Chapter LXVIII: **"The Virtuous Scholar Does Not Rely on Force"**

The virtuous scholar does not rely on force.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* Force is the lower path; the scholar’s virtue lies in cultivating *ren* and *yi* alone.
Confucius said: *"I have not studied military affairs."*

*The Causal Exegesis explains:* The maneuvers of the three armies and five weapons are but the lesser applications of virtue. What is lesser was practiced by the ancients, yet not their priority—thus, force is the lower path. The scholar’s aspiration lies in the Dao, so cultivating noble intent is paramount.
*The Records* say: *"The scholar’s first concern is intent."*
Zhuangzi said: *"The worthy scholar values intent."*
All speak of the scholar’s purpose being to uphold lofty and enlightened intent, not to labor over the lesser applications of virtue. What intent should be cherished? Simply *ren* and *yi*.

To dwell in *ren* is to find a secure abode, fostering a heart that cannot bear to harm others. Thus, to kill the innocent is not *ren*.
To follow *yi*’s straight path means taking only what is just. Thus, to take what is not yours is not *yi*.
The virtuous scholar does not rely on force, nor does he delight in killing to harm *ren*, nor in taking to harm *yi*.
Confucius’ teachings also say: *"I have not studied military affairs."*

Thus, if the scholar’s intent is in the Dao, how can he neglect the root—*ren* and *yi*—and act otherwise? This is why Zhongyou could be brave yet cowardly, earning reproach at the Sage’s gate.

The skilled warrior does not rage.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* The highest strategy is to subdue through planning, yet rage truly overcomes deliberation.
*The Causal Exegesis explains:* The威 of virtue is perfected in tranquility and strength. The Sage’s martial prowess shines without haste, yet its effects arrive without movement; divine martial might does not kill, its influence is unfathomable yet profound. By calculating strategy, victory is secured at ten thousand *li*—even the wisest cannot devise such schemes. Though military doctrine values the highest strategy of subduing through planning, it achieves victory without battle. Why then resort to clashing arms and indulge in rage?

The Five Phases’ principle holds that wood overcomes earth, and among the Seven Emotions, rage overcomes thought. What is meant by *"rage truly overcomes thought"* is that it stems not from true virtue, but is driven by external influences. Yet the highest strategy of subduing through planning requires no rage at all.

King Wen’s display of righteous fury was but human rage in response to human rage. To match the people’s rage with rage is to share their breath, differing only in that it is not private fury. What is called *"emitting rage without raging"* is rage arising from non-rage. Only by understanding this can one speak of the skilled warrior not raging.

The one who best conquers enemies does not contend.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* Contention is contrary to virtue. To fight for land fills the fields with the dead; to fight for cities fills the cities with the dead—victory and defeat remain undetermined.
*The Causal Exegesis explains:* Arms are laid down yet mobilized in due time. The virtuous one displays virtue, not force, aligning with the people’s hearts and following their desires—thus, contention is contrary to virtue. To contend is to crave battle and delight in slaughter: fighting for land fills the fields with the dead, yet external threats remain unchecked; fighting for cities fills the cities with the dead, yet internal strife persists. If so, cycles of expansion and contraction ensue, with victory and defeat still uncertain.

Only the virtuous scholar does not rely on force to perfect his martial virtue, thus embracing *ren* without fail and revering *yi* without hesitation—this is to campaign with *ren* and *yi*.
Only the skilled warrior does not rage to fulfill his rage, thus acting with purpose and achieving merit—this is to wield威 without raging.
Only the one who best conquers enemies does not contend to suppress contention, thus winning in battle and holding firm in defense—this is to conquer without contention.

By this, the realm is preserved, achievements secured, the people at ease, and the masses harmonized. The seven virtues of martial prowess lie here. These three principles all stem from virtue. Since they stem from virtue, their perfection is evident—thus, they are called *shàn* (virtuous).

This is the constant virtue underlying martial affairs—what makes *constant martial virtue* beautiful?

The skilled user of men is beneath them.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* Though wisdom encompasses heaven and earth, the Sage does not deliberate alone—thus, the wise are employed for counsel. Though ability exhausts the land, the Sage does not act alone—thus, the capable are employed for service. Though eloquence carves through all things, the Sage does not speak alone—thus, the eloquent are employed as messengers.

*The Causal Exegesis explains:* Those who embody the Dao act without action yet are honored; those who handle affairs act with action yet are burdened. The Sage must act without action to govern the world—this is embodying the Dao. The subjects must act with action to serve the world—this is handling affairs. Only by embodying the Dao does one assign responsibility to those who handle affairs. Only by acting without action does one employ those who act with action.

Thus, the Sage’s wisdom, though vast as heaven and earth, does not deliberate alone—this is to say wisdom permeates the divine, yet he employs others’ wisdom, so the wise are his counselors, as the saying goes: *"The utmost wisdom does not plot."*
Though ability exhausts the land, the Sage does not act alone—this is to say he employs others’ abilities, so the capable serve as his agents, as the saying goes: *"Great skill appears as clumsiness."*
Though eloquence carves through all things, the Sage does not speak alone—this is to say he employs others’ eloquence, so the eloquent serve as his messengers, as the saying goes: *"Great eloquence does not speak."*

Thus, by not self-using, others willingly serve him. This is called the virtue of non-contention.
*Huizong’s Annotation:* Virtue transcends reputation; knowledge arises from contention. Those whose talents are whole yet whose virtue is unseen have never been heard to initiate—only to harmonize with others.

The *Shuyi* says:

When nurturing harmony, one preserves internally without dissipation—why seek reputation?
The wise cultivate tranquility, and harmony arises naturally from one’s nature—why engage in contention?
If virtue transcends reputation, others will also vie for it through reputation.
If wisdom arises from contention, others will also contend with me in wisdom.
Only those whose talents are whole and whose virtue remains unseen—
who dwell in the harmony of virtue’s transformation of things,
seeing what is singular without perceiving what is lost—
then bear no burden of reputation or contention.

What is meant by *talents whole*?
Not disrupting harmony, yet bringing spring to all things.
What is meant by *virtue unseen*?
Not losing one’s nature, yet returning to the original virtue.

Standing alone in the realm of non-contention,
never leading but always following others,
as if riding the harmony of others without initiating—
this is surely one whose talents are whole and whose virtue remains unseen.
Thus, the Master said: *"Never heard to initiate—only to harmonize with others."*
If not the virtue of non-contention, what else could this be?
This is called *employing others’ strength*.

「Huizong’s commentary states: The *clever* exhaust their sight and hearing, the *capable* exhaust their planning and abilities, yet those who hold positions know nothing.

The *Explanations of Meaning* say: Those entrusted with the tasks of ears and eyes must surely possess *cleverness* to exhaust their sight and hearing. For those who discern through vision, clarity comes first; those who perceive through hearing, *cleverness* is prized. Only by letting the near reveal their clarity and the distant reveal their *cleverness* can one fully grasp the myriad circumstances of all things.

Those entrusted with the tasks of the heart and strength must surely possess *capable* wisdom to exhaust their planning and abilities. For those skilled in planning, their wisdom is unobstructed; those skilled in strength, their vigor is unflagging. Only by internally wielding their wisdom and externally diligently applying their strength can one fully achieve the results of their undertakings.

The Sage surveys widely and listens inclusively, entrusts the *clever* and employs the *capable*—this is merely an excess of affairs. What enables them to utilize the world without self-assertion is their embodiment of the Dao’s *non-action* (wuwei). The *Classic* says:

*"Cleverness and wisdom, guard them with simplicity;
Bravery and strength to pacify the world, guard them with caution."*

What is meant by *"those who hold positions know nothing"*? Does it not declare this truth? This is called *harmonizing with Heaven*—the ultimate of antiquity.

Huizong’s commentary states: *Non-action* (wuwei) for action is what is called *Heaven*. Without contention yet employing others, one can thus *harmonize with Heaven*. To *harmonize with Heaven* is to reach the utmost; nothing can be added beyond this, hence it is called the *ultimate of antiquity*. *Ultimate* means *utmost*. The *house ridgepole* is the *utmost* of wood.

The *Explanations of Meaning* say: Heaven acts through *non-action* (wuwei) to maintain its clarity, and all things flourish, each following their natural growth from *non-action*. Thus, *non-action* for action is what is called *Heaven*.

The Sage engages in *non-action* (wuwei), thus aligning with Heaven’s *virtue/power*. Without contention yet employing others, it is as though the boundless void nurtures life—creation and transformation occur subtly, and the Sage merely entrusts the Six Qi (六氣) to their own unfolding. Hence, one can *harmonize with Heaven*. Those who record rituals say:

*"High and bright, harmonizing with Heaven,
must be followed by *non-action* (wuwei) to complete,
thus aligning with Heaven’s *virtue/power*."*

To align with Heaven’s *virtue/power* is to ascend with the Creator of all things and transcend all existence—this is called *harmonizing with Heaven*. If so, then one has reached the *utmost*, beyond which nothing can be added, hence it is called the *ultimate of antiquity*. For *ultimate* speaks of the *utmost*—if like the *Great Ultimate* (太极), then its *utmost* is in *height*; if like the *Boundless Ultimate* (无极), then its *utmost* is in *distance*. The *house ridgepole* merely signifies the *utmost* of wood.

In the art of war, there is a saying: *"I dare not be the host but only the guest."*

「Thus, the *Huizong* commentary states: Those who *respond* are the *host* (主), those who *stimulate* are the *guest* (客). Only when pressed does one act, only when compelled does one rise—this is called *responsive warfare* (应兵). *Responsive warfare* is the *guest*’s way.

The *Explanatory Notes* say: The martial virtue of the sage does not rely on brute strength to turn the heavens and earth, yet the world cannot perceive its vigor; nor does it exhaust the wisdom of the gods, yet no one can fathom its depths. For those who command generals and lead armies like teachers, sharing the people’s sufferings—when *stimulated*, they *respond*, yet they dare not assert dominance. Only because they do not seek to overpower others, they are the *host* when *stimulated*, and the *guest* when *responding*. Pressed before they move, their actions are timely—this is the meaning of *"arms are laid aside but act when the moment comes."* Compelled before they rise, their rise is decisive yet unavoidable—this is the meaning of *"only when unavoidable do they act, and tranquility is the highest virtue."*

If so, then the first principle is to be *unconquerable*—waiting for the enemy’s conquerable moment to *respond* as *responsive warfare*, the *guest*’s way. As the *guest*, one displays the *prolonged weakness* (绵绵之弱), aligning with Sunzi’s saying: *"The skilled victor stands in unconquerable ground."* One does not dare advance an inch but retreats a foot.

The *Huizong* commentary states: Because one does not delight in killing, advancing is difficult yet retreating is easy.

The *Explanatory Notes* say: Facing arrows and spears, confronting blades and barbs—by means of arms, it is harsh; by nature, it is perilous. How could one willingly engage in such warfare? The general’s purpose is only to curb violence, lay down arms, pacify the people, and harmonize the multitudes. The essence of command lies in self-preservation in warfare, and in halting the blades in martial virtue. It is never the intent to revel in killing. Because one does not delight in killing, advancing is difficult yet retreating is easy. Difficult to advance speaks to having something to defend—not cowardice in advancing, but knowing that defense alone ensures steadfastness. Easy to retreat speaks to having something to heed—not recklessness in retreating, but avoiding profit and courting danger. In the *Great Marshal’s* (《大司马》) teachings on battle formations, during hunting exercises, one is trained in sitting, rising, advancing, and retreating, with rhythms of swiftness, slowness, density, and sparseness. Sometimes, when the drum signals advance, one strikes the *bracelet* (镯) to regulate it; when the drum signals retreat, one sounds the *gong* (铙) to halt it. How much more so when facing the enemy—should one not carefully weigh the timing of advance and retreat?

The *Art of War* states: *"Unmoving as a mountain, taking its steadfastness and stillness."* It also says: *"Its swiftness like the wind, taking its speed and agility."* Only by understanding this can one grasp the profound meaning behind *"not daring to advance an inch but retreating a foot"*—this is the true essence.

This is called *"action without action"* (行无行).

The *Huizong* commentary states: The skilled warrior does not rely on martial force but acts without trace.

The *Explanatory Notes* say: Martial force is inferior to the *Dao/Way*; it is the lowest form of *virtue/power*. The warrior’s aspiration lies in the *Dao/Way* and is rooted in *virtue/power*, hence the skilled warrior does not rely on martial force. Moreover, even the grand armies of three divisions and five kinds of troops depend on the *spirit* and *movement* of the general’s will—so subtle as to be formless. How could martial force be visibly displayed? It is fitting that one acts without trace, that martial resolve lies in the will rather than in the deeds, and that its subtlety cannot be observed from the sidelines. There should be no need for the *phoenix* to parade in splendid towers or the *steed* to stand idle in the imperial stables, should there?

*"Defend without limbs"* (攘无臂).

The *Huizong* commentary states: The skilled warrior does not rage.

The *Explanatory Notes* say: The benevolent must have courage, yet though they do not rage, their *威* (awe) remains. The *Odes* say: *"Like thunder, like fury."* Though described as fury, it arises from non-fury—this is the meaning of *"the skilled warrior does not rage."*

*"Unmatched without rival"* (仍无敌).

The *Huizong* commentary states: The skilled victor over enemies does not contend.

The *Explanatory Notes* say: The benevolent have no rivals, yet though they do not contend, they prevail. The *Odes* say: *"None dare contend in their season."* Only because they do not contend, others cease their contention—this is the meaning of *"the skilled victor over enemies does not contend."*

*"Hold without arms"* (执无兵).

The *Huizong* commentary states: One relies on others’ strength, hence there is no need to wield arms.

The *Explanatory Notes* say: The priority of arms is to attach to the people; the *Dao/Way* of warfare is to harmonize with them. Thus, the skilled in attaching to the people are the skilled in warfare. Benevolent rulers above and below, a hundred generals with one heart, three armies united in strength—like children serving their parents and brothers, like limbs protecting the head. When prolonged, it is like the long blade of *Mo Ye* (莫耶), and those who grasp it are severed; when sharp, it is like the keen edge of *Mo Ye*, and those who face it are routed. All this depends on the strength of others—what need is there to wield arms?

Mencius said: *"One who commands awe over the world does not rely on the sharpness of arms, but gains the *Dao/Way* and receives much assistance."* The utmost of receiving assistance means the world submits to it; there are some conflicts one does not engage in, yet when one does, victory is certain—this is the meaning of *"relying on others’ strength."* To form the *arc* and *arrow* to command awe over the world is not to say one does not rely on the sharpness of arms. It is to say that *root* triumphs over *branch*—the emphasis lies in the strength of others, not in the sharpness of arms. This is the meaning of *"no need to wield arms."*」

Mencius said: *"He who does not delight in killing can unite the world. Then it is known that those who delight in killing cannot achieve their ambitions in the world—this aligns with the words of Laozi without prior agreement.*

Thus, when armies clash in confrontation, the compassionate will prevail. The *Huizong* commentary states: *"The Sage’s use of arms is to rescue the people from fire and water, taking only the remnants of the wicked. Divine and martial, yet refraining from killing, the Sage treasures compassion as his jewel. Thus, *ren* (virtue/power of benevolence) permeates the world without fail, and *yi* (virtue/power of righteousness) commands reverence, making this the way of constant victory."*

The *Shuyi* (explanatory notes) say: *"The Sage rules the world, nurturing the people as a father. Heaven covers, Earth sustains, the seas embrace and spring nurtures—how could the Sage allow these people to perish in suffering without restraining their violence? Therefore, when forced to use arms to rescue the people from fire and water, taking only the remnants of the wicked, the tyrant has no place to stand.*

Who but the wise and enlightened, divine and martial yet refraining from killing, could match this? Indeed, the divine and martial who refrain from killing do not subjugate all things through sheer force—this is how they win hearts and minds, drawing them with *virtue/power* and treasuring compassion as their jewel. Yet, what is this *virtue/power*? It is merely *ren* (benevolence) and *yi* (righteousness).*

Thus, when *ren* (benevolence) permeates the world without fail, it is because the Sage abhors harming others—this is the meaning of *ren* (benevolence) embracing all. When *yi* (righteousness) commands reverence, it is because the Sage upholds order and abhors chaos—this is the meaning of *virtue/power* and awe inspiring fear. But if it is said that the Sage treasures compassion as his jewel and that *ren* (benevolence) embraces all, while *yi* (righteousness) commands fear, how is this possible?

It is because when *ren* (benevolence) and *yi* (righteousness) are practiced, both awe and love are achieved—compassion allows for courage. This aligns with Zhuang Zhou’s words: *‘Naturally benevolent and compassionate, yet never forgetting to use benevolence as a gift and righteousness as principle.’* If *ren* (benevolence) embraces all and *yi* (righteousness) commands fear, then the people will willingly submit, their hearts content and their minds sincerely obedient. The next level of victory is truly insignificant—this is the way of constant victory, is it not?

In ancient times, King Tang of Shang ruled with *‘not competing, not contending’* to spread his governance, thus exhausting the work of *ren* (benevolence). He wielded *‘not startling, not moving’* to display his courage, thus exhausting the work of *yi* (righteousness). Both *ren* (benevolence) and *yi* (righteousness) fully realized, he could act without constraint and yet gather the fruits of victory—who would dare resist? Is this not the way of constant victory rooted in *ren* (benevolence) and *yi* (righteousness)?

My words are very easy to understand, very easy to practice."

The world cannot know it, cannot practice it.

Huizong’s annotation: The Dao shines brightly and is easy to see, hence when it is expressed in words, it is very easy to know; it is essential and easy to uphold, hence when it manifests in deeds, it is very easy to practice.

Mencius said: "The Dao is like a great road—how difficult could it be to know?"

Thus, the Dao has no difficulty, and the world has no one who cannot grasp it. Yet there are those who deceive themselves into believing they cannot—it is only because they do not reflect and seek within themselves.

The *Cushui* commentary: The Dao is lofty and beautiful, shining brightly and easily seen, hence when expressed in words, it is as clear as the sun and stars, making it very easy to know. The Dao is unified and penetrates all things, essential and easy to uphold, hence when seen in deeds, it is precise as a level and plumb line, making it very easy to practice.

If we clarify this through the teachings of the Six Classics, the Dao becomes even more evident and easy to grasp. For instance:
- The *Book of Poetry* teaches *wēnróu dūnhòu* (gentle, kind, and thickly virtuous),
- The *Book of Documents* teaches *shūtōng zhīyuǎn* (open and far-reaching),
- And so on, extending to *guǎngbó yìliáng* (broad and easy to be good), *jiéjìng jīngwēi* (pure and refined), *gōngjiǎn zhuāngqīn* (reverent, frugal, and dignified), and the use of metaphor and analogy—all are manifestations of the Dao in words. By examining its expressions to seek its meaning, knowing it is not difficult.

The *Book of Poetry* guides the will,
The *Book of Documents* guides action,
And so on—guiding conduct, guiding harmony, guiding yin and yang, guiding names and distinctions—all are manifestations of the Dao in deeds. By examining its deeds to understand its principles, practicing it is not difficult.

Truly, Mencius’ words are profound: "The Dao is like a great road—how difficult could it be to know?" The Dao is the path all people share, like a great road. Traveling in and out, coming and going, none can stray beyond it. The world has no one who cannot grasp it, yet there are those who deceive themselves into believing they cannot—it is only because they walk in darkness, or wander into narrow paths, or turn back at cliffs, or abandon the way halfway. All this stems from their lack of reflection. The reason they cannot grasp it is that they do not reflect and seek within themselves.

If they knew that everyone holds something precious within themselves, and could reflect and seek within, then the Dao would be near—there is no need to seek it far away, and the Dao would dwell within them.

Laozi’s teaching must say, *"My words are very easy to know, very easy to practice."* Truly, he wished that all in the world and future generations would know to seek within themselves, to delve deeply into the Dao.

Words have their root, deeds have their master.

Huizong’s annotation: Words are boundless, yet principle is their foundation. Deeds are countless, yet the Dao is their sovereign.

The *Cushui* commentary: The expression of the heart’s voice arises spontaneously, without limit, and cannot be measured by clever calculations—this is why words are boundless. Yet they are expressed through metaphors and analogies, all of which are words, and though principle cannot be exhausted by these metaphors, it is never outside them. Thus, words have principle as their foundation.

The affairs of life are vast and endless, rushing by without measure—this is why deeds are boundless. Yet they are governed by standards and measures, all of which are deeds, and though the Dao cannot be sought in these standards, it is never separate from them. Thus, deeds have the Dao as their sovereign.

Zhuangzi said: *"When two households debate, which side’s argument is more biased in principle?"* If one’s words must return to ultimate principle, then it is clear that words have principle as their foundation. Yet principle is called the foundation because it aligns with the saying *"follow its root."*

Again, he said: *"To unite with the One is to complete all things."* If deeds encompass the Dao and advance to it, then it is clear that deeds have the Dao as their sovereign. Yet the Dao is also called the master because it aligns with the saying *"the key lies in the master."*

Principle can be followed but not defied—if one seeks it in accordance with principle, they will find it. When principle flows naturally, then words align with it, and though it can be followed, it cannot be lost. Only by following the Dao in action can one truly embody it, making the heart harmonize with the Dao. Then deeds align with the Dao, words align with principle, and deeds align with the Dao—holding this as verification and standard, there is no action that cannot succeed. The world has no difficulty.

To say it is easy to know and easy to practice—how could it not be true?

It is only the ignorant who do not know me.

Huizong’s annotation: The petty scholar’s knowledge does not transcend documents—though they claim knowledge, they truly lack it. How could they understand the Dao?

The *Cushui* commentary: With a mind emptied and still, one can observe both near and far, delve into mysteries, uncover the hidden, and reach the profound—then wisdom is great indeed. The petty scholar’s wisdom is blinded by self-interest, and what they know does not extend beyond documents. Their spirit is wasted in shallow pursuits. Though they claim wisdom, it does not extend beyond the four seas—knowing the minutiae of hair and fur while ignorant of great peace, they claim knowledge yet truly lack it. How could they understand the Dao?

Without the Dao, one cannot truly know—because their knowledge cannot reach it.

Zhuangzi said: *"To know the Dao is easy."* Only through profound insight and discernment, by treating ignorance as true knowledge, can one truly know. The petty and selfish in wisdom are not free from burdens, and to seek true knowledge is indeed arduous.

Few understand me, hence I am precious.

Huizong’s annotation: Those with lofty conduct in the world are condemned by the crowd, and those with unique wisdom are startled by the people. Thus, there are those who are noble precisely because they are few.

The *Cushui* commentary: To stand out and excel is lofty conduct in the world. Those with lofty conduct are rarely recognized by the common crowd, hence they are condemned by the masses, for the masses judge only by their own biased likes and dislikes. To preserve the spirit and seek the ultimate is wisdom beyond the petty—those with such wisdom are rarely understood by the narrow-minded, hence they are startled by the people, for the people are deeply mired in ignorance and delusion.

The censure of others arises from their difference, and the scorn of the people stems from their arrogance—like the cuckoo mocking the progress of the *Nanqi* stream, or the well-frog belittling the joys of the Eastern Sea. How often one sees their lack of measure!

To seek the reason for this, they are obscured by one-sided views, and their limited clarity is insufficient to attain true knowledge.

The *Transmission* says: There is nothing in the world more esteemed than the **Dao**. The **Dao** is esteemed above all because it cannot be known by knowing.

If shallow perception and meager understanding were sufficient to grasp the **Dao**, then what would make the **Dao** so precious?

Only because few understand me, am I precious.

The *Records* speak of those who are noble because they are few—truly, this lies in the fact that few understand me, hence I am esteemed by the world.

Thus, the Sage wears coarse robes while harboring precious jade.

**Huizong’s annotation:** The Sage is hidden within Heaven, yet does not peddle himself in the marketplace.

**Cushui commentary:** The subtle essence of the **Dao** is never openly displayed, and the origin of things from the **Dao** is never known. The Sage is the ultimate of the **Dao**, merging with Heaven in emptiness—his concealment is profound.

From the perspective of his completeness within Heaven, he follows the natural order of Heaven, rests in its equilibrium, and moves without progress as Heaven’s play, acts without strife as Heaven’s mechanism. Observing Heaven without interference, rejoicing in Heaven without sorrow—these are all harmonizing with Heaven through Heaven, employing wondrous yet effortless use, surpassing mere goodness. To exert force and conceal it is to be hidden within Heaven.

Now, to hide a boat in a ravine or a mountain in a marsh may be called secure, yet there are times when it is lost. To hide gold in mountains or pearls in the depths may be called secret, yet there are times when it is lost. This is because what is hidden is in things, not in the **Dao**.

The Sage returns to the origin of nature, uniting with Heaven as one—how different this is! How could he take pleasure in worldly affairs, selling himself like a merchant?

The pretense of virtuous conduct is akin to selling jade as stone. The self-promotion of skill is like hawking techniques for gold. Not selling himself in the marketplace, the brightest virtue appears as humiliation, and great virtue seems as foolishness—this is to show that it has never emerged from his lineage.

To show that it has never emerged from his lineage, it is fitting that even the later generations cannot compare.

To know what is unknown is exalted.

**Huizong’s annotation:** The essence of the supreme **Dao** is dim and obscure; the extreme of the **Dao** is hazy and silent. Knowing transcends it externally, while ignorance resides within it; knowing is shallow, while ignorance runs deep. To say "I do not know" is true knowing—the ultimate of the **Dao**, hence it is exalted.

**Cushui commentary:** To emerge and interact with things is to reach no limit; to retreat and discern within is to reach a limit. The **Dao** attains its ultimate when it retreats to discern within things.

To retreat and discern within things is beyond what can be known by knowing—how much more so when it is dim and obscure, merging in the undifferentiated chaos, the spirit of spirits, yet able to refine itself? How much more so when it is hazy and silent, hidden beyond speech and intent, where things cannot be measured, yet all consider it the extreme?

Who would say that knowing can seek its ultimate?

Broadly, Guangchengzi said: "The essence of the supreme **Dao** is dim and obscure; the extreme of the **Dao** is hazy and silent"—this is because the **Dao** is not something that knowing can know.

When Taiqing asked about the boundless, he said, *"I do not know."* When asked about non-action, he said, *"I know."* The **Dao** uses these two statements—how does one determine which is correct and which is false?

This is because one has not yet understood that *not-knowing* is knowing, and knowing is *not-knowing*. This is why there are distinctions of depth and shallowness, interior and exterior. Thus, it is said: knowing transcends it externally, while ignorance resides within it; knowing is shallow, while ignorance runs deep.

Only knowing what is unknown is true knowing.

If so, then transcending form and emerging beyond the realm of being and non-being, standing aloof without associating with things—one can intuitively grasp it in silence.

What in the world is more exalted than this? Hence it is said: *To know what is unknown is exalted.*

To not know what is known is a sickness.

**Huizong’s annotation:** To not know the essence of the supreme **Dao** while knowing the coarse aspects of things, to not know the extreme of the **Dao** while knowing the trivial ends of things—already enslaved to trifles, already entangled by things, yet they say they tread the path of sorrow and calamity. Hence, it is a sickness.

**Cushui commentary:** Clarity arises from emptiness, awareness from stillness. The realm of Taiqing is initially free of any obstruction. Once false perceptions arise, the empty and still mind is suddenly immersed in things, and what is known is coarse while the supreme essence remains unknown, what is known is trivial while the supreme extreme remains unknown—these are all cases of those who do not know what is known.

Already enslaved to trifles, already entangled by things, yet they daily sink into the domain of sorrow and exhaustion. What sickness is greater? How could those who seek detachment prescribe remedies to rid them of the eight flaws and four afflictions, so they may naturally attain the tranquil realm of Taiqing?

Only by recognizing sickness as sickness can one truly be free from sickness.

**Huizong’s annotation:** To know one’s own foolishness is not great foolishness. To know one’s own confusion is not great confusion. Great confusion lasts a lifetime without resolution, and great foolishness lasts a lifetime without enlightenment.

**Cushui commentary:** Foolishness is ignorance without wisdom; confusion is doubt without clarity—both are ailments of nature. These ailments align with Confucius’ statement that the people have three afflictions.

Only by knowing this and discerning it can one resolve the obscurations and dispel the confusions. Zhuangzi said: *To know one’s own foolishness is not great foolishness; to know one’s own confusion is not great confusion*—this is why.

Indeed, to know one’s foolishness, to know one’s confusion—this is the sick speaking of their sickness. Not great foolishness, not great confusion—this is the sick recognizing their sickness, yet still not sick.

「If one fails to recognize their own foolishness yet still insists on applying it, or if they harbor two doubts and thus fall into confusion, then they shall remain a lifetime without enlightenment or resolution.

This is like the man from Nanrong and Yue, who knew their ailments yet could not endure the potent medicine of the Dao/Way—thus they wished only to hear the *Sutras of Longevity* (衛生之經).

Yet if one treats the disease that afflicts them, then they shall not be afflicted by it themselves. The Sage’s freedom from disease lies in their treating the disease—hence, they are not afflicted.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** The Sage is naturally pure (素逝) and shuns worldly involvement (耻通於事), yet they establish the fundamental principles (立本) and comprehend divine unity (通於神)—this is true knowledge. Yet they always appear as though they do not know, and so they are not afflicted.

**Cushui commentary:** *Naturally pure* (素) means untainted by the mundane; *pure and unconstrained* (逝) means they do not fail to traverse all things. Only by being naturally pure and unconstrained can one shun worldly involvement (不蕲通於事), yet all worldly matters become transparent to them (事無不通). This is because they have established the fundamental principles of the Dao/Way (立之本原), and so their wisdom unites with the divine (知通於神).

For the fundamental principles (本原) are the essence of the Dao/Way. Only by first establishing the great Dao/Way can one merge with the divine—then, with broad vision and seated contemplation, they perceive all things without obstruction, possessing true knowledge. Yet they always appear as though they do not know, and so who could possibly weary their hearts?

Zhuangzi said that the man who is naturally pure and unconstrained, yet shuns worldly involvement, and who establishes the fundamental principles while comprehending divine unity, is a man of *Kingly Virtue/Power* (王德之人)—this is why.

**Chapter Seventy-Two of *The People Do Not Fear the Great Menace* (民不畏威):**
When the people do not fear the great menace, then the great menace has indeed arrived.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** The petty man regards minor vices as harmless and does not abandon them; thus, the vices accumulate and cannot be concealed. The *Yijing* (《易》) states: *Bearing a burden and losing one’s ears—disaster.* (荷校灭耳，凶)

**Cushui commentary:** Good deeds that do not accumulate are insufficient to establish one’s reputation; evil deeds that do not accumulate are insufficient to destroy one’s life. Yet the people are born with thick ignorance (民生厚), and their inclinations shift with external influences (因物有迁). They abandon truth for illusion (舍真逐妄), delight in superficial ornamentation (外悦纷华), and engage in conflict over paths of gain and loss (交战於利害之涂) while remaining oblivious to fear. They regard minor good deeds as useless and do not perform them, and minor vices as harmless and do not abandon them. Thus, their evils accumulate beyond concealment (恶积而不可揜), and their crimes grow vast beyond absolution (罪大而不可解).

This is what Zhuangzi meant by *those who suffer external punishments at night* (宵人之罹外刑), and what the *Yijing* meant by *bearing a burden and losing one’s ears—disaster.* (荷校灭耳，凶)

Does not their pursuit of gain and avoidance of loss reveal that they do not fear the great menace? (迹其所为得，非不畏其威致然耶？)

Do not narrow their dwellings,」

「Do not narrow their dwelling,」
---

**Huizong’s annotation:** Dwelling is the abode of one’s nature; human nature is boundless and cannot be confined, yet those of narrow understanding cannot be spoken to of the Dao/Way because they restrict their dwelling.

If expanded and filled, it will overflow to fill Heaven and Earth, embrace the six extremes, and nothing will be able to contain it. Mencius said: *Dwell in the broadest dwelling of the world.* The *cushui* commentary states: In the boundless expanse of the Great Peace, it fills Heaven and Earth without limit to its boundaries, circulates through the six voids without end to its traces, so that the abode of one’s nature is vast and cannot be confined—its grandeur is boundless.

Those in the world who are ignorant, confined by their own narrowness, see only things and not the Dao/Way. They dig through walls and plant weeds, and their dwelling begins to narrow—because those of narrow understanding cannot be spoken to of the Dao/Way.

If one seeks its vastness and fluidity to restore the nature’s original state, there must be those who act with resolve to open the way. Then obstacles will be overcome, blockages will be cleared, and if expanded and filled, it will rise to the heights and spread to the depths, covering all beneath Heaven and embracing the six extremes. Its dwelling will be the broadest, yet never will it be confined or petty.

This is what Mencius meant by *dwelling in the broadest dwelling of the world*—it is also because they do not narrow their dwelling.

Zhuangzi said: *The fenced enclosures of the Bowei, the orchards of the Yellow Emperor, the palaces of the Yu, and the halls of Tang and Wu.* Truly, the Dao/Way descends ever lower, and thus their dwelling becomes ever narrower.

**Do not weary of what they produce.**
Huizong’s annotation: Production is the gathering of *qi*; human life aligns with the transformations of all things, yet those who weary of their production say: *The deeds of a single day can lead to death or loss.* The reversals of this fate mean that the *qi* of night is insufficient to sustain them. Those who preserve the Great Harmony and avoid untimely death do so because they do not weary of what they produce.

The *cushui* commentary states: Human life is a mingling of coarse and refined, changing to form *qi*, *qi* changing to form shape, shape changing to form life—so life is but a fleeting gathering of *qi*. All who receive the *qi* of yin and yang to form their shape: at night, they rest with yin; within, they interweave with things. By day, they move with yang; without, they engage with things. Human life is inherently aligned with the Dao/Way of day and night and the transformations of all things.

Thus, when interweaving with things, all thoughts cease and turn inward; when engaging with things, all threads arise and respond fluidly. If one does not know how to preserve life to defend oneself, but instead exhausts oneself in myriad ways, it is as Mencius said: *The deeds of a single day can lead to death or loss.*

The reversals of fate are not uniform; even if the *qi* of night is insufficient to sustain them, their failure to preserve a good heart is rare indeed. Only those who guard pure *qi*, nourishing it with uprightness and without harm, will find that the Great Harmony of Heaven and Earth is enough to preserve them—keeping them from scattering, keeping them from departing.

Thus can they preserve life and fulfill their years, never meeting untimely death. Is it not because they do not weary of what they produce that this is achieved?

It is only because they do not weary that they do not weary. Huizong’s annotation: Misfortune and fortune come from no other source than one’s own actions.

The *cushui* commentary states: Honor and disgrace arrive as reflections of one’s *virtue/power*. Misfortune and fortune have no fixed gate—only human actions summon them. Thus, the rewards for good and evil, calamity and blessing, follow their kind. There is none who does not reap what they themselves have sown.

Then, if the refined *qi* of the Five Phases is attuned, and the spirit is prepared to receive all things, those endowed with nature’s original *virtue/power* who seek to avoid wearied production must truly not weary of their own life.

In ancient times, a farmer who tended crops recklessly plowed too deeply and hoed too harshly, and thus his crops suffered accordingly. But when he plowed deeply and harrowed carefully, his crops flourished and grew. Who would say that cultivating the body and refining the heart is not like the farmer’s lesson?

Those who cultivate life well should understand this. Hence, the Sage knows themselves but does not display themselves, cherishes themselves but does not exalt themselves—so they abandon that and take this.

Huizong’s annotation: The Sage has clarity in self-knowledge, yet does not parade their abilities; they have benevolence in self-cherishing, yet do not lord over others. Such beings act in the world without harming it, and the world cannot harm them.

They delight in the Dao/Way of Heaven and have no sorrow—what is there to fear in the threats of anger?

The Sage’s choices in abandoning and taking are now clear.

The *cushui* commentary states: Self-knowledge is clarity; the Sage has clarity in self-knowledge. Though their insight extends without limit, how could they parade themselves to boast of their abilities? Only by not parading themselves to boast of their abilities does one attain the height of wisdom. Those who parade themselves do not understand.

Self-cherishing is the utmost of benevolence; the Sage cherishes themselves with benevolence. Though they widely bestow and aid all, how could they exalt themselves to belittle others? Only by not exalting themselves over others does one attain the height of love. Those who exalt themselves are, in truth, belittled by others.

With this, they govern the multitude and thus can settle the world in peace, stability, and great harmony. Acting in the world without harming it, the world cannot harm them.

It is fitting that they delight in the Dao/Way of Heaven to preserve the world, yet fear no threats of anger.

The Sage’s choices in abandoning and taking—how could they be outside of self-knowledge without display and self-cherishing without exaltation?

---
**Boldly daring but not daring is death; boldly daring but not daring is life.**
Huizong’s annotation: The rigid and strong are doomed to destruction. The yielding and weak are preserved in life. Those who boldly dare are capable only of daring. Capable of daring yet unable to retreat is not true virtue—it is merely enough to destroy their body. Hence, Zilu loved daring, and Confucius said he was unfit for any purpose.

Those who boldly dare yet do not dare know how to hold back. To hold back is the Dao/Way of acting first.

Liezi said: *"There exists an eternal Dao/Way of victory in the world—it is called Softness."*

**Exegesis:** The rigid perishes; the sharp is blunted. The strong and unyielding are doomed to destruction. Softness overcomes hardness; weakness overcomes strength. Thus, the soft and weak are preserved in life.

Yet the world’s people know only to dare boldly, advancing resolutely without regard—never realizing that supreme softness can traverse the world’s greatest hardness. This is the Dao/Way’s true function: enduring and ever-present.

Zilu, though he wandered at the Sage’s gate, did not grasp the Dao/Way’s use. He could not restrain his reckless strength—daring yet unable to retreat—so where could he find true worth? It was enough only to destroy his body.

But those who know to embrace the雄 (heroic) while guarding the雌 (receptive) are not incapable of daring. Rather, they dwell in softness and stillness, yielding with things like a serpent’s coils, returning to the innocence of an infant. This is the daring of *not daring*—and thus, life.

Thus did Guangchengzi abide in harmony, refining his body for a thousand two hundred years without decay—precisely because he dared not dare.

The *Transmission* says: *"To act last is to act first."* To dare not dare is to know the Dao/Way of holding back. To hold back is to act first.

Only those who know the eternal Dao/Way of victory in softness can be spoken to thus. Hence, Liezi said: *"There exists an eternal Dao/Way of victory in the world—it is called Softness."*

These two paths—daring and not daring—bring either benefit or harm.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** Where there is rectitude, there is deviation. Where there is obstruction, there is suitability.

**Exegesis:** What humans call "arriving at the Dao" is inversion; what the Dao calls "arriving at humans" is inversion. Those who dare boldly may seem to gain, but Heaven truly harms them. Those who dare not dare may seem to suffer, but Heaven truly benefits them.

For the principles of the world, where there is rectitude, there is deviation; where there is obstruction, there is suitability. They alternate and transform, waxing and waning naturally. Knowing these two paths makes the logic of benefit and harm clearly manifest.

What does Heaven abhor? Who knows its reason?

**Huizong’s Annotation:** The strange in humans aligns with the companion in Heaven. What benefits humans, Heaven abhors—who can know this?

**Exegesis:** Humans have long been overcome by Heaven. Those blinded by human ways, thinking to conquer Heaven, act recklessly in ignorance. How could birds, strange to humans, align with Heaven?
