Thus, Heaven’s petty ones are humanity’s virtuous; Heaven’s virtuous are humanity’s petty—this is the natural order. Hence, even the Sage finds it difficult.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** Those who follow Heaven endure; those who defy it perish—even the Sage dares not alter this.

**Exegesis:** To follow is auspicious; to defy is ominous. Thus, those who follow endure; those who defy perish. To know human actions, one must know Heaven’s actions.

Zhuangzi said: *"To act in accordance with it—is that difficult? Those who find it easy, Heaven does not favor."*

The Sage aligns with Heaven, harmonizing with the divine to assist in transformation and nurturing. Though seemingly without difficulty, even the Sage does not dare to alter much—what of those beneath?

The Dao/Way of Heaven does not contend yet achieves victory.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** When things emerge, Heaven allows them to emerge without resistance. When things return, Heaven allows them to return without obstruction—this is called *not contending*.

To fill and empty, to wax and wane—Heaven participates with all things, yet all things receive their mandate from it. This is called *not contending yet achieving victory*.

**Exegesis:** Heaven’s movement knows no accumulation, and thus all things are completed. Only through movement without accumulation can it govern all things, transforming from non-being to being. The sun warms and nourishes all, so when things emerge, Heaven allows them to emerge without resistance. The yin consolidates, and all things are perfected, so when things return to their roots, Heaven allows them to return without obstruction.

As sovereign and regulator, it oversees myriad transformations, standing independently in the realm of non-contention. It sees the ebb and flow—one full, one empty—simply following the natural course of *qi*.

Moreover, though myriad things exist, none escape its pivot. Thus, all receive their mandate here, and what surpasses this as the Dao/Way of victory?

It speaks not, yet responds perfectly.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** What does Heaven say? It changes with thunder and wind, demonstrating with calamity and blessing—no deviation, only resonant response.

**Exegesis:** The thunder and wind stir all things. Heaven’s blessings reward the virtuous; its punishments befall the licentious. Heaven’s vastness cannot be rushed—its transformations are subtle, operating silently in the boundless void, embodying supreme beauty without speech.

Its means of change: perhaps the thunder stirs, the wind disperses—always in accordance with the season. Its means of instruction: perhaps it blesses the virtuous, punishes the licentious—always in accordance with their kind.

Yet its responses are swift, with no deviation—like shadows following form, echoes following sound. It never favors its responses. What does Heaven say?

It does not summon, yet they come.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** Those who receive their mandate can summon them. The chaos of myriad things is governed by Heaven’s decree—who can summon them? It simply follows the unceasing flow of *qi*.

**Exegesis:** The *Qian* (Creative) is sovereign, the first to emerge among all things; it is father, the origin of all. Ministers receive their mandate from the sovereign; children obey their father—thus, those who issue the mandate can summon them.

Only by issuing the mandate can one govern all things, commanding all movement.

「All living things, in their myriad confusion, exist within an incalculable expanse—none can be summoned by any means. Unable to summon, yet they come; unable to compel, yet they arrive. Endlessly circulating without pause, they move through the boundless void, their transformations subtle and imperceptible, their **Dao/Way** unceasing, as **qi** flows naturally of its own accord, knowing not whence it springs yet simply is. Tranquil yet shrewd in counsel.

**Huizong’s Annotation:** Virtue and power are always easy to discern, yet perilous to grasp. Tranquility is ease; shrewd counsel is knowing peril. The **Qian** (Creative) accumulates three **yang** to form its substance—this is called **ultimate health**. Though perilous, if virtue and power remain easy, even the **yin**’s dangers cannot ensnare them. Does the *I Ching* not speak in *The Upper Appendices* of **Qian** knowing through ease, and in the *Qian* hexagram’s ninth line, of discerning the emergence of a single **yin**—then using it to bring about auspiciousness? Is this not the meaning of tranquil yet shrewd in counsel?

The **Heavenly Net** is vast and sweeping, though its mesh is wide yet never misses. **Huizong’s Annotation:** Though dense, it has gaps—this is human action. Heaven, though sparse, has no gaps. Accumulated virtue brings blessings; accumulated vice brings calamities—each responds in kind, thus nothing is ever lost. Yet those who strive only to win, who speak only to be answered, are human. Heaven does not strive yet wins effortlessly, does not speak yet responds perfectly—when summoned, it comes. Only the foolish fail to grasp Heaven’s **Dao/Way**.

Heaven does not summon yet they come; tranquil yet shrewd in counsel—only the **sage** can embody this. Thus, they do not pursue self-interest nor obstruct harm, always benefiting without causing harm, aligning with Heaven’s **virtue/power** and differing from those who dare boldly. **Exegesis:** Heaven follows **principle** (*li*); humans follow **emotion** (*qing*). Following emotion means favoring personal views—though meticulous, they lack comprehensive insight, hence the gaps. Following principle means upholding universal harmony—though sparse, they cannot be evaded, hence no gaps. Thus, though dense with gaps, this is human action. Accumulated virtue must bring blessings; accumulated vice must bring calamities—each befalls its kind, never failing. Is the **Heavenly Net** then deliberate in this? Its judgments on misfortune and fortune arise from inherent causes, all self-inflicted.

The world’s people are blinded by partiality, ignorant of great **principle** (*li*), chasing trifles while forgetting the root—each path they tread brings new woes. Yet they fail to see that the **Dao/Way** of Heaven is clear and evident, always favoring the virtuous, while the wicked have no place to hide from its scrutiny. Moreover, to know Heaven’s actions and human actions is supreme. Striving only to win, speaking only to be answered, summoning only to bring about—these are all human. When humans cannot align with Heaven, they are but petty and self-serving. If you rid people of this petty wisdom, they will return to **principle**—how could Heaven’s **Dao/Way** be unaligned with humanity? How could humanity be unaligned with Heaven? Only the **sage** can embody this: turning countless petty defeats into grand victory, like Heaven’s effortless triumph; practicing the **Dao/Way** of silent teaching, like Heaven’s perfect response without words; acting in accordance with **principle** yet moving naturally, coming without being summoned, like Heaven’s spontaneous arrival; serene and unassuming, their spirit like divination—tranquil yet shrewd in counsel. If the **sage** does not act, misfortune and fortune do not arise—who then brings disaster? Thus, they do not pursue self-interest nor obstruct harm, always benefiting without causing harm. This passage begins with those who dare boldly—human—and ends with the **Heavenly Net**’s vast yet flawless judgment—divine. The **sage**’s alignment with Heaven’s **virtue/power** is fundamentally distinct from the bold daring of common folk. This is why the passage begins and ends as it does—to illuminate all under Heaven and for posterity. Scholars should ponder this deeply.

**The People’s Common Disregard for Death (Chapter 74)**
The people do not commonly fear the **sword and saw** (execution’s tools), nor death itself—how can you frighten them with death?

**Huizong’s Annotation:** The people have natural hearts, finding life joyous. If they lack this natural heart, why should they fear death? The **sword and saw** restrain them; the **measuring line and weights** (law’s strictures) kill them; the **mallet and chisel** (punitive tools) strike them—this is called frightening them with death. The people will only resist and never reform.

**Exegesis:** From birth, the people are not inherently distinct—**Heaven** bestows upon them a pure spirit, a simple nature. They are the most refined of all creatures, the noblest of Heaven and Earth. Who does not delight in life and abhor death? Water, unobstructed yet easily swayed by external things, lacks constancy of heart. If they lack this natural heart, they will flout laws and evade prohibitions, doing whatever they please—until they fall into crime, whereupon punishment follows. Thus, these are people who do not fear death yet are frightened by it.

Only when the **sage** cultivates the virtue of cherishing life deeply in the hearts of the people will they know to rejoice in their own lives, naturally moving toward goodness and away from crime—thus avoiding conflict with officials and rendering punishments obsolete. Yet there are those restrained by the **sword and saw**, killed by the **measuring line and weights**, struck by the **mallet and chisel**, who resist yet never reform—are these not the same? If you make the people constantly fear death, and those who act strangely, I shall seize and execute them—who would dare? **Huizong’s Annotation:** All under Heaven take joy in life yet weigh heavily the crime of breaking laws. Only then will strange words be punished, eccentric deeds prohibited—if your crimes against governance are indeed grave.

**Exegesis:** Once the people delight in life, nothing they desire is greater than life itself, nor do they abhor anything more than death—while breaking laws is the heaviest crime. Thus, the people each safeguard their natural inclinations toward life and death: words must have substance, so strange utterances cease, knowing they will be punished; actions must conform to **principle**, so eccentric deeds are rare, knowing they are forbidden. If you seize and execute them, the state will have enduring laws—seeking those who resist, I know none would dare. Indeed, if your crimes against governance are grave, this is precisely the intent.»

「If there is a fixed official to execute, let the fixed official execute. But if a substitute official executes, this is like a substitute carpenter wielding the axe.

**Huizong’s annotation:** The ruler must act without personal interference while the people act in accordance with the ruler’s will—this is an unchanging **Dao/Way**. If a substitute official executes, or a substitute carpenter wields the axe, this means the ruler and the people share the same **virtue/power**, reversing the **Dao/Way** and speaking contrary to it. How could such a ruler govern others if he himself is governed? King Wen of Zhou did not meddle in the myriad words, myriad legal cases, or myriad precautions, but only sought the proper officials to shepherd the people—this is why he succeeded.

**Exegesis:** The ruler follows the **Dao/Way**, the ministers handle affairs. Those who follow the **Dao/Way** act without interference and are honored, thus they serve the ruler’s will. Those who handle affairs act with purpose and bear burdens, thus they serve the **Dao/Way** for the people. The distinction between ruler and minister is an unchanging **Dao/Way**. Only when each has fixed responsibilities that cannot be altered does the legal official execute, the carpenter wield the axe, and the ruler merely maintain his own decorum and face south. Those who engage in affairs of governance never cross the boundary to inquire further—if the ministers act, the ruler must also act, then it is the substitute official executing, the substitute carpenter wielding the axe, and the ruler and ministers sharing the same **virtue/power**. How could such a ruler govern others? Thus, when speaking contrary to the **Dao/Way** and meeting it with falsehoods, he is one who others govern. The **sage’s** governance is without interference yet the world’s affairs are accomplished—this is the essential key to attaining governance. To know the **Dao/Way** is to act without interference, to have no affairs of one’s own, and yet the world serves its purpose.

Therefore, King Wen could settle the worthy and appoint them, ensuring they had no meddling in myriad words, myriad legal cases, or myriad precautions, but only in securing proper officials to shepherd the people did he see urgency—then it is clear that he had no substitutes to fear. Indeed, a substitute carpenter wielding the axe would rarely fail to harm his hands. **Huizong’s annotation:** If a substitute takes on new tasks without exemption from injury, how much more so when substituting for execution? This is why the ancients valued the **Dao/Way** of non-interference. Non-interference means the ministers bear full responsibility.

「The *Commentary on the Daoist Classic of Virtue and Power* states:

*"To grasp the great **symbol/principle** (大象, *dàxiàng*), the world follows; when it follows without harm, there is peace and ease. The **Dao/Way** of the Sage in governing the world lies in attending to affairs of **non-interference** (無為, *wúwéi*), and practicing instruction without words. Thus, the Sage could place the world in a state of peace and ease, and how could there be any harm from a substitute’s axe?

In *The Answer to the King* of *Zhuangzi*, it is said: *'The **Dao/Way** of non-interference means full responsibility for affairs.'* This is the **Dao/Way** of non-interference where those entrusted with duties bear full responsibility.

It is fitting that at the end of the chapter, it states: *'The **Perfect One’s** (至人, *zhìrén*) use of the heart is like a mirror—neither pushing nor pulling, responding without hoarding. Thus, the **Perfect One** can overcome things without harming oneself.'*

**Chapter 75 of the *Commentary on the Daoist Classic of Virtue and Power* (卷十三結):**
*"The people’s hunger arises from the excessive taxes of their superiors—this is why they starve."*

**Huizong’s Annotation:** *"When taxes are heavy, fields and crops are prone to neglect, and the people lack sufficient food."*

The *Commentary* states:
*"Mencius said: 'Transform their fields, reduce their taxes, and the people can be made rich; feed them according to season, use them with propriety, and wealth will be inexhaustible.' The Sage’s governance of the world ensures that the people can hold food to their chins and play, drum their bellies and stroll, saying that they enjoy sustenance and that the people are well-fed throughout the year. This is not merely achieved by lightening the taxes to nourish the people, but also by guiding them to abandon trivial pursuits and return to the fundamental **Dao/Way** (本, *běn*).

Indeed, **virtue/power** (德, *dé*) lies in benevolent governance, and governance exists to enrich the people. In ancient times, those who understood this would survey the twelve regions of soil to discern their suitability, then teach the people the appropriate cultivation methods for each. They would assign land responsibilities, establish tribute systems, and reward those who succeeded in their tasks with the honor of *鄙師* (lowly teacher), while punishing those who failed with the blame of *載師* (burdened teacher). Thus, the people would all exert themselves in their fields, take joy in their work, and strive for success—so the common folk would not suffer from hunger.

At that time, they relished their food, admired their attire, unaware of the **Imperial Dao/Way’s** (帝力, *dìlì*) influence upon them. How could there be any calamity of neglected fields and insufficient food?

The people are difficult to govern because their superiors meddle in affairs—this is why they are difficult to govern."*

**Huizong’s Annotation:** *"When governance is burdensome, deceit and fraud proliferate, and the people lose their simplicity."*

The *Commentary* states:
*"The Sage relies on the **Dao/Way** in the world and governs through affairs. Though responding to the myriad things, the Sage remains rooted in the **Dao/Way’s** essence of non-interference. Even in the application of rewards and punishments, the Sage does not depart from the fundamental principle of *渊虚* (profound emptiness, *yuānxū*). Loving tranquility, the people naturally align; without burdensome affairs, the people naturally thrive; without desires, the people naturally remain simple. This is so because the Sage depends on **Dao/Way** transformation rather than clever schemes. Relying on **Dao/Way** transformation means governance is not burdensome; not depending on clever schemes means deceit and fraud cease, and the people return to their simplicity.

The *Classic* says: *'When governance is obscure and gentle, its people are pure and honest; when governance is scrutinizing and strict, its people are weary and corrupt.'* When governance is burdensome, deceit and fraud arise, and the people lose their simplicity—this is the meaning of *'governance is scrutinizing and strict, its people are weary and corrupt.'*

People’s disregard for death arises from the depth of their attachment to life—this is why they lightly view death."*

**Huizong’s Annotation:** *"When life is cherished too deeply, the pursuit of gain becomes fervent, leading to reckless and licentious behavior, with no bounds."*

The *Commentary* states:
*"Life is what I desire, but there are things I desire more than life—this is why I would not obtain it at any cost. Death is what I abhor, but there are things I abhor more than death—this is why some misfortunes I would not avoid.

The people of the world do not understand this: they cling to what is weighty and discard what is light, thus excessively valuing life while neglecting righteousness. Those who would gain any advantage at all cost do so without restraint.

Because of their deep attachment to life, the people’s pursuit of gain is relentless—contending for fish, they are willing to get wet; chasing beasts, they are willing to run. To the point of losing their natural balance, they indulge in reckless and licentious behavior, doing whatever they please. Even when facing perilous risks, they regard death lightly. Though life is cherished too deeply, this very attachment leads to its destruction.

Only those who do not make life their primary concern are wiser than those who overly value it."*

**Huizong’s Annotation:** *"Zhuangzi said: 'Those who comprehend the essence of life do not labor for what life cannot provide.'*
*Those who do not make life their primary concern do not labor for what life cannot provide*—this is abandoning affairs while relinquishing life.

Abandoning affairs means the body is not taxed; relinquishing life means the essence is not depleted. When the body remains whole and the essence is restored, one aligns with the **Dao/Way** and becomes one with Heaven—this is why such individuals are wiser than those who overly value life. Though valuing life is different from regarding death lightly, abandoning life is even wiser than valuing it.

Extending the principles that benefit my own life to the people, we see lightened taxes, simplified punishments, households with sufficient provisions, and people well-clothed. Yet the people do not transgress, and this is the ultimate achievement of the **Imperial Dao/Way** (帝王, *dìwáng*)."*

The *Commentary* states:
*"The **Dao/Way** is fundamentally empty of things; your body is also empty. Even in the matters that concern your very existence, nothing is more vital than life—yet life is not truly yours to possess, for it is the **Heavenly Harmony** (天地之委和, *tiāndì zhī wěihé*) entrusted to you. Only by not claiming life as your own can you preserve it entirely. Thus, those who comprehend the essence of life—how could they labor for what life cannot provide?

Alas, the people of the world believe that nourishing the body is sufficient to sustain life, yet nourishing the body ultimately fails to preserve life. They do not realize that without burdens, one remains balanced and upright; when balanced and upright, corrupt **qi** cannot assail, and **Heavenly Harmony** will naturally arrive, blending with their own vitality. Thus, they attain a renewed life.

Without burdens, one does not make life their primary concern; not making life their primary concern means they do not labor for what life cannot provide. This allows one to abandon affairs while relinquishing life.

Abandoning affairs means the body is not taxed yet remains whole; relinquishing life means the essence is not depleted yet restored. When the body is whole and the essence is restored, **virtue/power** (德, *dé*) returns to its original state, and one aligns with the **Dao/Way**, becoming one with Heaven—this is why such individuals are wiser than those who overly value life. Though valuing life is different from regarding death lightly, abandoning life is even wiser than valuing it."*

The **Sage** examines the means by which we nurture our life, and when applied to the people of the world, they bring about abundant governance and thriving populace. Light taxes and levies are seen, and clear laws bring the people into submission. Harsh punishments are avoided, yet families are well-provided and people are content—those above have what they need, those below have what they nurture—so the people naturally incline toward the realm of **virtue/power** (仁寿, *rénshòu*). Needles and saws are not employed, axes and chisels are not used, clothing and headgear are simply adorned, and garments are distinguished—yet the people do not transgress.

Thus, nobles and commoners fulfill their roles, and the **virtue/power** (德, *dé*) of the wise and the unworthy aligns with their nature. The Sage achieves the **Dao/Way** of great peace, and its brilliance matches the winds of the ancient times.

Examining its source, one understands the principle of **non-action** (无为, *wúwéi*). The means by which we nurture our life is merely to apply it to the people.

Thus, the people have enough food and do not starve, return to simplicity and are not difficult to govern, and value life without regarding death lightly—this is the ultimate achievement of the **Emperor-King** (帝王, *dìwáng*), is it not?

**Human life spans seventy-six years.**
Human beings are born soft and weak, yet in death they become hard and strong.
Grasses and trees are born soft and brittle, yet in death they become withered and decayed.

Thus, those who are hard and strong are the followers of death, while those who are soft and weak are the followers of life.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** All things bear the yin and embrace the yang, blending their **qi** (冲气, *chōngqì*) to achieve harmony. The yang’s **virtue/power** (德, *dé*) lies in its ability to generate life, while the yin’s function is to bring about austerity and destruction.

When the yin’s austerity prevails, the harmony of blending is lost.
Hence, it is said: the hard and strong are the followers of death, while the soft and weak are the followers of life.

**Explanatory notes:** *Lǐzǐ* states: The transformations of yin and yang are called life and death.

All things flourish within Heaven and Earth, adorned by the strange pairings of yin and yang, confined by motion and stillness. None fail to bear the yin and embrace the yang, blending their **qi** (冲气, *chōngqì*) to achieve harmony.

Speaking of life and death, the yang **qi** is ever effusive, generating life as its **virtue/power** (德, *dé*), and all things thereby unfold and thrive—so the soft become hard, the weak become strong. The yin **qi** is ever congealed, bringing about austerity and destruction, and all things thereby wither and decay—so the hard are ruined, the sharp are blunted. As the **qi** subtly shifts, reaching the point of austerity and destruction, the harmony of blending is lost and returns to the utmost stillness.

Those who serve the yin and yang in the world know that life and death alternate, yet they do not grasp the constant **Dao/Way** of victory: softness (柔, *róu*), nor the constant **Dao/Way** of defeat: hardness (刚, *gāng*). Thus, they abandon the principle of life and move toward the realm of death.

For the **Dao/Way** is the interplay of one yin and one yang; all things follow it.

In terms of **affairs** (计事, *jìshì*), hardness and strength suffice to overcome softness and weakness. Yet in terms of the **Dao/Way**, softness and weakness suffice to overcome hardness and strength—this is why hardness and strength are the followers of death, while softness and weakness are the followers of life.

Hence, when armies are strong, they do not prevail.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** When opposing armies clash, the pitiful will triumph.

**Explanatory notes:** The deployment of the three armies and five weapons is but the end of **virtue/power** (德, *dé*).

The **Sage** mobilizes **spirit-qi** (精神, *jīngshén*), moves the **heart-methods** (动心术, *dòngxīnshù*), and focuses on the **root** (本, *běn*), from which the **branches** (末, *mò*) follow. Thus, Heaven’s awe is manifest, and the **spirit-martial** (神武, *shénwǔ*) does not resort to killing. This is seen in the **Sage**’s benevolence extending across the world, and righteousness inspiring awe throughout the world. Such an approach is indeed sufficient to uphold the **root** (大定功, *dàdìnggōng*) of great peace, to settle the people and harmonize the multitude, and to attain the constant **Dao/Way** of victory. How could one ever prioritize the strength of armies?

Therefore, those who assist the **Sovereign** (人主, *rénzhǔ*) with the **Dao/Way** do not rely on making armies strong throughout the world.

Yet when opposing armies clash, the pitiful triumph—this is because they hold victory well.

When wood is strong, it is split.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** The sturdy branches of *gōngbǎ* (拱把) mulberry and *zǐ* (梓) trees—people all know to cultivate them, yet when they become strong, they are cut down and split.

**Explanatory notes:** The *Shijing* says: *"The chairs are made of mulberry and zǐ wood."*

Indeed, mulberry and zǐ wood are gentle and refined materials, fit for ceremonial and musical use. When they first grow, they are but small branches, and people, if they wish to cultivate them, all know to nurture them because of their softness and weakness.

Yet when they rest day and night and are nourished by rain and dew, and can serve as the beams of halls or as tools and implements, then they are cut down and split—because of their hardness and strength.

Looking at plants, even they are felled first when strong, so how can one doubt that hardness and strength are the followers of death?

Thus, hardness and strength occupy the lower position, while softness and weakness occupy the higher.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** The victory of softness over hardness, and of weakness over strength, is the **Dao/Way** and **art** (道术, *dàoshù*) of the **Master Lao**.

*Zhuāngzǐ* says: *"To display meekness and weakness, and to practice humility and submission."*

**Explanatory notes:** Softness is the **Dao/Way**’s hardness, so the constant **Dao/Way** of victory lies in softness. Weakness is the **Dao/Way**’s strength, so the constant **Dao/Way** of defeat lies in hardness.

For softness accumulates into hardness, and the accumulation occurs first.
Weakness accumulates into strength, and the formation occurs later.

The first is above, the latter is below. Hardness and strength are naturally above, while softness and weakness are naturally below.

Observing this in Heaven and Earth, Heaven accumulates **qi** (气, *qì*), fulfilling its role in generating life and occupying the upper position—its accumulation is not hardness and strength. Earth accumulates masses (块, *kuài*), fulfilling its role in bearing form and occupying the lower position—its accumulation is not softness and weakness.

Observing this in the principles of things, water’s nature is to follow: if directed east, it flows east; if directed west, it flows west. Yet in attacking hardness and strength, it cannot overcome them. Wind, when it points at me, overcomes me; when it blows against me, it also overcomes me. Yet it alone can break down great trees—this is because it accumulates the unyielding force of countless small victories into a great victory.

Thus, the victor is above, and the vanquished are below.

The **Dao/Way** and **art** (道术, *dàoshù*) of **Master Lao** are often found in this principle: hardness leads to ruin, sharpness leads to blunting. Hence, one knows to be male yet preserve the female, to be bright yet embrace the dark. While others always take the lead, the Sage alone takes the rear, never leading yet always following others. This can be called attaining the constant **Dao/Way** of victory.

Observing his writings, the discussions on softness overcoming hardness are numerous: some say "preserve softness," others say "attain softness," yet again, "do not dare to seize strength." Furthermore, "the overbearing will not attain their death," and in this passage, the principle of strength and weakness is elaborately expounded.

Zhuangzi said that to display meekness and humility as a facade is not the Dao/Way of dwelling below. Once, Confucius, when questioned by Zilu, spoke of strength and said, *"Be broad and gentle in teaching, but do not answer the unrighteous strength of the southern lands."* Could this too be the Dao/Way of preserving softness?

Who says that Master Lao’s teachings and Confucius’ Dao do not align?

**Chapter Seventy-Seventh: The Dao/Way of Heaven**

The Dao/Way of Heaven—is it not like drawing a bow?

The high are lowered, the low are raised; the overabundant are diminished, the deficient are supplemented.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation: The Dao/Way knows no gain or loss, but things have fullness and emptiness. It is poured into yet never overflows, measured yet never exhausted—this is what the Sage preserves.

When descending into things, Heaven and Earth fill and empty with the times, waxing and waning. How much more so for humanity?

The Dao/Way of Heaven attains perfection in balance, thus lowering the high so they do not exceed, raising the low so they do not fall short. Those who are about to come are advanced, those who have succeeded are withdrawn. The four seasons run their course, each in its proper order.

The *Cushui* commentary: The Dao/Way in the world is vast, encompassing all boundaries, yet subtle, penetrating to the core. Its use fills the six voids, yet when abandoned, none know its whereabouts—what gain or loss is there? When descending from the Dao/Way, it is confined by things, bound by *qi*, and transformed by *qi*’s fluctuations; it adheres to numbers and is governed by them. One fullness, one emptiness—none can escape this.

Only the Dao/Way transcends *qi* and numbers, becoming the mystery of all things. Thus, those with *qi* and numbers all draw from it without depletion. Hence, it is poured into yet never overflows; though added to, it does not increase. It is measured yet never exhausted; though diminished, it is not diminished further. The more it gives to others, the more one retains; the more it bestows, the more one possesses. This is what the Sage preserves—what humanity cannot abandon.

Moreover, within the realm, vast as Heaven and Earth are, when the Dipper shifts south, all things flourish; when it shifts north, all things wither. It wanes as it wanes, flourishes as it flourishes—sometimes waning, sometimes flourishing, moving with the times. Even the Dipper’s single star in the void cannot escape *qi* and numbers.

Thus, Heaven and Earth fill and empty, waxing and waning with the times. How much more so for humanity? Therefore, rise and decline succeed one another, completion and ruin follow in turn—this is the unalterable principle of nature.

Only the Dao/Way of Heaven follows reason and balance, thus nothing exists that does not attain the mean. If mountains are steep and barren while marshes grow fat, if waters recede from depths while trees wither their branches, if those who gnaw with teeth lose their horns, if those who flap with wings lose their feet—

The high fear their arrogance and are lowered to bend and conform, not exceeding to the point of excess. The low fear their meanness and are raised to stand and reach, not falling short. Those who are about to rise are advanced, those who have succeeded withdraw, like the four seasons: spring and summer come first, autumn and winter follow. They shift without lingering, each in its proper order, all tending toward the mean.

Observe the Dao/Way of Heaven—is it not like drawing a bow?

The Dao/Way of Heaven diminishes the excessive and supplements the deficient.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation: Fullness invites loss; humility invites gain—this is the Dao/Way of time.

The *Cushui* commentary: The *Yijing* says: *"The Dao/Way of Heaven diminishes fullness and supplements humility."* Those who are full are diminished—this is diminishing the excessive. Those who are humble are supplemented—this is supplementing the deficient. To diminish the excessive and supplement the deficient is to balance through transformation.

The *Shujing* says: *"Fullness invites loss; humility invites gain—this is the Dao/Way of time."* Could this be what is meant?

But the Dao/Way of humanity is not so. It diminishes the deficient to serve the excessive.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation: Human hearts push down the low and elevate the high, oppressing the solitary and fearing the eminent.

The *Cushui* commentary: What arises naturally is the Dao/Way of Heaven. What is forced is the Dao/Way of humanity. The two are vastly distant.

Since the Dao/Way of humanity is burdened by artificiality, human hearts are ever precarious, never at ease. The low are pushed further down, the high are raised further up, chasing after things in deference and subservience, lacking steadfast principle. Thus, they oppress the solitary and fear the eminent.

The solitary are pitiful—if they are oppressed, none encourage them to cultivate virtue and do good. Who will take their side? The eminent are contemptible—if they are feared, none punish them for deceit and wickedness. Who will restrain them? This is merely diminishing the deficient to serve the excessive—how could they know the natural Dao/Way of Heaven?

Who among humanity can diminish the excessive and serve the deficient in the world? Only the Dao/Way.

Emperor Huizong’s annotation: It does not oppress the solitary, yet the impoverished receive from it. It does not fear the eminent, yet the wealthy are diminished by it. Only those with the Dao/Way can do this.

The *Cushui* commentary: The solitary are rejected and oppressed by the many. If one loves virtue, even if they are solitary and honored, they will be advanced and not oppressed—this is the impoverished receiving from it. The eminent are emulated and feared by the many. If one does not love virtue, even if they are eminent and high, they will be punished and diminished—this is the wealthy being taken from. Truly, this is the Dao/Way.

The Dao/Way acts impartially, neither favoring one nor neglecting another. It universally responds, fittingly addressing all, balancing the unbalanced to attain balance. The Sage embodies this to govern the world—who would suffer from partiality and deceit?

Thus, to diminish the excessive and serve the deficient is not to align with the Dao/Way of Heaven. Who can achieve this?

Zhuangzi said: *"The Master is the Dao/Way of Heaven."* Therefore, the Sage acts without relying on it, accomplishes without claiming it—does this not mean they do not desire to be seen as virtuous?

Emperor Huizong’s annotation: They do not rely on their actions, so they have no pride in self-praise.

The Sage does not dwell on their accomplishments, hence they have no pride of self-satisfaction. While others adorn their intelligence, they alone appear as if foolish; while others seek victory, they alone yield and preserve themselves—all to avoid being seen as virtuous. Thus, they are always without loss, attaining the Dao/Way of Heaven.

**Exegesis says:** Within the utmost emptiness, transformation gives rise to all things. The Sage embodies this utmost emptiness to fulfill all desires—how could they rely on their actions?

They harmonize all things without claiming righteousness, bestow benefits upon all ages without claiming benevolence—who would have pride in self-praise? The birds called *zhì* (至) and *qù* (去) do this—how could they dwell on their accomplishments?

Though their achievements cover the world, they seem not to be their own; they abandon merit and fame, returning them to the people—who would have pride of self-satisfaction? This is what is meant by the "spirit-person without merit."

Abandoning cleverness and artifice, they follow the natural principles of Heaven. The virtuous person’s excellence is such that their appearance seems foolish—this is what is meant by *"while others adorn their intelligence, they alone appear as if foolish."*

They conform and adapt with things, blending like waves, accumulating countless small defeats into great victory—this is what is meant by *"while others seek victory, they alone yield and preserve themselves."* All this is because they do not desire to be seen as virtuous.

Did the Master of the Five Chariots (列御寇) reject the gift of five jars of wine because of this principle?

Because they do not desire to be seen as virtuous, they remain humble and gain benefit while always remaining without loss—this is how they attain the Dao/Way of Heaven.

Compared to those who adorn their intelligence, startle the foolish, refine their conduct, and reveal their purity—brightly shining like the sun and moon in their actions—who cannot avoid blame, the difference is vast.

---
**Chapter Seventy-Eight: The Soft and Weak Conquer the World**

Nothing in the world is softer and weaker than water, yet nothing can surpass it in attacking the firm and strong—this is because nothing can replace it.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** The *Yijing* uses a well to symbolize nature, saying it does not change. Laozi says water is nearly the Dao/Way, because nothing can replace it.

If something could replace it, it would submit to others and lose itself—how could it conquer things? Only when nothing can replace it does it remain constant amid ten thousand changes, and nothing can conquer it.

**Exegesis says:** Water flows from the earth, passing through everywhere without obstruction; when dug, it becomes a well. The Dao/Way exists throughout the world, and when obtained, it becomes nature—thus, the *Yijing* uses a well to symbolize nature.

A well, when maintained, is never exhausted; though cities change, the well remains unchanged—this is because nature is constant, unaffected by nobility or lowliness, wisdom or foolishness. Though events vary without constancy, their essence does not change, just as a well does.

Heaven generates water from the One, and it is not far from the Dao/Way. It benefits all things, and all things receive its blessings and grace, yet it always remains in a state of softness, weakness, and non-contention. Among all things in the world, nothing can replace it—thus Laozi says water is nearly the Dao/Way, because nothing can replace it.

If things could replace it, then it would submit to others and lose its own nature—how could it attain the constant Dao/Way and conquer things? Only when nothing can replace it does it adapt to the terrain, becoming crooked or straight, and to the vessel, becoming square or round. Though it undergoes countless variations, it remains ever constant—truly, nothing can conquer it.

That the soft conquers the hard, the weak conquers the strong—this is known by all under Heaven, yet none can practice it.

**Emperor Huizong’s annotation:** Though they know it, benevolence cannot guard it.

**Exegesis says:** Intelligence is used to exhaust principles; benevolence is used to fulfill nature. The affairs of the world are not difficult to understand, but difficult to practice. If intelligence is sufficient to exhaust principles but benevolence is insufficient to fulfill nature, then they weary of what they uphold and lack sincere perseverance—how could they practice it?

The soft conquering the hard, the weak conquering the strong—common people may have sufficient intelligence to know it, but they often fail because they do not firmly cultivate it. Though they know it, benevolence cannot guard it; even if they attain it, they will lose it—what benefit would this have?

The Classic says: *"My words are very easy to know, very easy to practice, yet none under Heaven can know them, none can practice them."* This is in agreement.

The Sage thus speaks: *"To bear a state’s defilements is called the sovereign of the ancestral altars; to bear a state’s misfortunes is called the king of all under Heaven."*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Rivers and marshes receive filth, mountains and thickets harbor disease; a ruler contains defilements, embodying the Dao’s emptiness, and thus what he receives is ever broader, becoming the refuge of all things, while his governance extends ever farther."*

The Classic says: *"To know its glory and endure its humiliation is to be the valley of all under Heaven."*

The *Cushui* [commentary] explains: *"Water begins as a single ladle, yet accumulates to form rivers; thus the Yangtze and Yellow River gather waters to become vast. Earth begins as a single clod, yet accumulates to form fields; thus hills and mountains rise from lowly heaps to become lofty. When good deeds accumulate to form virtue, the divine clarity is naturally attained; the Sage’s heart flows accordingly, and so the great person harmonizes all to become the public good. What the *Classic of Transmission* says—‘Rivers and marshes receive filth, mountains and thickets harbor disease, a ruler contains defilements’—is precisely this.

Though a ruler’s undertakings may bring prosperity, his ascent to kingship is marked by the burden of what others flee. Hence, a king’s virtue lies in his capacity to receive. To be the king of all under Heaven, one must hold fullness without excess, preserve completion without loss—this is why one must bear the state’s misfortunes. This aligns with the Classic’s saying: *‘What people detest are solitude, widowhood, and barrenness, yet the king and duke take them as their calling.’*

To bear a state’s defilements and its misfortunes alike is to view honor and humiliation as one, without the heart of clinging or rejecting. In essence, it is only emptiness that can receive—hence the *Dao De Jing* says: *‘To know its glory and endure its humiliation is to be the valley of all under Heaven.’* This is the ultimate rectitude.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Words are not confined to one meaning; what seems contrary to things is in harmony with the Dao—this is called the ultimate rectitude of all under Heaven."*

The *Cushui* [commentary] explains: *"How can the Dao exist without words, or words exist without the Dao? One and words are two; two and one become three. From this point onward, even the most cunning cannot grasp it—thus words are not confined to one meaning. Yet, when the ultimate words are spoken, the common words prevail, giving rise to debates like ‘Firm and White’ [over differing interpretations], where people are bewildered and do not know where to turn. Then all under Heaven begins to see ‘rectitude’ as contrary to things.

Only those who understand the resolution of words—though they seem contrary to things, yet harmonize with the Dao—can speak all day and exhaust the Dao. What could be more ultimate than this rectitude? This is what Zhuangzi meant when he said, *‘Parables are the true words.’*"

**Chapter Seventy-Nine: Harmonizing Great Grievances**

*"To harmonize great grievances"* must leave lingering resentment—how then can it be called ‘good’?

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"Those who seek revenge do not break the spear; even those with vengeful hearts do not resent the windblown tile. Thus there is no lingering resentment."*

*"To love people is the root of harming them. To lay down arms is the root of creating them—how then can it be called ‘good’?"*

The *Cushui* [commentary] explains: *"The spear has no intent to harm, so those who seek revenge do not break it. The windblown tile has no intent to harm, so those with resentful hearts do not resent it. If one is always a judge or executioner, then people’s encounters with them are like the spear or the windblown tile—hence, all under Heaven is balanced, and there is no lingering resentment.

Moreover, benevolence loves people, so it hates those who harm them. Righteousness follows principle, so it hates those who disrupt order. If one uses cleverness to win favor, this is benevolence—yet it is the root of harming people. If one prohibits attacks and puts weapons to rest, this is laying down arms—yet it is the root of creating them. How then can harmonizing great grievances be truly good?

Thus the Sage holds only the left contract, and does not demand accountability from people."*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"The Sage follows great transformation without obstruction, receiving and rejoicing in it—hence no demand for accountability from people, nor do people demand it of him. Contracts have left and right, distinguishing giving and taking. To hold the left contract is to give alone."*

The *Cushui* [commentary] explains: *"The Sage empties himself to traverse the world; though myriad appearances are diverse, his heart remains ever clear. Though myriad transformations differ, his heart remains ever still, responding naturally to all under Heaven’s ways. He simply follows this moment, and in emptiness and tranquility, there is nothing to obstruct or muddy. This is what Zhuangzi said: *‘To follow great transformation without obstruction.’*

Thus he can float in response, intoxicated by the flow, never rejecting people or severing things, yet suddenly standing in the realm of no affairs. To act without action, to govern without governance, to treat things without harming them—while things cannot harm him—is why he holds only the left contract and does not demand accountability from people, nor do people demand it of him.

In ancient times, governance was achieved by knotted cords; later sages replaced them with written contracts. Officials used them for administration, and the people used them for clarity. Yet contracts have left and right: the left for giving, the right for taking. To hold only the left contract is to engage in nothing but giving. Though he gives without demanding accountability from people, things come naturally, without needing to be coerced or trusted.

Thus there is one with virtue who oversees the contracts."*

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"To distribute with virtue is called sage."*

The *Cushui* [commentary] explains: *"Virtue in people is shared universally; it cannot be monopolized. The Sage adjusts and responds to it, his virtue extending broadly. Since his heart is naturally shared with all, he simply returns to distributing it—then people’s alignment with the contracts is naturally effortless."*

「Zhuangzi records Guanzi’s words as saying: *"To distribute virtue among the people is called sage."* This is precisely what was meant.

Moreover, Master Kong also said: *"The noble person purifies his words and aligns with the universal—then there is harmony; he refines his speech and conforms with the natural—then there is response."* His intent is the same.

But one without virtue cannot oversee the contracts.

The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"He who finds pleasure in universal harmony is not a Sage."*

One without virtue does not find his own fulfillment, yet he seeks the fulfillment of others. While he strives to make all things harmonize, he harbors both conciliatory and resentful emotions. This is the root of obstruction—what is called *蔽* [obstructed/blinded].

Zhuangzi says: *"Those who lose themselves in things are called the obstructed and blinded people."*

The *Cushui* [commentary] explains: *"The measure of things is boundless, yet he exhausts himself in seeking their harmony. His wisdom is strained, his spirit cannot keep pace—how could he not be constrained? The Sage transcends fixed knowledge and precedent, follows the natural Dao of Heaven, and aligns with the spontaneous flow of things without private favor. He responds only when moved, always out of necessity, not delight. How could he take pleasure in forcing harmony upon all?

Those who are blind to this cannot establish deep roots through virtue nor set firm boundaries with moderation. They chase after things and forget their own path, failing to find their own fulfillment yet seeking that of others. They labor endlessly, striving only for universal harmony, while harboring both conciliatory and resentful emotions—this is the very essence of obstruction, the *蔽蒙* [blinded and deluded]. Thus, when Zhuangzi says, *'He who finds pleasure in universal harmony is not a Sage,'* is he not correct?

For obstruction means he cannot see clearly, and delusion means he lacks understanding. Burdened by things and hindered, who could truly diminish substance for harmony or attain emptiness for clarity?

The Dao of Heaven has no favorites—it always aligns with the good. The Emperor Huizong’s annotation states: *"If one is good, then Heaven aids him—what favoritism is there?"*

The *Cushui* [commentary] explains: *"Heaven follows natural principles—what is near or distant? Heaven has no partiality—what does it give or take? Though it does not relax in giving or taking, its response is based on things, and it aids only the good."*

For the wisdom of Heaven originates from the wisdom of the people. The good follow their own path, and the people follow them—thus it is fitting that Heaven always aids the good.

The *Book of Documents* says: *"Do good and Heaven will bestow a hundred blessings."* It also says: *"The August Heaven has no favorites—it aids only those with virtue."*

For when accumulated goodness forms virtue, Heaven has reason to aid them. This passage speaks of holding the left contract and ultimately aligning with the Dao of Heaven, to show how the Sage’s Way harmonizes with Heaven’s.

This is why the poem in *Dongzhuo* [洞酌] says, *'The August Heaven favors those with virtue and nourishes those who follow the Way.'*

From the *Small States, Few People* chapter, verse eighty:

*"Small states, few people..."*

Huizong’s annotation states: *"When lands are vast and populations numerous, governance cannot keep pace with demands, wisdom cannot discern all matters, and virtue declines while punishments arise—thus the chaos of later ages begins here."* Laozi, at the end of the Zhou, weary of its disorder, sought the original Dao and embedded this intent in his text, aiming to counter the excesses of ornate culture and restore simplicity. Hence, he spoke of *"small states, few people."*

Indeed, in the age of supreme virtue—from Róngchéng to Shénnóng, with twelve sovereigns renowned for perfect governance—this was their very essence.

The *Cushui* commentary explains:
*"Under Heaven’s vast expanse, all is the king’s land; within its borders, all are the king’s subjects."* Yet why, despite such boundless territory, does Laozi advocate for small states and sparse populations? For when lands expand and people multiply, cunning and deceit flourish, tasks proliferate beyond governance, and wisdom, clouded by artifice, fails to discern truth. Moral decay and licentiousness ensue, offenses multiply, and transgressions defy prohibition—thus the root of later disorder.

Laozi, pitying the decay of contemporary customs, traced the origins of virtue and illuminated profound truths. Through his text, he sought to dismantle the superficial and restore the pure simplicity of antiquity, consolidating scattered elements into unity and stripping away ornamentation to reveal substance. Thus, he spoke of *"small states, few people."*

From Róngchéng, Dàtíng, to Fúxī, and Shénnóng—twelve sovereigns celebrated for perfect governance—also governed by ensuring the people lacked knowledge and desire. In harmony with ignorance, their virtue remained untainted; in harmony with desirelessness, they embodied pristine simplicity. Such was the age of perfect order.

Laozi’s teachings aimed to return the people to this primordial state, hence his emphasis on this principle. He sought to render the people’s abundant tools useless:

Huizong’s annotation: *"Unity without factionalism, free from the burdens of crowds."*

The *Cushui* commentary elaborates:
*"The people possess innate nature—weaving cloth for garments, tilling fields for sustenance—this is called shared virtue. Since their virtue aligns, they need no external comparisons, and self-sustenance suffices. This is unity without factionalism."*

Unity lies in shared nature, while factionalism arises from unnecessary comparisons. When united yet uncompared, they dissolve into the boundless, and the people, until death, do not interact. Even with abundant tools, they find no use for them—how much less the burden of crowds?

Thus, the Sage detests crowds, for crowds breed factionalism. Shun, though thrice relocating to Chengdu, settled at Deng’s ruins with ten thousand households, yet his joy in governance knew no end. When crowds gathered, he welcomed them—Shun could not refuse. Yet such responses were but responses to dust and dross, the remnants of imperial affairs. To wield the world yet remain detached, achieving effortless governance—who but the most divine could do so?

He made death precious, so the people did not flee.

Huizong’s annotation: *"Life brings them joy, death offers burial—thus the people do not lightly abandon their land."*

Confucius said: *"When the ruler loses the Way, the people scatter—long has this been so."* Is this not the meaning of fleeing?

The *Cushui* commentary explains:
*"The people’s greatest concern is to live and die without regret. If life brings joy—honoring parents and nurturing offspring with provision—if death offers burial—garments, coffins, and grave goods are assured—then where is the delight in life or the sorrow in death? How could they lightly abandon their land for distant shores?"*

In Zhou’s heyday, the six provisions sustained the people, ensuring the poor were relieved, and the six customs secured them, guaranteeing ancestral burial. Five clans aided in mutual assistance, four neighborhoods shared funeral rites. Plowing together in fields, dwelling in shared quarters, laboring in common enterprises, defending against shared harms—thus the people did not lightly abandon their land. How much more so in a virtuous realm, where the people are simple and unspoiled, private desires few, and cravings scarce! With no paths through mountains, no bridges across marshes, how could they lightly abandon their land for distant shores?

Yet gathering them posed no difficulty: love them, and they grow close; benefit them, and they assemble. Govern through what they hate, and they disperse. The reason ancient peoples valued life and did not flee was that their rulers upheld the Way, loving and benefiting them.

Confucius said: *"When the ruler loses the Way, the people scatter—long has this been so."* Thus, if the people do not flee, it is because the Way is upheld. Though chariots and arms exist, they serve no purpose.

Huizong’s annotation states: *"Mountains without paths or tunnels, marshes without boats or bridges—this is akin to the people having no knowledge, yet their virtue remains unbroken. Without traces of perilous obstacles, even if chariots and boats exist, they serve no purpose to ride upon; without signs of warfare, even if arms and armor are present, they serve no purpose to deploy."*

The *Shu yi* commentary explains: *"Beyond natural inclinations, there is nothing but material things. When these things become one’s companions, then external pursuits become one’s primary concern. Cravings and calculations multiply, and the people wander and flee without end. If one seeks to draw them far away, then mountains must be carved with paths and tunnels to make passage possible; if one seeks to traverse difficulties, then marshes must be bridged with boats and ferries to be navigated. Even if one wishes to rest and leave no trace, it cannot be achieved. Only in an age of supreme virtue do the people return to their original nature: mountains lack paths or tunnels, marshes lack boats or bridges, yet they do not travel between one another. Their ignorance is like that of infants—dwelling without knowing what to do, moving without knowing where to go. Simple and tranquil, they leave no record of perilous obstacles, so even if chariots and boats exist, they serve no purpose to ride upon. Forgetting distinctions and judgments, they know no signs of warfare, so even if arms and armor are present, they serve no purpose to deploy. Thus, each finds contentment in their natural endowments alone.*

*If one were to restore the use of knotted cords for governance, Huizong’s annotation says: *"It is merely a record of essentials, requiring no written contracts."* The *Shu yi* commentary elaborates: *"The governance of the world must have some means to preserve and transmit enduring clarity for future generations. Thus, in the upper antiquity, knotted cords were used to rule, while later sages replaced them with written contracts, saying that words alone cannot fully record all matters, so they are verified by written texts; deeds alone cannot fully establish trust, so they are supplemented by seals. But when governance reaches its peak of effortless action, when the people are pure and affairs are simple, then bonds need not be enforced with glue or ropes. People act in accordance without needing to be bound by rules—even if knotted cords were used to record essentials, it would suffice to win the trust of the world. Why then require the meticulous detail of written contracts and seals to prevent deceit among the people? Thus, restoring the use of knotted cords is to return the people to the age of primordial simplicity.*

*They find their food sweet, their clothing fine, their customs settled, and their occupations joyful. Huizong’s annotation explains: *"They till the fields to eat, weave to clothe themselves, carry food in their mouths while playing, and drum their bellies in leisure—these are the people’s natural capacities. They are content with their portion, so their food is sweet; they reject ornamentation, so their clothing is fine; they are undisturbed, so their customs are settled; they preserve life, so their occupations are joyful."*

*The *Shu yi* commentary further clarifies: *"When the people return to their original nature, they abandon superficiality and focus on the essentials, spurning useless labor and valuing only practical matters. Their livelihood depends solely on farming and sericulture. Thus, when they till the fields to eat, the grain is unified under cultivation; when they weave to clothe themselves, the silk is unified under spinning. They rise with the sun and rest with the setting of it, with no need for cleverness or skill—only daily sustenance matters, so they carry food in their mouths while playing. Harm and benefit do not unsettle them; they simply adapt to their own ease, so they drum their bellies in leisure. The people’s essential tasks are fulfilled here, and beyond these four matters—sweet food, fine clothing, settled customs, and joyful occupation—there are no further concerns. Sweet food lies in moderation, not in aversion to eating; fine clothing lies in simplicity, not in adornment. Settled customs come from non-interference, free from reckless action. Joyful occupation lies in self-sufficiency, without covetous desires. Without the people returning to their original nature, how could this state be attained?*

*Even neighboring nations gaze upon one another, and the sounds of chickens and dogs intermingle, yet the people live to old age and death without ever visiting one another. Huizong’s annotation says: *"Their dwellings are close, their voices heard, yet they do not travel between one another. At that time, there were no cravings or desires, no artificial actions—only constant spontaneity. This is what is called supreme virtue."*

*The *Shu yi* commentary elaborates: *"Their dwellings are close, yet their paths are not marked by interaction. Their voices are heard, yet their places are distant in spirit. It is fitting that they protect and aid one another, share resources and welcome guests. But even when they reach a state of serene forgetfulness, living to old age and death without ever traveling between one another, it is because they do not know the distinctions that govern interaction—thus, there is no interaction at all. Though mountains lack paths and marshes lack bridges, it is so. When their nature returns to simplicity and desire vanishes, their hearts forget material things and seek nothing—only constant spontaneity. What else could this be but supreme virtue?"*

*Laozi, in the chapter on ‘The Supreme Ultimate,’ says: *"The people say of me that I am effortless."* Indeed, having already spoken of the governance of the Supreme Ultimate as effortless, it is clear that in an age of supreme virtue, the people act without artificial means yet remain constantly spontaneous. There is no doubt of this.

*The chapter on ‘Faithful Words Are Not Beautiful’ (81):*
*"Faithful words are not beautiful,*

「The Emperor Huizong annotates: *"When the Dao emerges in speech, it is bland to the point of tastelessness, yet it does not shame even the hundred sages nor decay through the ten thousand ages."*

*The Commentary explains: *"The Dao is not mere words; without words, its profundity cannot be revealed. Words are not the Dao; without the Dao, their foundation cannot be established. When the Dao manifests in words, it is bland yet without taste, rooted in principle and meaning—far beyond the sweetness of coarse grains and the beauty of rich delicacies. It can be practiced to verify truth and examined to resolve doubts. When combined, it aligns like matching seals; when upright, it is easy to follow. Thus, it does not shame even the hundred sages. When firm as metal and stone, it is essential yet easy to uphold. Thus, it endures through the ten thousand ages without flaw. If so, then faithful words grounded in the Dao—what need is there for them to be beautiful? Beautiful words lack faithfulness."*

*The Emperor Huizong annotates: *"‘Proper speech’ is ornate; those who indulge in elaborate rhetoric use mere embellishments as their core, thus lacking faithfulness."*

*The Commentary explains: *"Ornate speech lacks substance; what lacks substance is merely empty. Thus, ornate speech is empty, while profound speech is real. Indulging in elaborate rhetoric leads to words that fail to convey truth, using mere embellishments as their core, thus failing to grasp the essence. If such is the case, then the farther one strays from the Dao. Though the end may still be called ‘speech’ and the Dao may be exhausted, it is enough to please those who seek beauty in sound—yet where is the substance to demonstrate faithfulness? Thus, it lacks faithfulness."*

*"The good do not argue,*
*The Emperor Huizong annotates: *"Speech should emphasize essence and principle."*

*The Commentary explains: *"When one attains completeness, it is called ‘essence’; when all essences converge, it is called ‘principle.’ Speech that upholds essence and principle as its standard achieves the fullness of the Dao and contributes to ultimate truth. When such speech is established, it is always appropriate. Though it does not rely on elaborate argumentation, its profound meaning is fully preserved—enough to satisfy what people desire to hear. This is what is meant by ‘the good do not argue.’ Formerly, Confucius examined the Twelve Classics and said: ‘The essence lies in the Seven Meanings.’ Mencius, who followed Confucius, when pressed for words, said: ‘Do I truly enjoy argumentation?’ His words were: ‘I study extensively and explain in detail, only to return to concise speech.’ Yet Yang Xiong said: ‘The essence of speech is conciseness.’ Is this not the meaning of ‘the good do not argue’? Argumentation is not good."*

*The Emperor Huizong annotates: *"Excessive elaboration leads to many digressions, straying from the Dao’s ultimate correctness."*

*The Commentary explains: *"Excessive speech exhausts itself; it is better to uphold the middle. Indeed, the labor of argumentation is like that of a mosquito—far from the Dao’s ultimate correctness. As for Gongsun Long’s sophistry and Hui Shi’s manifold schemes, it is merely the toil of a mosquito. This is why argumentation is not good."*

*"The wise are not learned,*
*The Emperor Huizong annotates: *"To grasp the subtleties of the Dao, one must return to its simplicity."*

*The Commentary explains: *"The Dao’s simplicity does not complicate; those who know its subtleties understand it in a single word, preserving it in direct perception. A little suffices—why seek learning? The Classic says: ‘What is learned yet unnamed is called subtlety.’ Exploring subtlety reveals its formless, hidden nature. Only when one returns to simplicity and speaks to the extreme can this be known. Zhuangzi says: ‘Knowledge of it is shallow; ignorance of its depth is profound.’ The learned do not know. The Emperor Huizong annotates: ‘What is heard and seen is less valuable than simplicity.’ Zhuangzi says: ‘Learning is not necessary for knowledge; argumentation is not necessary for wisdom.’ The Commentary explains: ‘When studying the Dao, one must daily diminish. The Dao’s formless realm is perceived only by the spirit, its soundless expanse only by the qi. Vision and hearing do not depend on the ears and eyes—why value the multitude of what is heard and seen? The good progressors of the Dao say: ‘Uphold simplicity’ and ‘speak concisely.’ Indeed, ‘it is better to uphold simplicity.’ Laozi, in response to Confucius’ inquiry about the ultimate Dao, also said: ‘The learned do not know; the argumentative are not wise.’ Thus, the subtleties of knowing the Dao are not attained through mere learning and observation—this is clear."*

*"The Sage has no accumulation,*
*The Emperor Huizong annotates: *"There is accumulation; thus, it is insufficient. There is no hoarding; thus, there is abundance."*

*Zhuangzi says: *"The Dao of the Sage flows without accumulation."*

*Confucius says: *"I thus daily depart."*

*The Commentary explains: *"The Dao’s ultimate emptiness has never contained anything. Things are boundless, all confined within the Dao. The Dao is obscure in its nothingness, thus empty yet responsive; things are stagnant in their existence, yet how much do they differ? Like grass in its growth, it is taken as if it would never end, yet when gathered, it is sometimes exhausted. This is accumulation—thus, it is insufficient. But emptiness without accumulation is different. Like a mirror reflecting forms, all beauty and ugliness are revealed; like a valley echoing sounds, all goodness and evil are answered. This is why there is no hoarding, yet there is abundance. The Sage embodies the Dao’s ultimate emptiness, flowing without accumulation. Six senses and four limbs—there is nothing the Sage is not present in. Responding in time, it is merely the remnants of the Dao, never obstructing reality. Thus, the Sage can manifest in transformation and stand alone in heaven. Zhuangzi, in his discussion of the Dao of Heaven, the Dao of the Emperor, and the Dao of the Sage, all said: ‘It flows without accumulation.’ This is the case. Confucius attained this Dao, reaching boundless freedom and leaving no trace. It was not merely received and preserved—his form did not change until its end. Thus, he said: ‘I thus daily depart.’ Though he has departed, how could he be lost in mere emptiness and stillness, preventing students from entering his gate? He simply does not cling to traces of existence, offering them instead to forget in nothingness. Though he forgets the past ‘I,’ there is an ‘I’ that does not forget. Giving to others, he becomes ever more abundant; sharing with others, he becomes ever more plentiful."*

*The Emperor Huizong annotates: *"The good are generous and complete, yet never exhausted. All things borrow from them without depletion."*

The *Explanatory Commentary* states: The Dao reaches the utmost non-being, yet fulfills all things’ demands. It is poured into without overflowing, drawn from without exhaustion, and suffices all things without ever being depleted. All things flourishing between Heaven and Earth, their growth and formation—all draw from it without depletion, for it moves yet never accumulates.

The Sage attains the Dao, yet never hoards it for himself. Giving to others, he becomes ever more abundant; sharing with others, he ever more plentiful. Responding to the myriad needs from the foundation of emptiness and stillness, his embrace is boundless and all-encompassing, taking as needed and always sufficient—how could it not be amply abundant?

The Dao of Heaven benefits without harming.

*The Emperor Huizong annotates:* *"Only the *Qian* [Creative] can benefit all under Heaven with its beauty. Though it does not speak of what it benefits, all things are truly benefited, and there is never any harm."*

The *Explanatory Commentary* states: *Qian* is the Dao of Heaven. Great indeed is the primal *Qian*! All things draw their origin from it. Its transformative mechanism subtly shifts within the boundless void—it opens and gives birth, extends and circulates, and through this, all things are formed. All precious treasures flourish in the grand assembly of *Heng* [Abundance] and *Jia* [Auspiciousness], none but the beauty of benefiting all under Heaven.

The *Odes* sing of abundant harvests, saying: *"Much millet, much glutinous rice."* For millet thrives in high, dry, and cold places, while glutinous rice favors low, moist, and hot conditions. Speaking of them as "much" reveals Heaven’s beneficence knows no limits—though it does not name what it benefits, the benefit is inherent.

Zhuangzi said: *"Heaven and Earth possess great beauty, yet do not speak of their beneficence."* How then could there be any harm?

The Sage’s Dao acts yet does not contend.

*The Emperor Huizong annotates:*
*"It follows without opposing; its motion is like water.
It responds without hoarding; its stillness is like a mirror.
It harmonizes without leading; its response is like an echo.
Though it acts, its action arises from non-action—it embodies Heaven itself. What contention could there be?"*

This virtue aligns with the Dao, hence the *Classic of Virtue* concludes with it.

The *Explanatory Commentary* states: The Sage applies the Dao to all under Heaven, nurturing things according to their nature, aiding their spontaneous unfolding. Thus, it follows without opposing, its motion like water—what is called *"timely action."* Supplying all demands, it effortlessly transforms from non-being to being; responding without hoarding, its stillness is like a *yín* [balance scale]—what is called *"steadfast stillness."* Attending to things’ responses, gentle and still in itself, it harmonizes without initiating—its response is like an echo—what is called *"preserving the feminine."* These three aspects, within oneself without attachment, let things manifest naturally. It is neither non-action nor action, but non-action that encompasses all action.

Though establishing myriad laws, its action stems from non-action. Abandoning cleverness and fixed ways, it follows Heaven’s natural order.

The Dao of Heaven is a single *qi* in motion, all things flourishing in abundance. It acts without effort yet remains ever clear, conquers without contention. The Sage embodies Heaven to govern the world, playing with the Creator—its Dao is subtle yet vast, in motion weaving the myriad realms, in stillness mirroring Heaven and Earth. Universally responsive yet never at odds, thus it acts without contending. This virtue aligns with the Dao, hence the *Classic of Virtue* concludes with it.

Laozi, in concluding the *Classic of Virtue*, speaks of aligning with the Dao, for the Dao in others is called *dao*, while the Dao within is called *de*. Virtue encompasses the unity of the Dao; only when virtue advances beyond the Dao can one speak of its utmost expression. When Dao and virtue reach this state, is not the intent of the *Classic* profound?

I have often reflected: Though the Dao is unspeakable and hidden, it is not as clear as when spoken. Laozi, pitying the excess of rhetoric in his time, sought to restore the purity of Heaven and Earth and the essence of antiquity. To return to the primal simplicity, he composed ninety-nine chapters, elucidating the intent of Dao and virtue to awaken all under Heaven and future generations. He spoke only when necessary, hence in the final passage, he explains his purpose in writing the *Classic*: *"Faithful words are not ornate; eloquent speakers are not virtuous,"* urging students to delve beyond the words to grasp their mystery, transcending speech itself.

Like Zhuangzi’s *Book* concluding with the *Heaven Down*, it delves deeply into the origin of the Great Dao, forcefully rejecting the obfuscations of a hundred schools. He himself claimed his writing was "erratic and absurd," loosening the traces of his composition.

Examining the *Zhuzi*, none surpass Zhuangzi in wisdom to comprehend Laozi. Their works complement each other—how could it be merely a matter of verbal artistry?

Tracing his critique of the Six Classics, they reduce to the *Odes’* aspirations, the *Documents’* deeds, the *Rites’* practices, the *Music’* harmony, and the *Changes’* yin-yang, along with the *Spring and Autumn’*s distinctions of rank—hardly confined to pedantic commentary. Thus, the truly wise are not merely erudite.

Yet when discussing the varied excellences of myriad arts—from Mozi downward to Huishi’s multifarious schemes—his *Five Cartloads* of writings are contradictory and inconsistent, ever more distant from the Dao. This reveals that extensive knowledge without insight is blind.

Speaking of the Sage’s non-accumulation through emptiness, it is clear that giving to others increases his abundance, sharing with others multiplies his plenty.

Observing the Sage nurture all things and harmonize the world, he must take Heaven as his standard, demonstrating the Dao’s beneficence without harm.

Observing the establishment of the ever-present non-being, it must be expressed through gentle, weak, humble, and lowly conduct, revealing the Sage’s Dao acts without contending.

Though Laozi and Zhuangzi’s words differ, their Dao resonates without explicit agreement. Examining their concluding intent, this is the true grasp. Respectfully submitted.

*The Explanatory Commentary on the Authentic Classic of Dao and Virtue* concludes here, Volume Fourteen.
